# 孙子批注

**Sunzi with Commentaries**

**Title:** *Sunzi with Commentaries*. Also known as *Sunzi with Ten Commentaries*. Thirteen fascicles.
**Source Text:** *Daoist Canon of the Orthodox Tradition* (Zhengtong Daozang), Taiqing Section.
**Collated Editions:**
1. Song Dynasty edition of *Sunzi with Eleven Commentaries*
2. *Sunzi with Ten Commentaries* from *Collected Masters of the Hundred Schools*
3. Guo Huai’en’s *Sunzi with Eleven Commentaries*

**Table of Contents** *(added by the editor)*
Fascicle 1: *Planning*
Fascicle 2: *Waging War*
Fascicle 3: *Strategic Offensive*
Fascicle 4: *Form*
Fascicle 5: *Potential*
Fascicle 6: *Weakness and Strength*
Fascicle 7: *Military Contention*
Fascicle 8: *Nine Variations*
Fascicle 9: *Marching the Army*
Fascicle 10: *Terrain*
Fascicle 11: *Nine Terrains*
Fascicle 12: *Fire Attack*
Fascicle 13: *Using Spies*

---

**Sunzi with Commentaries, Fascicle One**
**Planning**

Cao Cao says: "Planning" entails selecting generals, assessing the enemy, measuring terrain, estimating troops, judging distances, and evaluating dangers—all deliberated in the ancestral temple.

Li Quan says: "Planning" is the foremost aspect of warfare. The *Taiyi Dunjia* first applies the spirit of calculation to the Palace of Virtue to determine victory or defeat between host and guest. Thus, Sunzi also places "planning" at the head of his treatise.

Du Mu says: "Planning" means calculation. What is calculated? The five matters below—namely, the Dao/Way, Heaven, Earth, the general, and the law. In the ancestral temple, one first calculates the relative strengths of these five matters between oneself and the enemy, then determines victory or defeat. Only after victory or defeat is settled does one mobilize the army. No aspect of warfare precedes these five matters, hence their placement at the beginning of the treatise.

Wang Xi says: "Planning" refers to evaluating the sovereign and generals, Heaven and Earth, laws and commands, troops and soldiers, rewards and punishments.

Zhang Yu says: Guanzi states: "Plans are first settled within before the army crosses the border." Thus, the Dao/Way of warfare takes planning as its foundation.

Some ask: If warfare values adapting to the enemy on the spot, why does Cao Cao speak of planning in the ancestral temple? The answer: How can one not first calculate the general’s wisdom or folly, the enemy’s strength or weakness, the distance of the terrain, or the size of the army? Yet when the two armies meet and circumstances shift in response, victory depends on the general’s judgment—it cannot be predicted by mere estimation.

---

Sunzi says: Warfare is a state’s great affair.

Du Mu says: The *Tradition* states: "A state’s great affairs lie in sacrifice and war."

Zhang Yu says: A state’s survival or peril rests on warfare. Thus, discussing and drilling the military is the foremost duty.

It is the ground of life and death, the Dao/Way of survival and extinction—it cannot but be examined.

Li Quan says: Warfare is an instrument of misfortune; life and death, survival and extinction hinge upon it. This is why it is treated with gravity, lest people take it lightly.

Du Mu says: A state’s survival or extinction, a person’s life or death—all depend on warfare, hence the need for careful examination.

Jia Lin says: "Ground" refers to the place—where armies are arrayed, troops are marshaled, and battles are fought. Gain its advantage, and one lives; lose its convenience, and one dies—hence "the ground of life and death." The "Dao/Way" is the Dao/Way of seizing opportunities to establish victory. Grasp it, and one survives; lose it, and one perishes—hence "it cannot but be examined." The *Book of Documents* says: "Those who possess the Dao/Way of survival are aided and strengthened; those who possess the Dao/Way of extinction are overthrown and destroyed."

Mei Yaochen says: Terrain has the potential for life or death; warfare has the Dao/Way of survival or extinction.

Wang Xi says: Once warfare is undertaken, life and death, survival and extinction are tied to it.

Zhang Yu says: The people’s life or death is foretold here, and thus the state’s survival or extinction is revealed there. That life and death are called "ground" and survival and extinction "Dao/Way" is because life and death lie in the terrain of victory or defeat, while survival and extinction depend on the Dao/Way of gain or loss. How could one not weigh and examine this with utmost care?

Therefore, it is tested by the five matters, compared through calculation, and the situation is sought.

Cao Cao says: This refers to the five matters and seven calculations below, through which the situation of oneself and the enemy is sought.

Li Quan says: This refers to the five matters below. "Compared" means measured. Measure and calculate distances to discern the situation and respond to the enemy.

Du Mu says: "Tested" means assessed; the "five" are the five matters mentioned below. "Compared" means weighed; "calculation" refers to the calculations at the beginning of the treatise. "Sought" means searched; "situation" refers to the situation of oneself and the enemy. This means one must first assess the strengths and weaknesses of the five matters, then weigh the gains and losses of the calculations, and only then can one search out the situation of victory or defeat between oneself and the enemy.

Jia Lin says: Weigh the calculations of oneself and the enemy, search out the true situation of both armies, and then strengths and weaknesses will be known, victory and defeat easily seen.

Mei Yaochen says: Order the five matters, weigh and determine the advantages of the calculations.

Wang Xi says: "Tested" means constant, or warp and weft. "Calculation" refers to the seven calculations below. "Sought" means exhausted. The great warp and weft of warfare do not exceed the Dao/Way, Heaven, Earth, the general, and the law. Compare them through the seven calculations, and only then can the situation of victory or defeat between oneself and the enemy be fully grasped.

Zhang Yu says: "Tested" means ordered. Above, one first orders the sequence of the five matters; below, one uses the five matters to weigh the strengths and weaknesses of oneself and the enemy, exploring the situation of victory or defeat.

First: the Dao/Way.

Zhang Yu says: Win the people’s trust through kindness and good faith.

Second: Heaven.

Zhang Yu says: Follow the seasons of Heaven above.

Third: Earth.

Zhang Yu says: Know the advantages of Earth below.

Fourth: the general.

Zhang Yu says: Entrust [command] to the worthy and capable.

Fifth: the law.

Du Mu says: These are called the five matters.

Wang Xi says: These are the five matters of the test.

The Dao/Way of warfare takes harmony among the people as its root, with Heaven’s timing and Earth’s advantage as its aids.

When these three are complete, only then may one discuss raising troops. To raise troops, one must first have a capable general; with a capable general, the laws are then refined. This is the sequence that Master Sun establishes.

Zhang Yu says: [The laws must be] strict and clear in regulation.

That the general and the law occupy the last positions among the five matters is because, whenever one raises troops to punish transgressions, in the ancestral temple one first examines the depth of kindness and good faith, then assesses the alignment of Heaven’s seasons, and next scrutinizes the ease or difficulty of the terrain. Only when these three are thoroughly understood does one appoint a general to lead the campaign. Once the army crosses the border, all laws and commands are then subject to the general. This is the proper sequence.

The Dao/Way means ensuring the people share the ruler’s intent.

Zhang Yu says: By governing the masses with kindness, good faith, and righteousness, the Three Armies become of one heart and gladly serve.

The *Yijing* says: "Delighted to face difficulty, the people forget death." Thus, one may share life and death with them, and they will not fear danger.

Cao Cao says: This means guiding them with teachings and commands.

"Danger" refers to peril and doubt.

Li Quan says: "Danger" means destruction. When the people are guided by the Dao/Way, they naturally transform; united in purpose, how could they perish?

Du Mu says: The Dao/Way is benevolence and righteousness. When Li Si asked Xun Qing about warfare, he replied: "Benevolence and righteousness are what govern the administration of state. When governance is well-ordered, the people grow close to their superiors, delight in their ruler, and readily die for them." Later, in addressing King Xiaocheng of Zhao on warfare, he said: "When a hundred generals share one heart and the Three Armies unite their strength, the ministers’ service to their lord and the subordinates’ service to their superiors become like a child’s service to a father or a younger brother’s to an elder—like arms defending the head and eyes or shielding the chest and belly. Only then can one ensure that they share the ruler’s intent, unite in life and death, and do not fear peril or doubt."

Chen Hao’s annotation agrees with Du Mu.

Meng Shi says: One version reads "the people do not doubt," meaning unwavering loyalty; another reads "the people are not in danger." The Dao/Way refers to guiding with government decrees and unifying through ritual instruction, thus transforming and winning over officers and people to share a single purpose with their superiors. Therefore, the subtlety of warfare lies in employing authority and strategy as the Dao/Way. When the Great Dao declines, laws arise; when laws decline, authority arises; when authority declines, power arises; when power declines, strategy arises; when strategy declines, calculations arise. As the Great Dao falls into decay and human nature grows deceitful, one can only achieve one’s aims through authority and calculation. Thus, the Dao/Way of authority and strategy ensures that superiors and subordinates advance together, share the same loves and hates, and unite in their interests. Therefore, the people’s hearts turn toward virtue, and one gains their strength to the utmost degree of selflessness. Thus, even with a million troops, their hearts become as one, and one may unite with them in risking death—moving as one without falling into peril or destruction.

The ministers’ service to their lord and the subordinates’ service to their superiors become like a child’s service to a father or a younger brother’s to an elder—like arms defending the head and eyes or shielding the chest and belly. Only then can one share the ruler’s intent, unite in life and death, and not fear peril or doubt.

Jia Lin says: When a general takes the Dao/Way as his heart and shares both profit and hardship with the people, the officers and soldiers submit, and their hearts naturally align with their superiors. To make soldiers cherish their commander like parents and view the enemy as hated foes is impossible without the Dao/Way.

The Yellow Stone Elder said: "Those who attain the Dao/Way prosper; those who lose it perish."

Du You says: This means guiding with government decrees and unifying through ritual instruction. "Danger" refers to doubt; when the ruler practices benevolence, the people are willing to give their lives. Thus, in facing life-and-death crises, they do not fear collapse or peril. For example, during the siege of Jinyang, when stoves sank and frogs bred, the people harbored no thoughts of rebellion or doubt.

Mei Yaochen says: "Danger" means perversity. When the ruler possesses the Dao/Way, governance and instruction prevail; when people’s hearts are united, perversity and danger depart. Thus, if the ruler is secure, the people are secure; if the ruler is in peril, the people are in peril.

Wang Xi says: The Dao/Way refers to the ruler possessing the Dao/Way and being able to win the people’s hearts. Those who win the people’s hearts gain their utmost effort; those who gain their utmost effort can overcome crises. The *Yijing* says: "Delighted to face difficulty, the people forget death." In such a case, how could they fear perilous or difficult matters?

Zhang Yu says: "Danger" means doubt. When soldiers are moved by kindness, they share life and death with their superiors, resolutely without fear or hesitation.

Heaven refers to yin and yang, cold and heat, and the constraints of the seasons.

Cao Cao says: Execute punishments in accord with Heaven, following the constraints of yin and yang and the four seasons.

Thus, the *Methods of Sima* states: "Do not mobilize troops in winter or summer, to show universal love for the people."

Li Quan says: Respond to Heaven and align with the people, seizing opportunities according to the seasons.

Du Mu says: Yin and yang refer to the Five Phases, the alternation of punishment and virtue, and the orientation of advance and retreat. The movements of the Five Planets are the most reliable indicators. Wu Xian, Gan De, Shi Shen, Tang Meng, Shi Mo, Zi Shen, and Bi Zao—all have written works, each claiming profound secrets. Examining their essential points, they all ultimately derive from human affairs.

The *Star Canon* states: "The region where Jupiter resides must not be attacked; attacking it will bring calamity upon oneself." In the *Zuozhuan*, thirty-second year of Duke Zhao: In summer, Wu attacked Yue, first deploying troops against it. Shi Mo said: "In less than forty years, Yue will possess Wu. Yue is favored by Jupiter, yet Wu attacks it—it will surely suffer misfortune." The commentary states: "The cycle of survival and destruction does not exceed three twelve-year periods, or thirty-six years. Hence, it is said 'less than forty years.'" That year, Jupiter was in the Star Marker, which governs Wu’s region. The state where Jupiter resides enjoys good fortune; Wu initiated the attack, so it suffered misfortune instead. In the twenty-second year of Duke Ai, Yue destroyed Wu—thirty-eight years had passed.

Li Chunfeng says: When the world rose against Qin, Jupiter gathered in the Eastern Well. Qin’s governance was tyrannical, losing Jupiter’s benevolent and harmonious principle, violating Jupiter’s reverent and solemn Dao/Way, rejecting remonstrance and trusting slander. Thus, Huhai ultimately perished.

It further states: When Jupiter appears clear, bright, and radiant, the state in its celestial division enjoys great fortune. If the ruler’s commands align with the seasons, Jupiter shines with joy, the year yields abundance, and the people live in peace. If the ruler is cruel and oppressive, causing hardship to the people, Jupiter’s color sharpens into angry spikes, and war arises. From this, we see that Jupiter’s presence may bring either fortune or calamity—does this not all stem from human affairs?

The rulers of Wu and Yue possessed equal virtue and matched strength. When Helü launched his campaign, his ambition was to annihilate Yue, not to relieve the people’s suffering. Thus, Jupiter bestowed fortune upon Yue and disaster upon Wu. Qin’s tyranny provoked the world to rise against it, aligning with Heaven’s will, so Jupiter brought ruin to Qin and favored Han.

Mars is the Star of Punishment. When Duke Jing of Song uttered a single virtuous word, Mars retreated three celestial stations, extending his reign by twenty-seven years. From this, we deduce: Jupiter, the Star of Benevolence, does not bless the unrighteous; Mars, the Star of Punishment, does not punish the virtuous. These two examples suffice—all else may be inferred. Moreover, the celestial divisions Jupiter traverses respond to the quality of governance: its color, brilliance, and spikes shift in size, heralding fortune or misfortune, each interpreted according to its time.

Li Chunfeng says: Physical forms manifest below, while subtle images correspond above. Observe the body: ears and eyes serve the liver and kidneys; nose and mouth sustain the heart and abdomen. Do they not influence one another? As the *Book of Changes* states: "In Heaven, images form; on Earth, shapes take form—transformation is evident." Yet all ultimately derives from human affairs.

The theories of "facing virtue and turning from punishment" are particularly unreliable. These are but celestial configurations: armies arrayed with their backs to water are doomed, while those facing mountains or slopes are defeated. Yet when King Wu attacked Zhou, he deployed with his back to the Ji River and faced mountains and slopes—with a mere twenty-two thousand five hundred men, he crushed Zhou’s millions and destroyed him.

Consider what we have witnessed: from the Yuanhe era to the present, in thirty years, the state has launched four campaigns against the Zhao rebels of the昭义 Army. With forces from multiple fronts, numbering a hundred thousand in name, they besieged Lincheng County. They attacked from the south but failed to breach it; from the north, east, and west—all in vain. Four sieges spanned ten years, during which time, in all directions, was there ever a moment when "facing virtue and turning from punishment" aligned with an auspicious hour? Their failure stemmed not from celestial omens but from the city’s strength: its walls were high, its moats deep, its grain abundant, and its people united.

Examining past events confirms this: Qin triumphed in war for generations, ultimately annihilating the six states. Was Heaven’s Dao fixed in the northwest for two hundred years, with fortune constantly residing in the Quail’s Head? Was it not rather that from Duke Mu onward, Qin humbled itself to seek scholars, focused on agriculture and warfare, and governed through clear laws and commands?

Thus, when King Hui of Liang asked Master Weiliao, "The Yellow Emperor had the arts of punishment and virtue, enabling him to win every battle—did he truly possess them?" Master Weiliao replied: "Not so. The Yellow Emperor’s 'punishment and virtue' meant using punishment to conquer and virtue to defend. This is not what the world calls punishment and virtue. Those who employ the worthy and utilize the capable do not rely on timing to succeed; those who clarify laws and enforce commands do not need divination to prosper; those who honor merit and nurture labor do not require prayers to receive fortune."

When King Wu of Zhou attacked Zhou, his army encamped at the Gongtou Mountain by the Si River. A violent storm arose—wind and rain lashed, drums and banners shattered, and the king’s attendant in the chariot trembled in terror, nearly dying of fright. The Grand Duke said: "In warfare, following Heaven’s Dao does not guarantee victory, nor does defying it ensure defeat. If human affairs are neglected, the entire army will perish. Heaven’s Dao and spirits are invisible and inaudible—thus the wise do not rely on them, while fools are bound by them. If one esteems the worthy and employs the capable, acts in accord with the times, then affairs will prosper without regard to timing, success will come without divination, and fortune will follow without prayer." He then ordered the advance.

The Duke of Zhou said: "Now we march against Jupiter’s position, the tortoise-shell cracks with ill omens, divination is inauspicious, and stars portend disaster—let us withdraw." The Grand Duke grew angry: "Zhou has dismembered Bi Gan, imprisoned Ji Zi, and entrusted governance to Fei Kang—why should we not attack? How can withered grass and rotten bones know anything?" He burned the tortoise shell, snapped the divining stalks, and led the charge, with King Wu following. Thus, they destroyed Zhou.

When Emperor Gaozu of Song besieged Murong Chao in Guanggu, he prepared to attack. His generals all remonstrated: "Today is a day of 'departing and perishing'—a taboo for military affairs." The emperor said: "If I depart, he perishes—what greater fortune could there be?" He ordered the troops to scale the walls, and Guanggu fell.

When Emperor Wu of Northern Wei campaigned against Murong Lin of Later Yan, he advanced on the last day of the month, the day of Jiazi. The Grand Astrologer Chou Chong memorialized: "In ancient times, Zhou perished on a Jiazi day." The emperor replied: "Did not King Wu of Zhou also triumph on a Jiazi day?" Chong had no answer. The emperor then crushed his enemy.

When Emperor Taiwu of Northern Wei campaigned against Xia’s Helian Chang at Tongwan City, his army encamped beneath the walls. Chang advanced with drums roaring and battle cries. Just then, wind and rain swept in from behind the enemy. The Grand Astrologer advised: "Heaven does not aid us. Our troops suffer from hunger and thirst—we should withdraw for now." Cui Hao countered: "A thousand *li* of victory hinges on this single day—how can we alter our course? The wind’s course lies with man, not with constancy." The emperor followed his counsel, and Chang’s forces were utterly routed.

Some ask: If this is so, then the alignment of yin and yang cannot be trusted—why then does *The Art of War* discuss it? The answer: Tyrannical and benighted rulers, obsessed with a single treasure or steed, will stop at nothing to satisfy their will. Without invoking the Way of Heaven or the spirits, who could restrain them? Thus, *The Art of War* addresses it, for there is profound meaning therein. Cold and heat, the qi of the seasons—these regulate advance and retreat.

When Zhou Yu aided Sun Quan in enumerating Cao Cao’s four defeats, the first was: "Now, in the depths of winter, horses lack forage, and the northern troops, crossing rivers and lakes, unaccustomed to the climate, will surely fall ill—this is a taboo in warfare." Cold and heat alike belong to the timing of Heaven, and so they are discussed together.

Master Meng said: "Warfare follows the movement of Heaven. Yin and yang are the ebb and flow of softness and strength. Employing yin yields emptiness, solidity, and stillness; employing yang yields swiftness, ferocity, and vehemence. To follow is to use yin; to lead is to use yang. Yin must not be concealed; yang must not be exposed." The forms of yin and yang have no fixed shape—thus, the art of war emulates Heaven. Heaven has cold and heat; war has life and death. Heaven responds to destruction by shaping things; war responds to opportunity by shaping circumstances. Hence, it is said: "This is Heaven."

Jia Lin said: "The 'timing' in 'timing and qi' refers to seizing favorable moments and assessing the advantages of climate." Du You said: "It means conducting punitive expeditions in accord with Heaven, following the yin-yang and four-seasonal regulations of hardness and softness." Mei Yaochen said: "Warfare must align with the Way of Heaven, conform to qi and climate, and be regulated by timing—this is what is meant by 'regulation.'"

*The Methods of the Sima* states: "No campaigns are launched in winter or summer, to show universal care for the people." Wang Xi said: "Yin and yang encompass the Way of Heaven, the Five Phases, the four seasons, wind, clouds, and atmospheric phenomena. Skillfully observing their waxing and waning aids military victory. Yet without extraordinary individuals imparting their secrets, such knowledge remains inaccessible. Consider Huangshi Gong bestowing his book upon Zhang Liang—this was the *Military Methods of the Grand Duke*. Is it not that the divine mechanisms of Heaven are too profound for ordinary minds to grasp? The myriad schools diverge in their views, and none can claim certainty."

Cold and heat, as Wu Qi noted, include fierce winds, bitter cold, scorching summers, and blazing heat. "Timing and regulation" means adapting to the advantages and disadvantages of the moment. Fan Li said: "When Heaven’s timing is not propitious, do not act as the invader." Zhang Yu said: "Yin and yang do not refer to the auspices of solitary emptiness or alignment. Rather, warfare itself possesses yin and yang." Fan Li said: "To follow is to use yin; to lead is to use yang. Exhaust the enemy’s yang rhythm, fill our yin rhythm, and seize the advantage." He also said: "Position the right as the female, strengthen the left as the male, and adjust timing to accord with the Way of Heaven."

Li Jing explained: "Left and right are the yin and yang of man; early and late are the yin and yang of Heaven; the extraordinary and the orthodox are the yin and yang of Heaven and man in transformation." All these speak of warfare’s inherent use of yin-yang and hardness-softness, not the yin-yang of astrological omens. Now, consider *The Master of Military Preparedness*—its chapter on celestial omens makes this meaning most clear. *The Tai Bai Yin Jing* also includes a section titled "Heaven Has No Yin or Yang," both placed at the beginning to dispel the world’s confusion.

The Grand Duke said: "The sages sought to end the chaos of later ages, so they composed cryptic texts, ascribing victory to the Way of Heaven—though it avails nothing in warfare." This, too, is the case. Emperor Taizong of Tang likewise said: "No weapon is more baleful than war. If military action benefits human affairs, why hesitate over taboos?"

"Cold and heat" refer to campaigning in winter or summer. When the Han dynasty campaigned against the Xiongnu, many soldiers lost fingers. When Ma Yuan campaigned against the Man tribes, many troops died of plague—both due to winter and summer campaigns. "Timing and regulation" means conducting punitive expeditions in accord with Heaven’s timing. *The Tai Bai Yin Jing* states that "Heaven’s timing" refers to natural disasters like floods, droughts, locusts, and hail, not the auspices of solitary emptiness or alignment.

"Terrain" encompasses distance and proximity, peril and ease, breadth and narrowness, life and death. Cao Cao said: "The nine types of terrain differ in form, and one must adapt to timing to exploit their advantages." This is discussed in the chapter on the nine terrains. Li Quan said: "Favorable terrain creates conditions of life and death." Mei Yaochen said: "Understand the advantages and disadvantages of terrain." Zhang Yu said: "In warfare, it is essential to first know the terrain. Knowing distance enables planning for indirect and direct routes; knowing peril and ease enables assessing the advantages of infantry and cavalry; knowing breadth and narrowness enables calculating the use of many or few troops; knowing life and death enables recognizing the dynamics of battle and retreat."

"Generals" embody wisdom, trustworthiness, benevolence, courage, and strictness. Cao Cao said: "A general should possess all five virtues." Li Quan said: "These five are the virtues of a general, hence the term 'elder' for a commander." Du Mu said: "The Way of the former kings placed benevolence first; among military strategists, wisdom takes precedence. Wisdom enables adaptability and discernment; trustworthiness ensures clarity in rewards and punishments; benevolence means loving people and pitying their toil; courage means decisive victory and seizing opportunities without hesitation; strictness means disciplining the army with authority and punishment."

When Shen Baoxu of Chu was sent as an envoy to Yue, King Goujian of Yue, preparing to attack Wu, asked about warfare. He replied: "In warfare, wisdom comes first, followed by benevolence, then courage."

Without wisdom, one cannot comprehend the people’s limits nor measure the multitude or scarcity of the realm. Without benevolence, one cannot share the hardships of hunger and toil with the three armies. Without courage, one cannot resolve doubts to initiate great plans.

Jia Lin said: "Relying solely on wisdom leads to treachery; partial benevolence results in weakness; rigid adherence to trustworthiness breeds folly; dependence on brute force turns to violence; excessive strictness becomes cruelty. Only when all five are balanced and applied appropriately can one become a commander."

Mei Yaochen said: "Wisdom enables strategy; trustworthiness ensures rewards and punishments; benevolence unites the masses; courage enables decisiveness; strictness establishes authority."

Wang Xi said: "Wisdom means foresight without confusion, the ability to plan, and adaptability to change. Trustworthiness means unified commands. Benevolence means compassionate governance that wins hearts. Courage means fearlessness in righteousness and resolute action. Strictness means disciplining the masses with authority. All five are interdependent; lacking one renders the rest incomplete."

Thus, Cao Cao said: "A general should possess all five virtues." He Shi said: "Without wisdom, one cannot assess the enemy or respond to circumstances. Without trustworthiness, one cannot train subordinates or lead troops. Without transformation (benevolence), one cannot unite the masses or comfort soldiers. Without courage, one cannot decide strategies or engage in battle. Without strictness, one cannot subdue the strong or unify the ranks. Mastery of these five talents defines a general’s essence."

Zhang Yu said: "Wisdom must not be confused; trustworthiness must not be deceived; benevolence must not be oppressive; courage must not be intimidated; strictness must not be violated. Only with all five virtues can one become a great general."

**Laws** refer to organizational structures, official hierarchies, and logistical systems.

Cao Cao said: "Organizational structures encompass the arrangement of troops, banners, drums, and signals. Officials refer to the division of ranks. Logistics pertain to supply routes. Logistics officers manage military expenditures."

Li Quan said: "Troops are organized into units; regulations govern their discipline. Officials oversee ranks and rewards. Logistics concern supply routes. Officers manage resources and expenditures—all standard military protocols under a commander’s governance."

Du Mu said: "Units are organized into formations with clear divisions. Regulations govern the use of drums, flags, and signals. Officials include officers and commanders, each with defined roles. Logistics involve the routes for encampments and deployments. Officers manage storerooms, supplies, and support staff. Expenditures cover chariots, horses, weapons, and all military necessities."

Xun Qing said: "Military equipment and supplies must be accounted for. Warfare depends on provisions; supply routes must be calculated before mobilizing troops."

Mei Yaochen said: "Organizational structures define troop formations and their regulations. Official hierarchies establish command chains for officers. Logistics officers manage resources, provisions, and all necessary supplies."

Wang Xi said: "Units refer to the composition of troops. Regulations govern their formations, advances, and retreats. Officials include officers and commanders. Logistics involve troop movements and deployments. Officers oversee their respective duties and resources. Military expenditures include supplies, provisions, and equipment."

Zhang Yu said: "Units refer to troop organizations; regulations govern their discipline. Officials divide command responsibilities. Logistics concern supply routes. Officers manage military resources; expenditures cover all necessary supplies. These six elements are essential to warfare and must be governed by established protocols."

All generals are familiar with these five principles; those who understand them prevail, while those who do not are defeated.

Zhang Yu said: "Though all are aware of these five principles, only those who deeply grasp their nuances will triumph; others will fail. Thus, they must be evaluated through calculation to discern their true conditions."

Cao Cao said: "All generals know these five principles; those who understand their nuances will prevail. Discerning their conditions reveals the outcome of victory or defeat."

Du Mu said: "Regarding the five principles, generals must assess and compare their own strengths and weaknesses against the enemy’s, then thoroughly investigate the situation to ensure victory—otherwise, defeat is inevitable."

Jia Lin said: "As the saying goes: 'Knowledge is not the difficulty; action is.'"

Wang Xi said: "One must fully comprehend them. Though the five principles are widely known, the seven calculations are needed to fully grasp their conditions."

Zhang Yu said: "The five principles have been outlined above; what follows is the evaluation of both sides’ advantages and disadvantages to determine the conditions of victory or defeat."

The questions are: Which ruler possesses the Way?

Cao Cao said: "Moral virtue and wisdom."

Li Quan said: "Which ruler truly embodies the Way? A virtuous ruler commands wise and capable generals. Fan Zeng’s departure from Chu and Chen Ping’s allegiance to Han exemplify this."

Du Mu said: "Which ruler—ours or the enemy’s—can distance flatterers, embrace the worthy, and trust their subordinates without doubt?"

Du You said: "A ruler is a sovereign; the Way refers to virtue. First, compare the two rulers to determine who is wise and who is not. For instance, Xun Xi predicted that Duke Yu of Jin, being gluttonous and avaricious, would fall prey to deception, while Gong Zhiqi, though timid, could not strongly remonstrate."

Mei Yaochen said: "Which ruler has won the people’s hearts?"

Wang Xi said: "Like Han Xin’s assessment of Xiang Yu—brute courage and womanly indecision, a ruler in name only who lost the hearts of the realm. Contrast this with the Han king’s entry into Wu Pass, where he harmed nothing, abolished Qin’s harsh laws, and the people of Qin all desired him as their ruler."

He Shi said: "As the Book of Documents states: 'Those who nurture me are my lords; those who oppress me are my enemies.' Which ruler governs with benevolence or tyranny?"

Zhang Yu said: "First, compare the two rulers to see who possesses the virtue of trust and unity—this is the Way of aligning the people’s will with their ruler’s."

"Like Han Xin’s assessment of Xiang Yu—though his benevolence and courage surpassed the High Ancestor’s, his refusal to reward merit was but womanly kindness. Which commander possesses true ability?"

Du Mu said: "Which commander possesses ability? This refers to the aforementioned qualities of wisdom, trustworthiness, *qi*, courage, and strictness."

Mei Yaochen concurred with Du Mu’s annotation.

Wang Xi said: "As when the Han king asked of Wei’s great general Bo Zhi, 'His mouth still reeks of milk—how could he withstand Han Xin?' and the like."

Zhang Yu said: "Examine the commanders of both sides to see who possesses the abilities of wisdom, trustworthiness, benevolence, courage, and strictness. This is akin to the High Ancestor’s assessment that Wei’s general Bo Zhi could not withstand Han Xin."

Which side holds the advantage of Heaven and Earth?

Cao Cao and Li Quan both said: "Heaven’s timing and Earth’s advantage."

Du Mu said: "Heaven refers to the aforementioned yin and yang, cold and heat, seasonal constraints; Earth refers to the aforementioned distance and proximity, peril and ease, breadth and narrowness, life and death."

Du You said: "Observe the positions of the two armies to discern who holds the advantage of Heaven’s timing and Earth’s terrain."

Mei Yaochen said: "Scrutinize the harmony of Heaven’s timing and the assessment of Earth’s advantage."

Wang Xi followed Du Mu’s annotation.

Zhang Yu said: "Observe the actions of the two armies to see who holds the advantage of Heaven’s timing and Earth’s terrain. For instance, when Emperor Wu of Wei campaigned against Wu in the depths of winter, or when Murong Chao failed to hold the Great Xian Pass—these were cases of losing Heaven’s timing and Earth’s advantage."

Whose laws and commands are most effectively enforced?

Cao Cao said: "Established yet unviolated, violated yet surely punished."

Du Mu said: "Laws and prohibitions are proclaimed, with noble and base treated as one. Examples include Wei Jiang’s execution of his servant or Cao Cao’s severing of his own hair."

Du You said: "Decrees and commands are issued—compare which side’s subordinates dare not transgress."

Mei Yaochen said: "Unite the multitude through law, bind them as one through command."

Wang Xi said: "Which side’s laws are clear and commands expedient, such that the people heed and obey?"

Zhang Yu said: "Wei Jiang’s execution of Yang Gan, Luan Ju’s beheading of Zhuang Jia, Lü Meng’s slaying of his fellow villager, and Zhuge Liang’s punishment of Ma Su—these exemplify 'established yet unviolated, violated yet surely punished.' Which side is thus?"

Whose forces are stronger?"

Du Mu said: "When superiors and subordinates are harmonious and united in courage for battle, their strength prevails; when soldiers are numerous and chariots abundant, their strength prevails."

Mei Yaochen said: "Internal harmony and external allegiance."

Wang Xi said: "Strength and weakness suffice to gauge one another."

Zhang Cheng said: "Chariots sturdy and horses fine, soldiers brave and weapons keen—who hears the drum with joy and the gong with fury? Which side is thus?"

**Which army’s troops are better trained?**
Du Mu said: "They discern banners, heed gongs and drums, understand opening and closing formations, know advance and retreat, are skilled in pursuit and archery, and practiced in striking and thrusting."

Du You said: "Know which side’s weapons are sharper and troops more disciplined and adept."

Thus, the King’s Son said: "Soldiers untrained in peacetime will panic in battle; generals untrained in peacetime will falter before sudden changes."

Mei Yaochen said: "Chariots and cavalry well-practiced—whose state excels in precision or coarseness?"

Wang Xi said: "Whose training is more refined?"

He Shi said: "How can bravery and cowardice, strength and weakness, be uniformly judged?"

Zhang Yu said: "The methods of assembling and dispersing, the commands for sitting and rising, advancing and retreating—who is thoroughly practiced in these?"

**Which side’s rewards and punishments are clearer?**
Du Mu said: "Rewards not excessive, punishments not indiscriminate."

Du You said: "Rewarding the good and punishing the wicked—know which side does so with clarity."

Thus, the King’s Son said: "Rewards without measure waste resources and fail to inspire gratitude; punishments without measure execute but lack authority."

Mei Yaochen said: "Reward the meritorious, punish the guilty."

Wang Xi said: "Who ensures rewards match merit and punishments fit the crime?"

Zhang Yu said: "Those deserving reward, though enemies, must be recognized; those deserving punishment, though kin, must not be spared." The *Methods of Sima* also states: "Rewards not delayed, punishments not deferred—whose system is thus clear?"

"By this, I know victory and defeat."

Cao Cao said: "By weighing these seven matters, victory and defeat are known."

Jia Lin said: "Comparing these seven aspects of their governance and ours reveals victory or defeat."

Mei Yaochen said: "If one probes their circumstances, victory and defeat become clear."

Zhang Yu said: "If all seven matters are superior, victory is assured before battle; if all are inferior, defeat is certain before battle. Thus, victory or defeat can be foreknown."

"If the general heeds my strategy, employing it ensures victory—remain; if the general rejects my strategy, employing it ensures defeat—depart."

Cao Cao said: "If plans cannot be settled, then withdraw and leave."

Du Mu said: "If the enemy is well-prepared and defies my strategy, with forces evenly matched and no advantage to be gained, engaging in battle will lead to defeat. Withdraw and depart, as the *Spring and Autumn Annals* states: 'When just, then return.'"

Chen Hao said: "Sun Wu presented his writings to King Helü, saying: 'If you heed and employ my strategies, you will surely defeat the enemy—I shall remain; if you reject my strategies, you will surely suffer defeat—I shall depart.' Thus, he moved King Helü, hoping to be employed. King Helü replied: 'I have studied all thirteen of your chapters.' At that time, King Helü often led armies himself, hence the reference to the general rather than the ruler."

Meng Shi said: "The general here refers to a deputy commander. If he heeds my plans and prevails, he remains; if he defies them and fails, he is removed."

Mei Yaochen said: "Sun Wu presented his thirteen chapters to King Helü of Wu, so the opening chapter begins with these words to move him. It means: if the king heeds my strategy and employs it in battle, victory is certain—I shall remain; if the king rejects my strategy and employs it in battle, defeat is certain—I shall depart."

Wang Xi said: "The general here means 'to act'; 'employ' refers to using troops. It means: if one acts upon this strategy, using troops will ensure victory—I shall remain; if one does not act upon this strategy, using troops will ensure defeat—I shall depart."

Zhang Yu said: "The general here is a particle. Sunzi says: 'If the general now heeds my presented strategy and employs troops, victory is certain—I shall remain; if the general does not heed my presented strategy and employs troops, defeat is certain—I shall depart to another state.' With these words, he sought to move the King of Wu and secure his employment."

"Once the advantageous strategy is heeded, then create momentum to support it externally."

Cao Cao said: "Beyond conventional methods."

Li Quan said: "Once the advantageous strategy is set, then seize the momentum of circumstances. Supporting it externally means acting beyond conventional methods."

Du Mu said: "Calculating advantage and harm is the foundation of military affairs. Once the advantageous strategy is heeded, then beyond conventional methods, further seek momentum to assist the endeavor."

Jia Lin said: "Having assessed the advantage and heeded the plan, grasped the enemy’s circumstances, I then devise extraordinary momentum to move them. Externally, this may involve flanking attacks or rear pursuit to support the main formation."

Mei Yaochen said: "Decide the strategy internally, create momentum externally to secure victory."

Wang Xi said: "Once the advantage of my strategy is heeded, one must also know how to adapt to support it externally."

Zhang Yu said: "Sunzi further states: if the advantage of my strategy is heeded, I must then create momentum to support the endeavor externally. For military conventions can be openly declared, but advantageous momentum must be shaped in response to the enemy. Momentum arises from seizing opportunity to exercise adaptability."

Cao Cao said: "Control stems from adaptability; adaptability is shaped by circumstances."

Li Quan said: "Plans adapt to circumstances."

Du Mu said: "From here onward, he speaks of momentum beyond conventional methods. Momentum cannot be foreseen in advance; one may perceive one’s advantage through the enemy’s harm, or one’s harm through the enemy’s advantage, then devise adaptable tactics to secure victory."

Mei Yaochen said: "Act on advantage, exercise adaptability to control them."

Wang Xi said: "Momentum means seizing change."

Zhang Yu said: "So-called momentum requires seizing advantageous circumstances to devise adaptable strategies and defeat the enemy—hence, it cannot be predetermined."

From this point onward, he briefly discusses adaptable strategies. Warfare is the Way of deception.

Cao Cao said: "Warfare has no constant form; deception is its Way."

Li Quan said: "In war, deception is never excessive."

Mei Yaochen said: "Without cunning, one cannot exercise adaptability; without adaptability, one cannot control the enemy."

Wang Xi said: "Deception is the means to seek victory over the enemy; governing the masses must be done with sincerity."

Zhang Yu said: "Though warfare is rooted in benevolence and righteousness, its path to victory necessarily lies in deception. Thus, dragging branches to raise dust was Luan Zhi’s cunning; ten thousand crossbows firing in unison was Sun Bin’s surprise; a thousand oxen charging together was Tian Dan’s adaptability; and damming the water with sandbags was Huaiyin’s deceit. All these were victories won through the Way of deception."

Therefore, when capable, feign incapability.

Zhang Yu said: "Truly strong yet feigning weakness, truly brave yet feigning cowardice—such were Li Mu’s defeat of the Xiongnu and Sun Bin’s execution of Pang Juan."

When active, feign inactivity.

Li Quan said: "Though one has truly deployed troops, outwardly display timidity." When Han general Chen Xi rebelled and allied with the Xiongnu, Emperor Gaozu sent ten envoys to assess the situation, all of whom reported that the enemy could be attacked. He then sent Lou Jing, who returned saying: "The Xiongnu cannot be attacked." The emperor asked why. Lou Jing replied: "When two states contend, they should proudly display their strengths. Yet when I went, I saw only the weak and elderly. This must be feigning incapability while truly capable. I believe they cannot be attacked."

Emperor Gaozu grew furious, saying: "This Qi ruffian won office by glibness, and now he recklessly demoralizes my forces!" He shackled Lou Jing in Guangwu. With three hundred thousand troops, he advanced to Baideng, where the emperor was besieged by the Xiongnu for seven days, running out of provisions. This exemplifies the principle of outwardly feigning cowardice.

Du Mu said: "This is cunning deception concealing one’s form. One’s form must never be revealed to the enemy; if the enemy sees it, they will surely respond." The *Zhuan* states: "A raptor about to strike must hide its form." This is like the Xiongnu displaying weakness and age to the Han envoys.

Du You said: "It means being truly capable and active while outwardly feigning incapability and inactivity, so the enemy does not prepare against us. Like Sun Bin reducing his chariots to outmaneuver Pang Juan."

Wang Xi said: "Feign weakness when strong, cowardice when brave, disorder when disciplined, emptiness when substantial, foolishness when wise, fewness when numerous, retreat when advancing, slowness when swift, abandonment when seizing, and this for that."

He Shi said: "Feigning incapability when capable is like the Chanyu luring Emperor Gaozu into encirclement at Pingcheng. Feigning inactivity when active is like Li Mu stationing troops in Yunzhong and crushing the Xiongnu."

Zhang Yu said: "Feigning retreat when intending to fight, slowness when intending speed—such were Ban Chao’s strike against Shache and Zhao She’s defeat of the Qin army. Feigning distance when near, feigning nearness when far."

Li Quan said: "To make the enemy drop their guard. When Han general Han Xin captured Wei King Bao, he initially arrayed boats as if to cross at Linjin, then secretly ferried troops in wooden jars via Xiayang to ambush Anyi, catching Wei unprepared. Geng Yan’s campaign against Zhang Bu also began by attacking Linzi, feigning a distant threat."

Du Mu said: "To launch a near ambush, one must display the form of a distant departure; to launch a distant ambush, one must display the form of a near advance. Han Xin massed troops at Linjin but crossed at Xiayang—this was feigning nearness to ambush from afar. Later, when Cao Cao and Yuan Shao clashed at Guandu, Yuan sent generals Guo Tu, Chunyu Qiong, and Yan Liang to attack Liu Yan, the governor of Dongjun, at Baima. Yuan led his army to Liyang, preparing to cross the river. Cao marched north to relieve Yanjin. Xun Yu said: 'Our forces are too few to match them; we must divide their strength.' Cao then led troops to Yanjin, pretending to cross, drawing Yuan’s forces to his rear. Yuan turned west to respond. Cao swiftly advanced on Baima, and when within ten *li* of the city, Yan Liang rushed to battle in surprise. Cao sent Zhang Liao and Guan Yu to the front, crushed the enemy, beheaded Yan Liang, and lifted the siege of Baima. This was feigning distance to ambush from nearness."

Jia Lin said: "The choice to advance or withdraw lies with us—how would the enemy know?"

Du You said: "Feigning distance when intending nearness, feigning nearness when intending distance. Deceive and dazzle the enemy by displaying distance while actually striking from nearness, as Han Xin did when ambushing Anyi."

Mei Yaochen said: "To prevent the enemy from discerning one’s plans."

Wang Xi’s note was the same as above.

He Shi said: "Feigning nearness when far is like Han Xin arraying boats at Linjin but crossing at Xiayang. Feigning distance when near is like the Jin lord attacking Guo by borrowing passage through Yu."

Zhang Yu said: "To ambush from nearness, feign distance. When Wu and Yue faced each other across the water, Yue positioned troops on both flanks, five *li* apart. At night, both sides advanced to the beat of drums, and Wu divided its forces to resist. Yue then forded the river, striking Wu’s central army, routing them. To ambush from afar, feign nearness, as Han Xin did when massing troops at Linjin but crossing at Xiayang.

Entice with advantage.

Du Mu said: "The Zhao general Li Mu let livestock and people roam freely across the fields. When the Xiongnu made a minor incursion, he feigned defeat, abandoning several thousand men. The Chanyu, hearing this, rejoiced and led a massive force to attack. Li Mu deployed multiple ambushes, flanking and crushing them, slaughtering over a hundred thousand Xiongnu cavalry."

Jia Lin said: "Lure them with advantage, and when they move, they reveal their form—this is how we exploit their form to secure victory."

Mei Yaochen said: "If they covet profit, entice them with goods."

He Shi said: "Enticing with advantage is like the Red Eyebrows abandoning their supplies to bait Deng Yu."

Zhang Yu said: "Display minor gains to lure and overcome them. When the Chu attacked Jiao, their commander said: 'Jiao is small and careless; let us not defend our woodcutters to entice them.' The next day, the Jiao forces rushed out, driving the Chu laborers into the mountains. Chu had set ambushes at the mountain’s base and crushed them."

Seize when disordered.

Li Quan said: "An enemy greedy for profit will surely fall into disorder. When Qin King Yao Xing attacked Tufa Nutan, he drove all his cattle and sheep into the wilds, allowing the Qin troops to plunder freely. The Qin, having gained advantage, lost all formation. Nutan secretly divided ten generals to ambush and strike, crushing the Qin and beheading over seven thousand."

Du Mu said: "When the enemy is confused, seize the opportunity to take them. The *Zhuan* states: 'Absorb the weak, attack the benighted, seize the disordered, humiliate the doomed—this is the fine art of war.'"

Jia Lin said: "Let our cunning disrupt them, then seize them when they are in chaos."

Mei Yaochen said: "When they are disordered, strike and seize them."

Wang Xi said: "Disorder means lacking control; seize implies ease."

Zhang Yu said: "Feign confusion to lure and seize them."

If Wu and Yue were to attack each other, Wu might deploy three thousand convicts, displaying disorder to lure Yue. As the convicts scattered or halted, the Yue forces would vie to seize them—only to be defeated by Wu. This illustrates that waiting for the enemy to fall into chaos before striking is not the correct approach.

According to the principles of the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, the term "seize" (*qu*) implies ease, as when the Lu army seized the state of Shi. When the enemy is solid and prepared, one must take precautions.

Cao Cao said: "If the enemy is well-ordered and substantial, one must prepare against them."
Li Quan said: "Prepare against the enemy’s solidity."
The Shu general Guan Yu, intending to besiege Fancheng in Wei, feared that the Wu general Lü Meng would strike from the rear. Thus, he left many reserve troops to guard Jingzhou. Lü Meng, discerning his intent, feigned illness. Guan Yu then withdrew his reserve forces, only to be captured by Lü Meng, and Jingzhou fell to Wu. This exemplifies the principle.

Du Mu said: "When armies face each other, regardless of whether the enemy is weak or strong, one must always be prepared. This means that in peacetime, when neighboring states share borders, if the enemy governs with solidity—ruling with love, enforcing rewards and punishments with clarity, and training soldiers with precision—one must prepare against them, not waiting until battle is joined to do so."
Chen Hao said: "If the enemy remains motionless and solid, we must be vigilant and strengthen ourselves to prepare against them."
Mei Yaochen said: "If they are solid, we must not fail to prepare."
Wang Xi said: "They may strike where we are unprepared."
He Shi said: "If the enemy appears solid but their vulnerabilities are unseen, we must conserve our strength and prepare against them."
Zhang Yu said: "The *Military Methods* states: 'Engage them to discern where they have surplus or deficiency.' Surplus means solidity; deficiency means emptiness. If the enemy’s forces are solid, we must adopt an invincible strategy and await them, not acting rashly. As Li Jing’s *Military Mirror* says: 'Observe their emptiness and advance; see their solidity and halt.'"

Avoid the strong.
Cao Cao said: "Avoid their strengths."
Li Quan said: "Measure one’s own strength."
When the Chu ruler attacked Sui, Sui’s minister Ji Liang said: "The Chu forces favor the left flank; you must take the left and avoid engaging their king. Instead, attack their right flank, where their forces are weak, and they will surely be defeated. Even a partial defeat will cause their army to waver." Shaoshi said: "Not confronting the king is no match for him." Sui’s ruler did not heed the advice. The Sui army was routed, and the Sui lord fled. This was the consequence of attacking the strong.

Du Mu said: "Evade their strengths. If the enemy is emboldened by superior force and keen morale, one must temporarily withdraw, awaiting their decline and seizing the moment of vulnerability to strike."
At the end of the Jin dynasty, the rebels Lu Xun and Xu Daofu exploited a gap to raid Jianye. Liu Yu, defending against them, said: "If the rebels advance directly to Xinting, we must avoid them and anchor at Caizhou—they will be captured." Xu Daofu wished to burn their ships and advance directly, but Lu Xun opposed it. They anchored at Caizhou and were ultimately defeated and destroyed.

Jia Lin said: "To overcome the strong with the weak, one must await change."
Du You said: "If their treasuries are full and their soldiers keen, one must retreat and await their laxity, observing developments to respond."
Mei Yaochen said: "If they are strong, we must avoid their sharpness."
Wang Xi said: "If the enemy’s troops are elite and our forces few, we must retreat."
Zhang Yu said: "The *Military Methods* states: 'Do not intercept a well-ordered banner; do not strike a well-formed battle array.' If the enemy’s formations are disciplined and their command strict, we must avoid them and not act recklessly. When Qin and Jin clashed and both withdrew in stalemate, it was to guard against defeat."

Provoke their anger to disrupt them.
Cao Cao said: "Await their decline."
Li Quan said: "Generals prone to anger are easily swayed, for their nature is not steadfast."
The Han minister Chen Ping schemed to disrupt Chu by presenting a lavish feast to a Chu envoy. The envoy was startled and asked: "Is this the doing of the Grand Tutor, or the king’s command?" This was a means of provoking anger to disrupt them.

Du Mu said: "A rigid and irascible commander can be provoked to anger, causing them to act impulsively, their resolve and spirit disrupted, disregarding their original plans."
Meng Shi said: "When the enemy is enraged, one should humble and disturb them."
Mei Yaochen said: "If they are quick-tempered and easily angered, provoke them to rashness and reckless battle."
Wang Xi said: "If the enemy is cautious, incite their anger to disrupt them."
He Shi said: "To provoke anger and disrupt them is exemplified by the Han forces striking Cao Jiu at Sishui."
Zhang Yu said: "If their nature is fierce and resentful, humiliate them to enrage them, causing their resolve and spirit to waver, leading them to act without strategy and advance recklessly. As when the Jin seized Wan Chun to provoke Chu."

The *Wei Liaozi* states: "The forbearing cannot be provoked to anger." This means that those of a patient disposition cannot be goaded into anger.

Humiliate them to make them arrogant.
Li Quan said: "Heavy bribes and sweet words reveal no small ambition."
Later Zhao’s Shi Le feigned submission to Wang Jun. Wang Jun’s advisors urged an attack, but he said: "Lord Shi comes to serve me." He threatened to execute anyone who spoke of attacking. He prepared a grand feast to receive Shi Le, who then drove tens of thousands of cattle and sheep, claiming they were tribute but in truth blocking the streets to prevent Wang Jun’s troops from mobilizing. Shi Le entered Jicheng, seized Wang Jun in his hall, and executed him, annexing Yan. This exemplifies humiliating them to make them arrogant.

Du Mu said: "At the end of the Qin, the Xiongnu chieftain Modu had just ascended. The powerful Donghu sent envoys to demand the thousand-mile horse of Modu’s father, Touman. Modu consulted his ministers, who all said: 'The thousand-mile horse is the state’s treasure; do not give it.' Modu replied: 'How can we begrudge a single horse to a neighboring state?' and gave it. Soon after, the Donghu sent another envoy, demanding one of Modu’s consorts. Modu again consulted his ministers, who were enraged: 'The Donghu are lawless, demanding even a consort! We must attack them.'"

Modu said: "How can we begrudge a single woman to a neighboring state?" and gave her. Soon after, the Donghu again sent word: "The Xiongnu have abandoned a thousand *li* of land; we wish to take it." Modu consulted his ministers, who all said: "We may give it or not; either is acceptable." Modu flew into a rage: "Land is the foundation of the state. How can we surrender our foundation?" He executed all who had spoken in favor of giving it. Mounting his horse, he ordered that any in the nation who lagged behind would be slain. He then launched a surprise attack on the Donghu. The Donghu had grown complacent toward Modu and made no preparations, so Modu struck and annihilated them. He then turned west to attack the Yuezhi, annexed the Loufan and Baiyang to the south and the lands south of the River, and invaded Yan and Dai to the north, fully recovering all the territory the Qin had seized from the Xiongnu through Meng Tian’s campaigns.

Chen Hao said: "What one desires must never be begrudged. Offer women to delude their hearts and jade and silk to inflate their ambitions—this was the strategy of Fan Li and Zheng Wu." Du You said: "When they mobilize their entire nation in fury, eager to advance, one should outwardly display submission to elevate their arrogance, then wait for their complacency upon return to ambush and strike them." Thus, the *Prince of Wang* said: "Those who wield the law skillfully are like a wildcat hunting a rat—matching strength with cunning, feigning humility, remaining still, and striking from below." Mei Yaochen said: "Display weakness to inflate their pride." Wang Xi said: "Feign weakness to make them arrogant; when they least expect it, strike at their vulnerabilities." Zhang Yu said: "Whether through humble words and lavish gifts or feigned retreat with a weary army, all serve to make them careless and overconfident."

When Wu attacked Qi, the Lord of Yue led his forces to pay homage, and the king of Wu bestowed gifts upon his officers and nobles. The people of Wu rejoiced, but Wu Zixu alone feared, saying: "This is fattening Wu for the slaughter." Later, Wu was indeed destroyed by Yue. When Chu attacked Yong, it retreated seven times. The people of Yong said: "Chu is not worth fighting." They ceased their preparations, whereupon the Lord of Chu divided his forces into two columns and attacked, annihilating Yong. All these exemplify the principle.

"Exhaust them through idleness,"

One variant reads: "Lure them and exhaust them." Cao Cao said: "Exhaust them with profit." Li Quan said: "When the enemy is at ease and we exhaust them, it is a skillful achievement." When Wu attacked Chu, Prince Guang sought counsel from Wu Zixu. Zixu said: "We may deploy three armies to harass them. When one of our armies arrives, they will surely muster all their forces to meet it; when they advance, we withdraw. Repeatedly harass them to wear them down, employ diverse tactics to confuse them, then follow with all three armies in succession—we shall surely achieve a great victory." The plan was adopted. From this point on, Chu began to suffer at Wu’s hands.

Du Mu said: Wu’s Prince Guang consulted Wu Yuan on attacking Chu. Yuan said: "We may form three armies to harass them. When one of our armies arrives, they will surely come out in full force; when they advance, we withdraw. Repeatedly harass them to leave them scarred, employ diverse tactics to mislead them, then follow with all three armies in succession—we shall surely achieve a great victory." The plan was adopted. As a result, Zi Zhong was forced to rush to and fro seven times in a single year. Thus Chu began to suffer at Wu’s hands, and Wu eventually entered Ying.

At the end of the Han, after Cao Cao had defeated Liu Bei, Bei fled to Yuan Shao and led his troops to challenge Cao. The adjutant Tian Feng said: "Cao is skilled in warfare; we must not act rashly. It would be better to wear him down over time. You, General, hold the strategic advantage of mountains and rivers, command four provinces, ally with heroes abroad, and cultivate agriculture and warfare at home. Then select your finest troops, divide them into surprise forces, and strike in turns where he is unprepared. Attack his right when he rushes to save his left, strike his left when he hurries to his right. Force him to exhaust himself in constant response, so his people cannot settle into their livelihoods. We shall not be wearied, yet he will be worn down. Within three years, we may triumph without effort. Now, to abandon this strategy of assured victory and stake all on a single battle—regret will come too late." Shao did not heed him and was defeated.

Mei Yaochen said: "With our ease, we await their exhaustion." Wang Xi said: "Employ many surprise forces. When they advance, we withdraw; when they withdraw, we advance. Strike their left when they rush to save their right, attack their right when they hurry to their left—this is how we exhaust them." He Shi said: "Sunzi has a method for managing strength: with ease, await exhaustion. Thus, when the enemy is at ease, we should employ diverse means to wear them down, then we may prevail."

Zhang Yu said: "We remain at full strength, while they become exhausted along the Way." When Jin and Chu contended over Zheng without resolution, Jin’s Zhi Wuzi divided the four armies into three divisions. Each time Jin moved once, Chu responded thrice. Thus, after three engagements, Chu could no longer compete. Another example is when Shen Gong Wu Chen instructed Wu to attack Chu, forcing Zi Zhong to rush to and fro seven times in a single year.

"Estrange those who are close." Cao Cao said: "Use spies to divide them." Li Quan said: "Break their alliances, sow discord between their ruler and ministers, then attack." Formerly, when Qin attacked Zhao, Qin’s Chancellor Ying applied pressure to the King of Zhao, saying: "Our only fear is that Zhao will employ Kuo; Lian Po is easy to deal with." The King of Zhao agreed and replaced Po with Kuo, whereupon Qin buried four hundred thousand Zhao soldiers alive at Changping—this illustrates the principle.

Du Mu said: "If the enemy’s superiors and subordinates are close, employ generous rewards to estrange them." Chen Ping said to the King of Han: "Now, Xiang Yu’s steadfast ministers are no more than Fan Zeng, Zhongli Mo, Long Ju, and Zhou Yin—only a few. If Your Majesty can truly offer tens of thousands of catties of gold, you may sow discord between them. They will surely turn on one another internally; then Han may raise its armies and attack—Xiang Yu’s destruction is certain." The King of Han agreed, providing forty thousand catties of gold to Ping to employ as counterintelligence. Xiang Yu indeed grew suspicious of Fan Zeng and did not press the attack on Rongyang, allowing the King of Han to escape.

Chen Hao said: "If they are stingy with ranks and emoluments, we must be generous; if they hoard wealth, we must be lavish; if they favor harsh punishments, we must be lenient. Exploit their mutual suspicions to carry out estrangement." Thus You Yu came to Qin, and Ying Bu aided Han.

Du You said: "Lure them with profit, so the five types of spies all infiltrate. Let eloquent persuaders rush to sow discord between their ruler and ministers, dividing their strength." For example, Qin sent counterintelligence to deceive the ruler of Zhao, causing him to dismiss Lian Po and appoint Zhao She’s son, leading to the defeat at Changping.

Mei Yaochen’s note is the same as Du Mu’s. Wang Xi said: "When the enemy is close, employ schemes to estrange them." Zhang Yu said: "Either estrange their ruler and ministers or divide their allies, causing them to become estranged and divided, then act against them." Ying estranged Zhao and removed Lian Po; Chen Ping estranged Chu and drove out Fan Zeng—these are examples of estranging ruler and ministers. When Qin and Jin allied to attack Zheng, Zhu Zhiwu went out by night and persuaded the Duke of Qin, saying: "If you gain Zheng, it will only benefit Jin, not Qin. It would be better to spare Zheng and make it your eastern host." The Duke of Qin understood and withdrew his troops—this is an example of dividing allies.

"Attack where they are unprepared; strike where they do not expect." Cao Cao said: "Strike their laxity, exploit their emptiness." Li Quan said: "Strike their laxity, raid their emptiness." Du Mu said: "Strike their emptiness, raid their laxity." Meng said: "Strike their emptiness, raid their laxity, so the enemy does not know how to prepare. Thus it is said: the subtlety of warfare lies in its formlessness." Taigong said: "No movement is more divine than the unexpected; no strategy is better than the unrecognized."

Mei Yaochen and Wang Xi’s notes are the same as above. He Shi said: "To attack where they are unprepared: When the Wei Emperor Taizu campaigned against the Wuhuan, Guo Jia said: 'The Hu rely on their remoteness and will surely not prepare. Exploit their unpreparedness and strike suddenly—they can be destroyed.' When Taizu reached the Yi River, Jia said: 'In warfare, speed is paramount. Now, marching a thousand *li* to strike, with heavy baggage, it will be difficult to seize the advantage. It would be better to advance with light forces by double-time marches to catch them unawares.'"

Thus he secretly advanced through the Lulong Pass, striking directly at the Chanyu’s court, engaging in battle and crushing them utterly.

During the Tang, Li Jing presented the *Ten Glories* to strategize against Xiao Xian, and the command of the three armies was entrusted wholly to him. In the eighth month, he assembled his forces at Kuizhou. Xiao Xian, considering it the season of autumn floods, with the Yangtze’s waters surging and the Three Gorges’ paths treacherous, believed Jing could not advance and thus made no preparations. In the ninth month, Jing led his troops forward, declaring: “In warfare, speed is divine, and opportunity must not be lost. Now, with our forces just assembled, Xiao Xian remains unaware. Riding the flood’s momentum, we shall appear suddenly beneath his walls—what is called ‘swift as thunder before one can cover one’s ears.’ Even if he learns of us, in his haste he will have no means to resist. This will surely end in his capture.” Advancing to Yiling, Xiao Xian at last grew fearful and summoned reinforcements from south of the river, but they could not arrive in time. Jing encircled the city, and Xiao Xian surrendered.

To strike where they are unprepared: At the end of the Wei, generals Zhong Hui and Deng Ai were sent to conquer Shu. The Shu general Jiang Wei defended Jian’ge, and though Zhong Hui attacked, he could not prevail. Deng Ai memorialized: “I request to take the Yinping route, advancing by a hidden path to emerge at Jian’ge and then pressing west into Chengdu. With an unexpected force striking at their heart, the troops at Jian’ge will surely rush back to Fuling, allowing Hui to advance in full strength. If the Jian’ge forces do not return, then those at Fuling will be few.” The *Military Treatise* states: “Attack where they are unprepared; strike where they do not expect.” Now, seizing their vulnerability, we shall surely break them. In the tenth month of winter, Ai marched from Yinping through seven hundred *li* of uninhabited land, cutting paths through mountains, constructing bridges and galleries—terrain so precipitous and valleys so deep that the journey was fraught with peril. Provisions nearly exhausted, they teetered on the brink of disaster. Ai wrapped himself in felt and rolled down the slopes; his officers and men climbed trees and scaled cliffs, advancing in single file. The first to reach Jiangyou, the Shu commander Ma Miao surrendered. Zhuge Zhan withdrew from Fuling to Mianzhu, arraying his forces in resistance. Ai routed them, slaying Zhan and the minister Zhang Zun. Advancing to Chengdu, the Shu ruler Liu Shan surrendered.

Another instance: Gao Huan, a general of Eastern Wei, led his troops to campaign against Western Wei, encamping at Puqi and constructing three floating bridges to cross the Yellow River. He also dispatched his general Dou Tai to press toward Tong Pass, while Gao Aocao besieged Luozhou. The Western Wei general Emperor Wen of Zhou marched his army to Guangyang and summoned his officers, saying: “The enemy now threatens us on three fronts and has built bridges across the river, signaling their intent to cross—all to pin down our forces so that Dou Tai may enter from the west. Prolonged stalemate will allow their plan to succeed; this is no sound strategy. Moreover, Gao Huan always uses Tai as his vanguard, and his troops are both keen and arrogant from repeated victories. Now, striking where they do not expect, we shall surely prevail. If we defeat Tai, Huan will flee without battle.” The officers all said: “The enemy is near; to abandon them and strike afar risks failure, and regret will be too late.” Wen replied: “When Huan previously attacked Tong Pass twice, our forces did not advance beyond Bashang. Now, with his full might arrayed, his troops have not yet left the outskirts. He presumes we will merely defend and have no intention of distant engagement. Emboldened by success, he holds us in contempt. Seizing this moment to strike, how can we not prevail? Though he builds bridges, he has not yet begun to cross. Within five days, we shall take Dou Tai.” The officers dared not oppose him. Wen then led six thousand cavalry back to Chang’an, feigning an expedition to Longyou. On the day *xinhai*, he secretly set out; by the morning of *guichou*, he reached Tong Pass. Dou Tai’s troops, suddenly hearing of his arrival, panicked and took position against the mountains, but before they could form ranks, Wen crushed them. Tai was slain, and his head sent to Chang’an. Gao Aocao, who had just captured Luozhou, upon hearing of Tai’s defeat, burned his supplies and abandoned the city in retreat.

Zhang Yu said: “To attack where they are unprepared means to strike at their laxity, where the enemy does not anticipate, as when the men of Yan feared Zheng’s three armies but did not guard against being outmaneuvered—and were thus defeated. To strike where they do not expect means to assail their vulnerabilities, where the enemy does not consider danger, as when Deng Ai conquered Shu by marching seven hundred *li* through uninhabited land.”

Such are the victories of military strategy, which cannot be foretold. Cao Cao said: “To foretell is to reveal. Warfare has no constant form, just as water has no constant shape. Adapting to the enemy’s changes cannot be predetermined.” Thus, assessing the enemy lies in the mind, and discerning opportunity in the eye.

Li Quan said: “To attack where they are unprepared and do not expect ensures victory—this is the essence of warfare, kept secret and not divulged.”

Du Mu said: “‘Foretold’ means spoken. What has been described above encompasses all the strategies for military victory, yet these are not fixed doctrines. Only when facing the enemy’s disposition can one act; it cannot be declared beforehand.”

Mei Yaochen said: “To respond to the enemy and devise measures as circumstances demand—how can this be spoken of in advance?”

Wang Xi said: “Calculating and conducting campaigns is the standard method, but seizing opportunity to secure victory cannot be predetermined.”

Zhang Yu said: “What has been described above are the victorious strategies of military affairs, which must be adapted to the enemy and cannot be foretold. Before battle, if the temple calculations foretell victory, it is because the calculations are many; if they foretell defeat, it is because the calculations are few. Many calculations bring victory; few bring defeat—how much more so when there are none? By this, I observe that victory and defeat are manifest.”

Cao Cao said: “By my Dao, I observe it thus.”

Li Quan said: “In warfare, one first secures victory in the temple hall before contending for advantage with others. Whether punishing rebels, pacifying distant lands, overthrowing the weak, or attacking the benighted—all arise from the circumstances of things. When internal and external hearts are divided, as in the armies of Shang and Zhou, this is what is meant by victory foretold in the temple. The method of *Taiyi Dunjia* sets calculations: above sixty is many, below sixty is few.”

When the guest’s calculations are many against the host’s few, the host is defeated; when the guest’s calculations are few against the host’s many, the host prevails. In these cases, victory and defeat are easily discerned.

Du Mu said: “‘Temple calculations’ refers to strategizing within the temple hall.”

Mei Yaochen said: “With many calculations, the temple deliberations secure victory before battle is joined; with few calculations, the temple deliberations fail to secure victory before battle is joined. Thus, one cannot be without calculations.”

Wang Xi said: “This is to caution learners against being misled by the notion that nothing can be transmitted beforehand, and so the meaning of the *Calculations* chapter is reiterated.”

He Shi said: “Calculations may be skillful or clumsy, and success or failure hinges upon them.”

Zhang Yu said: “In ancient times, when raising an army, the commander was always sent into the temple for purification rites, where he was given the completed calculations before being dispatched—hence the term ‘temple calculations.’ If the plans and strategies are profound and far-reaching, the calculations yield much, and thus victory is assured before battle. If the deliberations are shallow and short-sighted, the calculations yield little, and thus defeat is certain before battle. Many calculations triumph over few; how much more so when there are none? Thus it is said: victorious armies first secure victory, then seek battle; defeated armies first join battle, then seek victory. Whether one has calculations or none, victory and defeat are easily seen.”

*End of Commentaries on Sunzi, Scroll One*

Wang Xi remarked: Such is the case when the Han king inquired of Wei’s great general Bai Zhi, saying, “This man’s mouth still smells of milk—he cannot withstand Han Xin.”

Zhang Yu said: Examine the generals of both sides to see who possesses the abilities of wisdom, trustworthiness, benevolence, courage, and strictness.

*[Note: #7 In the Song edition, ‘德’ is written as ‘听’. #8 ‘诱’ was originally miswritten as ‘说’; corrected according to the Song edition. #9 ‘乌桓’ was originally miswritten as ‘乌栢’; corrected according to the Song edition.]*

**Sunzi’s Commentaries, Scroll Two: “Waging War”**

Cao Cao said: If one wishes to wage war, one must first calculate its costs and strive to procure provisions from the enemy.

Li Quan said: First establish the strategy, then prepare the military equipment—thus the chapter on waging war follows the chapter on calculations.

Wang Zhe said: Through calculations, one knows victory; only then does one raise an army and prepare military expenses, yet even so, one cannot sustain it for long.

Zhang Yu said: Once the calculations are settled, then repair the chariots and horses, sharpen the weapons, transport the grain and fodder, and budget the expenses to prepare for war—hence this chapter follows “Calculations.”

Sunzi said: In the general method of employing troops, there are one thousand light chariots, one thousand heavy chariots, and one hundred thousand armored soldiers.

Cao Cao said: Light chariots are swift chariots, each drawn by a team of four horses. Heavy chariots are supply wagons, referring to the weight of ten thousand cavalry. Each chariot is drawn by four horses, with a standard of thirty thousand troops. Two soldiers are assigned to cooking, one household retainer to safeguarding clothing and equipment, and two to tending the horses—five in total. For every ten infantrymen, a large ox-drawn wagon is provided. Two soldiers are assigned to cooking, one household retainer to guarding clothing and equipment—three in total. The hundred thousand armored soldiers refer to the number of troops.

Li Quan said: Light chariots are war chariots; heavy chariots are supply wagons; armored soldiers are infantry. Each chariot, drawn by a team of four horses, is accompanied by seventy infantrymen. Thus, an army of one thousand chariots includes seventy thousand armored soldiers and four thousand horses. Sunzi simplifies the figures for military resources, using one hundred thousand as the standard—from this, one may infer a million.

Du Mu said: Light chariots are indeed war chariots. In ancient times, chariot warfare employed heavy chariots—supply wagons carrying weapons, goods, and clothing. The *Methods of Sima* states: One chariot includes three armored soldiers, seventy-two infantrymen, ten household retainers for cooking, five for guarding clothing and equipment, five for tending horses, and five for gathering firewood and drawing water. A light chariot has seventy-five men, a heavy chariot twenty-five. Thus, two chariots together form a unit of one hundred men. For an army of one hundred thousand, with one thousand heavy chariots, the expenses and provisions can be calculated—from this, the needs of a million troops may be known.

Mei Yaochen said: Light chariots are swift chariots; heavy chariots are supply wagons. Each light chariot carries twenty-five armored soldiers and infantrymen, while each heavy chariot carries seventy-five. With one thousand of each, the total armored soldiers number one hundred thousand.

Wang Zhe said: Cao Cao said: Light chariots are drawn by teams of four horses, totaling one thousand chariots. I, Zhe, say: Light chariots refer to those drawn by heavy chariots. One chariot with four horses is a team; one thousand teams mean one thousand heavy chariots. Cao Cao said: Heavy chariots are supply wagons. I, Zhe, say: Heavy chariots are war chariots, part of the five-thousand-chariot levy of the great feudal lords. Cao Cao said: One hundred thousand armored soldiers refer to the number of infantry.
