I, Zhe, say: According to the well-field system, each district provides one war chariot, three armored soldiers, and seventy-two infantrymen—one thousand chariots total seventy-five thousand men. Here, it speaks of one hundred thousand armored soldiers; could this be an ad hoc measure of the time? Why the discrepancy?

He [Yan]shi said: One hundred thousand is a round number.

Zhang Yu said: Light chariots are attack chariots; heavy chariots are defense chariots. According to Cao Cao’s *New Book*: One attack chariot includes a frontal defense unit and two flanking units, totaling seventy-five men. One defense chariot includes ten cooking retainers, five for guarding equipment, five for tending horses, and five for gathering firewood and drawing water—twenty-five in total. Together, one attack and one defense chariot make one hundred men. Raising an army of one hundred thousand requires two thousand chariots, half light and half heavy—this aligns with the present text.

When transporting provisions a thousand *li*...

Cao Cao said: This means crossing a thousand *li* beyond the border.

Li Quan said: The road is extremely distant.

...the expenses at home and abroad, the costs of entertaining guests, the materials for glue and lacquer, and the upkeep of chariots and armor will amount to a daily expenditure of a thousand pieces of gold before an army of one hundred thousand can be mobilized.

Cao Cao said: This refers to rewards and purchases, which are additional.

Li Quan said: When the army is deployed abroad, the state treasury is exhausted at home. The mention of a thousand pieces of gold signifies immense expenditure. Transporting provisions a thousand *li* means twenty men must support one.

Du Mu said: The army engages in diplomatic exchanges with feudal lords, hence the term “guests.” The maintenance and repair of chariots, armor, and weapons—glue and lacquer are mentioned to represent minor details. A thousand pieces of gold signifies great expense, not including rewards and gifts.

Jia Lin said: If the costs are insufficient, one cannot raise an army or mobilize the masses. Thus, the Grand Marshal Li said: At the gates of the three armies, discussions with guests and advisors take place.

Mei Yaochen said: Mobilizing an army of one hundred thousand and transporting provisions a thousand *li* incurs such daily expenses—this is a warning against prolonged warfare.

Wang Shi said: “At home” refers to the state; “abroad” refers to the army’s location. “Guests” include envoys from feudal lords and banquets for officers and soldiers in the army. Glue and lacquer for chariots and armor are mentioned to encompass both minor and major expenses.

He [Yan]shi said: Prolonged warfare depletes resources—this is what the wise consider.

Zhang Yu said: When campaigning a thousand *li* from the state, one must rely on provisions from the enemy. If supplies must be transported, both the interior and exterior will be thrown into turmoil, exhausting the people on the roads and causing endless waste and depletion.

Guests refer to envoys on missions and wandering scholars; glue and lacquer denote materials for repairing and maintaining equipment; chariots and armor encompass axle grease, metal, and leather goods. Calculating these expenditures, daily costs amount to a thousand *jin* of gold before an army of one hundred thousand can be mobilized. A thousand *jin* signifies immense expense, and rewards for merit remain uncounted. If such an army is used in battle and prevails, prolonged warfare will dull its blades and blunt its edge. In besieging cities, its strength will be exhausted.

Cao Cao said: *Dun* means worn; *qu* means depleted.
Du Mu said: "Victory through prolonged warfare" refers to achieving victory only after protracted engagement. If victory comes only after prolonged struggle with the enemy, armor and weapons will be worn and broken, morale will be crushed, and in attacking cities, manpower will be utterly spent and broken.
Jia Lin said: Though one may defeat the enemy, prolonged warfare brings no advantage. The art of war values complete victory; dulling blades and crushing morale, with soldiers wounded and horses exhausted, leads to defeat.
Mei Yaochen said: Even if victorious and prolonged, weapons will grow dull and worn, and the army’s spirit will be blunted; in besieging cities for long, strength will be utterly spent.
Wang Zhe said: *Qu* means exhausted. Seeking victory through prolonged warfare will dull and break weapons, and in attacking cities, the toll will be even greater.
Zhang Yu said: When armies clash in battle, victory achieved only after prolonged struggle will leave weapons damaged and morale broken. Besieging cities a thousand *li* away will exhaust their strength. Prolonged exposure of the army abroad will deplete the state’s resources.

Meng Shi said: Prolonged exposure of the army in distant lands will strain both military and state finances, leaving them unable to sustain one another.
Mei Yaochen said: If the army remains exposed abroad for long, supplies will be insufficient.
Zhang Yu said: With daily expenses of a thousand *jin* of gold, prolonged exposure of the army will surely exhaust the state’s resources. As when Emperor Wu of Han pursued endless campaigns, exhausting the treasury until he issued a decree of remorse.

When blades are dulled, morale crushed, strength exhausted, and resources depleted, neighboring lords will seize the opportunity to rise. Even with a wise strategist, none can salvage the aftermath.

Li Quan said: Mobilizing an army of one hundred thousand with daily costs of a thousand *jin* of gold not only wears down the army abroad but also drains the state’s wealth. Thus the sage avoids prolonged warfare. In the early years of the Daye era, Emperor Yang of Sui amassed troops and delighted in conquest, yet his strength was spent at the Hangu Pass and his army broken at the Liao River. Dredging canals to divert the Huai, transport grew ever more vast, and with armies marching ten thousand *li*, the state’s resources were exhausted. Then Yang Xuangan and Li Mi rose to exploit his weakness—even had Su Wei and Gao Jiong been present, could they have devised a remedy?

Du Mu said: When an army is prolonged in war without victory, both finances and strength are exhausted, and neighboring lords will rise to exploit the weakness. Even with the wisest strategists, none can devise a sound plan thereafter.
Jia Lin said: With people dispersed and wealth depleted, even if Yi and Lü were reborn, they could not save such ruin.
Du You said: Though there may be tactical advantages in waging war at the time, none can prevent the ensuing calamity.
Mei Yaochen said: In seeking victory and besieging cities, if the army is exposed and prolonged, neighboring lords will exploit this weakness to strike. Even with wise generals, none can resist.
Wang Zhe said: Such exhaustion brings the peril of destruction.
He Shi said: *Thereafter* refers to when, having failed to prevail, the enemy seizes the moment of danger. Even the wise cannot devise a flawless plan to ensure survival.
Zhang Yu said: When the army is weary, strength spent, and resources exhausted, neighboring states will exploit this weakness to strike. Even with the wisest strategists, none can prevent the ensuing disaster. As when Wu invaded Chu and entered Ying, lingering too long before returning, Yue forces then invaded Wu. At that time, even with Wu Zixu and Sun Wu, how could they have devised a sound plan thereafter?

Thus in war, one hears of clumsy haste but never sees skillful slowness.

Cao Cao and Li Quan said: Though clumsy, speed brings victory. *Never seen* means it does not exist.
Du Mu said: In attack and conquest, though clumsy in strategy, speed is paramount. Without the calamities of prolonged warfare—exhausted finances, dulled blades—this is true skill.
Meng Shi said: Though clumsy, speed brings victory.
Chen Bu said: As in the saying, "Swift as thunder leaving no time to cover one’s ears, or lightning leaving no time to blink."
Du You’s annotation agrees with Meng Shi.
Mei Yaochen said: Clumsy haste is still preferable to skillful slowness.
Wang Zhe said: Prolonged warfare ages the army, exhausts finances, and impoverishes the people. Even the skillful cannot avoid calamity.
He Shi said: Haste, though clumsy, spares resources; slowness, though skillful, risks future disaster.

Later, Yao Chang of Later Qin clashed with Fu Deng. Yao Chang’s general, Gou Yao, occupied Niwan Fortress and secretly allied with Fu Deng. Yao Chang fought Fu Deng and was defeated at Matou Plain, then regrouped to fight again. Yao Shuode said to the generals: "Our lord is cautious in battle, always preferring strategy over direct engagement. Now that we have suffered defeat and press the enemy further, there must be reason." Yao Chang heard this and said to Shuode: "Fu Deng wages war slowly, unable to discern reality. Now he advances lightly to occupy our east, and Gou Yao has allied with him. If this drags on, the situation will shift, and the disaster will be incalculable. The reason for hastening battle is to prevent Gou Yao, that fool, from completing his schemes and deepening his treachery." Indeed, he crushed Fu Deng.

In the early years of Empress Wu, Xu Jingye raised troops in Jiangdu, claiming to restore the imperial house. He appointed the prison warden Wei Sigong as his chief strategist and sought his counsel. Wei replied: "My lord, having imprisoned the young emperor on behalf of the Empress Dowager, your aim is restoration. In war, clumsy haste is prized. You should swiftly cross the Huai to the north, personally lead the main force, and march directly on the eastern capital. The generals and soldiers of Shandong, knowing of your loyal endeavor, will follow you even unto death."

Thus, victory could be secured within days, and the realm would be pacified. Xu Jingye wished to adopt this strategy, but Pi Zhang advised him otherwise: "The land of Jinling bears the imperial *qi*; we should swiftly respond to this omen. Moreover, the great river forms a natural barrier, sufficient for our defense. Let us first seize Changzhou and Runzhou to lay the foundation of our kingly enterprise. Then, we may lead our troops northward, advancing with the force of a drumbeat—this way, we shall have a secure retreat and face no disadvantage in our advance. Truly, this is the soundest strategy."

Jingye deemed this counsel wise. He personally led four thousand men south across the river to strike Runzhou. In secret, Wei Sigong said to Du Qiuren: "Military strength must be concentrated, never divided. Now, Jingye fails to consolidate his forces, cross the Huai, and rally the masses of Shandong to converge on Luoyang—how can he possibly succeed?" As foretold, he met defeat.

Zhang Yu remarked: "Victory is better won through clumsy haste than cunning delay. When Sima Xuanwang campaigned against Shangyong, he planned for a year’s campaign in a single month, disregarding casualties and outpacing his grain supply—this was the essence of prizing clumsy haste. No state has ever profited from prolonged warfare."

Li Quan said: "The *Spring and Autumn Annals* states: 'War is like fire—if not restrained, it will consume itself.'"

Jia Lin observed: "When war drags on without success, the feudal lords grow restless."

Du You warned: "War is an instrument of misfortune; prolonged, it breeds disaster. When Zhibo besieged Zhao for over a year without returning, he was ultimately captured by Xiangzi, his person slain and his state divided. Thus, the *Xinxu* records: 'Those who delight in war and exhaust their martial strength shall perish without exception.'"

Mei Yaochen asked: "When strength is exhausted and resources depleted, what profit remains?"

Zhang Yu replied: "When armies grow weary and treasuries empty, what benefit accrues to the state? Only those who fully grasp the harms of warfare can fully comprehend its advantages."

Li Quan said: "Profit and harm arise from the same source. First understand harm, then profit becomes clear."

Du Mu explained: "Harm lies in exhausting the people and depleting wealth; profit lies in devouring the enemy and expanding territory. If one ignores the limits of one’s own strength, even those in the same boat become enemies—how then can one profit from the foe?"

Jia Lin cautioned: "When generals grow arrogant and soldiers indolent, when they covet gain and forget danger—this is the gravest harm."

Du You added: "He who schemes for a state or mobilizes an army without first weighing the perils of destruction cannot hope to secure advantage. When the Duke of Qin saw the opportunity to raid Zheng but ignored the disaster at Xiaohan, or when the King of Wu boasted of his triumph over Qi yet forgot the calamity at Gusu—such were their undoings."

Mei Yaochen noted: "To conscript but once, to transport grain but twice—this is profit. To empty the common folk and drain the public coffers—this is harm. Without understanding harm, how can one know profit?"

Wang Zhe observed: "Even if victory comes through prolonged effort, harm is inescapable. Speed alone exhausts all advantage."

Zhang Yu concluded: "Only by first recognizing the harms of weary armies and depleted resources can one grasp the benefits of capturing enemies and securing victory."

The adept commander does not conscript twice nor transport grain thrice.

Cao Cao said: "*Ji* means levy. It speaks of initially conscripting the people and achieving victory without returning home to raise more troops. At first, grain is carried, but later, provisions are taken from the enemy. Upon returning, no grain is sent to meet the army."

Li Quan explained: "*Ji* refers to records. To avoid conscripting twice is to prevent the people from growing weary and resentful. When Qin mobilized the forces of Guanzhong, it led to the uprisings of Chen and Wu."

When an army marches out, provisions are measured by distance and carried; when it returns, grain is sent to meet it—this is called "transporting thrice." But when crossing borders, one lodges and forages among the enemy, so there is no need for a third transport.

Du Mu said: "Assess whether the enemy can be attacked and whether your own forces are prepared for battle. Only then should you raise troops, ensuring victory before returning."

Zheng Xuan’s commentary on the *Rites of Zhou* states: "*Yi* refers to mobilizing troops; *ji* refers to the registers of the five-man squads. By organizing the people into groups of five and integrating civil administration with military command, troops are raised through these registers."

Chen Hao said: "*Ji* means to borrow; not conscripting twice means not repeatedly borrowing the people’s labor. Grain is carried on the outward journey and met upon return—thus, it is not transported thrice. This avoids exhausting the army and depleting the state, for speed brings advantage."

Mei Yaochen’s gloss aligns with Chen Hao’s. Wang Zhe’s aligns with Cao Cao’s.

Zhang Yu said: "*Yi* refers to the labor of mobilizing troops. The commentary on the *Hexagram of the Army* states: 'Undertaking great labor with heavy burdens—without success, it brings misfortune.' *Ji* refers to the registers for troop mobilization. Thus, the Han system included 'foot-long registers and five-man tallies.' It means that with a single mobilization, victory is won, and no further conscription is needed. Grain is carried at the outset, plundered beyond the border, and met upon return—thus, it is not transported thrice. This teaches that armies must not be exposed to prolonged hardship. By drawing supplies from the state and provisions from the enemy, the army’s food will be sufficient."

Cao Cao said: "Weapons and armor are taken from the state; grain is taken from the enemy."

Li Quan said: "Equipped with our own arms and relying on the enemy’s food, even a thousand *li* campaign will know no want."

Du You said: "Weapons and armor are taken from the state; grain is taken from the enemy. Resources are drawn from one’s own land, while provisions are seized from the enemy’s."

This was exemplified when the Jin army lodged and foraged in Chu.

Mei Yaochen said: "The army’s needs are supplied by the state; its rations are taken from the enemy."

He Shi explained: "*Yin* means that when troops cross borders, they seize and plunder the countryside, and upon capturing cities or defeating enemies, they take their stores."

Zhang Yu said: "Equipment is taken from the state because goods are light and easy to transport; grain is taken from the enemy because grain is heavy and difficult to move. Transporting grain over a thousand *li* leaves soldiers gaunt, so taking grain from the enemy ensures ample food. A state impoverished by war suffers from distant transport; distant transport impoverishes the people."

Li Quan said: "Frequent military campaigns lead to heavy taxation and levies."

Du Mu said: "Guanzi states: When grain is transported three hundred *li*, the state has no one year’s reserves; when transported four hundred *li*, it has no two years’ reserves; when transported five hundred *li*, the people show signs of hunger. This speaks to grain being a heavy commodity—light goods cannot be substituted for it. If substituted, both farmers and plow oxen abandon the fields, and thus the people cannot but grow impoverished."

Jia Lin said: "Distant transport depletes wealth on the roads and exhausts resources in transit, leaving the people increasingly destitute."

Meng Shi said: "When war chariots transport supplies beyond a thousand *li*, wealth is wasted on the roads, and the people suffer hardship."

Zhang Yu said: "To sustain an army of a hundred thousand with the resources of seven hundred thousand households at a distance of a thousand *li* leaves the people no choice but to grow poor. Those near the army sell at exorbitant prices; such profiteering drains the people’s wealth."

Cao Cao said: "Once the army crosses the border, those near the troops demand high prices for goods, and all such profiteering leaves the people utterly depleted."

Li Quan said: "Where the army encamps, trade flourishes, and the people, lured by profit, sell their property to follow, thus exhausting themselves."

Jia Lin said: "Where troops gather, prices soar. People seek extraordinary profits, selling their goods to exhaustion. Though they may gain much at first, they ultimately grow weary and their resources run dry. Moreover, extraordinary levies drive sellers to demand endless prices, and the people, straining to buy, naturally deplete both household and state."

Du You said: "Near the army, markets engage in extraordinary profiteering. At the time, people pursue quick gains, but in the end, wealth is exhausted and both households and the state are drained."

Mei Yaochen said: "Those far away supply labor for transport, while those nearby profiteer through exorbitant prices—both are paths to impoverishing the state and depleting the people."

Wang Zhe said: "Distant transport wearies the people and wastes resources; nearby markets inflate prices. Thus, prolonged warfare exhausts the state."

Cao Cao said: "Once the army crosses the border, those near the troops demand wealth and sell at high prices." (Wang Zhe noted this refers to the moment of crossing.)

Zhang Yu said: "The people near the army, seeking profit, inflate the prices of their goods for those who come from afar to transport supplies, leaving their wealth no choice but to be exhausted. When wealth is exhausted, urgency in levies follows."

Zhang Yu said: "When financial and material resources are depleted, the levies of the *qiu* and *jing* grow urgent and difficult to meet." (Some say *qiu* levies refer to the system of *qiu jia* instituted by Duke Cheng of Lu, where, in times of urgent need, *qiu* and *dian* levies were imposed, deviating from normal regulations. A *qiu* comprises sixteen *jing*, and a *dian* sixty-four *jing*.)

When strength is exhausted and wealth depleted, the Central Plains grow hollow within. The people’s expenses consume seven-tenths of their resources.

Cao Cao said: "*Qiu* refers to sixteen *jing*. When the army does not disband, grain transport exhausts the people’s strength in the fields, and seven-tenths of their resources are lost."

Li Quan said: "When war drags on without end, men and women grow resentful and lonely, burdened by transport and *qiu* levies. Strength is exhausted, wealth depleted, and the people’s expenses consume seven-tenths of their resources."

Du Mu said: "The *Methods of the Sima* states: Six *chi* make a *bu*, a hundred *bu* make a *mu*, a hundred *mu* make a *fu*, three *fu* make a *wu*, three *wu* make a *jing*, four *jing* make a *yi*, four *yi* make a *qiu*, and four *qiu* make a *dian*. A *qiu* thus comprises sixteen *jing*. A *qiu* provides one warhorse, four oxen; a *dian* provides four warhorses, sixteen oxen. A *qiu* supplies one chariot, three armored soldiers, and seventy-two foot soldiers. Here, with the army undispersed, *qiu* levies grow ever more urgent, the people’s grain and wealth are exhausted, and their strength is spent in the fields, with seven-tenths of their household resources consumed."

Chen Hao said: "*Qiu* means to gather. Gathering levies and corvée labor to meet military needs thus depletes wealth from the people, leaving none untroubled."

Wang Shi said: "Urgency refers to levies exceeding normal taxation, as when Duke Cheng of Lu instituted *qiu jia*. In such cases, the people’s expenses exceed half." (The text mentions "seven-tenths" to highlight the disparity between public and private expenses.)

Cao Cao said: "*Qiu* comprises sixteen *jing*. When the army does not disband, grain transport exhausts the people’s strength in the fields."

He Shi said: "The state is founded on the people, and the people depend on food for survival. Those who govern must cherish these resources."

Zhang Yu said: "Transporting grain exhausts strength; supplying provisions depletes wealth. The people of the fields see their household resources hollowed out, with seven-tenths of their expenses consumed."

The state’s expenses—broken chariots, worn-out horses, armor, bows and arrows, halberds, shields, large oxen, and heavy wagons—consume six-tenths of its resources. (One version reads seven-tenths.)

Cao Cao said: "*Qiu* oxen refer to oxen from *qiu* villages. Heavy wagons are long-hubbed carts."

Li Quan said: "*Qiu* means great. These items are all military necessities. The text speaks of the state’s expenses, near and far, with seven-tenths of its resources lost."

Mei Yaochen said: "The people’s contributions of wealth, grain, and labor to the army consume seven-tenths of their resources; the state’s contributions of oxen, horses, and equipment consume six-tenths. Thus, exhausting levies and pursuing war impoverish the people, while urgent corvée and poverty hollow out the state."

Wang Zhe said: "*Xun* means shield. *Bi* can be used for cover. *Lu* are large shields. *Qiu* oxen are what the ancients called "one horse and one *qiu* ox." *Da che* are ox-drawn wagons. The *Yi* states: "Great wagons bear their loads." Zhang Yu said: "Chariots and horses are the foundation of warfare, so the text first speaks of worn-out chariots and exhausted horses. *Bi* and *lu* are shields—today called *pengpai*. *Qiu* oxen are large oxen. *Da che* are leather-covered supply wagons. The first mention of broken chariots and weary horses refers to the war chariots used in attack and battle. The next mention of *qiu* oxen and *da che* refers to the leather-covered supply wagons. Even the state’s chariots, horses, and equipment suffer six-tenths loss. Thus, a wise commander ensures provisions are taken from the enemy: one *zhong* of enemy grain is worth twenty of ours, and one *dan* of enemy fodder is worth twenty of ours."

Cao Cao said: "Six *hu* and four *dou* make one *zhong*. *Cong* are bean stalks. *Gan* are grain stalks. One *dan* is one hundred twenty *jin*. The method of transport costs twenty *dan* to deliver one. Another version reads: *Cong*, pronounced *ji*, means beans. Seventy *jin* make one *dan*. 'Worth twenty of ours' refers to the cost of long-distance transport."

Du Mu said: "Six *shi* and four *dou* make one *zhong*. One *shi* is one hundred twenty *jin*. *Cong* are bean stalks. *Gan* are grain stalks. Some say *cong* refers to the leaves. When Qin attacked the Xiongnu, it mobilized the empire to transport grain, starting from the coastal commanderies of Huang and Langya, conveying it to Beihe. Roughly thirty *zhong* were expended to deliver one *dan*. During Emperor Wu of Han’s reign, when he opened communications with the Western Regions, tens of thousands labored. Transporting provisions a thousand *li* on foot required over ten *zhong* to deliver one *dan*. Comparing this to Sunzi’s statement—one *zhong* of enemy grain being worth twenty of ours—he likely refers to the cost of transporting provisions a thousand *li* on level ground, where twenty *dan* are spent to deliver one. Without specifying distance, the text may be incomplete. *Huang* is pronounced *zhirui* or *shui*, in Donglai. Beihe is today’s Shuofang Commandery."

Li Quan said: "Transporting one *zhong* of grain over long distances costs twenty *zhong* before it reaches the army. A wise commander seeks provisions from the enemy to spare his own expenses."

Meng Shi said: "Ten *hu* make one *zhong*. Calculating transport over a thousand *li*, with losses along the way, twenty *zhong* are needed to deliver one *zhong* to the army."

Mei Yaochen’s annotation agrees with Cao Cao’s.

Wang Zhe said: "Cao Cao states: *Cong* are bean stalks; *gan* are leaves. One *dan* is one hundred twenty *jin*. The method of transport costs twenty to deliver one. The earlier reference to transporting provisions a thousand *li* implies that the transport method applies to that distance. *Cong* is now written as *ji*. *Gan* was mistakenly written as *yu* in old texts and should be *gan*."

Zhang Yu said: "Six *shi* and four *dou* make one *zhong*. One hundred twenty *jin* make one *dan*. *Cong* are bean stalks. *Gan* are grain leaves. Transporting provisions a thousand *li* costs twenty *zhong* or *dan* to deliver one *zhong* or *dan* to the army. If the route is perilous, the cost is even greater. Thus, when Qin campaigned against the Xiongnu, thirty *zhong* were expended to deliver one *dan*. This shows that a capable commander seizes provisions from the enemy. Therefore, those who slay the enemy are roused by anger."

Cao Cao said: "By displaying wrath to intimidate the enemy."

Li Quan said: "Anger refers to military might."

Du Mu said: "Not all ten thousand men can be equally enraged; the commander must incite them with circumstances. When Tian Dan defended Jimo, he had the Yan army mutilate deserters and dig up the graves of the city’s dead—such acts exemplify this."

Jia Lin said: "Without anger, men will not kill."

Wang Xi said: "The army’s power lies in its wrath."

He Shi said: "When Yan besieged Jimo in Qi, they mutilated Qi’s deserters, enraging the people of Jimo and strengthening their resolve to defend the city. Tian Dan then spread rumors: 'We fear the Yan army will dig up our ancestral graves outside the city and desecrate our forebears—this chills our hearts.' The Yan army proceeded to excavate the graves and burn the corpses. The people of Jimo, watching from the city walls, wept and their fury multiplied tenfold. Knowing his soldiers were ready, Tian Dan crushed the Yan forces. Later, when Ban Chao was sent to the Western Regions and arrived in Shanshan, he gathered his thirty-six officers and men to drink. As the wine flowed, he roused them: 'We are all in this remote land, seeking great deeds and wealth. The barbarian envoys have arrived for only a few days, yet the king’s courtesy has already waned. If they hand us over to the Xiongnu, our bones will forever feed jackals.' His officers replied: 'In this land of peril, we live or die with you.' Ban Chao said: 'One who does not enter the tiger’s den will not catch its cubs. Our only course is to attack the barbarians by night with fire, making them think our numbers vast. Their terror will allow us to annihilate them. Destroying them will secure our success.' The men agreed: 'Well said.'"

At the first watch of the night, Ban Chao led his officers and men to rush the barbarian camp. Just then, a great wind arose. Ban Chao ordered ten men to take drums and hide behind the barbarians’ quarters, instructing them: "When you see the fire rise, all must beat the drums and shout loudly." The rest were to take bows and crossbows, lying in ambush on either side of the gate. Ban Chao set fire to the camp with the wind, and the barbarians, thrown into panic, were all burned to death.

Earlier, Pang Tong had urged Liu Bei to seize Yi Province from its governor, Liu Zhang. Liu Bei replied: "This is a grave matter; it cannot be done in haste." Later, when Liu Zhang sent Liu Bei to attack Zhang Lu, Liu Bei requested ten thousand troops and supplies from Liu Zhang for his eastward march. Liu Zhang granted only four thousand soldiers, and the rest of the provisions were halved. Thereupon, Liu Bei roused his men, saying: "I have led you to campaign against a formidable foe in Yi Province. Our forces have toiled without rest. Now, though we have amassed wealth in the treasury, we are too timid to reward merit. How can we expect the officers and men to fight with their lives?" Thus, they united to defeat Liu Zhang.

Zhang Yu said: "Rouse our soldiers so that all, from top to bottom, share the same anger, and the enemy can be slain."

*The Wei Liaozi* states: "What makes the people fight is *qi*. By this is meant that when *qi* is roused to anger, every man will fight of his own accord. What draws the enemy’s advantage is wealth."

Cao Cao said: "Without wealth, soldiers will not come; without rewards, soldiers will not go."

Li Quan said: "Wealth is what enriches the army’s resources."

Du Mu said: "What makes soldiers see the enemy’s advantage as worth taking is wealth. By this is meant that when they seize the enemy’s goods, these are used as rewards, so that all men are filled with desire and each fights for himself."

Later, during the Han dynasty, Du Shang, Inspector of Jing Province, campaigned against the rebel leaders Bu Yang and Pan Hong of Gui Province. Advancing into Nanhai, he broke three of their strongholds and seized many treasures. Yet Pan Hong’s forces still held together in great numbers, and the soldiers, grown arrogant and wealthy, had lost their fighting spirit. Du Shang said: "Bu Yang and Pan Hong have been rebels for ten years and are well-versed in attack and defense. We must combine the strength of several commanderies to defeat them. For now, let the army freely hunt and pursue game." The soldiers rejoiced, and all, great and small, went out to hunt. Du Shang then secretly sent men to set fire to their camp, reducing all their accumulated treasures to ashes. When the hunters returned, none could hold back their tears. Du Shang said: "Bu Yang and his men have enough wealth to enrich several generations. You, gentlemen, have only failed to unite your strength. What was lost was but a trifle—why take it to heart?" Hearing this, the men were all stirred to indignation and eager to fight. Du Shang ordered the horses to be fed and the men to eat their fill, then at dawn they marched straight for the rebels’ stronghold. Bu Yang and Pan Hong, unprepared, were overwhelmed by the soldiers’ ferocity and utterly defeated. This is just such a case.

Meng Shi’s commentary agrees with Du Mu’s.

Du You states: When men know that victory over the enemy brings rich rewards, they will brave flashing blades and face arrows and stones, advancing gladly into battle—all lured by the promise of wealth, merit, and recompense for their toil.

Mei Yaochen observes: To slay the enemy is to rouse our men with wrath; to seize the enemy’s goods is to entice our men with wealth.

Wang Zhe remarks: This means offering generous rewards. If the men are left to claim spoils for themselves, they may disregard discipline.

Zhang Yu says: By dangling wealth before the troops and letting each man fight for his own gain, the enemy’s riches may be taken. Thus it is said: Where rewards are great, brave men will surely appear.

During the reign of our dynasty’s Grand Ancestor, when generals were sent to conquer Shu, he instructed them: “Whatever cities and towns you capture, empty the treasury to feast the soldiers. What the state desires is only land and territory.” Thereupon, officers and men fought to the death, every place they reached fell, and Shu was pacified.

In chariot warfare, if ten or more enemy chariots are captured, reward the first to seize them. Cao Cao notes: When chariot forces capture ten or more enemy chariots, they are to be rewarded. Why does the text say “reward the first to seize them” rather than “reward those who capture ten or more chariots”? It is to make clear that the soldiers who actually took the chariots are to be rewarded.

The method for arraying chariots: five chariots form a squadron, led by one *pushe*; ten chariots form a company, led by one *zuchang*; when the number reaches ten chariots, two officers are assigned. Following this structure, rewards are bestowed to ensure that the commander’s favor reaches down to the ranks.

Some say: If a unit with ten or more chariots engages the enemy, only those who have rendered merit are rewarded. If fewer than ten chariots are captured—even if only one is taken—the other nine are also rewarded, to encourage all to advance. Li Quan states: Heavy rewards spur men forward.

Du Mu explains: Capturing ten or more chariots is the result of the whole force’s effort. If rewards are too narrowly given, they will not suffice. The state should use the captured chariots and their contents to generously reward those who first devised the plan or led the charge, thereby encouraging the troops. Thus the earlier passage states: “What entices men to seize the enemy’s advantages is wealth.” The mention of ten chariots is merely to set the standard.

Jia Lin remarks: This spurs those who have not yet succeeded to strive harder.

Mei Yaochen notes: Narrow rewards cannot reach all, so by rewarding one, a hundred are encouraged.

Wang Zhe says: Reward with wealth the soldiers who first seized the chariots.

Zhang Yu observes: A single chariot requires nearly seventy-five men. If our forces capture ten or more enemy chariots in battle, no fewer than a thousand of our men must have been involved. Because the number is so great, rewards cannot be narrowly given; instead, those who first broke the enemy lines and seized the chariots are generously rewarded to inspire the rest.

In ancient warfare, chariots were used to capture chariots, cavalry to capture cavalry, and infantry to capture infantry. Thus Wu Qi, when battling the Qin, commanded his three armies: “If charioteers do not capture enemy chariots, cavalrymen do not capture enemy cavalry, and foot soldiers do not capture enemy infantry, even if the army is victorious, no merit will be recognized.” He then changed the enemy’s banners and flags. Cao Cao adds: “To match our own.”

Li Quan says: "Their ill-favored colors match our own."
Jia Lin says: "So they will not be recognized."
Zhang Yu says: "Change the enemy’s colors to match our own. When chariots are mixed and ridden,
Cao Cao says: "Do not rely on them alone."
Li Quan says: "The banners of surrendered captives must have their colors altered and their functions intermingled before their chariots can be used."
Du Mu says: "When soldiers capture enemy chariots on their own, they may ride them freely without official record."
Mei Yaochen says: "Chariots may be mixed in use, but banners must not retain their former appearance."
Wang Shi says: "When enemy chariots are captured, they may be interspersed with our own."
Zhang Yu says: "Our chariots and the enemy’s are used together in mixed formation; they must not be relied upon alone. Treat the captured soldiers well and provide for them,
Zhang Yu says: "The captured soldiers must be nurtured with kindness and trust so they may be employed in our service."
This is called "defeating the enemy and increasing one’s strength."
Cao Cao says: "Increasing one’s own strength."
Li Quan says: "Later, Emperor Guangwu of the Han defeated the Tongma rebels in Nanyang, capturing tens of thousands of prisoners. He assigned them to various units, yet their hearts remained unsettled. Guangwu ordered them to return to their original camps and then visited them unguarded to offer his regards. They said to one another, ‘The Prince of Xiao lays his heart bare and places it in our bellies—how could we not pledge our lives to him?’ Thus the Han grew ever stronger. This is the meaning of the passage."

Du Mu says: To gain enemy soldiers is to utilize the enemy’s resources to augment one’s own strength.
Mei Yaochen says: When capturing soldiers, employ their strengths, nurture them with kindness, and thus turn them to our service.
Wang Zhe says: When enemy soldiers are captured, should they be treated the same as our own? The "good" here means not to humiliate them. If one lavishes care upon newly surrendered troops, it may instead lose their hearts.
He Shi says: By leveraging the enemy to defeat the enemy, how can one not grow stronger?
Zhang Yu says: To defeat the enemy and seize their chariots and soldiers, then employ them for our use, is to increase one’s own strength. This is like Guangwu laying his heart bare, causing each man to pledge his life in return.

Thus, in warfare, victory is valued, not prolonged campaigns.
Cao Cao says: Prolonged campaigns are disadvantageous.
War is like fire—if not restrained, it will burn itself out.
Meng Shi says: Speed in victory and swift return are paramount.
Mei Yaochen says: All the above emphasizes haste. Haste conserves resources and spares the people’s strength.
He Shi says: Sunzi begins and ends by discussing the perils of prolonged warfare, for he deeply understood that war must not be trifled with, nor military power abused.
Zhang Yu says: Prolonged campaigns exhaust troops and deplete resources, making mutiny more likely. Thus, only swift victory and rapid return are valued.

Therefore, the general who understands warfare is the arbiter of the people’s lives, the master of the nation’s safety.
Cao Cao says: A worthy general ensures the nation’s stability.
Li Quan says: The general holds the power of life and death, the authority to repel enemies. The people’s fate and the nation’s safety rest upon him.
Du Mu says: The people’s lives and the nation’s safety all depend on the general.
Mei Yaochen says: This speaks to the weight of the general’s responsibility.
Wang Zhe says: A worthy general preserves the people’s lives and secures the nation; otherwise, the people suffer and the nation is imperiled. How can a wise ruler not be discerning in his appointments?
He Shi says: The people’s lives and the nation’s order all hinge on the general. The difficulty of finding such a general has been a constant from ancient times to the present.
Zhang Yu says: The people’s survival and the nation’s safety depend on whether the general is worthy.

*End of Sunzi Annotation, Scroll Two*
*(Note: #1 "Ox" and #2 "Far" are textual variants from the Song edition of the Eleven Commentaries.)*

*Sunzi Annotation, Scroll Three*
*Strategic Assault*

Cao Cao says: To attack the enemy, one must first plan.
Li Quan says: Arraying forces for battle is called "war"; besieging cities is called "attack." This chapter follows "War" in sequence.
Du Mu says: When calculations in the ancestral temple are settled, war implements and provisions fully prepared, one may then plan the attack. Hence it is called "Strategic Assault."
Wang Xi says: In planning an attack, weigh the enemy’s advantages and disadvantages, employ comprehensive strategies to subdue them, and avoid recklessly resorting to armed assault or siege.
Zhang Yu says: Once plans are finalized and preparations complete, one may then employ wisdom and strategy to attack, hence this chapter follows "War."

Sunzi says: In the principles of warfare, subduing the enemy’s state intact is supreme; destroying the state is secondary.
Cao Cao says: Advancing deep into enemy territory, encircling their cities, cutting off their internal and external communications, and compelling the entire state to submit is supreme. Defeating them through force and seizing the state is secondary.
Li Quan says: Victory without slaughter is prized. Han Xin captured Wei Bao, seized Xia Yue, and slew Cheng Anjun—these were cases of destroying the state. Yet when he adopted Guangwu Jun’s strategy, turned north toward Yan, and dispatched a single envoy with a letter, Yan subsequently submitted without resistance—this was subduing the state intact.
Jia Lin says: To fully secure the enemy’s state while preserving one’s own is supreme.
Du You says: Compelling the enemy state to submit is supreme; defeating them through force is secondary.
Wang Xi says: As when Han Xin subdued Yan.
He Shi says: Using tactics and momentum to make the enemy surrender their state is the highest strategy.
Zhang Yu says: The *Weiliaozi* states: "Assess the enemy’s strength, disrupt their morale, and scatter their forces. Though their form remains intact, they become useless—this is victory through strategy. Breaking their armies, slaying their generals, scaling walls, triggering siege engines, assembling troops to seize territory—this is victory through force." Thus, what is called victory through strategy or force corresponds to subduing the state intact or destroying it. When punishing the wicked and aiding the people, complete victory is supreme; when compelled to resort to destruction, it is secondary.

Subduing the enemy’s army intact is supreme; destroying their army is secondary.
Cao Cao and Du Mu say: The *Methods of Sima* states: 15,500 men constitute an army.
He Shi says: Inducing their cities to surrender without destroying one’s own forces.
Subduing the enemy’s brigade intact is supreme; destroying their brigade is secondary.
Cao Cao says: 500 men constitute a brigade.
Subduing the enemy’s company intact is supreme; destroying their company is secondary.
Cao Cao says: From one company down to 100 men.
Li Quan says: 100 men and above constitute a company.
Du You says: From one company down to 100 men.
Subduing the enemy’s squad intact is supreme; destroying their squad is secondary.
Cao Cao says: From 100 men down to five.
Li Quan says: Below 100 men constitute a squad.
Du Mu says: Five men constitute a squad.
Mei Yaochen says: The greater the planning, the more complete the victory.
Wang Xi says: Whether an army, company, or squad, regardless of size, subduing them intact enhances one’s prestige and virtue, while destroying them diminishes it.
He Shi says: From armies to squads, all are ranked in order of superiority. The essence is to employ strategy to secure victory, for not only an entire army but even a single squad must not be destroyed without necessity.
Zhang Yu says: According to Zhou regulations, 12,500 men constitute an army, 500 a brigade, 100 a company, and five a squad. From armies to squads, victory without battle is supreme.

Thus, to fight a hundred battles and win a hundred victories is not the acme of excellence.
Cao Cao says: To make the enemy submit without fighting is the true victory.
Li Quan says: To defeat the enemy through strategy.
Chen Hao says: Because battle inevitably entails killing.
Jia Lin says: When military might spreads far and wide, compelling the enemy to surrender intact, this is supreme. Employing deception and stratagems to crush the enemy’s forces, inflicting harm and destruction before securing victory, is secondary.
Du You says: To make the enemy submit without fighting.
Mei Yaochen says: How detestable is killing and destruction!
Zhang Yu says: Victory achieved only after battle must involve much slaughter, hence it is not the highest excellence.

To subdue the enemy without fighting is the acme of excellence.
Cao Cao says: To make the enemy submit without fighting.
Du Mu says: To defeat the enemy through strategy.
Chen Hao says: Han Xin, employing Li Zuoche’s plan, dispatched a letter and seized Yan without battle.
Meng Shi says: This is victory through the ancestral temple.
Wang Xi says: Warfare values defeating the enemy’s strategy, not engaging in battle.
He Shi says: During the Later Han, Wang Ba attacked Zhou Jian and Su Mao. After battle, he returned to camp, and the enemy regrouped to provoke another fight. Ba remained resolutely in his tent. As he feasted his troops and musicians played, Mao’s arrows rained down, one striking the goblet before Ba, yet he sat unmoved. His officers said, "Mao is already defeated; now is the time to strike." Ba replied, "Not so. Mao’s troops are mercenaries, far from their supplies, and seek a quick victory through provocation. By closing our camp and resting our troops, we achieve what is called subduing the enemy without fighting—the acme of excellence." Mao then withdrew.
Zhang Yu says: Enforcing rewards and punishments, ensuring the reliability of signals, maintaining equipment, training troops, and exposing one’s strengths to make the enemy submit like grass bending in the wind—this is the greatest excellence. As when the King of Wu convened at Huangchi, the Jin, awed by his discipline, submitted.

Thus, the supreme military strategy is to attack the enemy’s plans.
Cao Cao says: When the enemy first formulates plans, it is easy to thwart them.
Li Quan says: To thwart their initial strategy. During the Later Han, Kou Xun besieged Gao Jun. Jun dispatched his strategist Huangfu Wen to meet Xun, but Wen’s words and demeanor were unyielding. Xun executed him and reported to Jun, "Your military advisor was insolent; I have slain him. If you wish to surrender, do so swiftly; if not, prepare to defend."

Gao Jun opened his fortifications and surrendered that very day. The generals asked, "How is it that by killing his envoy, you caused his city to surrender?" Kou Xun replied, "Huangfu Wen was Jun’s trusted advisor, the one who devised his strategies. Had we kept him, he would have gained insight into our plans; by killing him, we shattered Jun’s courage—this is what is meant by 'the supreme military strategy is to attack the enemy’s plans.'" The generals said, "This is beyond our understanding."

Du Mu says: When the Duke Ping of Jin sought to attack Qi, he sent Fan Zhao to observe their state. Duke Jing of Qi offered him wine. As the drinking grew lively, Fan Zhao requested to drink from the ruler’s own goblet. The Duke said, "Serve the guest from my goblet." After Fan Zhao drank, Yanzi removed the goblet and had another poured. Fan Zhao feigned drunkenness, rose in displeasure, and began to dance, saying to the grand master, "Can you play for me the music of Chengzhou? I shall dance to it." The grand master replied, "Your servant is untrained in it." Fan Zhao hastily departed. Duke Jing said, "Jin is a great state, and has come to observe our governance. Now you have angered their envoy—what is to be done?" Yanzi replied, "I saw that Fan Zhao was not one ignorant of ritual. Moreover, he sought to shame our state, so I did not comply." The grand master said, "The music of Chengzhou is the music of the Son of Heaven, to be danced only by the ruler. Now Fan Zhao, a mere subject, wished to dance to the Son of Heaven’s music—so I did not play it." Fan Zhao returned and reported to Duke Ping of Jin, saying, "Qi cannot yet be attacked. I sought to humiliate their ruler, but Yanzi saw through it; I sought to violate their ritual, but the grand master recognized it."

Confucius said, "Without overstepping the space between wine vessel and meat platter, one may yet repel an enemy a thousand *li* away—this describes Yanzi.

During the Spring and Autumn period, Qin attacked Jin, and Jin’s commander Zhao Dun led the defense. The deputy commander of the upper army, Yu Pian, said, ‘Qin cannot sustain a long campaign; let us deepen our ramparts and fortify our troops to await them.’ The Qin forces wished to fight, and the Duke of Qin asked Shi Hui, ‘How should we engage them?’ Shi Hui replied, ‘The Zhao clan has recently promoted one of their retainers, Yu Pian, who must be the architect of this strategy, intending to wear down our forces. The Zhao have a collateral kinsman named Chuan, son-in-law to the Lord of Jin, favored yet weak, uninvolved in military affairs, reckless and headstrong, and resentful of Yu Pian’s appointment as deputy commander of the upper army. If we provoke the impetuous among them, it may succeed.’

The Qin army ambushed Jin’s upper army. Zhao Chuan pursued but could not overtake them; returning in fury, he said, ‘We pack provisions and don armor, seeking battle—yet when the enemy arrives, we do not strike. What are we waiting for?’ The army’s officers replied, ‘The commander has his reasons.’ Chuan said, ‘I know nothing of strategy; I will go out alone.’ And so he led his troops forth.

Zhao Dun said, ‘If Qin captures Chuan, they will have taken a minister. If Qin returns victorious, how shall we answer?’ Thus, all forces sallied forth, and the armies clashed before withdrawing.

Yanzi’s response was this: when the enemy plotted to attack us, we first thwarted their scheme, so the enemy could not attack us. Shi Hui’s response was this: when we plotted to attack the enemy, the enemy devised a counter-strategy, so we thwarted their plan, and the enemy could not engage us in battle. Both cases involved thwarting strategy.

Thus, when the enemy plots against us, we strike at their unformed schemes; when we plot against the enemy, we shatter their already-formed plans—this is not limited to a single method.

Master Meng said, ‘Nine attacks and nine repulsions—this is strategy.’ Du You said, ‘When the enemy devises a plan to mobilize their forces, thwarting it is the highest course.’ Thus, the Grand Duke said, ‘He who excels at eliminating peril addresses it before it arises; he who excels at defeating the enemy triumphs where no form yet exists.’

Mei Yaochen said, ‘Victory through wisdom.’ Wang Xi said, ‘Subduing the enemy through strategy is the highest art.’ He Shi said, ‘When the enemy first plots to attack us, we strike first—this is easy. Discerning the enemy’s strategic intent and then deploying forces to attack the very inception of their resolve is the way.’ Zhang Yu said, ‘When the enemy first devises a plan, we attack it, and they will lose their scheme and submit—Yanzi’s thwarting of Fan Zhao is an example.’

Some say: *Thwarting strategy* means using strategy to attack others, achieving victory without battle through ingenious and clandestine calculations—the highest form of warfare. The next is *thwarting alliances*.

Cao Cao said, ‘Alliances are about to be formed.’ Li Quan said, ‘Thwart their initial alliances.’ When Su Qin bound the six states in a pact against Qin, Qin shut its passes for fifteen years, not daring to gaze eastward. Du Mu said, ‘It is not only about alliances about to be formed—any alliance can be thwarted.’ When Zhang Yi offered Qin’s six hundred *li* of land to King Huai of Chu, urging him to sever ties with Qi, and when Sui He killed Chu’s envoys in Han Xin’s presence to cut off Xiang Yu, when Cao Cao conversed with Han Sui on horseback to sow doubt in Ma Chao, when Gao Yang sent Xiao Shenming to sue for peace with Liang to cast suspicion on Hou Jing, leading to the fall of Taicheng—all these were cases of thwarting alliances. The art of power shifts and changes, not confined to one path.

Chen Hao said, ‘Some say: when the enemy has already mobilized and formed alliances, defeating them is the next best course.’ As when Duke Wen of Jin opposed Song, alienating Cao and Wei. Master Meng said, ‘Forming alliances with strong states deters the enemy from scheming.’ Mei Yaochen said, ‘Victory through might.’ Wang Xi said, ‘When one cannot fully subdue the enemy’s strategy, one should instead disrupt their alliances, causing them to dissolve. When they are allied, the enemy is vast and formidable; when they are not, the enemy is small and brittle.’ He Shi said, ‘Du’s four points above embody the principle of drawing close only to drive apart. *Thwarting alliances* means that when armies are about to engage, one deploys deceptive forces to intimidate the enemy, leaving them unable to advance or retreat, thus forcing their submission. With neighboring states as our allies, the enemy cannot but be isolated and weakened.’ Zhang Yu said, ‘When armies are about to join battle, strike before they unite. As the saying goes, “He who strikes first seizes the enemy’s will.” When two armies are about to engage, strike first—Sun Shu’ao’s defeat of Jin’s forces and the chef Pu’s rout of the Hua clan are examples.’

Some say: *Thwarting alliances* means using alliances to attack others. Before launching an attack on the enemy, first ally with neighboring states to create a pincer formation, thus strengthening ourselves and weakening the enemy. The next is *thwarting forces*.

Cao Cao said, ‘The enemy’s forces are already formed.’ Li Quan said, ‘Confronting the enemy in battle array is the lowest course.’ Jia Lin said, ‘Excelling in attack and conquest, leaving no strategy unexhausted, is the next best.’ Thus, the Grand Duke said, ‘He who contends for victory at the edge of the blade is no good general.’ Mei Yaochen said, ‘Victory through battle.’ Wang Xi said, ‘Battle is a perilous affair.’ Zhang Yu said, ‘When one cannot defeat the enemy’s initial strategy or disrupt their alliances, one must prevail with sharp weapons. *Forces* is the general term for arms and armor.’ The Grand Duke said, ‘The way to certain victory lies in weapons as treasure.’ The lowest is *attacking cities*.

Cao Cao said, ‘When the enemy has gathered their supplies and fortified their cities, attacking them is the lowest course.’ Li Quan said, ‘When the king’s army crosses the border, and the enemy opens their gates in submission, bearing coffins to the camp’s entrance, the people rejoicing—attacking them is the highest course. But if one lingers beneath a fortified city, the army grows weary, the soldiers indolent, the balance of attack and defense unequal, the disparity in strength between guest and host doubled—attacking under such conditions is the lowest course.’ Du You said, ‘Attacking cities and slaughtering towns is the lowest form of attack, for the harm it inflicts is great.’ Mei Yaochen said, ‘Expending wealth and labor is the lowest.’"

Wang Xi said: "Soldiers are killed and wounded, yet the city may still not be taken." Zhang Yu said: "Attacking cities and slaughtering towns not only exhausts the army and wastes resources, but also inflicts great harm—this is the lowest form of attack. The method of attacking cities is a last resort." Zhang Yu said: "Attacking cities depletes strength; the reason one must attack is simply because there is no other choice. Preparing *lu*, *fenwen*, and assembling siege engines takes three months to complete, and constructing siege ramps takes another three months."

Cao Cao said: "*Xiu* means to prepare. *Lu* is a large shield. *Fenwen* is shaped like a *fen*; its lower part has four wheels, and it is pushed from within to reach the city walls. *Ju* means to prepare. *Qixie* is the general term for mechanical devices used in attack and defense, such as flying towers and cloud ladders. *Juyin* refers to piling earth forward to approach the city walls." Li Quan said: "*Lu* is a shield used to cover the head while advancing to the city walls. *Fenwen* is a four-wheeled vehicle that conceals dozens of soldiers inside; it is pushed forward to fill moats, and neither wood nor stone can destroy it. *Qixie* includes flying towers, cloud ladders, wooden shelters, and bamboo screens. *Juyin* refers to earthworks built to scale the city walls. When Gao Huan of Eastern Wei besieged Jinzhou and Hou Jing attacked Taicheng, these were the devices they used. The labor is limited to three months to prevent the troops from growing weary over time."

Du Mu said: "*Lu* is what is now called a *pengpai*. *Fenwen* is a four-wheeled vehicle made of large wooden planks, covered with raw oxhide on top, and capable of holding ten men inside. It is used to transport earth to fill moats, and neither wood nor stone can harm it—what is now commonly called a *wooden donkey*. *Juyin* refers to piling earth to form what is now called a *rampart*. The three months mentioned refer to a season. It takes time to prepare the siege engines and construct the ramps properly, lest the harm to the soldiers be too great. As Guanzi said: 'Those who cannot govern their weapons will be trapped,' meaning they will have nothing to counter the enemy. Taigong said: 'The way to certain victory is to treasure one’s weapons.' The *Book of Han* records: 'Among the thirteen schools of military techniques, there are those who train hands and feet, master mechanical devices, and establish victory in attack and defense.' For attacking cities, there are battering rams, hooking carts, flying ladders, toad-shaped siege engines, articulated carts, fox-deer carts, shadow carts, high-barrier carts, horse-head carts, single-march carts, and earth-transporting pig-fish carts."

Chen Hao said: "Du Mu’s claim that *lu* is a *pengpai* is incorrect. If it were a *pengpai*, then this character *lu* should be used. Cao Cao’s description of it as a large shield is somewhat closer. The text states that preparing the siege engines takes three months and constructing the ramps another three, totaling six months. If the general grows impatient and attacks prematurely, many soldiers will be killed. Hence the following line: 'If the general cannot restrain his anger and orders the troops to swarm the walls like ants, it will be a disaster.'" Du You said: "*Fenwen* means 'cool above and warm below.' *Juyin* refers to piling earth forward to approach the city. Piling earth to form a mound is called *yin*, used to reach the enemy city and observe its defenses. The *Spring and Autumn Annals* records: 'Zifan, the Marshal of Chu, ascended the mound to observe the city of Song.'"

Mei Yaochen said: "When power and wisdom are insufficient to subdue the enemy, one has no choice but to attack the city, and preparing the siege equipment takes time." Cao Cao said: "*Lu* is a large shield. *Fenwen* is a *fen*-bed, with four wheels below, pushed from within to reach the city walls. *Qixie* is the general term for mechanical devices used in attack and defense, such as flying ladders." He added: "To call *lu* a large shield is incorrect. There are many tools of war—why single out the preparation of large shields? Nowadays, the defensive towers on city walls are called *lu*. Is *lu* not a *fen*-shaped structure with a leather roof to shield against arrows and stones?"

Zhang Yu said: "*Xiu lu* refers to large shields. The *Commentary* states: 'The Marquis of Jin ascended a *chao* cart to observe the Chu army.' The note explains: '*Chao* carts are carts with *lu* on top.' It also records: 'When the Jin army besieged Biyang, the men of Lu built wheels from large carts, covered them with armor to make *lu*, held them in the left hand, and wielded halberds in the right to form a unit.' The note explains: '*Lu* are large shields.' From this, it is clear that *xiu lu* refers to large shields. *Fenwen* is a four-wheeled vehicle that can cover dozens of men inside, used to transport earth to fill moats. *Qixie* is the general term for siege engines. The three months mentioned is an approximate time for completion. Some say: Sunzi warns against hasty attacks driven by anger, so he speaks of three months to prepare the engines and three months to build the ramps—though in reality, it may not take three months. If the city still cannot be taken, earth is piled to match its height, allowing soldiers to ascend, observe its defenses, or destroy its towers and shields, ensuring its capture. Such earthworks are called *yin*, as when Zifan of Chu ascended a mound to observe the city of Song. The completion of *qixie* is mentioned to emphasize the time required for preparation. The completion of *juyin* is noted because it takes time to finish the ascent. Both are described as last resorts. If the general cannot restrain his anger and orders the troops to swarm the walls like ants, killing a third of the soldiers while the city remains untaken, this is the disaster of attacking cities."

Cao Cao said: "If the general, in his anger, does not wait for the siege engines but orders the soldiers to scale the walls like ants climbing a wall, many will be killed or wounded." Li Quan said: "If the general, enraged, does not wait for the siege engines but orders the soldiers to scale the walls with their bare bodies like ants clinging to a wall, and a third are killed by wood and stone while the city remains untaken, this is the disaster of attacking cities." Du Mu said: "This refers to being humiliated by the enemy and unable to restrain one’s anger."

In the Later Wei, Emperor Taiwu led a hundred thousand troops to besiege Zang Zhi of the Liu-Song at Xuyi. Initially, Emperor Taiwu requested wine from Zhi, who sealed a jar of urine and sent it to him. Enraged, Taiwu ordered an assault on the city. He commanded his soldiers to scale the walls with their bare bodies, rotating in shifts—those who fell were replaced, none retreating, until the corpses piled as high as the ramparts. He also executed his High Beam King. After thirty days of such attacks, over half his forces perished. When Taiwu learned that Pengcheng had cut off his retreat and saw the multitude of sick and wounded, he withdrew. As the saying goes: "A single woman defending the city can match ten men." By this measure, the cost was far greater. Jia Lin said: "If the people’s hearts turn outward and the soldiers’ morale collapses within, the city falls of itself." Du You said: "If the siege drags past two seasons and the enemy remains unsubdued, the general, unable to restrain his fury, will send his soldiers swarming the walls like ants, killing or wounding a third of his own troops. Such hasty assaults, failing to take the city, only bring harm upon oneself." Thus Han Fei said: "A single defeat, and all is lost." He Shi said: "When the general’s heart is consumed by rage, he drives his soldiers to scale the walls like ants, and over half die, yet the city still stands—this is the disaster of attacking cities." Zhang Yu said: "If the siege exceeds two seasons and the enemy remains defiant, the general, impatient and unable to endure, will send his soldiers swarming the walls like ants. A third of his troops will be slaughtered by the defenders, yet the fortified city remains untaken—this is the calamity of attacking cities. Or it may be said: if the general, driven by fury, does not wait the full six months but hastily attacks, the harm will be the same."

Thus, one who excels in warfare subdues the enemy’s forces without battle.
Li Quan said: "To subdue the enemy through strategy, not combat."
The Jin general Guo Huai besieged Qucheng, and the Shu general Jiang Wei came to relieve it. Huai marched to Niutou Mountain, cutting off Wei’s supply lines and retreat. Wei was shaken and fled without a fight, and Qucheng surrendered. This is the meaning of subduing without battle.
Du Mu said: "When Zhou Yafu confronted the Seven Kingdoms, he encamped northeast of Changyi, abandoning Liang to Wu, and sent light troops to sever Wu’s supply lines. Wu and Liang exhausted each other, their provisions spent, and Wu retreated, whereupon Yafu pursued and crushed them. The Shu general Jiang Wei ordered Gou An and Li Shao to defend Qucheng, which the Wei general Chen Tai besieged. When Jiang Wei came to relieve them, emerging from Niutou Mountain to face Tai, Tai said: 'The essence of warfare lies in subduing the enemy without battle. Now, if we cut off Niutou, Wei will have no path of return—he will be ours for the taking.' The armies were ordered to hold their positions and not engage, severing Wei’s retreat. Wei, terrified, fled, and An and the others surrendered."
Mei Yaochen said: "Battle brings harm to men."
Wang Xi said: "As when Li Zuoche advised Cheng Anjun to dispatch three thousand elite troops to intercept Han Xin at Jingxing."
He Shi said: "This speaks of defeating the enemy’s strategies and alliances without resorting to battle." Thus the *Methods of Sima* states: "The highest strategy avoids combat." Its meaning is clear.
Zhang Yu said: "What was described earlier applies only to mediocre generals. One who excels in warfare acts otherwise: he may shatter the enemy’s plans, disrupt their alliances, sever their supplies, or cut off their retreat, thereby subduing them without battle. As when Tian Rangju clarified laws and commands, rallied his soldiers, and the states of Yan and Jin fled without fighting."

One who captures the enemy’s cities does so without assault.
Li Quan said: "By strategy."
During the Later Han, the Marquis of Zan, Zang Gong, besieged rebels at Yuanwu for months without success, his soldiers afflicted by disease. The Prince of Donghai said to Gong: "To encircle an enemy doomed to die with your army is no strategy. You should withdraw the siege, open a path for them, and feign retreat—once they scatter, a single constable could capture them." Gong followed this advice and took Yuanwu. The Wei assault on Huguan followed the same principle.
Du Mu said: "Sima Wenwang besieged Zhuge Dan at Shouchun. Many urged a swift assault, but Wenwang, knowing the city was well-fortified and heavily garrisoned, feared that a prolonged siege would exhaust his strength, and if reinforcements arrived, he would be trapped between two fires—this was the path to peril. Instead, he employed a comprehensive strategy to contain them. Dan rebelled in the second year, fifth month, and was destroyed in the third year, second month. The six armies remained encamped, digging deep moats and raising high ramparts, while Dan’s forces withered of their own accord. When the general of the Former Yan, Murong Ke, led troops to subdue Duan Kan at Guanggu, his officers urged him to attack swiftly. Ke said: 'In warfare, some victories come through patience, others through urgency. If our strength is evenly matched and they have strong external support, but we can contain them, we should encircle and wait for them to collapse.' He then built shelters and plowed the fields, tightening the siege. In the end, he took Guanggu without shedding blood."
Meng Shi said: "This speaks of subduing the enemy through awe and discipline, capturing cities without assault—like when the Earl of Zheng bared his chest to welcome King Zhuang of Chu."
Mei Yaochen said: "Assault wastes resources."
Wang Xi said: "As when Emperor Taizong of Tang accepted the surrender of Xue Rengao."
Zhang Yu said: "One may attack what the enemy must rescue, luring them to abandon the city and come to its aid, then ambush and capture them. As when Geng Yan attacked Linzi and took Xi’an, or threatened Juli and slew Fei Yi. Or one may sever their strong reinforcements and hold them in prolonged siege, waiting for them to collapse—as when the Chu army built shelters and plowed the fields to subdue Song. These are all examples of capturing cities without assault."

One who destroys the enemy’s state does so without protracted warfare.
Cao Cao said: "To annihilate a state, one must not expose the army for long."
Li Quan said: "By strategy, one destroys a state swiftly."
Emperor Wen of Sui asked his Prime Minister Gao Jiong for a strategy to conquer Chen. Jiong replied: "The harvest season beyond the Yangtze differs from that within the Central Plains."

"Seize their farming season when we are at leisure, mobilize troops for a surprise attack, forcing them to abandon farming for defense. Wait until they gather their forces, then withdraw. Repeat this two or three times, and their farming will be ruined. Moreover, the southern lands are low-lying, with dwellings made entirely of thatch and bamboo, and granaries and storehouses built among them. Secretly dispatch agents to set fires when the wind rises, then strike again when they rebuild their camps. Carrying out this plan will sicken Chen from the start.

Du Mu said: Exploit the enemy’s vulnerable position without missing the opportunity—like crushing withered wood. When Gaozu entered the Pass, when Jin subdued Sun Hao, and when Sui conquered Chen, none took long. Jia Lin said: War cannot be prolonged; protraction breeds change. Yet to destroy a state, one need not harm its people—when King Wu attacked Yin, the Yin people called him ‘father.’ Du You said: To punish tyranny and destroy an enemy state, one must not expose the army for long. Mei Yaochen said: Prolonged war invites change. Wang Xi’s note agrees with Mei Yaochen. He Shi said: A skilled attacker does not assault with arms but entraps with strategy, forcing the enemy to uproot itself, to destroy itself—no need for exhausting sieges. Zhang Yu said: Using right to punish wrong, wisdom to overcome folly, the army is not exposed for long, yet the enemy state falls—why delay for six months? One must contend for all under heaven with completeness, so the army is not worn down, and advantage is preserved. This is the method of strategic attack.

Cao Cao said: Do not engage the enemy in battle, yet secure complete victory, establishing supremacy without wearying the army or shedding blood. Li Quan said: Contend for all under heaven with a strategy of total victory, thus achieving success without weariness. Mei Yaochen said: ‘Complete contention’ means no battles, no assaults on cities, no prolonged destruction—all subdued by strategy. This is called ‘strategic attack.’ Thus, the army is not blunted, and advantage is preserved intact. Zhang Yu said: No battles mean no soldiers harmed; no assaults mean no strength exhausted; no protraction means no resources wasted. Establishing complete victory over all under heaven avoids the harm of wearying the army and shedding blood, while securing the benefits of a wealthy state and strong army. This is the art of strategic attack for a good general.

Thus, the method of using troops is: with ten times the enemy’s strength, surround them.

Cao Cao said: With ten against one, surround them—this assumes equal generals in wisdom and courage, and equal troops in sharpness and dullness. If the ruler is weak and the guest strong, one need not wait for tenfold strength, as when Cao Cao doubled his troops to surround Xiapi and capture Lü Bu. Du Mu said: ‘Surround’ means encircling on all sides to prevent the enemy’s escape. When encircling, one must position forces slightly farther from the enemy city, occupying a broad area with strict defenses. Without sufficient troops, gaps will appear—hence the need for tenfold strength. Lü Bu’s defeat stemmed from internal distrust: Hou Cheng seized Chen Gong and surrendered Lü Bu, enabling capture. This was not due to Cao Cao’s military strength. If distrust and disunity exist, even without a siege, the enemy would collapse—how much more so when surrounded? Thus, Sunzi’s ‘tenfold strength to surround’ assumes equal generals and troops, not that the enemy will self-destruct. Cao Cao’s claim of doubling troops to subdue Lü Bu was not due to siege pressure—this cannot serve as a model. Li Quan said: With equal wisdom and courage, ten times the enemy’s strength allows encirclement, for attack and defense are different in posture. Du You said: With ten against one, surround them—this assumes equal generals and troops. If the ruler is weak and the guest strong, tenfold strength is unnecessary, as when Cao Cao doubled his troops to surround Xiapi and capture Lü Bu. If the enemy’s fortifications are sturdy and they rely on natural barriers, then tenfold strength is required to encircle. Even if the enemy is strong, if their position is unfavorable, one need not wait for tenfold strength to surround them. Mei Yaochen said: With one against ten, encirclement is possible. He Shi said: ‘Surround’ means encircling a city with troops on all sides. Assessing the balance of forces and the generals’ wisdom and courage, if one has tenfold the enemy’s strength, then ten against one allows encirclement, preventing escape. Zhang Yu said: With ten times the enemy’s strength, encircle them on all sides—this assumes equal generals and troops. If the ruler is weak and the guest strong, tenfold strength is unnecessary for encirclement. The *Weiliaozi* states: In defense, one can hold against ten, ten against a hundred, a hundred against a thousand, a thousand against ten thousand—meaning ten defenders can hold against a hundred besiegers, aligning with this principle.

With five times the enemy’s strength, attack them.

Cao Cao said: With five against one, use three parts as the main force and one part as the奇 (unorthodox). Li Quan said: With five times the enemy’s strength, attack them—attack and defense differ in posture. Du Mu said: ‘Method’ here means ‘way.’ With five against one, take three parts of one’s forces as three routes to attack one side of the enemy, holding back two parts to exploit their unpreparedness with an unorthodox strike. In the late Western Wei, the Liangzhou Prefect Yuwen Zhonghe occupied the province and refused to submit. The Wei general Dugu Xin led troops to suppress him, and Zhonghe fortified the city and defended resolutely. Xin ordered his generals to attack the northeast with siege ladders by night, while he personally led troops to strike the southwest, thus capturing the city. Chen Hao said: With five times the enemy’s strength, one naturally has surplus force, while the enemy’s strength is divided—why limit the attack to three routes? This refers specifically to assaulting cities, as the following text states: ‘A small enemy’s stubbornness is the great enemy’s capture.’ Du You said: If the enemy consolidates their forces to defend and refuses battle, with five against one, one may attack."

Should the enemy lack both internal and external support, an assault need not await a fivefold advantage. Mei Yaochen concurs with Du You’s annotation.

Wang Xi said: If one encircles ten to seize five, the attacking force will invariably possess surplus strength, without needing to await the enemy’s exhaustion or laxity. The following also pertains to circumstances where wisdom, courage, advantage, and dullness are evenly matched.

He Shi said: When wit and bravery are equal, and one’s forces outnumber the enemy fivefold, one may divide troops—three parts to assault the city, two parts to execute a surprise maneuver and secure victory.

Zhang Yu said: With five times the enemy’s strength, one should feign an advance from the front while striking from the rear, or create clamor in the east while attacking from the west. Without a fivefold advantage, such stratagems cannot be employed. The Duke of Shou asserted that three methods constitute the orthodox approach, while two serve as the extraordinary—is this not so? If the enemy lacks external reinforcements but one possesses internal allies, an assault need not await a fivefold advantage.

When outnumbering the enemy, divide your forces.
Cao Cao said: With twice the enemy’s strength, one method serves as the orthodox, the other as the extraordinary.
Li Quan said: When one’s forces are double the enemy’s, half may be deployed as the extraordinary. With numerical superiority, movement becomes difficult to counter. Fu Jian, arriving at Feishui, failed to divide his forces and was defeated; Wang Sengbian, reaching Zhanggongzhou, divided his and triumphed.

Du Mu said: This is incorrect. When outnumbering the enemy two to one, one should dispatch a portion to strike the enemy’s vital points or attack what they must defend, compelling them to further divide their already divided forces, thereby enabling one’s own division to strike. The art of war does not hinge on numerical superiority; every formation possesses both orthodox and extraordinary elements, and the extraordinary is not contingent upon numerical advantage. At Wujiang, Xiang Yu, with but twenty-eight cavalry, did not mass them together but still arrayed them in orthodox and extraordinary formations, circulating and reinforcing one another—how much more so in other circumstances?

Chen Hao said: This simply means that when one’s forces are double the enemy’s, one should divide troops to strike what they must defend, thereby doubling the advantage within the doubled force to attack the enemy’s divided strength. Though Du grasped this, he did not fully expound it.

Du You said: With twice the enemy’s strength, one method serves as the orthodox, the other as the extraordinary. When outnumbered two to one, the disparity is insufficient to create variation, hence one employs feigned maneuvers to disperse the enemy’s forces. Thus, the Grand Duke said: Without the ability to divide and maneuver, one cannot speak of the extraordinary.

Mei Yaochen said: When outnumbered two to one, one may divide the enemy’s strength.
Wang Xi said: To divide means to split into two armies, compelling the enemy to face attacks from front and rear, thereby securing a twofold advantage.

He Shi said: When outnumbering the enemy, divide half as the extraordinary. With numerical superiority, one may readily divide forces. When host and guest are evenly matched, the superior commander prevails.

Zhang Yu said: With twice the enemy’s strength, one should divide into two forces: one to engage the front, the other to strike the rear. If the enemy responds to the front, attack from the rear; if they respond to the rear, attack from the front—this is what is meant by one method as orthodox, the other as extraordinary. Du failed to comprehend that division constitutes the extraordinary, while concentration constitutes the orthodox, and hastily criticized Cao Gong—how mistaken.

When evenly matched, one may still engage.
Cao Cao said: When one’s forces equal the enemy’s, the adept commander still employs ambushes and extraordinary maneuvers to secure victory.
Li Quan said: When host and guest are evenly matched, only the adept prevail.
Du Mu said: This is incorrect. Whenever one’s forces, whether numerous or few, and whether wise or dull, brave or hesitant, directly oppose the enemy, engagement is permissible. Ambushes may be set before or behind the enemy, or amid dense forests and thickets, or under the cover of dusk or darkness, or within narrow passes and mountain slopes, striking where the enemy is unprepared—this is called an ambush, not an extraordinary maneuver.

Chen Hao said: When one’s forces are equal to the enemy’s, one should first devise extraordinary plans to ensure victory before engaging. Thus, the following text states: If not superior, one should avoid battle. Du’s explanation of extraordinary and ambush tactics is apt.

Mei Yaochen said: When strength is equal, engage.
Wang Xi said: This refers to the adept commander’s ability to inspire troops to fight to the death. If one employs extraordinary maneuvers and ambushes to secure victory, this denotes superior wisdom, not mere numerical equality.

He Shi said: *Di* here denotes equality. Only the adept can secure victory.
