Zhang Yu said: When evenly matched, one transforms the orthodox into the extraordinary and the extraordinary into the orthodox, creating endless variation to confound the enemy. Engaging thus is what is meant by employing extraordinary maneuvers and ambushes to secure victory. Du failed to grasp that every formation possesses both manifest and concealed elements, and that ambushes are not confined to mountains and forests—this is incorrect.

When outnumbered, one should evade.
Cao Cao said: Fortify high ramparts and strong defenses; do not engage.
Li Quan said: When one’s strength is inferior, fortify defenses and refuse battle to blunt the enemy’s edge, awaiting their exhaustion before launching a surprise attack. The Qi general Tian Dan, defending Jimo, set fire to ox tails and slew Qi Jie—this exemplifies the principle.

Du Mu said: When outnumbered, avoid the enemy’s edge and await an opportunity to strike decisively for victory. The "adept" refers to one who can endure humiliation and insult, refusing to emerge even when provoked—unlike Cao Jiu at Sishui.

Chen Hao said: This is incorrect. When the enemy outnumbers one’s forces, one should evade to inflate their arrogance, thereby facilitating future plans—not endure humiliation. The Taizong Emperor humiliated Song Laosheng to capture his troops—this was not due to unequal strength.

Jia Lin said: When outnumbered, conceal one’s forces to prevent the enemy from detecting them. One should set ambushes to await the enemy, employ deception to sow doubt—this too is a path to victory. Another interpretation: Conceal one’s forces so the enemy remains unaware of their disposition, fearing deception, and the entire army flees.

Du You said: Fortify high ramparts and strong defenses; do not engage.

When their forces are numerous and ours are few, and they cannot be opposed, one must flee and conceal one’s form. Mei Yaochen said: When their forces are numerous and ours are few, withdraw and do not engage. Wang Xi said: To flee is to lie in ambush—meaning one must rely on strong defenses and concealment to hold one’s ground. As the *Commentary* states: "The army fled into the palace of the lady." Yet there are cases where few troops prevail, for their generals are superior and their soldiers strong.

He Shi said: With few troops, fortify defenses, observe developments, and conceal one’s form—advancing only when opportunity arises. Zhang Yu said: When their forces are numerous and ours are few, it is fitting to withdraw and avoid battle—this is also a matter of equal generalship, courage, and the sharpness or dullness of arms. But if our forces are disciplined and theirs are chaotic, if we are vigorous and they are sluggish, then even if the enemy is numerous, we may still join battle. As when Wu Qi, with five hundred chariots, broke Qin’s five hundred thousand; or when Xie Xuan, with eight thousand soldiers, defeated Fu Jian’s million—how could one flee then? If not so, then one must evade. Cao Cao said: Withdraw the troops to avoid them.

Du Mu said: "Not being their equal" refers to being inferior in both strength and alliances; one must swiftly depart and not delay. If the enemy guards our critical positions, seizes our crossings, and encircles us, then even the desire to withdraw may no longer be possible. Du You said: When leading troops to prepare defenses, if strength is unequal and circumstances are not favorable, then withdraw the army to avoid them and await an opportune moment to act. Mei Yaochen said: If one’s power is inferior, then withdraw and evade. Wang Xi said: When both general and troops are inferior, encountering the enemy’s attack will surely lead to defeat. Zhang Yu said: If in military strength, strategy, and courage one is all inferior to the enemy, then one must withdraw and evade, awaiting their moment of vulnerability. Thus, a small force that stands firm becomes the great enemy’s captive.

Cao Cao said: The small cannot withstand the great. Li Quan said: A small force that does not assess its strength and stubbornly fights will surely be captured by the greater force. As when the Han Commandant Li Ling, with five thousand infantry, faced a hundred thousand enemy troops and perished in the land of the Xiongnu. Du Mu said: "Standing firm" means the general’s nature is stubborn and unyielding, unable to flee or evade, and thus is captured by the greater force. The Meng clan said: The small cannot withstand the great. It means that a small state, not measuring its strength, dares to make an enemy of a great power; though it may temporarily fortify its cities and hold fast, it will ultimately be captured. The *Spring and Autumn Annals* states: "Neither strong enough nor weak enough—this is why they were defeated." Mei Yaochen said: If one neither flees nor evades, though one stands firm, one will still be captured. Wang Xi’s annotation agrees with Mei Yaochen.

He Shi said: For example, when the Right General Su Jian and the Front General Zhao Xin led over three thousand troops, marching separately from the Grand General Wei Qing, they alone encountered the Xiongnu chanyu’s army of tens of thousands. They fought fiercely for a day, and the Han soldiers were nearly annihilated. The Front General Xin, a Hu by origin who had surrendered and become a marquis, was enticed by the Xiongnu and led his remaining eight hundred cavalry to defect. The Right General Su Jian lost his entire army and barely escaped alone to return. The Grand General consulted his officials Zheng Hong, Secretary An, and Gentleman-Consultant Zhou Ba on what to do with Su Jian. Zhou Ba said: "Since the Grand General took command, not a single subordinate general has been executed. Now Su Jian has abandoned his army—he should be executed to demonstrate authority and gravity." Zheng Hong and Secretary An said: "Not so. The *Art of War* states: *A small force that stands firm becomes the great enemy’s captive.* Now Su Jian, with only a few thousand, faced the chanyu’s tens of thousands, fought fiercely for a day, and his remaining soldiers did not waver in loyalty. If we execute him upon his return, we signal to later generations that there is no point in returning." Zhang Yu said: A small force that does not assess its strength and stubbornly fights will surely be captured by the greater force. This is seen in Xi Hou’s submission to the Duke of Zheng and Li Ling’s surrender to the Xiongnu. Mencius said: "The small cannot oppose the great, the weak cannot oppose the strong, the few cannot oppose the many."

Now, the general is the state’s assistant. If the assistance is complete, the state will surely be strong. Cao Cao said: When the general is thorough, plans do not leak. Li Quan said: "Assistant" means aid. If the general’s talent is sufficient, the army will surely be strong. Du Mu said: "Complete" refers to talent. Jia Lin said: The state’s strength or weakness necessarily depends on the general. If the general assists the ruler with complete talent, the state will be strong; if he does not assist the ruler and harbors disloyalty within, the state will be weak. The selection and appointment of personnel must be done with utmost care. He Shi said: "Complete" means possessing both talent and wisdom. Only with a general of complete talent and wisdom will the state be secure and strong. If the assistance is flawed, the state will surely be weak. Cao Cao said: Flaws become visible externally. Li Quan said: "Flaw" means deficiency. If the general’s talent is incomplete, the army will surely be weak. Du Mu said: "Incomplete" refers to talent. Mei Yaochen said: With worthy men, one is complete; without them, one is flawed. Wang Xi said: "Complete" means the general is virtuous and possesses both loyalty and talent; "flawed" means there is some deficiency. He Shi said: His talent must be complete, and his handling of affairs must be thoroughly understood.

Thus, when a general is in command of the army, he must first understand the five factors, the six conducts, the use of the five weights, as well as the nine changes and the four opportunities. Only then can he internally govern his troops and externally assess the situation of battle. If he is ignorant of these, he cannot remain in command of the three armies even for a single day. Zhang Yu said: If the general’s plans are thorough and tight, the enemy cannot spy on them, and thus the state is strong; if there is even a slight flaw, the enemy will exploit it and invade, and thus the state is weak. Taigong said: "Those who gain worthy men prosper; those who lose them perish."

There are three ways a ruler may bring harm to his army. Mei Yaochen said: These are harms the ruler may not realize. The Meng clan said: The following words are correct. Zhang Yu said: The three matters below. Not knowing the army should not advance yet ordering it to advance; not knowing the army should not retreat yet ordering it to retreat—this is called "tying up" the army. Cao Cao said: "Tying up" means restraining. Li Quan said: "Tying up" means binding.

Not knowing when to advance or retreat ensures the army’s defeat—like a fettered steed that cannot gallop. The Chu general Long Ju pursued Han Xin and was routed, for he knew not when to advance; the Qin general Fu Rong wheeled his troops back slightly and was defeated, for he knew not when to retreat. Du Mu said: It is like driving with reins and hobble, allowing no freedom. The ruler is the lord of the state. To bring harm to the army is to create peril for the army. When the axe is bestowed and the ominous gate pushed open, matters beyond the threshold are for the general to decide. As when Zhao Chongguo wished to garrison and farm, yet Emperor Xuan of Han insisted on fighting in sequence; or when Sun Hao faced annihilation, yet Jia Chong still requested to withdraw the army—these are what is meant by not knowing when to advance or retreat. Jia Lin said: The army’s advance or retreat should be determined by the general in response to circumstances; when the ruler’s commands govern from within, no greater harm exists. Thus Taigong said: The state cannot be governed from without, nor the army commanded from within. Du You said: *Mi* means to control; *mi* is the reverse of *wei*. The ruler, not knowing the army’s situation, yet wishes to command it from within. Mei Yaochen said: When the ruler does not know the proper times for advance or retreat, yet insists on dictating them, this is to fetter the army—what the *Six Strategies* calls "the army cannot be commanded from within." Wang Xi said: *Mi* means to bind. To remove this harm, one must entrust command to a minister of unshakable loyalty and complete talent. Zhang Yu said: When the army should not advance yet is ordered to advance, or should not retreat yet is ordered to retreat, this is called fettering the army. Thus it is said: If advance and retreat are dictated from within, success is difficult to achieve.

Not knowing the affairs of the three armies yet meddling in their governance confuses the soldiers. Cao Cao said: Military bearing does not enter the state, nor state bearing enter the army—rites cannot govern troops. Li Quan said: Appointing a general unsuited to the task. When the Yan general Murong Ping led his forces, he sold firewood and water wherever he encamped, amassing goods with greed and baseness, yet served as commander of the three armies, knowing nothing of governance. Du Mu said: Military discipline and laws have their own procedures; if one applies the ordinary ways of governing the state, the soldiers will be confused. As when Zhou Yafu saw the Son of Heaven and did not bow, and Emperor Wen of Han knew his courage could not be violated; or when Wei Shang defended Yunzhong, presented enemy heads, and was impeached by the officials—why Feng Tang spoke in outrage. Du You said: Governing the state values propriety and righteousness, while warfare prizes stratagem and deception; their circumstances differ, and their teachings are not the same. If the ruler does not understand this change and applies a single policy to both state and army, the soldiers will be perplexed, not knowing what to do. Thus the *Art of War* says: In the state, trust; in the army, deception. Chen Hao said: Not knowing the affairs of the three armies yet opposing the majority and thwarting counsel. The *Zuozhuan* records that Jin’s Zhi Ji did not follow the army commander’s plan but advanced with a detached force first, and was ultimately defeated by Chu. Mei Yaochen said: Not knowing the duties of military governance yet interfering in its administration confuses the masses. Cao Cao cited the *Methods of the Sima*: "Military bearing does not enter the state, nor state bearing enter the army." He Shi said: The bearing of state and army differ; their governance is distinct. To apply the laws of state governance to the army causes confusion among the ranks. Zhang Yu said: Benevolence and righteousness can govern the state but not the army; adaptability and deception can govern the army but not the state—this is the principle. Duke Guo did not cultivate kindness and love and was destroyed by Jin; the Marquis of Jin did not uphold the four virtues and was defeated by Qin—this is not applying benevolence and righteousness to govern the state. The Marquis of Qi did not shoot gentlemen and was defeated by Jin; the Duke of Song did not capture gray-haired men and was routed by Chu—this is not applying adaptability and deception to govern the army. Thus, when benevolence and righteousness are applied where stratagem and cunning are needed, the state is imperiled—as with Jin and Guo. When rites and propriety are upheld where adaptability and deception are required, the army is defeated—as with Qi and Song. Therefore, the ways of governing the state cannot govern the army.

Not knowing the authority of the three armies yet imposing the same duties upon them sows doubt among the soldiers. Cao Cao said: Failing to match the right person to the task. Du Mu said: If the general lacks authority and wisdom, cannot assess the soldiers’ strengths, and assigns tasks uniformly without utilizing their talents, the three armies will grow doubtful. The *Three Strategies of Huang Shigong* says: One who excels at employing people deploys the wise, the brave, the greedy, and the foolish. The wise delight in establishing merit, the brave in fulfilling their ambitions, the greedy in pursuing profit, and the foolish in disregarding death. Chen Hao said: When the general in the field lacks independent authority and unrestricted command, the soldiers of the three armies naturally doubt. Du You said: Not matching the right person to the task. When the ruler appoints a general, he must choose carefully; if the general does not understand adaptability, he cannot be entrusted with power. To appoint the wrong person leads to missteps and military defeat—as when Zhao did not use Lord Guangwu but instead employed Cheng Anjun. Mei Yaochen said: Not knowing the ways of authority and strategy yet interfering in their duties causes the masses to waver. Wang Xi said: Governance and authority, if entrusted to those who do not understand them, lead to contradictions and constraints, causing the army to doubt. This is why Pei Du memorialized to remove the supervising commissioner when pacifying Caizhou. All these stem from the ruler’s failure to entrust worthy generals with full authority, instead imposing uniformity—hence they are collectively called the three harms. He Shi said: Not knowing how to employ those skilled in military authority and strategy as generals leads to an unruly army and doubtful soldiers. Zhang Yu said: If among the military officers there are those who do not understand the authority and strategy of warfare yet are placed in command, orders will not be unified, and the army will doubt.

Should the battle of Bì prove instructive: the central army’s commander Xún Línfǔ wished to withdraw, yet his deputy general Xiān Gǔ refused to comply, and thus they were defeated by Chǔ. In more recent times, the practice of appointing eunuchs as military supervisors has brought the same harm. When Gāo Chóngwén campaigned against Shǔ, he abolished this practice and thereby achieved success.

When the Three Armies are both confused and doubtful, the troubles from the feudal lords will soon arrive—this is called “disordering one’s own army and inviting victory [for the enemy].” Cáo Cāo said: “Inviting means seizing.” Lǐ Quán said: “Inviting means seizing.”

Warfare is the art of authority and strategy; it cannot be misapplied or entrusted to the unfit. As the Zhao minister Lìn Xiàngrú warned: “Zhào Kuò merely reads his father’s books but does not understand adaptation.” Yet the king now appoints him by reputation alone, like gluing the tuning pegs of a zither. This is to ignore the authority of the Three Armies while imposing uniformity upon their command. The king of Zhao did not heed this advice, and so suffered the defeat at Chángpíng, bringing the troubles of the feudal lords upon himself.

Dù Mù said: “When our army is doubtful and self-disordered, it is as if we invite the enemy to seize victory from us.” Mèng Shì said: “When the Three Armies doubt those they are commanded by and are confused by what they are ordered to do, neighboring states and feudal lords will exploit their discord and bring trouble upon them.” Tài Gōng said: “A doubtful resolve cannot respond to the enemy.” Méi Yáochén said: “The ruler merely knows how to control his generals but does not know how to employ the right men, instead imposing uniformity upon their command and causing the masses to doubt—thus the troubles from the feudal lords arise. This is to disorder one’s own army and forfeit one’s own victory.”

Wáng Xī said: “Inviting the feudal lords to seize victory from oneself.” Hé Shì said: “When soldiers are doubtful and unafraid, disorder ensues, and enemy states can exploit our vulnerabilities to strike.” Zhāng Yù said: “When soldiers are doubtful and unwilling to follow orders, the armies of the feudal lords will seize the opportunity to attack. This is to collapse one’s own army and forfeit one’s own victory.”

Thus, there are five ways to know victory:

Lǐ Quán said: “These refer to the five conditions below.” Zhāng Yù said: “The five conditions below.”

Victory belongs to those who know when they can fight and when they cannot;
Lǐ Quán said: “Assess the alignment of human affairs, then use the *Taiyi* divination and the *Dunjia* method to calculate the three gates, encountering auspicious omens and the five generals without obstruction. Those who press the advantage in the balance of host and guest will surely prevail.” Dù Mù said: “This refers to what is later called ‘knowing the enemy and knowing oneself.’” Mèng Shì said: “Those who can discern the enemy’s situation and examine their strengths and weaknesses will prevail.” Méi Yáochén said: “Knowing the proper timing of what can and cannot be done.” Wáng Xī said: “If it is possible, advance; if not, halt—this is the way to secure victory.” Hé Shì said: “Examine oneself and the enemy.” Zhāng Yù said: “If battle is possible, advance and attack; if not, retreat and defend. Those who can discern the proper timing of attack and defense will never fail to prevail.”

Victory belongs to those who understand the use of the many and the few;
Lǐ Quán said: “Assessing one’s strength.” Dù Mù said: “First know the enemy’s numbers, then raise troops to respond accordingly. As when Wáng Jiǎn campaigned against Jīng, he declared: ‘Without six hundred thousand men, it cannot be done.’” Dù Yòu said: “The nature of warfare is such that there are times when the many cannot strike the few, or the weak can overcome the strong—those who can adapt to these circumstances will prevail. Thus, the *Spring and Autumn Annals* states: ‘Victory in war lies in harmony, not in numbers.’” Méi Yáochén said: “Act according to one’s strength.” Wáng Xī said: “This refers to assessing the enemy’s numbers and responding with encirclement, attack, division, or battle.” Zhāng Yù said: “The art of warfare includes victories won by the few over the many and by the many over the few. The key lies in measuring what is needed and not losing the proper balance. As the *Wuzi* states: ‘When using many, seek open ground; when using few, seek narrow passes.’”

Victory belongs to those whose superiors and subordinates share the same desire;
Cáo Cāo said: “Ruler and ministers share the same desire.” Lǐ Quán said: “Observe the hearts of the soldiers—when superiors and subordinates share the same desire, as if avenging a personal grudge, they will prevail.” Chén Hào said: “When superiors and subordinates share the same interests and desires, the Three Armies will have no resentment, and the enemy can be defeated. As the saying goes: ‘If you follow others’ desires, you can make others follow your desires—rarely does this fail.’” Dù Yòu said: “When ruler and ministers are in harmony and fight with courage, they will prevail. Thus, Mencius said: ‘Heavenly timing is not as favorable as earthly advantage; earthly advantage is not as favorable as human harmony.’” Méi Yáochén said: “Hearts aligned as one.” Wáng Xī said: “Superiors and subordinates of one mind. As when Xiān Gǔ’s stubbornness led to defeat, or Lǚ Bù’s disunity brought about his downfall—both resulted from superiors and subordinates not sharing the same desire.” Hé Shì said: “The *Book of Documents* states: ‘Though I have hundreds of thousands of people, their hearts are divided; but I have ten ministers of disorder, and their hearts are as one.’ The Shang perished, and the Zhou rose.” Zhāng Yù said: “When a hundred generals share one heart and the Three Armies unite in strength, with every person eager to fight, they will be unstoppable.”

Victory belongs to those who are prepared for the unprepared;
Lǐ Quán and Dù Mù said: “Having prior preparation.” Mèng Shì said: “*Yú* means to anticipate. The *Zuǒzhuàn* states: ‘Without preparation and foresight, one cannot wage war.’ Wait for the enemy’s moment of vulnerability.” Chén Hào said: “This means first establishing an invincible army and waiting for the enemy’s moment of vulnerability.” Dù Yòu said: “*Yú* means to anticipate. Strike the enemy’s undisciplined forces with one’s own well-ordered army.” Méi Yáochén said: “Be vigilant against the unexpected.” Wáng Xī said: “Use one’s own preparedness to await the enemy’s unpreparedness.” Hé Shì said: “During the Spring and Autumn period, in the battle of Chéngpú, Jin was unprepared for Chu and was defeated at Bì. In the battle of Bì, Chu was unprepared for Jin and was defeated at Yān. From the time of Jùn onward, Jin never failed to prepare and further strengthened this with propriety and harmony—thus Chu could not overpower Jin. Later, when the people of Jīng attacked Chén, Wú came to its aid. After marching thirty *li*, it rained for ten days and nights, and the stars were not visible. The grand historian Yǐ Xiàng said to the general Zǐqī: ‘It has rained for ten days and nights, armor has been gathered, and troops assembled—the men of Wú will surely arrive. It is better to prepare.’”

Then they arrayed their forces, and the men of Wú arrived. Seeing that Jīng was prepared, they turned back. The grand historian said: “They turned back for sixty *li*; their nobles were at the rear, and their commoners were foraging. We have marched thirty *li*—strike them, and we shall surely prevail.” They followed his counsel and thus crushed the army of Wú.

When the great general of Wèi campaigned south against Wú and reached Jī Hú, the Wèi general Mǎn Chǒng led the various armies at the front, facing the enemy across the water. Chǒng commanded the generals: “Tonight the wind is fierce; the enemy will surely come to burn our camp. We must prepare in advance.” All the armies were alerted. At midnight, the enemy indeed dispatched ten units to burn the camp, but Chǒng ambushed and routed them.

Again, in the Spring and Autumn period, the people of Wèi, with the army of Yān, attacked Zhèng. Zhèng’s Jì Zú, Yuán Fán, and Xiè Jià arrayed three armies at their front, while sending Màn Bó and Zǐyuán to conceal an army at their rear. The men of Yān feared Zhèng’s three armies but did not guard against an ambush. In the sixth month, Zhèng’s two noble sons, with their ambush force, defeated Yān’s army at Běi Zhì.

The noble man says: “Without preparation for the unforeseen, one cannot take the field.”

Again, Chǔ’s Zǐ Chóng, advancing from Chén, attacked Jǔ and besieged Qúqiū. The walls of Qúqiū were in disrepair, and its people, overwhelmed, fled to Jǔ. Chǔ entered Qúqiū, and the people of Jǔ imprisoned Chǔ’s Prince Píng. The men of Chǔ said: “Do not kill him; we shall return your captives.” The people of Jǔ killed him. Chǔ’s army besieged Jǔ, whose walls were likewise in disrepair. On the day *gēngshēn*, Jǔ collapsed, and Chǔ then entered Yùn. This was because Jǔ had no preparations.

The noble man says: “To rely on one’s insignificance and fail to prepare is the greatest of faults; to prepare in advance for the unforeseen is the greatest of virtues.” Jǔ, trusting in its insignificance, did not repair its walls and ramparts. Within eleven days, Chǔ captured its three cities—all for want of preparation.

Zhāng Yù says: “Always make oneself invincible to await the enemy.” Thus Wú Qǐ said: “Go forth as if facing the enemy.” Shì Jì said: “With preparation, one does not fail.” A capable general with a ruler who does not interfere will prevail.

Cáo Cāo says: “The *Methods of Sīmǎ* states: ‘Advance and retreat only as the time demands; do not say, “I am but one man.”’”

Lǐ Quán says: “A general in the field, whose ruler’s commands he does not always obey, will prevail—this is a true general.”

When Wú attacked Chǔ, Wú’s Prince Guāng’s younger brother, Fúgài, arrived with the king and requested to strike Chǔ’s Zǐ Cháng, but permission was not granted. Fúgài said: “This is what is meant by seeing what is right and acting without awaiting orders. Today, if I die, Chǔ can be entered.” With his five thousand followers, he struck Zǐ Cháng first and defeated him. From this, we see that a capable general with a ruler who cannot interfere prevails.

When Jìn’s Xuān-dì resisted Zhūgě [Liàng] at Wǔzhàngyuán, the Son of Heaven sent Xīn Pí, bearing the staff of authority, to the army gate, saying: “Whoever dares to ask for battle shall be beheaded.” [Zhūgě] Liàng, hearing this, laughed and said: “If he could restrain me, would I beg for battle from a thousand *li* away?” Pretending that the Son of Heaven had forbidden it, he displayed his martial intent to the troops—this was a general who could not prevail.

Dù Mù says: “The *Book of Wèi Liáo* states: ‘The general is not restrained above by Heaven, below by Earth, or in between by men. Thus, arms are instruments of ill omen; the general is an office of death.’”

Dù Yòu says: “When the general is skilled and well-versed in the dynamics of war, and the ruler can entrust him with sole command, without interference from the court, then—as the *Sayings of the Royal Son* put it—‘The direction comes from the ruler, but the decision to do battle rests with the general.’”

Méi Yáochén says: “Beyond the threshold, the general commands.”

Wáng Xī says: “A ruler who interferes with a capable general cannot entirely eliminate doubt and jealousy. If the sovereign is wise and enlightened, he must surely know men and should entrust them with responsibility for results, pushing the chariot forward and bestowing the battle-axe—this is the proper way. In matters of attack and battle, he should concentrate authority in one man, without interference from the court, so as to unify command and fully employ his talent. How much more so when facing the enemy and seizing the moment, where a hair’s breadth cannot be spared—how can one control from afar?”

Hé Shì says: “In ancient times, when dispatching a general at the Grand Temple, the ruler would personally wield the battle-axe, grasp its head, and present its handle, saying: ‘From this upward to Heaven, the general commands.’ Then he would again grasp the handle and present the blade, saying: ‘From this downward to the abyss, the general commands.’ Thus, when Lǐ Mù served as general of Zhào, stationed at the frontier, the taxes from the military markets were all used by him to feast his troops. Rewards and punishments were decided in the field, without interference from the court. When Zhōu Yǎfū’s army was at Xìliǔ, only the general’s commands were heard in the camp, not the Son of Heaven’s edicts. For the art of war is such that with every step, a hundred changes may occur—advance when opportunity arises, retreat when difficulties are known. To say, ‘There is the king’s command here,’ is like sending a child to fight a fire: before the order can be returned, the embers have long since turned to ash. To say, ‘There is a supervising officer here,’ is like building a house by the roadside: plans cannot be settled, and nothing can be accomplished. Thus, to interfere with a capable general and demand the suppression of cunning foes is like hobbling a Hànlú hound and expecting it to catch a swift hare—how is this different?”

Zhāng Yù says: “When a general possesses wisdom and courage, he should be entrusted with responsibility for success and not interfered with from the court. Thus it is said: ‘Matters beyond the threshold are decided by the general.’ These five principles are the Dao of knowing victory.”

Cáo Cāo says: “These are the five matters mentioned above.” Thus it is said: “Know the enemy and know yourself, and in a hundred battles you will never be in peril.”

Lǐ Quán says: “Measure your strength to resist the enemy—what peril is there?”

Dù Mù says: “Assess the enemy’s governance by your own; assess the enemy’s generals by your own; assess the enemy’s troops by your own; assess the enemy’s provisions by your own; assess the enemy’s terrain by your own. Once the comparison is complete, and the strengths and weaknesses, advantages and disadvantages are all foreseen, then raise your troops—thus you will win a hundred battles out of a hundred.”

Mèng Shì says: “Carefully discern the enemy’s and your own strengths and weaknesses, advantages and disadvantages—though you fight a hundred battles, you will truly never be in peril.”

Méi Yáochén says: “Know these five matters of the enemy and yourself completely, and you will not fail.”

Wáng Xī says: “Dài means peril. If you thoroughly compare the circumstances of both yourself and the enemy, and only engage in battle when victory is assured, then you will never be in peril, even in a hundred battles.”

Zhāng Yù says: “‘Knowing the enemy and knowing yourself’ refers to attack and defense. Knowing the enemy allows you to attack; knowing yourself allows you to defend. Attack is the mechanism of defense, and defense is the strategy of attack. If you truly understand these, you will never be in peril, even in a hundred battles.” Some say: “Shì Huì discerned that the Chu army could not be opposed, and Chén Píng assessed the strengths and weaknesses of Liú Bāng and Xiàng Yǔ—these are examples of knowing the enemy and knowing oneself. If you do not know the enemy but know yourself, you will win one and lose one.”

Lǐ Quán says: “If you consider yourself strong but do not assess the enemy, victory or defeat remains uncertain.” When Fú Jiān, ruler of Qín, led a million troops south to invade, someone warned him: “They have men of talent—Xiè Ān and Huán Chōng—outstanding figures south of the Yangtze. You must not take them lightly.” Fú Jián replied: “With the forces of eight provinces and a million soldiers and horses, I could dam the river with my whips thrown into it. What difficulty could there be?” Later, he was indeed defeated—this illustrates the principle.

Dù Mù says: “Relying on one’s own strength while failing to recognize that the enemy cannot be attacked results in alternating victory and defeat.” When Wáng Mèng was on his deathbed, he admonished Fú Jiān: “Though the Jìn dynasty resides south of the Yangtze, it holds the Mandate of Heaven. Xiè Ān and Huán Chōng are outstanding figures there—you must not attack them.” Yet when Fú Jiān launched his southern campaign, he declared: “With a million soldiers and horses, I could cross the river with my whips.” He then suffered defeat at Féishuǐ—this is the meaning.

Chén Hào says: “Dù Mù’s explanation refers to an army relying on brute strength without just cause, attacking an innocent state—this is why it failed. It does not mean alternating victory and defeat.”

Dù Yòu says: “Though one may not understand the enemy’s situation, if one relies on one’s own ability to overcome it, victory and defeat will be evenly matched.”

Méi Yáochén says: “If one only knows oneself, victory and defeat will be evenly split.”

Wáng Xī says: “If one only calculates one’s own strengths but does not know the enemy’s, victory or defeat will be uncertain.”

Zhāng Yù says: “Emperor Tàizōng of Táng said: ‘Though today’s generals and ministers may not fully know the enemy, if they merely know themselves, how could they not prevail?’ What is meant by ‘knowing oneself’ is maintaining one’s qi and awaiting the right moment. Thus, if one knows defense but not attack, victory and defeat will be evenly matched. If one knows neither the enemy nor oneself, one will be in peril in every battle.”

Lǐ Quán says: “This is called reckless banditry—what awaits but defeat?”

Dù Yòu says: “Failing to assess the enemy externally and not knowing oneself internally, one will surely be in peril if one engages in battle.”

Méi Yáochén says: “If one knows nothing, how can one win?”

Wáng Xī says: “This is complete blindness in strategy.”

Zhāng Yù says: “If one knows neither the methods of attack nor defense, defeat is certain in battle.”

*Commentary on Sunzi, Scroll Three concludes.*

*1. Originally ‘不’ (not), amended to ‘后’ (later) according to the Song edition.*
*2. Originally ‘池’ (pond), amended to ‘地’ (terrain) according to the Song edition.*
*3. Originally ‘起’ (rise), amended to ‘趋’ (rush) according to the Song edition.*
*4. Originally ‘高壁圣’ (high walls, sage), amended to ‘高壁坚垒’ (high walls, strong ramparts) following Guo Huàruò’s translation of *Eleven Commentaries on Sunzi*.*
*5. Originally ‘女’ (woman), amended to ‘去’ (depart) according to the Song edition.*

*Sunzi Commentary, Scroll Four: The Shape Chapter.*

Cáo Cāo says: “This concerns the shape of an army. When I move and the enemy responds, both sides observe each other—this is the nature of circumstance.”

Lǐ Quán says: “‘Shape’ refers to the configurations of host and guest, attack and defense, the eight formations, the five encampments, yin and yang, and the orientations of front and rear.”

Dù Mù says: “Circumstance is revealed through shape. What is formless conceals circumstance; what is formed exposes it. Concealment brings victory, exposure brings defeat.”

Wáng Zhé says: “‘Shape’ means fixed form—the definite strengths and weaknesses of two opposing forces. One skilled in warfare alters their shape to adapt to the enemy and secure victory.”

Zhāng Yù says: “This speaks of the shapes of attack and defense in two armies. When concealed within, others cannot discern it; when revealed externally, the enemy seizes the opportunity to strike. Shape becomes manifest through attack and defense, hence it follows *Strategic Offensive*.”

Sūnzǐ says: “Of old, those skilled in warfare first ensured they could not be defeated.”

Zhāng Yù says: “This is what is meant by knowing oneself.”

“They awaited the enemy’s moment of vulnerability.”

Méi Yáochén says: “Concealing their shape and cultivating internal order, they watched for the enemy’s laxity.”

Zhāng Yù says: “This is what is meant by knowing the enemy.”

“Inability to be defeated lies with oneself; the opportunity to defeat lies with the enemy.”

Cáo Cāo says: “One cultivates their own order to await the enemy’s laxity.”

Lǐ Quán says: “Those skilled in warfare, when defending, fortify their ramparts, stockpile provisions, and refine their training. When attacking a city, they employ siege towers, scaling ladders, earthen mounds, and tunnels. In formation, they position themselves among rivers and hills, turn their backs to isolation and face emptiness, strike where doubt arises, and exploit gaps. The adept in battle aligns their forces in interlocking angles, ensuring head and tail respond as one—this is how they become invincible. Yet while they can make themselves invincible, they cannot force the enemy to be vulnerable. Thus it is said: victory can be known but not forced. Invincibility lies in defense; vulnerability lies in attack. These principles determine victory.”

Dù Mù says: “One orders their forces and maintains constant readiness against the enemy, concealing their traces and shape so the enemy cannot gauge their intentions. Then, when the enemy presents an exploitable opening, one emerges to attack.”

Dù Yòu says: “First, one consults in the ancestral temple, weighing dangers and difficulties. Then, one raises high ramparts and deep moats, drills the troops, and thus prepares for defense. By awaiting the enemy’s flaws, victory becomes possible. The means to control the enemy lies externally, so one cultivates their own order to await the enemy’s laxity. Only when the enemy’s vulnerabilities become apparent can victory be secured.”

Wáng Shì says: “Invincibility lies in cultivating the Dao and preserving the law; vulnerability lies in the enemy’s gaps.”

Zhāng Yù says: “The cause of defense lies with oneself; the cause of attack lies with the enemy. Thus, the skilled in warfare can make themselves invincible.”

Dù Mù says: “Invincibility refers to what was explained above—ordering one’s forces and concealing one’s shape. This lies within one’s power, hence it is said one *can* achieve it.”

Zhāng Yù says: “By concealing one’s shape and obscuring one’s traces, maintaining constant vigilance, one ensures their own capability.”

“But they cannot make the enemy vulnerable.”

Dù Mù says: “If the enemy reveals no shape to discern, no laxity to exploit, then even if one possesses the means to victory, how can they prevail?”

Jiǎ Lín says: “If the enemy is wise and deeply prepared, one cannot forcibly compel them to be unprepared.”

Dù Yòu says: “It lies with oneself to drill the troops and align with the Dao; if the enemy is deeply prepared, one cannot forcibly defeat them.”

Méi Yáochén says: “It lies with oneself, hence one can achieve it; it lies with the enemy, hence one cannot force it.”

Wáng Shì says: “It lies with the enemy, not with oneself.”

Zhāng Yù says: “If the enemy’s strengths and weaknesses are not externally apparent, how can one determine victory over them? Thus it is said: victory can be known, but not forced.”

Cáo Cāo says: “One sees the formed shape.”

Dù Mù says: “‘Knowing’ means recognizing one’s own strength as sufficient to defeat the enemy.”

Chén Háo says: “Victory is discerned through shape, hence it can be known.”

“But it cannot be forced.”

Cáo Cāo says: “Because the enemy is prepared.”

Dù Mù says: “This means one cannot make the enemy lax, providing the means for one’s own victory.”

Jiǎ Lín says: “If the enemy is concealed and formless, one cannot forcibly determine victory or defeat.”

Dù Yòu says: “If the enemy is prepared, having assessed their shape, victory or defeat may be known. But if the enemy is concealed and formless, one cannot forcibly compel their defeat. As Fàn Lǐ said: ‘When the time is not ripe, one cannot forcibly bring it about; when matters are not exhausted, one cannot forcibly complete them.’”

Méi Yáochén says: “If the enemy has flaws, victory can be known; if the enemy has no flaws, it cannot be forced.”

Hé Shì says: “Victory that can be known lies with oneself—one is prepared. Victory that cannot be forced lies with the enemy—they are formless.”

Zhāng Yù says: “If one is prepared, victory can be known; if the enemy is prepared, it cannot be forced.”

“Invincibility lies in defense.”

Cáo Cāo says: “Concealing one’s shape.”

Dù Mù says: “When one does not yet see the enemy’s exploitable shape, one conceals their own form, preparing to be invincible, and thus defends.”

Dù Yòu says: “Concealing one’s shape. If the enemy’s shape is unseen, and their numbers are greater while one’s own are fewer, one defends.”

Méi Yáochén says: “One waits in readiness.”

Hé Shì says: “If the enemy’s disposition of strength and weakness remains unclear, with no evident opportunity for victory, one should firmly defend.”

Zhāng Yù says: “Knowing one cannot yet prevail, one guards their qi and awaits the moment.”

“Vulnerability lies in attack.”

Cáo Cāo says: “When the enemy attacks, one can then prevail.”

Lǐ Quán says: “Those skilled in warfare, when defending, raise high ramparts and fortify their walls. When attacking, they employ siege towers, scaling ladders, earthen mounds, and tunnels. In formation, they position themselves with rivers and marshes to the left, hills to the right, turn their backs to isolation and face emptiness, strike where doubt arises, and exploit gaps. They discern the five commands to regulate their forces, align their momentum, and ensure head and tail respond as one—this is how they become invincible.”

Without these conditions, one may deem it possible to prevail.

Dù Mù says: “When the enemy presents an opportunity for victory, one should then sally forth and attack.”

Dù Yòu says: “Only when the enemy attacks may one prevail. Once their disposition is revealed—when they are few and we are many—then one may attack.”

Méi Yáochén says: “One strikes at their gaps.”

Wáng Zhé says: “Defense arises from insufficiency in securing victory; attack arises from surplus in securing victory.”

Zhāng Yù says: “Knowing the enemy harbors a principle of vulnerability, one attacks their resolve and seizes them. Defense stems from insufficiency; attack, from surplus.”

Cáo Cāo notes: “What compels us to defend is insufficiency of strength; what compels us to attack is surplus of strength.”

Lǐ Quán says: “Insufficiency of strength permits defense; surplus of strength permits attack.”

Méi Yáochén says: “Defense acknowledges insufficiency of strength; attack acknowledges surplus of strength.”

Zhāng Yù says: “What compels us to defend is the Dao of securing victory being as yet incomplete, so we bide our time. What compels us to attack is the matter of overcoming the enemy already possessing surplus, so we strike. This means one does not engage without certainty of victory, nor breach without absolute assurance. Later scholars who equate insufficiency with weakness and surplus with strength miss the mark. Those skilled in defense conceal themselves beneath the Nine Earths; those skilled in attack move from the Nine Heavens above—thus they preserve themselves and achieve total victory.”

Cáo Cāo says: “Relying on the solidity of mountains, rivers, and hills, one hides beneath the Nine Earths; adapting to the changes of heaven’s seasons, one moves from the Nine Heavens above.”

Lǐ Quán says: *“The Classic of the Celestial Unity and Hidden Stem* states: ‘From the Nine Heavens above, one may array troops; beneath the Nine Earths, one may conceal and hide.’ Typically, one adds the Straight Talisman to the hour’s stem, with the first position it governs as the Nine Heavens, and the second as the Nine Earths. Earth is still and favors concealment; Heaven moves and favors action. Thus, the Martial Lord of Wèi misunderstood the two *dùn* systems, taking the Nine Earths as mountains and rivers, and the Nine Heavens as heaven’s seasons. Yet by mastering the subtle mysteries of the Celestial Unity and Great Unity *dùn*, one attains completeness. The classic says: ‘Know the Three, avoid the Five, stand alone and towering—master the Three and Five, and traverse the world unopposed.’ Applying this method without constraint yields its true meaning.”

Dù Mù says: “Defense veils its voice and erases its traces, as deep and unfathomable as ghosts and spirits, concealed beneath the earth, unseen. Attack is swift and thunderous, swift as lightning, descending like a force from heaven, leaving no time for preparation. The number nine signifies the utmost in height and depth.”

Chén Háo says: “In the three months of spring, *yín* and *gōngcáo* govern the Nine Heavens above, while *shēn* and *chuánsòng* govern the Nine Earths below. In the three months of summer, *wǔ* and *shèngxiān* govern the Nine Heavens above, while *zǐ* and *shénhòu* govern the Nine Earths below. In the three months of autumn, *shēn* and *chuánsòng* govern the Nine Heavens above, while *yín* and *gōngcáo* govern the Nine Earths below. In the three months of winter, *zǐ* and *shénhòu* govern the Nine Heavens above, while *wǔ* and *shèngxiān* govern the Nine Earths below.”

Dù Yòu says: “Those skilled in defense rely on the barriers of mountains and rivers, the solidity of hills, making their position unassailable—so deep and hidden they lie beneath the Nine Earths. Those skilled in attack exploit the changes of heaven’s seasons, terrain, water, and fire, leaving the enemy unaware of where to prepare—so swift and thunderous they strike from the Nine Heavens above.”

Méi Yáochén says: “The Nine Earths signify depths beyond knowing; the Nine Heavens, heights beyond measuring. Thus, defense is secretive, and attack is swift.”

Wáng Zhé says: “Defense, when no advantageous opportunity for attack is evident, should conceal its form, remain still and silent, preventing the enemy from discerning its intentions. Attack, when an advantageous opportunity is evident, should be lofty, swift, and sudden, striking before the enemy can react. The number nine merely expresses the extreme.”

Hé Shì says: “The Nine Earths and Nine Heavens denote profound subtlety.”

*Wèiliáozi* states: “Those who command troops act as if hidden in the earth, as if concealed in the heavens—so secret and remote is their depth.”

During the Later Hàn, the rebel Wáng Guó besieged Chéncāng. The Left General, Huángfǔ Sōng, and the vanguard commander Dǒng Zhuó were sent to relieve the city. Zhuó urged a swift advance, but Sōng refused. Zhuó argued: “The wise do not lag behind the moment; the brave do not linger. A rapid relief saves the city; delay dooms it. The fate of survival or destruction rests here.” Qín replied: “Not so. To win a hundred battles is not as good as subduing the enemy without fighting. Thus, one first ensures invincibility, awaiting the enemy’s vulnerability. Invincibility lies with us; vulnerability lies with them. Their defense is insufficient; our attack is in surplus. Surplus moves from the Nine Heavens above; insufficiency sinks into the Nine Earths below. Though Chéncāng is small, its defenses are solid—it is not a pitfall of the Nine Earths. Though Wáng Guó is strong, he attacks where we do not relieve—his momentum is not that of the Nine Heavens. Without the momentum of the Nine Heavens, the attacker suffers harm; without the pitfall of the Nine Earths, the defender does not falter. Now Wáng Guó has fallen into a position of harm, while Chéncāng holds a small but unassailable stronghold. We may achieve total victory without exhausting our troops—why rush to its relief?” He refused to comply. Wáng Guó besieged Chéncāng from winter to spring, over eighty days, yet the city held firm and could not be breached. The rebels, weary and depleted, eventually withdrew.

Zhāng Yù says: “Concealed beneath the Nine Earths, one is as hidden and unknowable as the depths; moving from the Nine Heavens above, one strikes as swiftly and unpredictably as lightning. *Wèiliáozi* says: ‘As if hidden in the earth, as if concealed in the heavens’—this is the meaning. Defense is solid, ensuring self-preservation.”

Thus, to attack is to seize total victory. To perceive victory only as far as the common crowd perceives it is not the highest excellence. Cáo Cāo says: “One must see what has not yet sprouted.” Lǐ Quán says: “To know no more than the crowd knows is not excellence.” When Hán Xìn crushed Zhào, he marched through the Jǐngxíng Pass before dawn and declared, “We shall break Zhào and feast.” His generals murmured in confusion, feigning assent, saying, “Very well.” Then he arrayed his troops with their backs to the river. Zhào’s forces, observing from their ramparts, burst into laughter, calling the Hán generals inept. Yet Hán Xìn crushed Zhào, feasted, and beheaded Chéng’ānjūn—this was beyond what the crowd could foresee. Dù Mù says: “What the crowd sees is the breaking of armies and the slaying of generals, and only then do they recognize victory. What I see is the deliberation in the ancestral temple, the question posed over the sacrificial vessels—already I know victory or defeat.” Jiǎ Lín says: “To defend is to be solid and unshakable; to attack is to be certain and triumphant. To preserve oneself wholly and never falter; to perceive victory before it manifests, to foresee defeat before it arises—this is subtle, profound, and transcendent, beyond the sight of the common crowd.” Mèng Shì says: “To perceive what has not yet sprouted means that when two armies have already clashed, though one may foresee victory or defeat, one’s strategy cannot surpass others’—one sees only the near, not the distant.” Tài Gōng says: “One whose wisdom is the same as the crowd’s is no master of state.” Méi Yáochén says: “To see only what others see is not excellence.” Wáng Zhé says: “The common crowd sees how victory is won but does not know the configuration that secures it.” Zhāng Yù says: “What the crowd knows is what has already taken shape and become manifest; what I see is what has not yet formed, what has not yet sprouted.”

To win a battle and have all under heaven call it excellent is not the highest excellence. Cáo Cāo says: “Contending at the point of the blade.” Lǐ Quán says: “To contend by force of arms, though all under heaven may see it, is not excellence.” Dù Mù says: “‘All under heaven’ here is as the crowd mentioned above. When all under heaven praise a victorious battle, they mean the breaking of armies and the slaying of generals. My excellence lies in covert planning and subtle maneuvering, in attacking through strategy and subduing through counsel, so that the day of victory comes without bloodshed.” Chén Hào says: “To subtly deploy one’s wisdom and focus on breaking the enemy’s strategy, to subdue the enemy’s forces without battle—this is the highest excellence.” Méi Yáochén says: “To see no further than the crowd, though one may win the battle and all under heaven praise it, is still not called excellence.” Wáng Shì says: “To subdue others through strategy is true excellence.” Zhāng Yù says: “To win only after battle, so that all under heaven call it excellent, is to have a reputation for wisdom and deeds of valor—thus it is not the highest excellence. But to discern the subtle and perceive the hidden, to seize victory from the formless—this is true excellence.”

Thus, to lift a autumn hair is not considered great strength; to see the sun and moon is not considered keen vision; to hear thunder is not considered sharp hearing. Cáo Cāo says: “These are easily seen and heard.” Lǐ Quán says: “These are easily seen and heard.” To regard victory in attack and battle as excellence is not what all under heaven calls excellent. The general of profound wisdom is unfathomable to others, for he devises deep strategies. Thus Sūn Wǔ says: “Hard to know as the dark of night.” Wáng Zhé says: “What the crowd knows is not wisdom; to win by force of arms is not excellence.” Hé Shì says: “This speaks of what the crowd sees and hears as nothing extraordinary. Of old, Wū Huò lifted a tripod of a thousand jūn by strength; Lí Zhū observed the finest fibers at a hundred paces by keen sight; Shī Kuàng heard the tread of a mosquito or mantis by sharp hearing. But when the form of battle is already manifest, who cannot see it? Thus, to triumph before form arises is to truly know warfare.” Zhāng Yù says: “All can do these things—this is used to illustrate how the crowd perceives victory. An autumn hair refers to the fur of a hare, which becomes stiff and fine in autumn, meaning something exceedingly light.”

The ancients called those who excelled in warfare victors over the easily vanquished. Cáo Cāo says: “One probes the subtle and easily vanquished, attacks what can be overcome, and does not attack what cannot be overcome.” Dù Mù says: “When the enemy’s plans first show signs of sprouting, I subtly maneuver to attack them. With little effort and minimal exertion, victory is assured—thus it is called easily vanquished.” Méi Yáochén says: “To lift an autumn hair by strength, to see the sun and moon by keen vision, to hear thunder by sharp hearing—these do not surpass what the crowd can do. Thus, to see what is manifest is to win with difficulty; to see what is subtle is to win with ease.” Hé Shì says: “When the enemy’s plans first show signs of sprouting, I subtly maneuver to attack them. With little effort and minimal exertion, victory is assured—thus it is called easily vanquished.” Zhāng Yù says: “To engage in close combat and only then overcome the enemy is to win with difficulty. To perceive the subtle and discern the hidden, to break the enemy before form arises, is to win with ease. Thus, the excellent warrior always attacks what is easily vanquished and does not attack what is difficult to overcome.”

Therefore, the victory of one who excels in warfare bears no reputation for wisdom, no deeds of valor. Cáo Cāo says: “When the enemy’s forces have not yet taken shape, to defeat them brings no resounding glory.” Lǐ Quán says: “To defeat the enemy without all under heaven knowing—what reputation for wisdom is there?” Dù Mù says: “To triumph before anything has sprouted, so that all under heaven does not know—thus there is no reputation for wisdom. Without bloodshed, the enemy state submits—thus there are no deeds of valor.” Méi Yáochén says: “Great wisdom is not displayed; great deeds are not proclaimed. To see the subtle and win with ease—what valor, what wisdom is there?” Hé Shì says: “When disaster is dissolved before it takes shape, who will call it wisdom? To subdue without battle, who will speak of valor? The Hán’s Zǐ Fáng and the Táng’s Péi Dù were capable of this.” Zhāng Yù says: “Through covert planning and subtle maneuvering, to seize victory from the formless, so that all under heaven does not hear of one’s wisdom in assessing the enemy and securing victory, does not see one’s deeds in capturing banners and beheading generals—this is like the Marquis of Liú, who never had battlefield exploits.”

Thus, their victories in battle are not martial. Lǐ Quán says: “To win a hundred battles out of a hundred—what doubt is there?”

This Li Quán takes "martial" to mean "doubt." Chén Háo says: "Plans are not made in vain; strategies are not launched idly." Zhāng Yù says: "To seek victory through brute force, even the skilled may sometimes fail. But if one perceives what has not yet taken shape and discerns what has not yet formed, then one may win a hundred battles out of a hundred without a single lapse in martial prowess. 'Not martial' means one’s measures ensure victory over an already defeated foe."

Cáo Cāo says: "To discern where the enemy may be defeated is to avoid lapses in martial prowess." Lǐ Quán says: "To secure victory over an already defeated army—where is the martial valor in that? Troops exhausted and soldiers demoralized, commands inconsistent—this is what it means to be already defeated." Dù Mù says: "措 means to arrange or place. 武 refers to lapses in martial prowess. Why can I arrange victory without such lapses? Because I first perceive the enemy’s already defeated state, then attack, and thus achieve complete victory without any lapse in martial prowess."

Jiǎ Lín says: "Read 措 as 错, meaning 'to intermingle' or 'assess.' To seize victory from the enemy follows no other principle, hence one must intermingle and calculate. One constantly perceives the enemy’s defeat before victory has taken shape." Méi Yáochén says: "To see where they may be defeated is to ensure victory without lapse." Hé Shì says: "This is skillful calculation." Zhāng Yù says: "The reason one can win without lapse is that one first discerns the enemy’s vulnerabilities, then deploys troops accordingly."

Thus, the skilled warrior stands on ground of invincibility and does not miss the enemy’s defeat. Lǐ Quán says: "Armies that secure terrain thrive; those that lose it perish. Terrain refers to strategic positions. When the Qín army defeated Zhào, the first to seize the northern mountain prevailed. When the Sòng forces attacked Yān, victory came after crossing the Great Xiàn Pass—both secured advantageous ground." Dù Mù says: "Ground of invincibility means devising plans to ensure the enemy cannot defeat me. Not missing the enemy’s defeat means observing their vulnerabilities with perfect precision." Chén Háo’s annotation aligns with Lǐ Quán’s; Dù Yòu’s aligns with Dù Mù’s.

Méi Yáochén says: "To skillfully await the enemy’s gaps is to ensure constant victory." Wáng Zhé says: "Constantly render oneself invincible, await the enemy’s vulnerability, and never miss the opportunity." Hé Shì says: "Relying on preparedness ensures no negligence; constantly watching for the enemy’s gaps ensures victory without fail. Standing on ground of invincibility is advantageous—it means I am always positioned for victory." Zhāng Yù says: "By refining our commands, clarifying rewards and punishments, preparing our equipment, and cultivating martial valor, we stand on ground of invincibility. With disciplined forces, the enemy will naturally rush into defeat."

Therefore, victorious armies first secure victory, then seek battle; defeated armies first fight, then seek victory. Cáo Cāo says: "This refers to having strategy versus acting without forethought." Lǐ Quán says: "This refers to planning versus not planning. Thus, Lord Xuē knew Xiàng Yǔ was doomed to defeat, and Tián Fēng foresaw Cáo Cāo’s victory—this is the principle at work."

Dù Mù says: "Guǎnzǐ states: 'Heaven’s timing, terrain’s advantage, their quantities and key points all emerge from calculation.' Thus, the way of attack and conquest is first settled through planning within, then troops are dispatched beyond the borders. Without understanding the enemy’s governance, one cannot strike; without knowing their resources, one cannot constrain them; without knowing their generals, one cannot lead the vanguard; without knowing their troops, one cannot array the battle lines. Therefore, to strike the few with the many, order with chaos, wealth with poverty, ability with inability, trained soldiers with raw recruits—this is how one wins a hundred battles out of a hundred. This is the meaning of first securing victory, then seeking battle."

Duke Wèi’s Lǐ Jìng says: "A general’s highest duty lies in keen discernment and harmonious troops, deep strategy and far-reaching foresight, understanding heaven’s timing and human principles. If one does not assess capabilities, fails to grasp adaptability, and only at the critical moment hesitates, glancing left and right, bereft of plans, swayed by conflicting advice, wavering in advance or retreat, with units in disarray—how is this different from driving the people into boiling water and fire, or herding cattle and sheep to feed wolves and tigers? This is the meaning of first fighting, then seeking victory."

Jiǎ Lín says: "Without knowing the enemy’s and one’s own conditions, rashly deploying troops in the hope of victory will only lead to defeat." Méi Yáochén says: "If one fights when victory is assured, victory follows; if one fights when victory is not yet apparent, can victory be attained?" Hé Shì says: "In warfare, one must first devise a plan for certain victory before deploying troops. Without prior strategy, relying on brute strength does not guarantee victory."

Zhāng Yù says: "By first securing victory through planning, then raising troops, one prevails in battle. As the *Wèi Liáozi* states: 'Without certainty of victory, one does not speak of battle; without certainty of success, one does not speak of attack.' This means perilous affairs must not be undertaken lightly. It also states: 'Victory here ensures victory there; failure here ensures failure there.' This is the principle. Like Zhào Chōngguó, who always planned before fighting, one avoids reckless advance in the hope of luck, which leads to defeat in battle."

The skilled in warfare cultivate the Dao and uphold the law, thus they govern victory and defeat. Cáo Cāo says: "The skilled in warfare first cultivate invincibility and uphold the law, ensuring they do not miss the enemy’s disorder." Lǐ Quán says: "To punish the rebellious with righteousness, not to attack blameless states, to arrive without plunder, not to fell trees or poison wells, to purify and sweep the mountains, rivers, city altars, and ancestral shrines one passes, and to avoid repeating the deeds of fallen states—this is called the Dao and the law."

The army is stern and disciplined, accepting death without transgression; rewards and punishments are trustworthy and righteous. A general who embodies this can overcome the enemy’s disordered governance.

Dù Mù says: "Dao is benevolence and righteousness; law is the system of regulations. The skilled in warfare first cultivate benevolence and righteousness, uphold the legal system, and establish invincible governance. They await the enemy’s vulnerabilities, then attack and secure victory."

Jiǎ Lín says: "One must constantly refine the Dao of victory in warfare and uphold the laws of rewards and punishments. Thus, one determines victory or defeat—this is called the governance of victory and defeat."

Méi Yáochén says: "Attack and defense depend on self-cultivation; laws and orders rely on self-preservation. It rests with oneself."

Wáng Zhě says: "Law refers to the five matters below."

Zhāng Yù says: "Cultivate the Dao of warfare and uphold the laws of controlling the enemy, thus ensuring certain victory."

Some say: "First refine Dao and virtue to harmonize the troops; then uphold laws and orders to restrain subordinates. Make the people both love and fear you, and only then can you govern victory and defeat."

The military method consists of five matters:
First, *du* (measurement).
Jiǎ Lín says: "*Du* refers to land."
Wáng Zhě says: "It means length and distance."

Second, *liàng* (assessment).
Jiǎ Lín says: "*Liàng* refers to the number of people, the fullness or emptiness of granaries."
Wáng Zhě says: "It means capacity and volume."

Third, *shù* (calculation).
Jiǎ Lín says: "*Shù* is calculation. By calculation, one knows the multitude or fewness, the emptiness or fullness."
Wáng Zhě says: "It means hundreds and thousands."

Fourth, *chēng* (weighing).
Jiǎ Lín says: "Having known the multitude or fewness, one further knows the weight of virtue and achievement, the length and shortness of talent between oneself and the enemy."
Wáng Zhě says: "It means balance and scale."

Fifth, *shèng* (victory).
Cáo Cāo says: "The governance of victory and defeat, the method of warfare—one must weigh these five matters to know the enemy’s situation."
Zhāng Yù says: "This speaks of the method of encampment and formation."
Lǐ Wèigōng says: "Training soldiers is like placing pieces on a board; without marked paths, how can the pieces be used? The land gives rise to *du*."

Cáo Cāo says: "*Du* means measuring based on terrain."
Lǐ Quán says: "Having measured the situation, one assesses the enemy to control them."
Dù Mù says: "*Du* means calculation. It refers to calculating the size of one’s territory, the number of households, tax revenue, registered chariots, the peril or ease of mountains and rivers, the circuitousness or directness of roads. One measures these against the enemy before raising troops. The small cannot scheme against the large, the weak cannot strike the strong, the near cannot raid the distant, the plain cannot assault the perilous—all arise from the land, thus measurement comes first."
Méi Yáochén says: "Measure military strength based on the land."
Wáng Zhě says: "Land is what people tread upon. Raising troops and waging war begins with the land, from which *du* arises. *Du* measures length to know distance."
Hé Shì says: "Land refers to distance, peril, and ease. *Du* means calculation. Before sending out troops, first calculate the enemy’s peril or ease, the circuitousness or directness of roads, the number of arms, and whether their courage or cowardice prevails. Only after calculating the feasibility of attack should one mobilize troops, ensuring success."
*Du* gives rise to *liàng*.

Dù Mù says: "*Liàng* means deliberation. Having measured the land thoroughly, one then deliberates on the strength and weakness of oneself and the enemy."
Méi Yáochén says: "Assess the enemy’s situation based on measurement."
Wáng Zhě says: "It means assessing size. Having known the calculation of distance, one must further assess the land’s size."
Hé Shì says: "Deliberate on the forms and circumstances of oneself and the enemy."
*Liàng* gives rise to *shù*.

Cáo Cāo says: "Knowing distance and breadth, one knows the number of people."
Lǐ Quán says: "Assessing the enemy’s distance, strength, and weakness requires preparing the number of soldiers and military resources to secure victory."
Dù Mù says: "*Shù* means strategic calculation. Having determined strength and weakness, one can then employ strategic changes."
Jiǎ Lín says: "Measuring the land’s distance and breadth reveals the enemy’s number of people."
Méi Yáochén says: "From assessment comes the number of the multitude or few."
Wáng Zhě says: "*Shù* records multitude or fewness. Having known the enemy’s size, one further calculates their number, whether refined or inferior."
Cáo Gōng says: "Know their number of people."
Hé Shì says: "*Shù* means strategic change. First deliberate on the advantages and disadvantages of strength and weakness between oneself and the enemy, then devise strategies."
Zhāng Yù says: "The land has forms of distance and breadth; one must first measure and know them, then assess the number of people it can hold."
*Shù* gives rise to *chēng*.

Cáo Cāo says: "*Chēng* means weighing which side is superior."
Lǐ Quán says: "Having determined the number of worthy and wise, those who gain worthies are heavy; those who lose them are light. Like Hán Xìn’s analysis of Chǔ and Hàn, one must know the weight, distinguish the worthy and foolish, and arrange them appropriately to become strong."
Dù Mù says: "*Chēng* means comparison. Having employed strategic calculations, one can then compare the victory or defeat of oneself and the enemy."
Méi Yáochén says: "Weigh light and heavy based on numbers."
Wáng Zhě says: "*Chēng* knows weight and lightness, illustrating the forms of strength and weakness. Fully knowing the calculation of distance, the undertaking of size, and the number of multitude or fewness, and comparing them with the enemy, one knows where the weight lies."
Hé Shì’s note is the same as Dù Mù’s.
*Chēng* gives rise to *shèng*.

Cáo Cāo says: "From the calculation of weighing, one knows where victory or defeat lies."
Lǐ Quán says: "Weighing light and heavy reveals the calculation of victory and defeat."
Dù Mù says: "Having compared thoroughly, the victory of oneself and the defeat of the enemy become clearly visible."
Méi Yáochén says: "From light and heavy comes the knowledge of victory and defeat."
Wáng Zhě says: "The heavy overcomes the light."
Chén Háo, Dù Yòu, and Lǐ Quán’s notes on the five matters are the same as Dù Mù’s.

Hé Shì says: "The five matters above are the methods for calculating victory before battle begins. Thus, Master Sun cites ancient principles to elucidate the essentials of victory and defeat."

Zhāng Yù says: "*Chēng* means 'proportion.' When terrain and troop numbers are in proper proportion, density and spacing are fitting, and thus victory is possible." *Wèi Liáozi* states: "No error lies in measurement and calculation. *Dù* refers to units of length, *shù* to formations of ten and five. Measure land with *dù*, troops with *shù*; when land and troops are in proportion, victory follows. All five matters arise from terrain, and thus victory is born of it." This is exemplified by Lǐ Jìng’s five formations, which adapt to terrain.

"Thus, victorious troops are like weighing a *yì* against a *zhū*."

Méi Yáochén says: "Strength makes lifting easy."

"Defeated troops are like weighing a *zhū* against a *yì*."

Cáo Cāo says: "The light cannot lift the heavy."

Lǐ Quán says: "Twenty *liǎng* make a *yì*." Between *zhū* and *yì*, light and heavy occupy opposite positions; so too do the calculations of victory and defeat.

Méi Yáochén says: "Strength makes control difficult."

Wáng Zhé says: "The terms *zhū* and *yì* clarify the extremes of light and heavy."

Zhāng Yù says: "Twenty *liǎng* make a *yì*, and twenty-four *zhū* make a *liǎng*. This speaks of disciplined troops facing undisciplined ones—light and heavy are incomparable."

"When victorious troops engage the people, it is like damming water in a thousand-*rèn* ravine—this is *xíng*."

Cáo Cāo says: "Eight *chǐ* make a *rèn*." Water dammed to a thousand *rèn* possesses swift and towering force.

Lǐ Quán says: "Eight *chǐ* make a *rèn*—this describes its power." When Dù Yù campaigned against Wú, he said the army advanced like splitting bamboo: after the first few joints, the rest cleaved apart effortlessly. This is the same principle.

Dù Mù says: "Water pooled in a thousand-*rèn* ravine is unfathomable, like my defense, which reveals no form. When the dam breaks, the torrent rushes forth unstoppably, like my attack, which cannot be withstood."

Méi Yáochén says: "Water dammed in a thousand-*rèn* ravine moves too swiftly to gauge; an army striking from the ninth heaven leaves no trace. This is the *xíng* of war."

Wáng Zhé says: "A thousand-*rèn* ravine is sheer and precipitous—this illustrates the form of the invincible facing the conquerable. Seizing the moment to attack is like releasing the dammed water."

Zhāng Yù says: "Water’s nature is to avoid heights and rush downward; when dammed, it surges into the deep ravine, swift and irresistible. The *xíng* of war resembles water: exploit the enemy’s unpreparedness, strike where unexpected, avoid the solid and attack the hollow—none can withstand it."

Some say: "A thousand-*rèn* ravine refers to an unfathomable abyss, whose depth no one can measure. When the dam breaks, its force cannot be resisted. Likewise, a skilled defender conceals form and obscures tracks, hiding in the depths of the nine earths, so the enemy cannot gauge strength or weakness. Yet when seizing the hollow to strike, its momentum cannot be met."

Cao Cao says: "The use of troops depends on *shi* (momentum)."
Li Quan says: "Formations are arranged to create structure, like the force of a dam’s breach—thus this chapter follows the preceding one."
Wang Zhe says: "*Shi* is the transformation of accumulated momentum. A skilled commander harnesses *shi* to secure victory without exhausting strength."
Zhang Yu says: "Once military *shi* is established, victory is achieved by relying on it—hence it follows the chapter on *xing* (form)."

Sunzi says: "Governing many is like governing few—this is a matter of *fenshu* (division and numbers)."
Cao Cao says: "Regiments are divisions; squads are numbers."
Li Quan says: "A master of warfare need only strike a gong or raise a banner, and the entire army responds. With orders fixed, it is as if commanding few."
Du Mu says: "*Fen* means division; *shu* means numbers. Troops in regiments and ranks are divided by their numbers, each assigned to subordinate officers. Training, promotions, and demotions are their responsibility—thus what I govern is few."
Han Xin says: "The more, the better—this is the principle."
Chen Hao says: "When troops are numerous, they must be organized into multiple units, each led by minor officers. Dividing their numbers ensures disciplined order. When facing the enemy, they are given tactics—thus though I command many, what I govern remains few."
Meng Shi says: "*Fen* refers to formations; *shu* to the total count of troops. The divisions and numbers are predetermined."
Mei Yaochen says: "The divisions of *qi* (unorthodox) and *zheng* (orthodox) formations each have their command structure."
Wang Zhe says: "*Fenshu* refers to regiments. Subordinate officers each have their divisions and assigned numbers, such as *shi* (divisions), *lü* (brigades), *zu* (companies), and *liang* (platoons)."
Zhang Yu says: "With many troops, first assign responsibilities to subordinate officers and fix the numbers in ranks to prevent disorder—only then can they be deployed. The method of governing troops is as follows: one man is *du* (solitary), two are *bi* (paired), three are *can* (triad). *Bi* and *can* form a *wu* (squad), five men a *lie* (file), two *lie* a *huo* (fire team), five *huo* a *dui* (platoon), two *dui* a *guan* (company), two *guan* a *qu* (regiment), two *qu* a *bu* (battalion), two *bu* a *xiao* (brigade), two *xiao* a *pi* (division), and two *pi* an *jun* (army). Each level commands the next, all subject to training—thus governing a million is like governing few."

Governing many is like governing few—this is a matter of *xingming* (form and designation).
Cao Cao says: "Banners are *xing* (form); gongs and drums are *ming* (designation)."
Du Mu says: "Banners and drums are also used by the enemy—how can they alone be *xingming*? Governing many is like governing few. *Xing* refers to formations; *ming* to banners. The *Art of War* states: 'Between formations, space is left for drawing blades.' Within a large formation, smaller formations occupy their own sectors, each with its distinct *xing*. Banners follow directional colors or bear animal emblems; each general and formation has its own name. Once *xingming* is established, focus is unified and momentum concentrated. Men fight as if alone—defeat or victory is their own. Thus commanding a million is like commanding a single man."
Chen Hao says: "With many soldiers spread across a broad front, they cannot recognize one another in battle. Hence banners serve as *xing* for identification. Movement and timing cannot be heard, so gongs and drums regulate them. The command is: 'Advance at the drum, halt at the gong.' Cao Cao’s explanation is correct."
Mei Yaochen says: "*Xing* is conveyed by banners; *ming* by insignia. Commands are executed swiftly, without delay."
Wang Zhe says: "Cao Cao says: 'Banners are *xing*; gongs and drums are *ming*.' I say: *Xing* refers to the system of banners, gongs, and drums; *ming* to their specific names."
Zhang Yu says: "*Military Regulations* states: 'When voices cannot be heard, drums and bells are used; when sights cannot be seen, banners are raised.' With many troops, distances are great, and sight and hearing fail. Thus soldiers advance or retreat by watching banners and move or halt by listening to gongs and drums—so the brave cannot advance alone, nor the timid retreat alone. This is the method of commanding many."

"Enabling the entire army to engage the enemy without defeat lies in the use of *qi* (unorthodox) and *zheng* (orthodox)."
Cao Cao says: "The first to engage is *zheng*; the later is *qi*."
Li Quan says: "Facing the enemy is *zheng*; flanking is *qi*. Without *qi* troops, one cannot contend for advantage."
The Han king of Wu, Ao, led his forces into Daliang. His general Tian Bolu advised: "If the army masses and advances west without alternative routes, success will be difficult. Let me take fifty thousand men to follow the Yangtze and Huai rivers upstream, seize the lands of Chu and Changsha, and enter Wuguan to rendezvous with Your Majesty. This would be one *qi* strategy." The king did not heed him.

Thus, he was defeated by Zhou Yafu. This was a case of having *zheng* without *qi*.

Du Mu says: "The explanation lies in the text below."
Jia Lin says: "Confronting the enemy with *zheng* formations secures victory, while *qi* troops strike from front, rear, left, and right in coordinated response—thus one constantly prevails without defeat."
Mei Yaochen says: "Movement is *qi*; stillness is *zheng*. Remain still to await the enemy, move to overcome them."
Wang Zhe says: "*Cha* should be *bi*—a scribal error. *Qi* and *zheng* mutually generate one another, hence one can engage the enemy without defeat."
He Shi says: "The nature of warfare is endlessly mutable, tangled and indistinct—yet nothing is not *zheng*, nothing is not *qi*. When troops are raised for a just cause, that is *zheng*; when facing the enemy, adapting to circumstances is *qi*. My *zheng* should appear as *qi* to the enemy; my *qi* should appear as *zheng*. *Zheng* becomes *qi*, *qi* becomes *zheng*. Generally, all warfare employs *qi* and *zheng*. Victory without *qi* or *zheng* is mere luck—a reckless gamble. As when Han Xin arrayed his troops with their backs to the river, led forces along the mountains, and seized the Zhao banners to shatter their state: the riverbank was *zheng*, the mountain circuit was *qi*. Later, Han Xin massed troops at Linjin while sending wooden jars across the Xiayang ford to ambush Anyi and capture King Bao of Wei: Linjin was *zheng*, Xiayang was *qi*. From this, we see that engaging the enemy without defeat depends on *qi* and *zheng*."

The *Wei Liaozi* states: "Now, with the sharpness of a *zhenyou* blade, the hardness of rhinoceros and buffalo hide, and the might of the Three Armies—if one employs *qi* and *zheng*, none in the world can withstand their assault."
Zhang Yu says: "Though the Three Armies are vast, enabling every soldier to engage the enemy without defeat lies in *qi* and *zheng*. The explanations of *qi* and *zheng* differ among scholars. The *Wei Liaozi* says: '*Zheng* troops value going first; *qi* troops value following after.' Cao Cao says: 'The first to engage is *zheng*; the later is *qi*.' Li Jing says: 'Troops advancing forward are *zheng*; those retreating are *qi*.' All these take *zheng* as *zheng* and *qi* as *qi*, never addressing their cyclical transformation. Only Emperor Taizong of Tang said: 'Use *qi* as *zheng*, making the enemy perceive it as *zheng*—then strike with *qi*. Use *zheng* as *qi*, making the enemy perceive it as *qi*—then strike with *zheng*. Blend them into one method, leaving the enemy unable to discern—this is the most thorough explanation. Where troops strike, it is like hurling a stone at an egg: this is the interplay of *xu* (void) and *shi* (solid).'"

Cao Cao says: "Strike the utmost void with the utmost solid."
Li Quan says: "*Xia* (flaw) is solid; *mao* (egg) is void. Striking void with solid is effortless."
Meng Shi says: "*Xia* is stone. If troops are trained to perfection, ranks and commands clear, and one thoroughly assesses the enemy’s situation to discern void and solid, then attacking with such troops is like hurling a stone at an egg."
Mei Yaochen says: "*Xia* is stone, pronounced *xia*. Striking void with solid is like breaking the brittle with the firm."
Wang Shi says: "*Yin* is iron-forging."
He Shi says: "In warfare, recognizing the dynamics of void and solid ensures invincibility."
Zhang Yu says: "The following chapter states: 'The skilled commander directs others and is not directed by them.' This is the method of *xu* and *shi* between self and enemy.
