Luring the enemy to come makes their force perpetually void; refusing to go to them keeps our force perpetually solid. Striking void with solid is like casting a stone at an egg—it shatters effortlessly. When assembling troops and gathering forces, first establish divisions and numbers. Once divisions are clear, practice formations and signals. When formations and signals are correct, distinguish *qi* and *zheng*. Once *qi* and *zheng* are discerned, void and solid become apparent. These four matters follow in sequence. In all battles, *zheng* engages, *qi* secures victory."

Cao Cao says: "*Zheng* confronts the enemy; *qi* troops strike from the flank where they are unprepared."
Li Quan says: "Without deception in battle, victory over the enemy is difficult."
Du You says: "*Zheng* confronts the enemy; *qi* strikes from the flank where they are unprepared. Engage in battle with *zheng*, secure victory through *qi* variation."
Mei Yaochen says: "Use *zheng* to engage in battle, *qi* to overcome the enemy."
He Shi says: "For example, when Lian Po was Zhao’s general, Qin sent a messenger saying, 'Qin fears only Zhao Kuo.' Lian Po was easy to deal with and would soon surrender. Hearing of Qin’s reverse inquiry, Zhao replaced Po with Kuo. Upon arrival, Kuo led troops to attack Qin. Qin feigned defeat and retreated, then deployed two *qi* forces to ambush him. Zhao pursued the apparent victory, pressing to Qin’s ramparts, which held firm and refused entry. Meanwhile, Qin’s *qi* troops—twenty-five thousand—cut off Zhao’s rear, while five thousand cavalry severed Zhao’s supply lines. Zhao’s forces were split in two, their provisions cut off, and Kuo was ultimately defeated.

Another example: When the Türks invaded the frontier, Emperor Yang of Sui ordered Tang Gaozu and Wang Renyong, governor of Mayi, to lead troops in defense. As bandits besieged Mayi, Renyong, outnumbered, grew fearful. Gaozu said, 'Now the emperor is distant, this isolated city cut off from aid. Without a fight to the death, survival is impossible.' He personally selected four thousand elite cavalry as a mobile force, living and moving with the Türks, following water and grass, indistinguishable from them. When they encountered Türk scouts, they merely galloped and hunted, feigning ease. Upon meeting the enemy, they formed a wedge-shaped formation, selecting skilled archers as a separate unit to hold their bows at full draw. The Türks, unable to gauge their strength, dared not engage. Gaozu then unleashed *qi* troops to rout them, capturing the Türk chieftain’s prized steed and beheading over a thousand.

Later, Emperor Taizong selected over a thousand elite cavalry as *qi* troops, all clad in black armor, divided into left and right wings, bearing great banners. He appointed generals Qin Shubao and Cheng Yaojin to command them. Whenever facing the enemy, Taizong donned black armor to lead the vanguard, advancing at the opportune moment. Wherever they struck, resistance crumbled. Often outnumbered, they still struck terror into the hearts of bandits."

During the Five Dynasties, when the founding emperor of the Later Han resided in Jinyang, Guo Jin sought refuge with him. The Han founder admired his talent. When northern barbarians sacked Anyang City, he dispatched Jin to retake it. The barbarians fled, and Jin was appointed Prefect of Fangzhou. When the barbarian chieftain died en route, the Han founder launched *qi* troops through Jingxing Pass. Jin, taking the road ahead, entered the northern marshes and thereby pacified Hebei. These were all advances achieved through *qi* victories.

Zhang Yu remarked: When two armies face each other, first engage with regular troops; then slowly unleash *qi* troops, either striking their flanks or attacking their rear to secure victory. This resembles how the Duke of Zheng repelled the Yan forces, deploying three armies at their front while concealing another to strike their rear.

Thus, those skilled in deploying *qi* are as inexhaustible as heaven and earth. Li Quan said: In movement and stillness. As unceasing as the rivers and seas. Li Quan said: Flowing without end. Du You said: This speaks of responding to change with inexhaustible *qi*. Zhang Yu said: It means adapting with endless *qi* and variation.

Ending yet beginning anew—such are the sun and moon. Dying yet reviving—such are the four seasons. Li Quan said: *Qi* transforms like the sun, moon, and four seasons, their waxing and waning, cold and heat unceasing. Du You said: The sun and moon revolve, setting and rising again; the four seasons alternate, flourishing and declining. This describes how *qi* and regular forces shift and change, like the sun and moon’s advance and retreat, the four seasons’ rise and fall. Zhang Yu said: The sun and moon revolve, setting and rising again; the four seasons alternate, flourishing and declining. This illustrates how *qi* and regular forces intermingle in boundless transformation, beginning and ending without end.

There are no more than five notes, yet the variations of the five notes are beyond hearing. Li Quan said: Gong, Shang, Jiao, Zhi, and Yu. Li Quan said: Their variations enter the eight sounds, and the melodies played are endless. There are no more than five colors, yet the variations of the five colors are beyond sight. Li Quan said: Blue, yellow, red, white, and black. There are no more than five flavors, yet the variations of the five flavors are beyond tasting. Li Quan said: Sour, pungent, salty, sweet, and bitter. Cao Cao said: From "as inexhaustible as heaven and earth" onward, all illustrate the endlessness of *qi* and regular forces. Li Quan said: The variations of the five flavors are like a chef’s culinary mastery. Du Mu said: From "as inexhaustible as heaven and earth" onward, all compare the eight formations’ *qi* and regular forces. Zhang Yu said: The variations of the five notes, five colors, and five flavors illustrate the endless generation of *qi* and regular forces.

The dynamics of war are no more than *qi* and regular forces, yet their variations are inexhaustible. Li Quan said: Ambushes, interceptions, feints, and raids—countless tactical forms cannot be exhausted. Mei Yaochen said: The variations of *qi* and regular forces are like those of the five notes, five colors, and five flavors—endless. Wang Zhe said: *Qi* and regular forces are the key and pivot of warfare, the crux of victory. Adapting to the enemy in battle, they cycle endlessly; exhaustion leads to defeat. Meng Shi said: The *Six Secret Teachings* states: *Qi* and regular forces emerge from an inexhaustible source. Zhang Yu said: The dynamics of battle formations are limited to *qi* and regular forces alone, yet their variations in application are myriad, with countless paths—how could they be exhausted?

*Qi* and regular forces generate each other, like a cycle without end—who can exhaust them? Li Quan said: *Qi* and regular forces depend on each other to arise, like a ring without beginning or end. Mei Yaochen said: Their transformations and movements are infinite. Wang Zhe said: The enemy cannot exhaust us. He Shi said: *Qi* and regular forces arise and transform in turn, like tracing a ring to seek its beginning and end—both are unfathomable. Zhang Yu said: *Qi* becomes regular, regular becomes *qi*; their transformations generate each other like a cycle without origin or end—who can exhaust their meaning?

The swiftness of rushing water can even float stones—this is *shi*. Meng Shi said: When *shi* is steep, even heavy stones cannot resist. Du You said: Water is soft by nature, stone hard and heavy, yet rushing water can carry great stones and hurl them downstream—all due to the force of its swift, surging flow. Zhang Yu said: Though water is soft, when channeled through narrow, critical paths, its swift flow gains the *shi* to turn massive stones.

The speed of a startled bird can break and shatter—this is *jie*. Cao Cao said: Launching an attack on the enemy. Li Quan said: If soft *shi* can turn the hard, how much more so for warfare? The reason slingshots can strike flying birds is their mastery of speed and timing. Du Mu said: *Shi* is like water flowing from high to low, gaining swift, perilous force—thus it can float stones. *Jie* is measuring distance to strike—thus it can shatter objects. Du You said: Launching an attack on the enemy is like an eagle or falcon’s strike—able to crush and break prey because of keen observation and precise timing. Master Wang said: When an eagle strikes, no bird can contest its *shi*; when a fierce tiger rouses, no beast can rival its might. Mei Yaochen said: Though water is soft, its swift *shi* can float stones; though a bird is small, its forceful *jie* can shatter objects. Wang Zhe said: The speed of a startled bird is also *shi*, for *shi* enables its striking *jie*. The text first speaks of perilous terrain, hence the analogy of floating stones. He Shi said: Water can move stones through the *shi* of high and low; a bird can seize prey by timing its distance. Zhang Yu said: When eagles and falcons seize birds and sparrows, they measure distance, observe carefully, and strike—thus they can shatter objects. The *Master Wei Liao* states: Prepare our weapons, cultivate our martial valor, and unleash them like a bird’s strike.

Li Jing said: When a startled bird is about to strike, it flies low and draws in its wings—all speak of awaiting the moment before unleashing. Thus, those skilled in warfare make their *shi* perilous.

Cao Cao and Li Quan said: *Perilous* means swift.
Du Mu said: *Perilous* refers to the momentum of war—once unleashed, it slays men, hence the later analogy of a drawn crossbow.
Wang Zhe said: *Perilous* means bending to achieve speed, like water gaining force through narrow straits.

Their *jie* is short.
Cao Cao and Li Quan said: *Short* means near.
Du Mu said: It refers to proximity in timing.
Like a startled bird’s strike, when near, it seizes its prey—strength is whole, focus is single, and thus it captures.
Du You said: *Short* means near; *jie* means decisive. *Short* and *near* speak of seizing victory from danger by striking close.
Mei Yaochen said: *Perilous* is swift, *short* is forceful—thus the momentum of battle should be swift and close.
Wang Zhe said: A startled bird’s strike succeeds because its release is precise, its incoming *shi* distant, yet the *jie* of its grasp is exceedingly short. Seizing the moment in warfare should be like this.
Cao Cao said: *Short* means near.
Meng’s commentary agrees with Du Mu’s.

Zhang Yu said: *Perilous* is swift; *short* is near. Those skilled in warfare first measure the distance and breadth of the terrain, then array their formations, ensuring ranks and files are not far apart. In advancing to strike, they take fifty paces as their *jie*, never exceeding it—thus their *shi* is swift and hard to resist, their *jie* near and easy to prevail. Their *shi* is like a drawn crossbow, their *jie* like the release of its trigger.

Cao Cao said: Within range, the shot will strike true.
Li Quan said: A crossbow must be swift to reach far; an arrow must be near to hit its mark. *Shi* values speed, *jie* demands swiftness.
Du Mu said: *Strong* means drawn. Like a crossbow drawn taut, once released, it kills—hence the earlier phrase *its shi is perilous*. The trigger must be measured by proximity to strike true—hence the earlier phrase *its jie is short*, for *short* means near. This warns that formations must not pursue the enemy too far, lest their ranks scatter and break, leaving them vulnerable to counterattack. Thus, the *Oath of Muye* says: *Six steps, seven steps; four strikes, five strikes*—this is the virtue of proximity.
Chen Hao said: A crossbow’s trigger, when released near, strikes easily; meeting the enemy swiftly brings quick victory. If advance is not swift or strike not near, one cannot overcome the foe and secure triumph.
Jia Lin said: The *shi* of battle is like a drawn crossbow; the *shi* of troops is like the release of its trigger.
Mei Yaochen said: *Strong* (pronounced *huò*) means drawn taut. Like a crossbow drawn, its *shi* brooks no delay; like the trigger released, its *jie* is near and strikes true.
Wang Shi said: The *shi* of battle is like a drawn crossbow—it awaits the moment. When an exploitable *shi* appears, it is like releasing the trigger.
He Shi said: *Perilous* is swift; *short* is near. This speaks of seizing the moment in battle, like a drawn crossbow releasing its trigger—*shi* should be swift, *jie* advantageous in proximity, and pursuit must not overreach. Thus, the *Tai Gong* said: *Strike like releasing a trigger*—this is how to shatter the enemy’s subtlety.
Zhang Yu said: Like a drawn crossbow, *shi* cannot be slow; like a released trigger, *jie* cannot be distant. This speaks of hastening toward advantage and valuing proximity in striking. Thus, the *Tai Gong* said: *Strike like releasing a trigger*—this is how to shatter the enemy’s subtlety.

Amidst the fluttering and swirling, the state is in chaos yet cannot be disordered;
In the murky and formless, the formation is round and cannot be broken.
Cao Cao said: The banners are in disarray—displaying chaos to the enemy while unifying with gongs and drums. Cavalry and infantry wheel about, their formation round, their entry and exit orderly.
Li Quan said: Though appearing disordered in their fluttering, they show readiness; with banners arrayed and gongs sounded in rhythm, they cannot be disordered. *Murky* means mixed; *round* means without front or rear. Though appearing vulnerable to defeat, they cannot be broken—this is due to the unity of commands.
Du Mu said: This speaks of formation tactics. The *Wòqí* text of the *Wind Duke* says: *Four as the regular, four as the奇 (unconventional), the remainder as the握 (reserve).* The *奇* (pronounced *jī*) may be collectively called the vanguard, first sent to secure both flanks—this is the method.
*Qi* means *remnant*.

There are nine formations in total, with the central *zero* held immovably by the commanding general to control the four directions and eight arrays, thereby establishing the standard. In the ranks of soldiers, each face aligns with another, and each back supports another.

The *Zhou Rituals* records the *Sōu* and *Miáo* grand hunts, where chariots galloped and infantry rushed forward, halting only upon reaching the *biao*—their advance and retreat, speed and slowness, sparsity and density all regulated as in battle formations. The *biao* is a banner, for banners are where the people pledge their rendezvous below.

The *Wòqí* text states: *The vanguard is first sent to secure both flanks.* This vanguard carries the standard of its own direction, first establishing the boundaries before the troops assemble beneath the banner. Only then do the *qi* (unconventional) and *zheng* (regular) forces emerge, transforming into formations.

The *Zhou Rituals*’ accounts of the *Sōu* and *Miáo* hunts—where chariots charged and infantry surged until reaching the banner—preserve the legacy of the eight arrays and the *Wòqí* method. Beyond this, all other passages were later additions by subsequent authors to amplify the difficulty of the matter.

Among the five weapons, none surpasses the efficacy of the bow and arrow in overawing the realm. The five weapons share governance, yet only the bow and arrow are represented by a celestial constellation. The sages uniquely declare that the bow and arrow alone can overawe the world—why do they not speak of the other weapons? Because the tactical advantage of the bow and arrow is realized only through formation. Thus, the Yellow Emperor triumphed over Chiyou by employing the chariots and infantry of the Central Plains to subdue the cavalry of the barbarian tribes—this was the power of the bow and arrow.

In more recent times, this can be verified: during the reign of Emperor Wu of Jin, the Qiang tribes overran Liangzhou. Marshal Ma Long requested to recruit three thousand valiant soldiers to pacify them. He enlisted those who could draw crossbows of thirty-six *jun* and bows of four *jun*, testing them with marked targets. As his army crossed the Wen River to the west, the rebel Tufa Shujineng, with tens of thousands of troops, blocked Ma Long’s path. Long adhered to the eight-array method, advancing while fighting. Wherever his bows and arrows struck, men fell at the twang of the string, and tens of thousands were slain, thereby pacifying Liangzhou.

During the Sui dynasty, the Göktürks invaded. Yang Su engaged them in battle. Previously, other generals, wary of the Göktürk cavalry’s charges, had interspersed war chariots with infantry, arranging them in square formations with *shenglu* and *yulin* shields, while cavalry remained within. When Yang Su arrived, he abolished these old methods, ordering each division to operate independently as infantry and cavalry. Upon hearing this, the Göktürks clapped their foreheads and looked to the heavens, exclaiming, “Heaven has granted them to us!” They swiftly amassed over a hundred thousand elite cavalry. Yang Su crushed them in a single decisive battle.

This is how infantry overcomes cavalry—without formation tactics and mastery of opening and closing, advance and retreat, how could one secure victory? As the *Qu Li* states: "March with the Vermilion Bird ahead and the Dark Warrior behind, the Azure Dragon to the left and the White Tiger to the right; the Dipper’s handle points above, swiftly kindling its wrath." Zheng Sinong explains: "The four beasts represent the army’s formation, mirroring the heavens." Kong’s commentary adds: "This speaks of military formations modeled on celestial patterns, though the specifics remain unclear." He Che elaborates: "The four beasts are painted on banners to mark the front, rear, left, and right of the formation. 'Swiftly kindling its wrath' describes the soldiers’ fierce and formidable might, like the anger of heaven. *Zhaoyao* refers to the seventh star of the Dipper’s handle; by naming it, the other six stars are implied. The formation mirrors the celestial patterns—specifically, the Northern Dipper."

It further states: "Advance and retreat follow measure." Zheng Sinong annotates: "Measure refers to strikes and step counts." Kong’s commentary cites the *Muye Oath*: "Six steps, seven steps; four strikes, five strikes." It continues: "Left and right have their divisions." Zheng Sinong explains: "Divisions refer to troop allocations." Kong’s commentary clarifies: "The army’s left and right flanks each have their sectors; when advancing, they engage the enemy; when retreating, they rejoin their ranks, never falling into disarray."

The text proceeds: "An enemy to one’s father is one with whom one cannot share the sky; an enemy to one’s brother is one against whom one does not sheathe the blade; a betrayal by a friend is one that bars them from the same land. When ramparts rise on all four borders, this is the shame of ministers and grandees." This speaks of enmity and disgrace escalating to war, where victory is the sole aim—hence the necessity of mastering formation tactics. The passage’s sequence reveals the profound intent of sages and worthies.

The *Military Records* state: "Within a formation, space allows for deployment; within a unit, space permits facing the enemy. The front guards the front, the rear holds the rear, the left defends the left, the right protects the right. In march, they move in single file; in stance, they align in ranks. The long supports the short, the short reinforces the long. When the army wheels and the formation shifts, the former front becomes the rear, the former rear becomes the front—advancing, there is no rout; retreating, no flight. With four heads and eight tails, wherever touched becomes the lead; if the enemy strikes the center, both ends rush to aid." This aligns with the *Qu Li*’s principles. The numbers begin at five and culminate in eight.

Today, before the Kuizhou prefectural hall, Zhuge Liang of the Shu Han arranged stones in eight intersecting rows to form a square formation. From this arose the interplay of *qi* (unconventional) and *zheng* (conventional) tactics—*qi* becomes the *zheng* of *zheng*, and *zheng* becomes the *qi* of *qi*, their mutual employment endless. When Zhuge emerged from Xie Valley with few troops, he relied solely on the *zheng* deployment of six divisions; remnants of his old ramparts still stand in the Zhengwu Sizhu Garden. Sima Yi, with a hundred thousand infantry and cavalry, dared not engage, for he recognized Zhuge’s skill.

Du You states: "Banners appear disordered; to the enemy, they seem chaotic, yet drums and gongs unify them. Fluttering banners mimic the swarming of soldiers. The banners twist and turn, now converging, now dispersing; troops advance and withdraw, now advancing, now retreating—appearing scattered, seemingly in disarray. Yet their commands are long established, duties clearly defined, each with their place; though stirred, they are not disordered. Chariots and cavalry turn in unison, their form circular, their movements orderly, their entry and exit disciplined. *Hunhun* describes the turning of chariot wheels; *dundun*, the galloping of infantry. Though their formation stretches and contracts, round yet not square, every maneuver has its response."

Thus, the Prince of Qi said: "To appear inwardly enlightened yet outwardly dim, inwardly ordered yet outwardly chaotic—this is how one shows the enemy their own contempt." Mei Yaochen remarked: "With divisions fixed and designations set, dispersion and assembly may seem chaotic, yet cannot be so. The formation lacks head or tail, responds without front or rear; it revolves and shifts, yet cannot be broken." Wang Zhe noted: "Cao Cao said: 'Banners appear disordered; to the enemy, they seem chaotic, yet drums and gongs unify them.' I say this describes their seemingly tangled, broken appearance. That they cannot be disordered stems from strict discipline." Cao Cao also said: "When chariots and cavalry turn, their form is round, their movements orderly, their entry and exit disciplined." Wang Zhe added: "The *hundun* (chaotic) roundness is unfathomable; that it cannot be broken arises from its seamless unity and unpredictability."

He Shi observed: "This speaks of a state’s power. A skilled commander’s troops may seem to advance and retreat in disarray, yet men and horses are well-trained, banners follow rhythm—this is no disorder. The *hundun* form shifts between separation and union; though appearing broken, commands remain clear, cohesion intact—it cannot be defeated. The round form lacks fixed ranks."

Zhang Yu stated: "This is the eight-formation method. Long ago, the Yellow Emperor established the well-field system and thereby regulated troops. Thus, the well divides into four paths, with eight families residing within. The well’s shape forms nine squares; five serve as formation, four as open ground—hence the saying, 'numbers begin at five.' The center remains empty, where the commander resides, encircled by the four sides, each unit connected—hence 'culminating in eight.' When adapting to enemy changes, troops disperse and gather in seeming chaos, yet the method remains unbroken; though the form is round, the force does not scatter—this is 'dividing into eight, reuniting as one.' Later generations, from Zhuge Liang’s square formation to Li Jing’s six-petal array and Emperor Taizong of Tang’s *Breaking Formation Dance*, all inherited this tradition."

"Disorder arises from order, cowardice from courage, weakness from strength." Cao Cao said: "All involve concealing form and masking intent." Li Quan added: "Relying on strict order yet neglecting to soothe subordinates breeds resentment—disorder will arise. When Qin unified the realm, it melted weapons and burned books, converting states into commanderies and counties. Yet Qin declared itself the First Emperor, entrenched in Guanzhong, believing its might would endure for ten thousand generations."

Huhai’s arrogance and extravagance allowed Chen Sheng and Wu Guang to seize the moment of decay and rise in rebellion—this is "disorder arising from order." Defeated by the enemy.

King Fu Jian of Qin marched his armies to attack Jin—this was courage; yet upon his defeat, the rustling of wind and the cries of cranes made him mistake them for Jin forces—this was cowardice, "cowardice arising from courage."

King Fuchai of Wu’s troops were unmatched under heaven at Huangchi, and he humiliated Yue at Kuaiji—this was strength; yet later, his city gates lay unguarded, his palace besieged by soldiers, Fuchai slain, and his kingdom annexed—this is "weakness arising from strength."

Du Mu said: To feign disorder to lure the enemy, one must first attain perfect order, then one may simulate disorder. To feign cowardice to deceive the enemy, one must first attain perfect courage, then one may simulate cowardice. To feign weakness to provoke the enemy, one must first attain perfect strength, then one may simulate weakness.

Jia Lin said: Relying on order breeds disorder; relying on courage and strength breeds cowardice and weakness.

Mei Yaochen said: With order, one may feign disorder; with courage, one may feign cowardice; with strength, one may feign weakness.

Wang Zhe echoed Mei Yaochen’s annotation.

He Shi said: This speaks of employing the dynamics of the extraordinary and the orthodox in battle to break the enemy. If our troops are inherently ordered, our warriors inherently courageous, our momentum inherently strong, how can we entice the enemy without concealing our order, courage, and strength? We must display the semblance of chaos, cowardice, and weakness to lure the enemy. Once they are deluded by our deceptive guise, their defeat is assured.

Zhang Yu said: To show the enemy confusion is to conceal one’s order; to show the enemy timidity is to conceal one’s courage; to show the enemy frailty is to conceal one’s strength. All involve masking one’s true form to mislead the enemy.

Order and disorder stem from *shu* (numerical organization).
Cao Cao said: This refers to organizing troops by divisions and ranks to prevent disorder.
Li Quan said: It refers to the cyclical reckoning of time. The calamities of the "hundred and six" arise from the numerical patterns of yin and yang, not from human action, but from the convergence of the times.
Du Mu said: Troops must have clear divisions and ranks, with each unit assigned its place and number. Only then can order be achieved, and only then can one feign disorder. Feigning disorder involves irregular movements, scattered foraging parties, and lax guard shifts.
Jia Lin said: The distinction between order and disorder lies in their respective measures.
Mei Yaochen said: To feign disorder while maintaining order depends on numerical organization.
Wang Zhe said: Order and disorder are transformations of *shu*, where *shu* refers to systems and regulations.
Zhang Yu said: To truly be ordered yet feign disorder is to rely on the numerical organization of troops and ranks. As stated earlier, "governing the many as one governs the few" depends on *shu* (numerical organization).

Courage and cowardice arise from *shi* (momentum).
Li Quan said: When troops seize the advantage of *shi*, even the cowardly become brave; when they lose it, even the brave become cowardly. There is no fixed method in warfare—success depends on adapting to *shi*.
Du Mu said: This speaks of feigning cowardice while possessing courage. By remaining motionless when presented with a favorable opportunity, the enemy will perceive us as truly cowardly.
Chen Hao said: Courage is swift and vigorous; cowardice is sluggish and hesitant. If the enemy sees us hesitate to advance, they will deem us cowardly, growing careless. We may then exploit their complacency and strike with the advantage of *shi*. Examples include Long Ju underestimating Han Xin and the people of Zheng deceiving our forces.
Meng Shi’s annotation aligns with Chen Hao’s.
Mei Yaochen said: To feign cowardice while possessing courage is to display no intent to engage.
Wang Zhe said: Courage and cowardice are transformations of *shi*.
Zhang Yu said: To truly be courageous yet feign cowardice is to leverage *shi*. When the Wei general Pang Juan attacked Han, the Qi general Tian Ji came to its aid. Sun Bin told Ji: "The troops of the Three Jin are fierce and brave, and they scorn Qi, calling us cowardly. A skilled commander exploits such *shi* to his advantage." Thus, the Qi army entered Wei territory, reducing its encampments daily. Upon hearing this, Pang Juan rejoiced, saying, "I always knew the Qi were cowards!" He pursued them with double-speed marches and was defeated at Maling.

Strength and weakness arise from *xing* (form).
Cao Cao said: This depends on the appropriateness of form and momentum.
Du Mu said: To feign weakness while possessing strength, one must display the proper form. The Xiongnu chanyu Modu deceived Lou Jing by appearing feeble and aged.
Chen Hao said: King Zhuang of Chu disbanded his central army to embolden the enemy, planning for a later strike—this is another example.
Mei Yaochen said: To feign weakness while possessing strength is to display frailty and timidity.
Wang Zhe said: Strength and weakness are transformations of *xing*.
He Shi said: The form and momentum are temporarily altered to provoke the enemy into battle—this is neither true cowardice nor true weakness. Feigning disorder while remaining ordered means the ranks are fundamentally disciplined.
Zhang Yu said: To truly be strong yet feign weakness is to reveal one’s form. When Emperor Gaozu of Han sought to attack the Xiongnu, he sent envoys to scout them. The Xiongnu concealed their strong warriors and sturdy horses, displaying only weak soldiers and emaciated livestock. Ten envoys reported that the Xiongnu could be attacked. Only Lou Jing said: "When two states clash, each should flaunt its strengths. Now, all we see are the old and weak—there must be hidden forces. We cannot attack." The emperor ignored him and was indeed besieged at Baideng.

Thus, one who excels at moving the enemy presents a *xing* (form), and the enemy will invariably follow it.
Cao Cao said: This means displaying a feeble form.
Li Quan said: One who excels at luring the enemy may command a strong army yet control the enemy’s advances and retreats. When the Jin attacked Qi, they scouted the mountains and marshes, even where they did not venture, yet they adorned and spread out their formations, dragging branches to raise dust. The Qi forces observed from the mountains and, seeing the banners and dust, believed the Jin army was vast. They fled under cover of night—thus, Jin feigned weakness to make Qi appear strong. When Qi attacked Wei, General Tian Ji followed Sun Bin’s strategy, reducing their encampments daily as they advanced toward Daliang. The Wei general Pang Juan pursued them, saying, "How cowardly the Qi and Lu are!"

Half had perished upon entering our borders. At Maling, they were defeated by the Qi forces, where Pang Juan was slain and the Crown Prince of Wei captured before they retreated. This was feigning weakness to make the enemy follow.

Du Mu said: It is not merely about appearing feeble. If I am strong and the enemy weak, I display a weak form to lure them; if I am weak and the enemy strong, I display a strong form to drive them away. The enemy’s movements must all follow my design.

Sun Bin said: Qi had a reputation for cowardice, and the Three Jin states belittled them. Upon entering Wei territory, they set up campfires for one hundred thousand troops, then the next day for fifty thousand. Pang Juan pursued them, saying, "How timid the Qi are!" By the time they entered our land, over half their forces had perished. Spurred on, he pressed the chase until they reached Maling, where the path narrowed. Sun Bin had trees felled and inscribed: "Pang Juan shall die beneath this tree." He laid an ambush with crossbows, ordering, "Loose only when you see the fire." When Pang Juan arrived, he struck flint to read the inscription—ten thousand crossbows fired at once, and Pang Juan was slain. This was displaying a weak form to move Pang Juan, drawing him to follow and be destroyed.

Emperor Yang of Sui was besieged at Mamen by Shibi Khan of the Türks. Prince Taizong volunteered to relieve him, serving under General Yun Dingxing. Before departing, he said to Dingxing: "Display many banners and drums to create the illusion of a vast army. Shibi Khan dares besiege the Son of Heaven only because he believes we have no reinforcements. Let us expand our military presence—by day, let banners stretch unbroken for tens of *li*; by night, let drums and torches echo one another. The Türks will think our reinforcements have arrived, and seeing the dust, they will flee. Otherwise, outnumbered as we are, we cannot hold for long." Dingxing followed his advice. When the army reached Yong County, Shibi Khan retreated. Here, though weak against a strong enemy, we displayed strength to drive them away. Thus, whether the enemy comes or goes, all follows the form I present.

Mei Yaochen said: Display disorder and weakness to make them follow.
Wang Zhe said: Lure the enemy to follow.
He Shi said: Alter form and shift momentum to entice the enemy into action. If the enemy is blind to the ruse, they will fall into my trap and come—then my strength will be sufficient to subdue them.
Zhang Yu said: Display a weak form, and the enemy will follow. When Jin and Chu clashed, Miao Zihuang advised the Marquis of Jin: "If you make Fan and Yi appear easy to attack, the two Zhongs of the Central Army will surely defeat the two Mus." Indeed, they routed the Chu forces. Later, when Chu invaded Sui, they feigned a weak formation. Ji Liang said, "Chu’s weakness is a lure." Both instances exemplify these two principles.

Offer it, and the enemy will surely take it.
Cao Cao said: Use profit to entice the enemy, drawing them far from their fortifications to strike their exposed and isolated forces.
Du Mu said: When Cao Cao and Yuan Shao clashed at Guandu, Cao moved west along the Yellow River, and Yuan crossed to pursue him. Cao camped at Nanban, dismounting and removing his saddle. As his supply train set out, his generals, seeing the enemy cavalry’s numbers, urged retreat. Xun Yu said, "This is bait—how can we leave?" Yuan’s general Wen Chou and Liu Bei advanced with five or six thousand cavalry, some heading for the supplies. Cao said, "Now is the time." They mounted—though their cavalry numbered fewer than six hundred—and crushed the enemy, slaying Wen Chou.
Mei Yaochen said: Display fear and cowardice to make them take the bait.
Wang Zhe said: Entice the enemy to take it.
*Yu* and *yu* are interchangeable.
Zhang Yu said: Lure them with small gains, and the enemy will come to take it. Wu used prisoners to bait Yue; Chu used firewood gatherers to bait Jiao—both are examples.

Move them with profit, and await them with troops.
Cao Cao said: Use profit to move the enemy.
Li Quan said: When the Grand Marshal Deng Yu of the Later Han attacked the Red Eyebrows, they feigned retreat, abandoning their supplies. Their carts were loaded with earth, covered with beans. Deng Yu’s army, starving, rushed to seize them, breaking formation. The Red Eyebrows’ ambush struck, and Deng Yu was routed—this illustrates the principle.
Du Mu said: Move the enemy with profit, and once they follow, prepare your troops to await them. The preceding explanation covers this.
Mei Yaochen said: In all these cases, entice the enemy to follow, then await them with elite troops.
Wang Zhe said: Whether making them follow or take the bait, first prepare your troops to await them.
He Shi said: If the enemy values what I offer, they will abandon formation. Once profit moves them, strike with your waiting troops—victory is assured. When Cao Cao campaigned west against Ma Chao, they faced each other across the Pass. Cao feigned urgency while secretly dispatching Xu Huang and Zhu Ling to cross the Puqi Ford by night and establish a camp west of the river. As Cao crossed north from Tong Pass, Ma Chao rushed the boats in a fierce battle. Cao released oxen and horses to bait the enemy. The rebels scattered to seize them, allowing Cao to cross. He followed the river, building a walled road south. The rebels withdrew to the Wei River mouth. Cao then set up numerous decoy troops while secretly ferrying soldiers into the Wei by boat to build a floating bridge. At night, he divided his forces to encamp south of the river. The rebels attacked the camp at night, but the ambush struck, breaking them.

During the Sixteen Kingdoms period, Tufa Rutan of Southern Liang defended Guzang. Yao Xing of Later Qin sent General Yao Bi to the city walls. Rutan drove oxen and sheep into the wilds; Yao Bi’s troops plundered them, and Rutan’s forces struck, crushing them. In the late Northern Wei, the Grand General Guangyang Prince Yuan Shen campaigned against the Northern Di. He sent Yu Jin alone into enemy territory to display kindness and good faith. Thus, the chieftains of the Western Tiele, including Qiliehe and others—over thirty thousand households—all submitted and migrated south together. Guangyang intended to meet Yu Jin at Zhefushan Ridge to welcome them. Yu said, "Poluohan Baling’s forces are not few. Hearing of Qiliehe’s submission, they will surely intercept them."

If they seize the strategic passes first, it will be difficult to contend with them. Now, using Poluohan and others as bait, they will surely vie to plunder. Then, we set an ambush and wait—defeating them will be as easy as pointing to our palm. Guangyang approved his plan. Baling indeed came to intercept, crushing Qiliehe’s forces on the ridge, and his entire army was annihilated. The hidden troops sprang forth, and the enemy was utterly routed, with all of Qiliehe’s followers recovered.

Zhang Yu said: When the form is already compliant, and you further entice them, you move them with profit so they come—then await them with elite troops. Li Jing regarded soldiers as the foundation; awaiting them with the foundation means employing disciplined regular forces.

Thus, the skilled warrior seeks victory through strategic advantage, not by demanding it of men. Du You said: The way of victory or defeat lies in self-preparation, not in blaming subordinates or forcing them to advance through anger. Like Duke Mu of Qin, who repented his error and did not dismiss Meng Ming.

Therefore, one must select men and leverage the strategic advantage. (An alternate version reads: *Therefore, one must select men and employ them.*) Many commentators adopt the "leverage the strategic advantage" reading.

Cao Cao said: Seeking victory through strategic advantage means relying entirely on adaptability. Not demanding it of men means clarity in tactical flexibility.

Li Quan said: When fighting with strategic advantage, even the timid can be brave. Thus, one must select those capable of bearing the task. The brave may be used for battle, the cautious for defense, the wise for persuasion—nothing is to be discarded.

Du Mu said: The skilled warrior first assesses the military situation, then evaluates men’s abilities, assigning tasks according to their strengths and weaknesses, without demanding the impossible of those unsuited.

When Cao Cao campaigned against Zhang Lu in Hanzhong, he left Zhang Liao, Li Dian, and Yue Jin with over a thousand men to guard Hefei. He entrusted a sealed order to Protector-General Xue Ti, with instructions to open it only upon the enemy’s arrival. Soon after, Sun Quan of Wu besieged Hefei with a hundred thousand troops. The generals opened the order, which read: *If Sun Quan arrives, Generals Zhang and Li will sortie, General Yue will hold the city, and the Protector-General must not engage in battle.* The commanders were doubtful. Zhang Liao said: "The Lord is campaigning afar. By the time reinforcements arrive, they will have already broken us. The order directs us to strike before they consolidate, breaking their momentum to steady the men’s hearts. Then we can hold. The pivot of victory or defeat lies in this single action." Li Dian joined Zhang Liao in the sortie, and they crushed Sun Quan’s forces, sapping the morale of the Wu troops. Upon returning, they reinforced the defenses, and the men’s hearts were settled. Sun Quan besieged the city for ten days without success, then withdrew.

Sun Sheng commented: War is a deceptive art. In the defense of Hefei, with scant forces and no aid, relying solely on the brave would invite reckless conflict, while relying solely on the timid would make fear difficult to overcome. Moreover, their numbers were vast, and the many are prone to complacency and indolence. We, with a determined force, strike at their indolent troops—our position is superior. Victory secured, we then hold, and our defenses become unassailable. Thus, the Martial Lord of Wei selected warriors of diverse temperaments, blending their differences, issuing secret instructions, and regulating their deployment. When the moment came, they responded as if by preordained agreement.

Jia Lin said: The reading is *select men and leverage the strategic advantage*, meaning one presents an irresistible momentum to compel obedience, rather than externally demanding victory or defeat from men. Select men of courage or caution and assign them roles of advance or retreat.

Chen Hao said: The skilled warrior focuses entirely on strategic advantage, seizing opportunities swiftly, not allowing the enemy to act first. He relies entirely on tactical adaptability, not demanding success from men. If compelled to employ men, he must select and assign them appropriately.

Du You said: Clarity in adaptability means placing men according to one’s own strategic advantage.

Mei Yaochen said: Employing men through strategic advantage is easy; demanding effort from them is difficult. The capable lie in selecting men and leveraging strategic advantage.

He Shi said: Gaining strategic advantage ensures victory without solely demanding effort from men.

Wang Xi said: If a general can select men and leverage strategic advantage in battle, victory comes naturally. (Does "men" here refer to subordinates?)

Zhang Yu said: The method of employing men: use the cautious, the foolish, the wise, and the brave, each according to their natural strategic advantage, without demanding what they cannot do. Thus, one selects and assigns them according to their abilities.

The *Wei Liaozi* said: Utilize their strengths. Among the three armies, some excel in infantry, others in cavalry—employ them according to their abilities, and each will fully display their strengths. This is like the Marquis of Jin, who assessed abilities and assigned tasks accordingly.

Leveraging strategic advantage in battle is like rolling logs and stones: logs and stones are naturally still when stable, move when precarious, stop when square, and roll when round.

Cao Cao said: It means relying on natural strategic advantage.

Li Quan said: Commanding troops through strategic advantage should be like this.

Du You said: Placed on stable ground, they are stable; placed on perilous ground, they are perilous—without knowing to avoid it. Leveraging strategic advantage is natural. The shapes of square and round resemble the forms of military victory or defeat.

Mei Yaochen said: Logs and stones are heavy objects, easily moved by strategic advantage but difficult to shift by force. The three armies are vast, suited for battle through strategic advantage, not through forced effort—this is the natural Way.

He Shi’s note agrees with Mei Yaochen’s.

Zhang Yu said: The nature of logs and stones is such that placed on stable ground, they are still; placed on perilous ground, they move; square, they stop; round, they roll—this is natural strategic advantage. The three armies, when deeply committed, fear nothing; when without recourse, they hold firm; when compelled, they fight—this, too, is the natural Way. Thus, the skilled warrior’s strategic advantage is like rolling a round stone from a thousand-*ren* mountain—it is the strategic advantage. (Li Quan said: The *Shuowen* interprets *ju* as a steep slope, like a ball rolling downhill—speaking of its ease.)

Du Mu said: Rolling a stone from a thousand-*ren* mountain cannot be stopped—this lies in the mountain, not the stone.

The warrior who possesses the courage of a hundred victories, where strength and weakness become one, lies in strategic advantage, not in the individual.

Du Yuankai said: In the past, Yue Yi relied on the single battle of Jixi to conquer the mighty state of Qi. Now, with military might already established, it is like splitting bamboo—after the first few joints, the rest yield effortlessly, requiring no further effort. This is strategic advantage. Such advantage must not be lost. Thus, he advanced to Jianye and ultimately destroyed Wu.

This chapter largely speaks of the importance of relying on strategic advantage in warfare, taking perilous swiftness and speed as its foundation, thereby achieving great success with minimal effort.

Mei Yaochen said: A round stone on a mountain stands firm in its strategic advantage; though one man may push it, a thousand cannot restrain it.

Wang Zhe said: The stone cannot turn itself—it is the mountain’s strategic advantage that makes it unstoppable. Victory in battle cannot be recklessly achieved—it is the army’s strategic advantage that makes it irresistible.

Zhang Yu said: The stone rolling down the mountain cannot be stopped because strategic advantage compels it. An army positioned in peril cannot be controlled, for strategic advantage compels it as well.

Li Jing said: Warfare has three strategic advantages: When the general scorns the enemy, the soldiers delight in battle, their aspirations soar like clouds, and their spirit is as swift as the whirlwind—this is *qi* strategic advantage. Where narrow passes, winding paths, and constricted gates allow one man to hold off a thousand—this is terrain strategic advantage. When the enemy is lax, weary, hungry, or thirsty, when their vanguard has not yet encamped and their rearguard is only halfway across—this is *yin* strategic advantage. Thus, employing strategic advantage in warfare is like a ball rolling down a steep slope: the effort is minimal, yet the success is great.

*End of Commentary on Sunzi, Scroll Five*
*Commentary on Sunzi, Scroll Six*

**On Emptiness and Fullness**

Cao Cao said: To discern emptiness and fullness in both enemy and self.
Li Quan said: The adept in warfare makes emptiness appear full; the adept in breaking the enemy makes fullness appear empty. Thus follows this chapter.
Du Mu said: In warfare, one avoids fullness and strikes emptiness—first one must recognize the emptiness and fullness of both enemy and self.
Wang Zhe said: In defense, one guards with fullness; in attack, one strikes with emptiness.
Zhang Yu said: The *Form* chapter speaks of attack and defense; the *Strategic Advantage* chapter discusses the orthodox and the extraordinary. The adept in warfare first understands the methods of balancing attack and defense, then comprehends the orthodox and the extraordinary; first grasps the mutual transformation of the orthodox and the extraordinary, then perceives emptiness and fullness. For the orthodox and the extraordinary arise from attack and defense, while emptiness and fullness emerge from the orthodox and the extraordinary. Thus follows *Strategic Advantage*.

Sunzi said: Generally, he who first occupies the battlefield and awaits the enemy is at ease.
Cao Cao and Li Quan both said: His strength is abundant.
Jia Lin said: He who first occupies a strategically advantageous position to await the enemy is well-prepared and at ease, his men and horses rested.
Du You concurs with Jia Lin’s annotation.
Wang Shi concurs with Cao Cao’s annotation.

Zhang Yu said: In terrain of strategic advantage, if I first occupy it to await the enemy’s arrival, then men and horses remain at ease, and strength is abundant. He who arrives later at the battlefield and rushes into battle is wearied.

Li Quan said: His strength is insufficient.

The *Taiyi Dunjia* states: When the enemy comes to attack me, I am the host, and he is the guest. The host has the advantage, the guest the difficulty. Thus the *Taiyi Dunjia* speaks of the principle of determining strategy. Therefore, the conditions of toil and ease differ, and the strategic advantages of first and last are not the same.

Du Mu said: Later, the Zhou dispatched generals and Turkic forces to press the Qi, and Qi’s general Duan Shao resisted them. After a great snowfall, the Zhou forces advanced with infantry as their vanguard, descending from the west two *li* from the city. The Qi generals wished to meet them in counterattack. Shao said: “The strength and momentum of infantry are inherently limited. Now that the snow lies thick, counterattack is not advantageous. It is better to array our forces and await them. They will be wearied, we at ease—we shall surely break them.” When battle was joined, they crushed the enemy, annihilating the vanguard, and the rest fled.

Jia Lin said: If the enemy occupies advantageous terrain, we should not advance, but instead lead our troops to occupy another position, showing that we dare not engage their forces. The enemy, believing us to be without strategy, will then come to attack. In this way, we reverse their fatigue and remain unwearied.

Meng Shi said: If the enemy has already secured a strategically advantageous position while we hasten toward profit, our men and horses will be wearied and exhausted—this is disadvantageous.

Mei Yaochen said: Arriving first to await the enemy preserves one’s strength; arriving late to rush into battle exhausts it.

He Shi said: During the Warring States period, the Qin army attacked Han and besieged Banyu. Zhao dispatched General Zhao She to relieve it. A soldier named Xu Li said: “The Qin did not expect the Zhao army to arrive here so soon. Their momentum is fierce. You, General, should thicken your ranks to await them; otherwise, defeat is certain.” He added: “Whoever first occupies the northern mountain will win; whoever arrives later will be defeated.” Zhao She immediately dispatched ten thousand men to rush there. The Qin forces arrived later and, unable to seize the mountain, contended for the heights. Zhao She unleashed his troops and struck, crushing the Qin army and lifting the siege of Banyu.

In the early years of the Later Han, various generals campaigned against Wei Ao but were defeated by him. Emperor Guangwu ordered his entire army to advance toward Gouyi. Before they arrived, Wei Ao, emboldened by victory, sent his general Wang Yuan and Xing Xun with over twenty thousand men down from the Long region, dispatching Xun to seize Gouyi. The Han general Feng Yi galloped ahead, intending to occupy it first. The other generals all said: “The enemy’s forces are strong and newly triumphant; we cannot contend with them. It is better to halt our army here and slowly devise a strategy.” Feng Yi replied: “The enemy, now at their peak, presses close to our borders, seizing small gains and thus advancing deeply. If they take Gouyi, the Three Auxiliaries will be shaken—this is my concern. The attacker is insufficient; the defender is abundant. Now, if we first occupy the city to await the weary with ease, this is not how one contends for advantage.” He then stealthily advanced, shut the gates, and stilled the drums and banners. Xing Xun, unaware, rushed toward the city. Feng Yi, seizing the moment of surprise, suddenly struck the drums, raised the banners, and sallied forth. The Xun’s army, thrown into chaos, fled in panic, and he pursued and crushed them.

During the Eastern Wei, General Qi Wushuang campaigned against the Western Wei, marching past Putong, fording the Luo River, and reaching Xuyuan. The Western Wei general Yuwen Tai advanced his army to Shayuan. Hearing of Yuwen Tai’s arrival, Qi Wushuang led his forces to meet him. The cavalry scout of the next morning reported that Qi Wushuang’s army was approaching. Yuwen Tai’s infantry general Li Bi said: “Their numbers exceed ours; we cannot array our forces on level ground. Ten *li* to the east lies a bend in the Wei River where we may first occupy a position to await them.” The army then advanced to the Wei River bend, arraying their forces with their backs to the water, facing east and west. When battle was joined, they crushed the enemy.

Zhang Yu said: If the enemy has already occupied advantageous terrain and we hasten toward them to fight, our men and horses will be wearied and our strength insufficient. Some say that in the terrain of battle, we should arrive first, array our forces, and await the enemy—thus we remain at ease. If the enemy has already formed ranks and we arrive later, we will be wearied. This is like the Song forces already in formation while the Chu army had not yet finished crossing. Therefore, the skilled warrior draws the enemy to himself and is not drawn by the enemy.

Li Quan said: Thus, one can exploit the enemy’s fatigue and avoid being lured into ease.

Du Mu said: Induce the enemy to come to you. You should conserve your strength to await them, not go to the enemy, lest you become weary.

In the Later Han, Zhang Bu’s general Fei Yi dispatched his brother Fei Gan to guard Juli. Geng Yan advanced his troops and first threatened Juli, ordering the felling of many trees and declaring an intention to fill the moats and trenches. After several days, a deserter reported that Fei Yi, hearing of this, planned to come to Juli’s aid. Geng Yan then strictly ordered his army to hasten the preparation of siege equipment, publicly commanding all units that after three days they would exert all their strength to attack Juli. Secretly, he allowed the deserter to escape. The deserter informed Fei Yi of Geng Yan’s deadline. On the appointed day, Fei Yi indeed led over thirty thousand elite troops to relieve Juli. Geng Yan, delighted, said to his generals: “I prepared siege equipment to lure Fei Yi here. Now that he has come, it is exactly what I sought.” He immediately detached three thousand men to guard Juli, then led his elite troops up the hillside, seizing the heights and crushing the enemy. He then advanced to the battlefield and slew Fei Yi.

Du You said: When two armies are distant and evenly matched in strength and weakness, you may cause the enemy to traverse perilous terrain to come to you, but you should not traverse perilous terrain to go to them. You must be able to draw the enemy to you without going to them.

Mei Yaochen said: If you can make the enemy come, the enemy will be weary; if you do not go to them, you remain at ease.

Wang Zhe said: To draw the enemy is to strike the weary with ease; to be drawn by the enemy is to strike the at-ease with weariness.

He Shi said: Cause the enemy to come of their own accord.

Zhang Yu said: If you draw the enemy to battle, their strength will always be depleted; if you do not go to battle, your strength will always be full. This is the expansion of the mutual depletion and fullness of the enemy and ourselves. Geng Yan first threatened Juli to lure Fei Yi to approach. To make the enemy come of their own accord is to entice them with profit.

Cao Cao said: Entice them with profit.

Li Quan said: Entice them with profit, and the enemy will come from afar.

The Zhao general Li Mu lured the Xiongnu—this was his meaning.

Du Mu said: Li Mu greatly expanded his herds and pastures, filling the fields with people. When the Xiongnu made a minor incursion, he feigned defeat and retreated, abandoning several thousand men to them. The Chanyu, greatly pleased, led his forces to invade. Li Mu crushed them, killing one hundred thousand Xiongnu cavalry. The Chanyu fled, and for over a year dared not approach the border.

Mei Yaochen said: How can they be made to come of their own accord? Show them profit.

He Shi said: Entice them with profit to come, so that we rest while the enemy toils.

Zhang Yu said: The reason we can induce the enemy to come is simply by enticing them with profit. Li Mu feigned retreat to lure the Xiongnu, and Yang Su destroyed his chariots to entice the Göktürks—these are examples.

To prevent the enemy from coming is to harm them.

Cao Cao said: Attack where they must rush, strike where they must rescue.

Li Quan said: Harm what they urgently need, and they will abandon their pursuit of us to secure themselves.

When the Wei forces invaded Zhao’s Handan and Ye, Zhao begged Qi for aid. The Qi general Tian Ji wished to rescue Zhao, but Sun Bin said: “One who unravels a tangle does not grasp the coils; one who rescues a state does not grapple at close quarters. Strike at their vital points and exploit their weaknesses, and the situation will resolve itself. Now that the two states are locked in combat, their finest troops are exhausted abroad while their infirm and elderly languish within. If we strike at their weakness, they will abandon their siege and rush to defend, which is what we mean by ‘in one move preserving Zhao and exhausting Wei.’” Later, the Wei forces indeed abandoned Zhao and hastened to Daliang, where they encountered the Qi army at Maling and suffered a crushing defeat.

Du Mu said: When Cao Cao attacked Hebei, his army halted at Dunqiu while the Black Mountain bandit Yu Du attacked Wuyang. Cao Cao then led his troops west into the mountains to strike Yu Du’s main camp. Hearing this, Yu Du abandoned Wuyang and turned back, only for Cao Cao to intercept and decisively defeat him.

Chen Hao said: This is like Wu Zixu exhausting the Chu forces or Sun Bin outmaneuvering the Wei general.

Du You said: Compel them to run where they must, attack where they must rescue, and hold the critical choke points of peril—then the enemy cannot come of their own accord. Thus the saying of the King’s Son: “One cat at the burrow, and ten thousand mice dare not emerge; one tiger at the ford, and ten thousand deer dare not cross.”

Mei Yaochen said: To prevent the enemy from coming, one must restrain them with harm.

Wang Zhe said: Present them with harm, and the enemy, fearing it, will not approach.

Zhang Yu said: The reason we can prevent the enemy from coming is by harming what they cherish. Sun Bin’s direct march on Daliang to relieve the siege of Handan and Ye is an example.

Thus, if the enemy is at rest, we can weary them.

Cao Cao said: Burden them with tasks.

Li Quan said: Attack where they least expect, forcing the enemy to exhaust themselves in hasty maneuvers.

Du Mu said: Gao Lei presented his strategy for pacifying Chen to Emperor Wen of Sui: “The land north of the Yangtze is cold, and its harvests come late; south of the river, the soil is warm, and the water fields ripen early. Time your mobilization to coincide with their harvest season, and raise the alarm of an impending raid. They will muster troops to defend, thus losing their farming season. Once they have gathered their forces, we will stand down. This will begin to afflict the Chen.”

Mei Yaochen said: Harass them so they cannot rest.

Wang Zhe said: Outmaneuver them with skill.

He Shi said: During the Spring and Autumn period, King Helü of Wu asked Wu Zixu: “How should we attack Chu?” Wu Zixu replied: “Chu’s government is overstaffed, with no one willing to rest. Deploy three armies to harass them: when one arrives, they will all sally forth; when that one withdraws, another will advance. As they rush back and forth, they will wear themselves out. Harass them repeatedly to exhaust them, confuse them with multiple feints, and once they are spent, follow with the full force of the three armies—then you will surely prevail.” Helü followed his advice, and Chu began to falter. Wu then entered Ying.

Zhang Yu said: This is the method of “confusing them with multiple feints” to prevent them from resting.

Some say: If the enemy occupies the battlefield first and awaits us, then they are at rest, and we should not engage them. If we do not advance, they will come to us, thus turning their rest into toil.

If the enemy is well-fed, we can starve them.

Cao Cao said: Cut off their supply lines to starve them.

Li Quan said: Burn their stores, scorch their crops, and sever their supply routes.

Du Mu said: If we are the defenders and the enemy the invaders, we can cut off their supply lines to starve them. But if we are the invaders and they the defenders, how should we proceed? Answer: Starving the enemy is not limited to cutting supply lines—any means that achieves this end suffices.

Gao Lei’s strategy for pacifying Chen stated: “The soil south of the Yangtze is poor, and dwellings are mostly thatched with bamboo. Their stores are not secure. Secretly dispatch men to set fires when the wind rises, and once the enemy rebuilds, burn them again. Within a few years, their resources and strength will be depleted.” This strategy was implemented, and the Chen grew increasingly distressed.

During the Three Kingdoms period, when Zhuge Dan and Wen Qin occupied Shouchun and sought aid from Wu, Sima Jingwang campaigned against them. He told his generals: “They may attempt to break the siege, staking everything on a single morning’s battle, or they may believe our army cannot endure long, reducing rations to await developments. I judge their intentions to lie in one of these two. We must confuse them with multiple feints.” He then ordered the encirclement tightened and sent emaciated troops to forage north of the Huai, each soldier carrying only three sheng of beans. When Dan and Qin heard this, they rejoiced. Jingwang further exaggerated his army’s weakness, and Dan and his allies grew complacent in their consumption. Soon the city’s grain ran out, and it was taken by storm.

At the end of the Sui dynasty, Yuwen Huaji led his forces to attack Li Mi at Liyang. Knowing Huaji’s supplies were scant, Li Mi feigned a truce to exhaust his troops. Huaji was overjoyed, allowing his soldiers to consume freely in hopes that Li Mi would provide. When their provisions were exhausted, his generals Wang Zhilüe and Zhang Tongren led their troops to defect to Li Mi, one after another. Huaji was thus defeated.

Chen Hao said: Starving the enemy depends on seizing opportunities as they arise.

Mei Yaochen said: Cut off their supplies so they cannot sustain themselves.

Wang Zhe said: If the enemy is well-provisioned, we must render them hungry and depleted.

Cao Cao said: Sever their supply lines.

Wang Zhe added: Burning their stores also achieves this.

He Shi said: When Wu and Chu rebelled, Zhou Yafu said: “The Chu troops are swift and impetuous.”

It is difficult to contend with them at their peak; it is better to yield Liang to them, cut off their supply lines, and thus bring them under control.

When Zhou Yafu mustered his troops at Rongyang, Wu attacked Liang, and Liang urgently requested reinforcements. Yafu led his army northeast to Changyi, fortified his camp, and sent light cavalry under the Marquis of Gonggao to sever the Wu and Chu forces’ supply lines. Deprived of provisions, the enemy grew hungry and sought to retreat. Though they repeatedly challenged Yafu to battle, he refused to engage, and they eventually withdrew. Yafu then dispatched his elite troops in pursuit and crushed them.

At the end of Wang Mang’s reign, when the realm was in chaos, Emperor Guangwu’s elder brother, Bo Sheng, raised troops to punish Mang but was defeated by Mang’s generals Zhen Fu and Liangqiu Ci. Regathering his forces, he retreated to Jiyang for defense. Fu and Ci, emboldened by victory, left their baggage train at Lanxiang and led over a hundred thousand elite troops south across the river, encamping between two mountains with their backs to the Bishui. They destroyed the bridge behind them, signaling their resolve to fight to the death. Bo Sheng then feasted his soldiers, swore oaths, and rested his troops for three days. Dividing his army into six divisions, he launched a night attack on Lanxiang, seizing all their supplies. At dawn, he attacked Zhen Fu from the south while the Xiajiang troops struck Liangqiu Ci from the southeast. Deprived of food and trapped in disarray, they slew Fu and Ci.

During the Tang, the rebel Fu Gongyou dispatched his false generals Feng Huiliang and Chen Dangshi to station their naval forces at Bowang Mountain, while Chen Zhengtong and the Prince of Hejian, Wang Xiaogong, led infantry and cavalry to Qingzhou Mountain. When the Prince of Hejian, Wang Xiaogong, arrived, he fortified his camp and refused battle, instead sending elite troops to cut off the rebels’ supply lines. As the rebels grew increasingly famished, they launched a night assault on Xiaogong’s camp, but he remained unmoved. The next day, he sent a feigned weak force to attack their ramparts, while Lu Zushang arrayed elite cavalry in ambush. Soon, the attacking force feigned retreat, and when the rebels pursued for several *li*, they encountered Zushang’s troops and were routed. Zhengtong abandoned his camp and fled.

Zhang Yu said: If we strike first, we become the guest, and they the host; as guests, our supplies are insufficient, but as hosts, they are more than ample. If we seize their stores, plunder their fields, draw provisions from them, and quarter our troops in enemy territory, we will be well-fed while they starve. Thus, we transform from guest to host. Without burning their supplies or disrupting their farming, we cannot starve the enemy. If they are the guests, we must sever their supply lines—this is what the Lord of Guangwu intended when he proposed sending elite troops to cut off Han Xin’s rear. How could they not be moved to action?

Cao Cao said: Attack what they cherish, strike where they must rush, and they will have no choice but to come to its defense.

Li Jing said: Strike where they must rush, attack where they least expect, assail what they hold dear, and they will have no choice but to rescue it.

Du Mu said: When Sima Xuanwang attacked Gongsun Wenyi in the east of Liaodong, Wenyi blocked the Liao River to repel the Wei forces. Xuanwang said: “The rebels fortify their camp and raise high ramparts to wear down our army. To attack them is to fall into their trap. The ancients said: ‘Though the enemy fortifies their camp, they cannot but engage us if we attack what they must rescue.’ Now, if we march straight for Xiangping, their forces will be gripped by fear and seek battle—then we shall surely crush them.” He then advanced in battle formation. Seeing the Wei troops emerge behind them, the rebels indeed came to intercept; Xuanwang launched a full assault and routed them, ultimately pacifying the east of Liaodong.

Chen Hao said: When Chu attacked Song, Song appealed to Jin for aid. Xian Zhen of Jin said: “If we seize the lord of Cao and distribute the lands of Cao and Wei to the people of Song, Chu cherishes Cao and Wei and will not consent. Joy and anger will clash—how can they not fight?” Thus, they defeated the Chu forces. Meng’s commentary aligns with Cao Cao’s.

Mei Yaochen said: Rush toward what they cherish, and they cannot remain still.

Wang Zhe’s commentary aligns with Li Jing’s.

He Shi said: Attack what they love—how can they remain unmoved?

Zhang Yu said: If the enemy is entrenched, believing themselves secure, and unwilling to engage quickly, we must attack what they must rescue, forcing them to emerge. When Xi Pian fortified his camp, the Earl of Qin provoked his subordinate generals, and they all sallied forth to battle—this is such a case. Strike where they do not expect, rush where they do not anticipate.

Cao Cao said: Thus, the enemy cannot come to each other’s aid.

He Shi said: Now, if the enemy must respond to us, marching a thousand *li* without fatigue is achieved by advancing through unoccupied land.

Cao Cao said: Strike the empty and the weak, avoid their defenses, and attack where they do not expect.

Li Jing said: Strike where the enemy is unprepared, advance through the solitary to attack the weak—what resistance can they offer?

Du Mu said: During the reign of Emperor Yuan of Liang, when Western Shu declared itself an empire and marched east to attack Yuan, the Western Wei general Zhou Wendi said: “To pacify Shu and subdue Liang—this is the moment.” Many of his generals disagreed. Wendi asked General Yuchi Jiong: “I entrust the campaign against Shu entirely to you—what is your plan?” Jiong replied: “Shu has been isolated from the Central Plains for over a hundred years, relying on its mountainous terrain and never expecting our army’s arrival. We should dispatch elite cavalry in a swift night raid. On level ground, advance at double speed; on difficult terrain, proceed cautiously. Strike where they do not expect, pierce their heart, and their defenses will collapse like wind-blown leaves.” Thus, Shu was pacified. The phrase “without fatigue” refers to advancing through empty land, where no enemy is expected, and movement is at our discretion—hence, no fatigue.

Chen Hao said: The term “empty and weak” does not merely mean the enemy is unprepared. Even if they are prepared, if their defenses are lax, their camp insecure, their generals weak, their troops disordered, their provisions scant, and their position isolated, when we advance with disciplined forces, they will collapse at the mere sight of our banners. Thus, we suffer no hardship, as if marching through uninhabited land.

Mei Yaochen said: Strike where they do not expect.

He Shi said: When Cao Cao marched north to campaign against the Wuhuan, his strategist Guo Jia advised: “In warfare, speed is paramount. Now, to strike a thousand *li* away with a ponderous baggage train would make it difficult to seize the advantage. Moreover, if the enemy learns of our approach, they will have time to prepare. It would be better to leave the baggage behind, advance with light forces at double speed, and take them by surprise.” Cao Cao then secretly emerged from Lulong Pass and marched straight for the chanyu’s court. The barbarians, suddenly hearing of his arrival, panicked and engaged in battle, only to be utterly routed. Even their chanyu and tribal chieftains were slain. Later, when the Tuyuhun raided the frontier, Emperor Taizong appointed Li Jing as Grand Chief Commander of the Western Sea Route. With a light force, he traversed two thousand *li* of empty land, pacified the Tuyuhun, and returned. Thus, Taizong remarked: “Moreover, Li Jing’s three thousand light cavalry penetrated deep into the barbarian court, recaptured and secured Dingxiang—such a feat is unmatched in ancient or modern times.”

Zhang Yu said: Seize their emptiness, strike their unpreparedness—even a thousand-*li* march will not weary the men. As when Deng Ai conquered Shu by taking the Yinping route, traversing over seven hundred *li* of uninhabited land. To attack and surely take is to strike where they do not defend.

Li Quan said: Where there is no vigilance, conquest is easy.

Du Mu said: Feint an attack on their east, strike their west; lure them forward, then raid their rear. During the Later Han, when Zhang Bu, governor of Ju, stationed his brother Lan at Xi’an and another general at Linzi—forty *li* from Linzi—Geng Bing encamped his forces between them. Observing that Xi’an’s walls were small but sturdy and Lan’s troops well-trained, while Linzi, though nominally larger, was in fact vulnerable, Bing ordered his officers to prepare siege equipment. Five days later, he launched an assault on Xi’an, then released prisoners to spread word. Hearing this, Lan kept watch day and night. On the appointed day, at midnight, Bing ordered his generals to eat their fill. By dawn, they had reached Linzi’s walls. His adjutant, Gou Liang, and others urged an immediate attack on Xi’an, but Bing replied: “Xi’an has heard of our intent and prepared day and night. Linzi, taken by surprise, will be thrown into chaos. If we strike now, we can take it in a single day. Once Linzi falls, Xi’an will be isolated—this is what we call striking one to gain two.” His plan unfolded exactly as foreseen.

Late in the Later Han, Zhu Jun attacked the Yellow Turban leader Han Zhong at Wan. Jun constructed a long siege wall and raised earthen mounds to overlook the city. Then, sounding drums, he feigned an assault on the southwest. The rebels rushed their entire force to meet it, whereupon Jun led five thousand elite troops to raid the northeast, scaling the walls and entering the city. Zhong retreated to the inner citadel, terrified, and begged to surrender.

Chen Hao said: When the state campaigned against Shangdang, Wang Zai knew that Liu Qi relied on the natural barrier of Tianjing Pass and had made no preparations for a stout defense. Zai concentrated his forces to seize and hold it, depriving Qi of his advantage and ultimately collapsing his stronghold.

Mei Yaochen said: Feign an attack on the south, but strike the north.

Wang Zhe said: Strike their emptiness—where the general is incompetent, the troops untrained, the ramparts weak, the defenses lax, relief slow, provisions scant, and morale divided.

Zhang Yu said: A master of attack moves as if from the nine heavens, leaving the enemy no time to prepare. With no time to prepare, what we strike is precisely where they do not defend. Geng Bing’s conquest of Linzi and Zhu Jun’s suppression of the Yellow Turbans are but two examples.

To defend and surely hold is to defend where they do not attack.

Du Mu said: If they do not attack, we still defend—how much more so where they do?

When the Han Grand Commandant Zhou Yafu campaigned against the Seven Kingdoms at Changyi, the rebels rushed to fortify the southeast corner of the ramparts. Yafu ordered his men to prepare for an assault on the northwest. Soon after, the rebels’ elite troops attacked the northwest but failed to breach it, then fled in disarray. Yafu pursued and crushed them.

Chen Hao said: Do not worry that the enemy will not attack—worry that we will not defend. To be prepared for attack from any quarter and to defend every point is the essence of military readiness.

Mei Yaochen said: If the enemy strikes our west, we must also prepare for the east.

Wang Zhe said: Defend with substance—where the general is capable, the troops well-trained, the ramparts strong, the defenses strict, relief swift, provisions ample, and morale unified.

Zhang Yu said: A master of defense hides as if beneath the nine earths, leaving the enemy no way to gauge their intent. With no way to gauge, what we defend is precisely where they do not attack. Zhou Yafu’s preparation for the northwest while the enemy struck the southeast is but one example.

Thus, the master of attack leaves the enemy uncertain of where to defend; the master of defense leaves the enemy uncertain of where to attack.

Cao Cao said: The situation must not be leaked.

Li Quan said: A master of attack possesses abundant weaponry—Gao Huan of Eastern Wei’s assault on Ye is an example. A master of defense? It lies in thorough preparation—Wei Xiaokuan’s defense of Jinzhou is another.

Du Mu said: The intentions of attack and defense must not be revealed.

Jia Lin said: When commands are obeyed, hearts are united, and defenses are solid, subtle and formless, the enemy hesitates, their strategies left in disarray.

Mei Yaochen said: A master of attack keeps their plans secret; a master of defense leaves no gaps.

Wang Zhe said: A master of attack waits for the enemy’s exploitable weakness, strikes swiftly, and thus prevents them from defending. A master of defense constantly ensures invulnerability, thus preventing the enemy from attacking. "Uncertainty" means the enemy cannot discern the source of attack or defense.

He Shi said: The strategies of attack and defense must be made unfathomable.

Zhang Yu said: In defense, one appears insufficient; in attack, one appears abundant. This "insufficiency" is not true weakness but a feint to provoke the enemy’s assault—this is how the enemy remains uncertain of where to attack. This "abundance" is not true strength but a display to make the enemy retreat into defense—this is how the enemy remains uncertain of where to defend. When intentions are not leaked, they accumulate into the essence of attack and defense. Subtle, oh so subtle, to the point of formlessness; divine, oh so divine, to the point of soundlessness—thus one becomes the arbiter of the enemy’s fate.

Li Quan said: The dual arts of attack and defense employ the orthodox and the extraordinary, their subtleties and wonders beyond verbal expression. So profound and wondrous that the enemy’s life and death hang upon my designs—hence, the arbiter of fate.

Du Mu said: Subtlety lies in stillness; divinity lies in motion. Stillness governs defense; motion governs attack. The enemy’s life and death rest entirely in my hands—thus, like the celestial arbiter of fate.

Du You said: Their subtleties are invisible. Their transformations are swift as spirits, enabling them to foresee the enemy’s fate as if by celestial decree.

Mei Yaochen said: Formless, their secrets cannot be glimpsed; soundless, their swiftness cannot be perceived.

Wang Zhe said: Secrets are hard to discern; swiftness is hard to counter—thus one controls the enemy’s fate.

He Shi said: The military treatise’s methods of emptiness and fullness reach their pinnacle in the divine and subtle, where ultimate success is achieved. My fullness is made to appear empty to the enemy; my emptiness is made to appear full. The enemy’s fullness, I can render empty; the enemy’s emptiness, I can discern as false. For the enemy cannot grasp my emptiness or fullness, while I can accurately assess theirs. When I wish to attack, I know their defended points are full and their undefended points are empty. I avoid their strength, strike their weakness, target their vulnerabilities, and exploit their gaps. When the enemy wishes to attack me, I know their targets are non-essential while their neglected points are critical. I display emptiness to the enemy while concealing my fullness. If they feint east, I prepare in the west. Thus, when I attack, they know not where to defend; when I defend, they know not where to attack. The transformations of attack and defense arise from the principles of emptiness and fullness. Sometimes hiding beneath the nine earths to signify defense; sometimes moving above the nine heavens to symbolize attack. Erasing traces to remain unseen, muffling sounds to remain unheard—emerging as if from the earth, vanishing as if into the sky, swift as meteors, stealthy as ghosts, penetrating the realm of no gaps, circling the abyss of the nine springs. So subtle as to be imperceptible, so divine as to be inaudible. Even the keenest eyes cannot perceive their form; the sharpest ears cannot detect their sound. The tangible becomes formless; the audible becomes soundless. Not that they lack form or sound, but that the enemy cannot perceive or hear them. The ultimate transformation of emptiness and fullness.

A master of defense understands the interplay of emptiness and fullness, thus entering the profound mysteries of the divine and subtle. The unskilled, however, rigidly pursue subtlety and divinity, clinging to the traces of warfare. Unable to erase form and sound, they remain within the realm of perception—failing to grasp the wonders of the divine and subtle, which lie in the interplay of emptiness and fullness.
