Even with a vast army of a million, how can there be no form or sound? It is merely that the enemy cannot perceive or hear them.

Zhang Yu said: The strategies of attack and defense, subtle and divine, reach such perfection that no form can be observed, no sound can be heard. Thus, the enemy’s life and death lie entirely in my hands. Advancing, they cannot be resisted—because I strike their emptiness. Retreating, they cannot be pursued—because I am swift and unreachable.

Cao Cao said: Swiftly advance to attack their emptiness and laxity; retreat with equal speed.

Li Quan said: Advancing means exploiting gaps and laxity; retreating means sending supplies ahead, moving far before the main army withdraws—thus making pursuit impossible. When Shi Le of Later Zhao was encamped at Gebei, plagued by rain and planning to return to Ye, he feared Jin forces would pursue. Following Zhang Bin’s advice, he sent supplies ahead, making them unreachable—this is what Quan means by "swift" as "distant."

Du Mu said: Having attacked their emptiness, the enemy is defeated. After defeat, how can they pursue me? Thus, I can retreat swiftly.

Chen Hao said: Du’s interpretation is incorrect. When Cao Cao besieged Zhang Xiu, the city remained unbreached, and his forces unexhausted—yet he departed. Zhang Xiu’s troops pursued, and Jia Xu urged him to stop. Xiu refused and was indeed defeated by Cao.

Jia Xu said to Zhang Xiu: "Since you could foresee their defeat, you should also be able to foresee their victory." Xu replied: "Then send the defeated troops to ambush them again." Xiu followed his advice, and Cao Cao was indeed defeated. How could it be that after suffering defeat, the enemy cannot pursue? Rather, it means advancing by exploiting their emptiness, so the enemy does not know what to defend; retreating while chasing advantage, so the enemy does not know what to pursue.

Du You said: "The divine seizes their emptiness and void."

Mei Yaochen said: "Advancing by exploiting their emptiness, none can resist me; retreating by taking advantage of their exhaustion, none can pursue me."

He Shi said: "When the army advances, it strikes at emptiness; when it retreats, it gains by speed. I can control the enemy, but the enemy cannot control me."

Zhang Yu said: "When facing each other across the lines, if you see the enemy’s gaps and swiftly advance to strike, how can they resist? Having gained advantage and retreated, swiftly returning to fortify your defenses, how can they pursue? The nature of warfare values speed—swift as wind and lightning, so the enemy cannot control it. Thus, if I wish to fight, even if the enemy has high ramparts and deep moats, they cannot but engage me, for I attack what they must rescue. As Cao Cao and Li Quan said: cut off their supply lines, guard their retreat routes, and attack their ruler. Du Mu said: If I am the host and the enemy the guest, cut off their provisions and guard their retreat. If I am the guest and the enemy the host, attack their ruler. When Sima Xuanwang attacked Liaodong, he marched straight for Xiangping—this is the principle. Mei Yaochen said: Attack their vital points. Wang Zhe said: Cao Cao said: Cut off their supply lines, guard their retreat routes, and attack their ruler. I say: If the enemy holds firm, simply attack what they must rescue, and they will engage me. For example, when Geng Yan wished to attack Juli to lure Fei Yi is the same principle. He Shi said: When the Wei general Sima Xuanwang campaigned against Gongsun Wenyi, he secretly crossed the Liao River by boat, built long siege walls, then suddenly abandoned the enemy and marched toward Xiangping. His generals said: Not attacking the enemy but building long walls does not show our strength to the masses. Xuanwang replied: The enemy has fortified camps and high ramparts, intending to wear down our troops. The ancients said: Even if the enemy has high ramparts, they cannot but engage me if I attack what they must rescue. The enemy’s main force is here, so their lair is empty. I march straight for Xiangping, and the enemy will be seized by inner fear. Fearful, they will seek battle, and their defeat is certain. He then ordered the army to advance in formation. Seeing the troops emerge behind them, the enemy indeed intercepted them."

Xuanwang addressed his generals: "The reason we did not attack their camp was precisely to bring about this outcome—we must not let this opportunity slip." He then unleashed his troops to meet the enemy head-on, crushing them decisively and securing victory in three successive battles.

When Ma Sui campaigned against Tian Yue, his forces were few, and Yue entrenched himself deeply, refusing battle. Sui ordered his armies to carry ten days’ rations and advanced to Cangkou, where they encamped across the Wen River from Yue’s forces. Li Baozhen and Li You questioned him: "With so little grain, why press so deep?" Sui replied: "Scant supplies favor swift battle. The art of war excels in drawing the enemy to us, not in being drawn by them. Now, Tian Yue and the armies of Zi, Qing, and Yan form a chain, intending to wear us down without fighting. If we divide our forces to strike their flanks, our numbers will be too few to break them, and Yue will come to their aid—leaving us caught between two fronts. As the military classics say: *Attack what the enemy must rescue*, and they will be compelled to fight." Sui thus united his armies and crushed them.

He then constructed three bridges to cross the Wen River, provoking battle daily, yet Yue dared not emerge. However, the troops of Ze Prefecture, fearing absorption by Sui’s smaller force, withdrew to join Yue. The next day, as Yue and Sui exchanged taunts, Yue concealed ten thousand soldiers in ambush, intending to intercept Sui. Sui ordered his armies to eat before midnight, then at the first crow of the cock, they sounded drums and horns, stealthily marching along the Wen River toward Weizhou. He commanded: "When you hear the enemy approach, halt and form ranks." He also dispatched a hundred cavalry with drums and horns to remain behind, carrying bundles of firewood. Once the main force had departed, they ceased their clamor, concealed themselves, and waited for Yue’s army to cross before burning the bridges.

After marching ten *li*, Yue led over forty thousand infantry and cavalry from Zi, Qing, and Yan Prefectures, crossing the bridges to strike Sui’s rear. A wind rose, and they set fires, advancing with war cries. Sui, clad in armor, ordered his troops to hold fast, then commanded the vanguard to clear a hundred paces of grass and brambles for a battle line. He recruited five thousand valiant men to form the front ranks, awaiting the enemy’s arrival. By the time Yue’s forces arrived, their fires had burned out, their *qi* was spent, and their strength waned. Sui unleashed his troops, and Yue’s army collapsed. Yue fled to the bridges, but they had already been burned. His forces plunged into the river in chaos; twenty thousand were beheaded, and the armies of Zi and Qing were nearly annihilated.

Zhang Yu remarked: "When I am the invader and the enemy the defender, my troops are strong but provisions few, while their position is weak but grain abundant—then swift battle is advantageous. Though the enemy may have ramparts of metal and moats of boiling water, they cannot hold their ground but must come to fight me, for I strike at what they cherish, forcing them to rescue it. When the Chu besieged Song, the Jin prepared to relieve it. Hu Yan said: ‘Chu has just taken Cao and newly wed into Wei; if we attack Cao and Wei, Chu must rescue them, and Song will be spared.’ The plan was followed, and the siege lifted. Likewise, when Jin’s Emperor Xuan campaigned against Gongsun Wenyi, he suddenly abandoned the enemy and marched on Xiangping, striking at their lair. The enemy indeed emerged to intercept him, and he met them head-on, winning three battles in succession—this follows the same principle.

When I do not wish to fight, I mark the ground and hold it. Cao Cao said: 'An army should not be overburdened.' Li Quan said: 'This means defending one’s borders.' If entering enemy territory, employ the *Tianyi Dunjia* method of the Perfected One, closing the Six *Wu* by drawing a blade to mark the camp.' Meng Shi said: 'Marking the ground with objects to hold it illustrates its ease. For if I can thwart the enemy’s intentions, they will not dare approach.' That the enemy cannot engage me in battle is because I thwart their aims. Cao Cao said: '*Guai* means to thwart. Thwart their path, display advantages and harms, and make the enemy hesitate.' Li Quan said: '*Guai* means divergence. Deploying strange tactics to sow doubt prevents the enemy from engaging me.' When Han’s Governor of Shanggu, Li Guang, loosed his horses and removed his saddles, it was to create doubt. Du Mu said: 'If the enemy attacks me and I do not fight, I employ deceptions to confuse them, making their resolve diverge from their initial purpose, so they dare not engage me.'

When Cao Cao contended for Hanzhong, the First Ruler of Shu resisted him. General Zhao Yun, guarding a separate encampment, rode out lightly with a few dozen cavalry and suddenly encountered Cao’s main force. Yun retreated while skirmishing. Cao’s army pursued and surrounded him. Upon reaching his camp, Yun flung open the gates, stilled the banners, and silenced the drums. Cao’s forces suspected an ambush and withdrew. When Zhuge Liang encamped at Yangping, he sent Wei Yan and other generals east with the bulk of the army, leaving only ten thousand men to hold the city. A scout reported to Sima Yi: 'Liang is in the city with few troops and weak strength.' The officers paled, but Liang remained composed. He ordered the army to lower all banners and silence the drums, forbade anyone to venture out, and had the four gates swept and sprinkled with water. Yi suspected an ambush and led his forces away, hastening toward the northern mountains. Liang told his advisors: 'Sima Yi thought I had set an ambush and fled along the mountains.' Yi later learned of this and deeply regretted it.

When Cao Cao clashed with Lü Bu, Cao’s army went out to harvest wheat, and Bu suddenly arrived with his forces. Cao’s camp had only a thousand men arrayed for battle, half concealed behind a dike. Bu hesitated, unwilling to advance, saying: 'Cao Cao is full of tricks—do not fall into an ambush.' He then withdrew his troops. Chen Hao said: 'In the *Zuozhuan*, when Chu’s Prime Minister Ziyuan attacked Zheng, he entered through the Pure Gate and reached the Distant Market, but the portcullis remained raised. Ziyuan said: “There are men here.” And so he retreated.' Jia Lin said: 'Place decoy troops where the enemy fears, encamp in terrain of strategic advantage—though you have not dug trenches or raised ramparts, the enemy will not dare attack.' Mei Yaochen said: 'Marking the ground illustrates ease. Thwart their path and display advantages to make them hesitate and dare not advance.'"

King Wei swore: "Marking the ground signifies ease, and moreover, it clearly demonstrates that control of the command bell follows the Dao."

Zhang Yu said: "When I am the host and the enemy the guest, with my provisions plentiful and my troops few, while their food is scarce and their soldiers numerous, it is advantageous not to engage in battle. Though we do not fortify our camp with ramparts, the enemy will not dare to attack us—this is achieved by displaying deceptive formations and thwarting their intended path. As when the men of Chu attacked Zheng, and Zheng left its portcullis raised, the valiant Chu forces spoke and withdrew, and the Chu army dared not advance but fled. Again, when Sima Yi besieged Zhuge Liang, Liang furled his banners and beat his drums outside, opening his gates and sweeping the ground. Yi, suspecting an ambush, led his troops away. This is the same principle. Thus, if you reveal the enemy’s form while remaining formless yourself, you concentrate your strength while the enemy disperses theirs."

Du You said: "I remain unified while the enemy scatters."

Mei Yaochen said: "When others have form and I remain unseen, the enemy must divide their forces to guard against me."

Zhang Yu said: "Make my orthodox forces appear unorthodox to the enemy, and my unorthodox forces appear orthodox—this is revealing the enemy’s form. By turning the unorthodox into the orthodox and the orthodox into the unorthodox, shifting endlessly, you render the enemy unable to discern your true form. Once the enemy’s form is revealed, I then concentrate my forces to confront them. If my form remains concealed, the enemy must divide their strength to prepare defenses. I am unified as one, while the enemy is split into ten—thus, I attack with ten against their one."

Du You said: "When I discern the enemy’s form and assess their emptiness or fullness, my preparations are few, and I remain concentrated in one place. With my unity, I strike their scattered troops—this is ten attacking one."

Mei Yaochen said: "Dividing one into ten, I always strike one part with tenfold force. Thus, I am many and the enemy few."

Du You said: "I am unified as one, hence many; the enemy is divided into ten, hence few."

Zhang Yu said: "Seeing the enemy’s emptiness or fullness, I need not prepare extensively, so I remain concentrated in one place. The enemy, unable to perceive my form, divides into ten. Thus, my ten strike their one. Therefore, I cannot but be many, and the enemy cannot but be few. He who can strike the few with the many engages only a limited force in battle."

Du Mu said: "Limited, meaning few. I dig deep trenches and raise high ramparts, erase my tracks and conceal my sounds, moving without form, attacking and seizing unpredictably. Sometimes with light cavalry and swift horses, I strike their vulnerabilities; sometimes with strong crossbows and longbows, I seize their critical points. I harass their left and tread upon their right, charge their rear and startle their front. By day, I confuse them with banners; by night, I bewilder them with drums. Thus, the enemy, fearful, divides their forces to guard against threats. It is like gazing down from a mountain upon a city, or observing the outside from behind a curtain—the enemy’s dispersed formations are all known to me, while my strategies of attack and defense remain hidden from them. Thus, I am unified, and the enemy is divided. Unity makes strength complete; division makes strength weak. Striking the weak with the complete ensures victory."

Du You said: "This means few are easily overcome."

Mei Yaochen said: "Striking the divided with the unified means I face only a few."

Wang Zhe said: "Create many deceptive forms to make the enemy prepare against them, while my actual attack remains unguarded—thus, I am unified and the enemy divided. Unified is many; divided is few. 'Ten attack one' is a general statement."

He Shi echoed Du Mu’s annotation.

Zhang Yu said: "When force is concentrated, it is strong; when troops are scattered, they are weak. Striking weak and scattered forces with a strong and concentrated host means expending little effort for great success. The place where I engage in battle must remain unknown."

Du You said: "My movements are subtle and concealed, my intentions unseen. The enemy may know where I emerge but not where I strike; they may know where I strike but not where I gather."

Zhang Yu said: "This is because my form is invisible. If it is unknown, the enemy must prepare in many places."

Mei Yaochen said: "If the enemy does not know, they prepare everywhere."

Cao Cao said: "Concealing form makes the enemy suspicious, so they divide their forces to guard against me. This means few are easily struck."

Wang Zhe said: "The place where I engage the enemy must not be known to them; if known, they will concentrate their strength to resist me."

Cao Cao said: "Concealing form makes the enemy suspicious."

Zhang Yu said: "Unable to predict where my chariots will emerge, where my cavalry will come from, or from which direction my infantry will advance, the enemy divides their forces to prepare everywhere. Thus, their troops become scattered and weak, their power divided and diminished. Therefore, where I engage, I confront their isolated forces with my full strength. Thus, preparing in front leaves the rear weak; preparing in the rear leaves the front weak; preparing on the left leaves the right weak; preparing on the right leaves the left weak. Preparing everywhere leaves everything weak."

Du You said: "When the enemy prepares in many places, their soldiers are inevitably dispersed and few."

Mei Yaochen said: "Wherever they prepare, they are weak."

The few are those who prepare against others; the many are those who make others prepare against them.

Cao Cao said: "As stated earlier, concealing form makes the enemy suspicious, so they divide their forces to guard against me."

Li Quan said: "The place where you array your troops must not be known to the enemy. If they suspect, they will believe your forces are divided and prepare against you."

Du Mu said: "The place where you engage in battle must not be known to the enemy. If my form remains concealed, the enemy will not know my left or right, front or rear, near or far, perilous or secure terrain. They will not know where I will attack or where we will meet in battle. Thus, they divide their troops to defend everywhere. Concealing form makes many; division makes few. Therefore, the many will win, and the few will lose."

Meng Shi said: "Preparing against others scatters me; preparing against me divides them."

Du You said: "When the enemy is scattered and few, it is because they have prepared against others first. When the enemy must prepare extensively against me, it is because I am concentrated and numerous." Mei Yaochen said: "The more the enemy prepares, the fewer they become." Wang Xi said: "When they prepare in all directions—left, right, front, and rear—they are few in every direction." He Shi agreed with the various commentaries.

Zhang Yu said: "When there is no place left unprepared—left, right, front, and rear—then there is no place where their troops are not few. The reason they are few is that their forces are divided and widely dispersed to prepare against others. The reason we are numerous is that our strength is concentrated, forcing others to prepare against us. Thus, if one knows the place of battle and the day of battle, one may march a thousand *li* to meet the enemy in combat."

Cao Cao said: "By measuring and assessing, one knows the emptiness and the day of engagement." Li Quan said: "Knowing the place of battle determines the convenience of boats, chariots, infantry, and cavalry." When the Martial Lord [Cao Cao] had not yet pacified the northern lands, he abandoned saddles and horses and relied on boats and oars to contend with Wu and Yue for supremacy—thus he suffered defeat at the hands of Huang Gai. When King Fuchai of Wu hastily drove the forces of Wu and Chu to gallop between Liang and Zheng, this was a case of not knowing the place or day of battle. Therefore, the *Taiyi Dunjia* states: "By calculating the three gates and five generals, the outcome of host and guest can be known." Thus, one may march a thousand *li* to meet the enemy and prevail.

Du Mu said: "When Emperor Wu of Song sent Zhu Lingshi to campaign against Qiao Zong in Shu, the emperor said: 'In previous years, Liu Jingxuan advanced via the inner waters toward Huangwu but withdrew without success. The enemy now expects that if we come again, we will follow the outer waters, yet they suspect we may still take them by surprise and come via the inner waters. Thus, they will surely station heavy troops at Fucheng to guard the inner route. If we advance toward Huangwu, we will fall into their trap. Now, with our main force, we will take Chengdu from the outer waters while sending a feint toward the inner waters—this will be an unexpected maneuver to control the enemy.' Fearing that this plan might leak in advance and the enemy would discern the truth, he sealed the full orders in a letter and entrusted it to Lingshi. On the edge of the letter, he wrote: 'Open only upon reaching Baidi.' None of the armies knew the disposition of forces. Upon reaching Baidi, the letter was opened, revealing: 'All forces shall take Chengdu via the outer waters. Zang Xi and Zhu Lin shall take Guanghan via the middle waters. Send a few weak troops aboard tall ships via the inner waters toward Huangwu.' Qiao Zong indeed stationed heavy troops to guard the inner waters, and Lingshi destroyed him."

Chen Hao said: "Du Mu’s note speaks only of knowing the place of battle, not the day. If I attack the enemy and they fail to engage me at the appointed time, or if the enemy invades me and I must prepare to respond, how can one make an agreement? When Xiang Yu told Cao Jiu, 'I will pacify Liang in fifteen days and then meet with you again,' if he did not know the appointed day of battle, how could he make such a promise?"

Meng Shi said: "By measuring and assessing emptiness, first knowing the terrain of the battlefield, and then carefully determining the day of battle, one may set a rendezvous a thousand *li* away and arrive first to await the enemy. If the enemy arrives first, one need not go to exhaust them."

Du You said: "The skilled warrior must know both the day and the place of battle. By calculating the route and setting the date, dividing the army and mixing the troops, those far away advance first, and those nearby set out later. A thousand-*li* rendezvous may be made to converge at the same time, as if meeting in a marketplace. On the day of meeting at the appointed place, do not let the enemy know. If they know, they will prepare in fewer places; if they do not know, they will prepare in many. Few preparations mean concentration; many preparations mean division. Division scatters strength; concentration unifies it."

Mei Yaochen said: "If one can determine both the place and the day of battle, even a thousand *li* away, one may set a date and engage the enemy." Wang Zhe said: "First knowing the terrain and the enemy’s situation, then using the measurements of military strategy to calculate distances, one may discern emptiness and assess where the enemy will respond and when the battle will occur. In this way, even a thousand *li* away, one may meet the enemy in battle and break them. Thus, Cao Cao’s words—'by measuring and assessing, one knows the emptiness and the day of engagement'—are correct."

Zhang Yu said: "Whenever one raises troops to attack the enemy, the place of battle must be known in advance. By the day the army arrives, one must ensure the enemy comes as scheduled to fight. Knowing the place and day of battle allows preparations to be concentrated and defenses to be firm. Even a thousand *li* away, one may advance to battle. For example, Sai Shu knew the Jin would intercept the army at Yao—this was knowing the place of battle. Chen Tang predicted the Wusun siege would be lifted in five days—this was knowing the day of battle. Another example is Sun Bin’s ambush of Pang Juan at Maling, where he calculated the time of dusk and the enemy’s arrival. Without knowing the place or day of battle, the left cannot rescue the right, the right cannot rescue the left, the front cannot rescue the rear, and the rear cannot rescue the front—how much less those tens of *li* away, or even just a few *li*?"

Du Mu said: "Guanzi said: 'To send out troops without a settled plan is to fight and destroy oneself.'" Du You said: "If the enemy has already seized advantageous terrain and I rush toward profit, desiring battle, then left, right, front, and rear will be hesitant in advance and retreat, unable to rescue one another—how much more so over ten or more *li*?" Mei Yaochen said: "Inability to rescue is due to being few. If left, right, front, and rear cannot rescue one another, how much less those far away?"

Zhang Yu said: "If one does not know where the enemy will meet the army or on what day they will engage in battle, then preparations will not be concentrated, and defenses will not be firm. If one suddenly encounters the enemy, hastily engaging them, left and right, front and rear will still be unable to aid one another—how much less those separated by great distances? By my estimation, even if the Yue people’s troops are many, what benefit will it bring to victory or defeat?"

Cao Cao said: "The Yue people gather in confusion, knowing nothing." Some say: "Wu and Yue are neighboring states." Li Quan said: "Yue means 'to exceed.' If one does not know the place or day of battle, even if one’s troops exceed others, how can one know victory or defeat?"

Chen Hao said: "Sunzi discoursed on military affairs for King Helü of Wu. As Wu and Yue were mutually pitiful, he spoke of Yue, referring to troops that exceed others—this is not righteous."

Jia Lin said: "If one does not know the battlefield or the day of battle, even with many troops, one cannot control the governance of victory or defeat—what benefit is there?"

Mei Yaochen said: "Wu and Yue were enemy states. Though the Yue people are many, they can still be divided by me to become few."

Wang Zhe said: "This is Wu appraising the enemy according to the times. Though the Yue troops are many, if they do not aid one another well, it is also of no benefit to the calculations of victory or defeat."

Zhang Yu said: "The character *wu* (吴) was mistakenly written as *wu* (吾). Wu and Yue were neighboring states, frequently invading one another, hence the following text states: 'The people of Wu and the people of Yue detest each other.' Though the troops of Yue are many, if they do not know the battlefield or the day of battle, their strength should be divided to weaken them. Thus it is said: 'Victory can be made.'"

Du Mu said: "Victory lies with me, hence it is said it can be made."

Meng Shi said: "If the enemy does not know the battlefield or the appointed day, our victory can be constant."

Mei Yaochen’s annotation agrees with Du Mu’s. Wang Shi and He Shi’s annotations agree with Meng Shi’s.

Zhang Yu said: "Victory lies with me, hence it is so. The *Xing* chapter states: 'Victory can be known but cannot be made.' Why then does it say here that victory can be made? In the *Xing* chapter, the discussion concerns the circumstances of attack and defense, stating that if the enemy is prepared, one cannot necessarily make victory. Here, however, the focus is on the Yue troops, estimating that the Yue people certainly cannot know the battlefield or the day of battle, hence it is said that victory can be made. Though the enemy is numerous, they can be made to have no gaps."

Du Mu said: "By measuring the following four matters, though the enemy’s troops are many, they can be made unable to compete with me in victory."

Meng Shi said: "Though the enemy has many troops, I can employ many deceptions, divide their formations, and prevent them from joining forces."

Jia Lin said: "Though the enemy is numerous, if they do not know my military situation, they will constantly be forced to prepare hastily and have no leisure to scheme or punish."

Mei Yaochen said: "If they can be made few, what gaps will there be?"

Wang Zhe said: "If many cannot aid one another, what can they rely on?"

Zhang Yu said: "By dispersing their strength so they cannot advance in unison, how can they contend with me? Thus, by strategizing, one knows the calculations of gain and loss."

Li Quan said: "Those who employ troops use the military methods of securing victory. The calculations of the *Taiyi Dunjia* and the Five Generals determine the numbers of encirclement and ambush—gain and loss can be known."

Meng Shi said: "By assessing the enemy’s situation and observing their actions, their calculations can be known."

Jia Lin said: "In the quiet of the command tent, through strategizing, the enemy’s gains and our losses in calculation are all known beforehand."

Du You said: "By assessing the enemy’s situation and observing their actions, their calculations can be known."

Mei Yaochen said: "Their calculations of gain and loss are known through my strategizing."

Wang Zhe said: "By assessing the enemy’s situation, the numbers of gain and loss become apparent."

Zhang Yu said: "By planning the enemy’s situation, one knows the success or failure of their calculations, as when Lord Xue appraised Jing Bu’s three strategies."

"By acting, one knows the principles of movement and stillness."

Li Quan said: "Observing cloud omens, wind and birds, and human sentiments, the principles of movement and stillness can be known. During Wang Mang’s reign, when Wang Xun campaigned against Kunyang, clouds like a collapsing mountain descended upon the camp several yards above the ground, and Guangwu knew he would surely be defeated. When Liang’s Prince Jiang Bian’s camp had clouds like a dike, Hou Jing knew he would surely prevail. Wind birds are creatures like those that eat jackals. Here, Quan takes *zuo* (作) as *hou* (候)."

Du Mu said: "*Zuo* means to provoke. By provoking the enemy to respond to me, one then observes the patterns of their movement, stillness, order, and disorder. King Wu of Wei said: 'When two armies face each other, how can one know what their generals will do?' Wu Qi replied: 'Order lowly but brave soldiers to lead the vanguard and attack, then feign defeat and retreat without punishing them. Observe the enemy’s advance and retreat, their rising and sitting—if their governance is orderly, if they do not pursue those in flight, and do not seize advantages, such a general is cunning. If they pursue in full force, their flags and banners are disordered, their movements and halts are chaotic, they covet gains and seek only to obtain them—such a general’s commands are not followed. Attack without hesitation.'"

Chen Hao said: "*Zuo* means to create. By creating advantages and harms to entice the enemy, one knows the principles of their advance and retreat."

Jia Lin said: "Those skilled in observation must know the principles of the enemy’s movement and stillness."

Du You said: "By examining their expressions of joy and anger and their actions, their sentiments and principles can be discerned. Thus, one knows the dynamics of movement and stillness and the changes for victory or defeat."

Mei Yaochen said: "The enemy’s principles of movement and stillness are revealed through what I initiate."

Wang Zhe said: "One awaits whether their principles warrant movement or not."

Zhang Yu said: "By acting over time and observing their joy and anger, the principles of their movement and stillness can be known. When Duke Wen of Jin detained Wan Chun to anger Chu’s general Ziyu, Ziyu then attacked the Jin army—this was his impetuous movement. When Zhuge Liang sent women’s headdresses to anger Sima Xuanwang, Xuanwang ultimately did not come out to fight—this was his calm stillness."

"By manifesting, one knows the terrain of life and death."

Li Quan said: "Breaking formations and setting up surprises, one may lower flags and silence drums to manifest weakness, or display empty arrays with lightning and banners to manifest strength. One casts them into death to lead them to life—thus life and death are shaped by the terrain. When Han Xin descended Jingxing and Liu Yu crossed Daxian, this was the principle."

Du Mu said: "The terrain of life and death refers to the battlefield. Casting them into death leads to life; placing them in life leads to death."

"I speak of employing manifold deceptions to harass the enemy, observing their responses to my maneuvers, then adapting accordingly—thus the terrain of life and death becomes known.

Chen Hao said: Once the enemy shows movement or stillness, I perceive their configuration. Those with strategy occupy ground that ensures life; those without strategy are cast into ground that seals death.

Meng Shi said: By examining the enemy’s configuration and observing their positions, the strategic advantage of the terrain—whether it leads to life or death—can be discerned.

Jia Lin said: Seeing how they deploy their forces reveals where their death lies.

Mei Yaochen said: Their terrain of life and death is recognized through the forms I manifest.

He Shi’s note concurs with Du Mu’s commentary.

Zhang Yu said: If I manifest weakness, the enemy will advance; if I manifest strength, they will retreat. By observing their advances and retreats, I know whether the ground they occupy leads to life or death. As stated earlier, ‘One skilled in moving the enemy manifests forms to which the enemy must respond.’ Death ground refers to treacherous terrain; life ground refers to advantageous terrain.

By measuring, one knows the enemy’s excesses and deficiencies.

Cao Cao said: *Jiao* means to measure.

Li Quan said: *Jiao* means to measure. By gauging their strength and courage, their emptiness or fullness becomes known.

Du Mu said: *Jiao* means to measure. I measure the enemy’s excesses with my own excesses, and their deficiencies with my own deficiencies. As Guanzi said: ‘A skilled attacker assesses the enemy’s numbers to attack their numbers, their provisions to attack their provisions. If their provisions are exhausted, do not attack; if their defenses are unprepared, do not attack.’ When Sima Xuanwang campaigned against Liaodong, Sima Chen Gui said: ‘Formerly, when we attacked Shangyong, eight divisions advanced day and night without rest, enabling us to capture the fortified city and slay Meng Da in half a month. Now, arriving from afar, you proceed so leisurely—I am deeply perplexed.’ The king replied: ‘Meng Da’s forces were few, and his provisions lasted a year. My forces are four times his, yet our grain will not last a month. To plan for a year with a month’s supplies—how can we not act swiftly? Attacking with four against one, even if half our forces perish, we must press on. Thus, we disregard casualties and race against their grain.’ Now, with the enemy numerous and we few, the enemy starving and we sated, the rains so heavy that no progress is made, and their grain nearly exhausted, we should feign incapacity to lull them. Later, when the rains ceased, we attacked day and night and ultimately pacified Liaodong.’

Mei Yaochen said: By measuring, I discern the enemy’s excesses and deficiencies.

Wang Zhe said: *Jiao* means to compare. Comparing our strength with theirs reveals their excesses and deficiencies, allowing us to plan advantageous attacks or defenses. All of the above pertains to measuring the enemy to know how to fight.

Zhang Yu said: Excess means strength; deficiency means weakness. Measuring the enemy’s configuration reveals their strong and weak points.

Emperor Taizong of Tang said: When approaching battle, I always match my strength against the enemy’s weakness, and my weakness against their strength. Without measurement, how could this be known?

Thus, the pinnacle of manifesting forms is to reach formlessness. When formless, even deep spies cannot pry, and the wise cannot scheme.

Li Quan said: The subtlety of manifesting forms lies in attaining formlessness—where spies cannot pry and wisdom cannot scheme—this is what is meant by *manifesting*.

Du Mu said: This speaks of the Way of warfare reaching its ultimate refinement in formlessness. When formless, even deep spies sent to observe cannot discern our emptiness or fullness. Strength and weakness are not revealed externally, so even the most astute cannot devise schemes against us.

Mei Yaochen said: Warfare inherently has form; when emptiness and fullness are concealed, it becomes formless—this is the ultimate achievement. Even if spies attempt to probe with cunning or the wise try to calculate, can they succeed?

Wang Zhe said: Controlling the form of warfare to achieve formlessness is the ultimate attainment—who can pry or scheme against it?

He Shi said: Formations are visible externally, but strategic changes occur internally. Adapting to forms to control change makes one’s intentions difficult to discern—this is called knowing the subtle.

Zhang Yu said: Initially, one uses emptiness and fullness to manifest forms to the enemy, leaving them unable to fathom our intentions. Thus, the ultimate refinement culminates in formlessness. With no form to observe and no pattern to seek, spies cannot exploit gaps, and the wise have no means to devise plans.

Victory is established through forms, yet the masses cannot perceive it.

Cao Cao said: Victory is established based on the enemy’s form.

Li Quan said: *Cuo* means to place. By setting up perilous formations, leveraging the soldiers’ courage, and seizing victory—military affairs prioritize secrecy, which the masses cannot comprehend.

Du Mu said: Discerning forms to establish victory or defeat is beyond the capacity of the wise, let alone the masses.

Mei Yaochen said: The masses know I can establish victory but do not understand that it arises from the enemy’s form.

He Shi said: Victory established through the enemy’s form is beyond the masses’ understanding.

Zhang Yu said: Victory is established through the enemy’s shifting forms, which the masses cannot comprehend.

All know the forms by which I prevail, yet none know the forms by which I secure victory.

Cao Cao said: Do not rely on a single form to prevail over all forms. Or: Do not fully reveal one’s knowledge.

The means of securing victory: all know how I prevail, but none know how I adapt the enemy’s form to secure victory.

Li Quan said: People know of the victory but not the subtle, hidden methods of securing it.

Du Mu said: After victory, people only see that I controlled the enemy, causing them to falter—this originated from me, enabling my triumph. As stated earlier: ‘Appear near when distant, appear distant when near; entice with profit, seize when disordered; prepare when substantial, avoid when strong; provoke when enraged, humble when arrogant; exhaust when at ease, divide when united.’ These are all methods of securing victory, unknown to others."

Chen Hao said: People only know my skill in defeating the enemy, but none know how I exploit the enemy’s faltering form.

Mei Yaochen said: They recognize the traces of victory but not the configurations that produce it.

Wang Zhe said: As with Han Xin’s troops arrayed with their backs to the river. People only saw the desperate battle of the army on the water, believing it could not be defeated, and when the Zhao forces panicked and fled in disarray, none understood by what means I had compelled them to do so.

Zhang Yu said: The breakthrough of victory is known to all, yet none perceive how I shaped it by adapting to the enemy’s form. Thus, victories in battle are never repeated, but respond to form in endless variation.

Cao Cao said: Do not act in repeated patterns but respond accordingly.

Li Quan said: Do not reuse past strategies to secure victory; adapt to circumstances and control change.

Du Mu said: Whenever the enemy presents a form, I can then respond and prevail.

Du You said: This is a fixed principle.

Jia Lin said: Responding to the enemy’s form to secure victory is inexhaustible.

Mei Yaochen said: Do not cling to old postures; respond to form with opportunity.

Wang Shi said: The principle of securing victory is singular, yet the forms of victory are infinite.

He Shi said: Once a method of victory has been used, it is not employed again. Respond to the enemy’s approach without following prior methods—thus it is inexhaustible.

Zhang Yu said: After victory, do not reuse previous strategies; instead, respond to the enemy’s form and deploy endless variations.

The form of warfare resembles water.

Meng Shi said: The momentum of warfare, like the swift or slow flow of water, is without constancy.

The form of water avoids heights and rushes downward.

Mei Yaochen said: Its nature.

The form of warfare avoids the substantial and strikes the insubstantial.

Mei Yaochen said: Its advantage.

Zhang Yu said: Water rushing downward follows the current; warfare striking the insubstantial gains advantage.

Water shapes its flow according to the terrain.

Du Mu said: Following the land’s descent.

Mei Yaochen said: Adapting to high and low.

Zhang Yu said: Whether square, round, slanted, or straight, it takes form according to the land.

Warfare shapes victory according to the enemy.

Li Quan said: Without adapting to the enemy’s momentum, how could we prevail?

Light troops cannot sustain prolonged engagement; when besieged, they must be reinforced. Provoke enraged troops, pacify strong ones, humble arrogant commanders, entice greedy ones, and reverse the suspicions of doubtful ones—thus victory is shaped by the enemy.

Du Mu said: Exploiting the enemy’s vulnerabilities.

Jia Lin said: Observing the enemy’s waxing and waning, I establish victory accordingly.

Du You said: Just as water shapes its flow according to the land’s slope, warfare secures victory by exploiting the enemy’s deficiencies.

Mei Yaochen said: Adapting to the insubstantial and substantial.

Wang Zhe said: Like dredging and guiding embankments.

He Shi said: Achieving success by adapting to the enemy’s strength or weakness.

Zhang Yu said: Whether insubstantial or substantial, strong or weak, victory is seized by adapting to the enemy.

Thus, warfare has no constant momentum.

Mei Yaochen said: Momentum arises in response to the enemy.

Zhang Yu said: The enemy changes and moves, so there is no constant momentum.

Water has no constant form.

Mei Yaochen said: Form arises according to the terrain.

Meng Shi said: Warfare has transformations; terrain has square and round.

Zhang Yu said: Terrain has high and low, so there is no constant form.

One who can adapt to the enemy’s changes and secure victory is called divine.

Cao Cao said: When momentum wanes, it must be revived; when form is exposed, it must be concealed. Thus, one who can adapt to the enemy’s changes and secure victory is like a spirit.

Li Quan said: One who understands this Way is called a divine army.

Du Mu said: The momentum of warfare is revealed only in response to the enemy; momentum does not reside in me, so it has no constancy. Like the form of water, which arises only in response to the terrain; form does not reside in water, so it has no constancy. Water, following the land’s descent, can float stones; warfare, responding to the enemy’s movements, can transform like a spirit.

Wang Zhe said: Warfare has constant principles but no constant momentum; water has constant nature but no constant form. The constant principle of warfare is striking the insubstantial; the lack of constant momentum lies in responding to the enemy. The constant nature of water is flowing downward; the lack of constant form lies in shaping itself to the terrain. When the momentum of warfare shifts, even defeated troops can be made to strike victorious ones—how much more so the elite?

He Shi said: Adapting and responding with wisdom; when wisdom is unfathomable, it is divine.

Zhang Yu said: Once the momentum of warfare is established, one who can adapt to the enemy’s changes and respond to secure victory is divinely subtle.

Thus, the Five Phases have no constant victor.

Du You said: The Five Phases alternate dominance.

Wang Zhe said: They mutually overcome one another.

The four seasons have no fixed position.

Du You said: The four seasons alternate in function.

Wang Zhe said: They mutually succeed one another.

The sun has short and long days; the moon has death and life.

Cao Cao said: Warfare has no constant momentum; its expansion and contraction follow the enemy.

Li Quan said: The Five Phases—rest, decline, ascendancy, and mutual overcoming—alternate. The four seasons—cold and heat—come and go without fixed constancy. The sun and moon traverse the heavens in 365 and a quarter degrees. The hundred divisions of the day: at the spring and autumn equinoxes, day and night are equal; at the summer solstice, the day has sixty divisions and the night forty; at the winter solstice, the day has forty divisions and the night sixty—unequal in length. The moon’s first day is the new moon, the eighth day the first quarter, the fifteenth day the full moon, the twenty-fourth day the last quarter, and the thirtieth day the dark moon—thus its death and life. Sunzi believed that the Five Phases, four seasons, and the waxing and waning of the sun and moon have no constancy—how much less can the forms of warfare remain fixed?

Mei Yaochen said: All these exemplify warfare’s adaptation to the enemy.

Wang Zhe said: All these illustrate the transformations of warfare, which follow no single Dao.

Zhang Yu said: The waxing and waning of the Five Phases, the succession of the four seasons, and the fullness and decline of the sun and moon—all resemble the unfixed nature of military momentum.

*End of Commentary on Sunzi, Scroll Six*
*(Textual notes: #1 "必" originally written as "不", amended according to Guo Huairuo’s *Eleven Commentaries on Sunzi*; #2 "大" originally "生", amended per Song edition; #3 "分" originally "忿", amended per Song edition; #4 "敌" originally omitted, supplied per Song edition; #5 "钓" originally "伪", amended per Song edition; #6 "无" originally "有", amended per Song edition.)*

*Commentary on Sunzi, Scroll Seven*
**“Military Strife”**

Cao Cao said: When two armies contend for victory.

Li Quan said: “Strife” means pursuing advantage. Only when emptiness and fullness are determined may one contend for profit with others.

Wang Xi said: “Strife” means contending for advantage—gaining it brings victory. One should first assess priorities, calculate indirect and direct routes, and prevent the enemy from exploiting one’s fatigue.

Zhang Yu said: The title “Military Strife” refers to two armies facing each other to contend for advantage. Only after discerning the enemy’s and one’s own emptiness and fullness may one contend for victory, hence its placement after “Emptiness and Fullness.”

Sunzi said: In all methods of employing troops, the general receives the command from the ruler,
Li Quan said: Receiving the ruler’s mandate.

executes the temple’s victorious calculations, and reverently carries out Heaven’s punishment.
Zhang Yu said: Receiving the ruler’s command to punish rebels.

assembles the army and gathers the masses,
Cao Cao said: Mobilizing the state’s people, organizing ranks, selecting officers, and establishing camps to form battle arrays.

Mei Yaochen said: Gathering the state’s masses to form an army.

Wang Xi said: A great state’s three armies total thirty-seven thousand five hundred men; if all conscripts are levied, the total reaches seventy-five thousand. This is what is meant by “assembling the army and gathering the masses.”

Zhang Yu said: Uniting the state’s people into an army and assembling troops into formations.

and encamps after engaging in accord.
Cao Cao said: The army gate is called the “accord gate,” the left and right gates are “banner gates,” a camp formed with chariots is a “wheeled gate,” and one formed with men is a “human gate.” When two armies face each other, it is called “engaging in accord.”

Li Quan said: Intermingling and harmonizing. After assembling the army, strengths and weaknesses, courage and timidity, length and brevity, front and rear are interspersed and balanced, combining forces before uniting the camps to contend with the enemy.

Du Mu said: The *Rites of Zhou* uses banners for the left and right accord gates. Zheng Sinong said: The army gate is called the “accord gate,” now known as the camp gate, marked by two banners to order entry and exit. “Engaging” means facing the enemy’s camp and encamping with accord gates aligned.

Jia Lin said: “Encamping” means halting. Soldiers intermingle and harmonize, halting in camp before moving to seize advantage.

Mei Yaochen said: The army gate is the accord gate; two armies face each other and encamp.

He [Yan]shi said: With accord gates in sight, the moment before battle is joined is the most difficult task for military strategists.

Zhang Yu said: The army gate is the accord gate; when facing the enemy’s camp and encamping, the gates align. Alternatively, it means harmonizing with superiors and subordinates before deploying troops to establish camps. Thus, Wuzi said: “Without harmony in the state, one cannot deploy the army; without harmony in the army, one cannot deploy formations.”

Nothing is more difficult than military strife.
Cao Cao said: From the initial receipt of the command to engaging in accord, military strife is difficult.

Du Mu said: Difficult in contending for advantage and harm.

Mei Yaochen said: From receiving the command to this point is the most difficult.

Zhang Yu said: Contending for advantage while facing the enemy is the most difficult thing in the world.

The difficulty of military strife lies in turning the circuitous into the direct and misfortune into fortune.
Cao Cao said: Feigning distance to hasten the journey and arriving before the enemy.

Du Mu said: To contend for advantage, one must first present the circuitous as direct and misfortune as fortune, deceiving the enemy to make them careless before swiftly advancing.

Chen Hao said: Assembling the army and gathering the masses, engaging in accord and uniting—all follow established systems, but military strife is the most difficult. Without knowing how to turn the circuitous into the direct and misfortune into fortune, one cannot contend with the enemy.

Jia Lin said: Marching the entire army to contend for advantageous terrain and seizing it first—if one fails to secure the ground, one cedes victory to the enemy, making this the most difficult.

Du You said: The enemy’s route is originally circuitous, and the misfortune lies in its distance; thus, one must first occupy terrain of strategic advantage. Hence it is said: “turning misfortune into fortune.”

Mei Yaochen said: The difficulty lies in transforming the circuitous into the near and misfortune into fortune.

Wang Xi said: Cao Cao said: Feigning distance to hasten the journey and arriving before the enemy. I say: Feigning distance means making the enemy unprepared to march, or dispatching an extraordinary force via a hidden route.

He [Yan]shi said: When the target state’s roads traverse perilous mountains and are circuitous and distant, to contend for advantage, one must divide forces and dispatch extraordinary troops, following local guides via direct routes to exploit the enemy’s unpreparedness and strike swiftly. Though this entails the misfortune of hazardous terrain, the advantage is gained quickly. For example, when Zhong Hui invaded Shu, Deng Ai took an extraordinary route and arrived first, catching Shu unprepared and forcing its surrender. Thus, the text below states: “Without local guides, one cannot gain the advantage of terrain.”

Zhang Yu said: Transforming the circuitous into the direct and misfortune into fortune—this is the difficulty of military strife. Thus, by making the route appear circuitous and enticing the enemy with advantage, one sets out later but arrives first. This is the strategy of knowing the circuitous and the direct.

Li Quan said: Thus, by making the route appear circuitous, one feigns a lack of haste, setting out later but arriving first. Employing such tactics in warfare transforms misfortune into advantage.

Du Mu said: The preceding explanation states that "turning the circuitous into the direct" means presenting the enemy with a deceptively distant route, causing their vigilance to slacken. Further enticing them with advantage distracts their focus. Then, by doubling the pace and advancing with all speed, one strikes unexpectedly, thus setting out later but arriving first to seize the contested strategic point. When Qin attacked Han at Yanyu, King Zhao of Zhao dispatched Zhao She to relieve it. After advancing thirty *li* from Handan, Zhao She issued a military order: "Whoever remonstrates on military matters shall be put to death." The Qin army was west of Wu’an, and as their drums and war cries resounded, the tiles on the roofs of Wu’an trembled. A scout in the Zhao camp urged an immediate rescue of Wu’an, but Zhao She executed him on the spot. He fortified his position and remained stationary for twenty-eight days, further strengthening his ramparts. When a Qin spy arrived, Zhao She treated him well and sent him back. The spy reported to Qin, and the Qin general rejoiced, saying: "Though they halted just thirty *li* from their capital, they have not advanced and have only reinforced their defenses. Yanyu is no longer Zhao’s territory." After dispatching the Qin spy, Zhao She ordered his troops to don armor and march swiftly, covering the distance in two days and one night. He then stationed skilled archers fifty *li* from Yanyu. When the Qin forces learned of this, they advanced in full armor. A soldier said: "Whoever seizes the northern mountain first will prevail." Zhao She dispatched ten thousand men to occupy it, and when the Qin forces arrived to contest it, they could not dislodge them. Zhao She then launched a full assault, crushing the Qin army and relieving Yanyu.

Jia Lin said: Though the enemy’s route is originally direct, one can make it appear circuitous by deploying decoy forces or offering minor advantages on alternate paths, preventing the enemy from rushing to the contested point.

Mei Yaochen said: By lengthening the route and enticing with advantage, one lulls the enemy. By setting out later but arriving first, one seizes the initiative. He who understands this employs the strategy of transforming the circuitous into the direct and misfortune into fortune.

He Yan said: The "circuitous route" refers to the intended path. By dividing forces and launching a surprise attack, one presents the intended route as circuitous, creating a deceptive situation to lure the enemy. Enticing them with minor gains distracts them, allowing the surprise force—though setting out later—to arrive first. Thus, contending for advantage requires assessing the dynamics of the circuitous and the direct to execute a surprise maneuver. Hence, the text below states: "Variations of division and concentration are as swift as the wind."

Zhang Yu said: In terrain of strategic importance, seizing it ensures victory. To contend for advantageous ground nearby, one first leads the army far away, then entices the enemy with minor gains, causing them to neither expect one’s advance nor resist the offered advantage. Thus, one sets out later but arrives first. This is what is meant by "turning the circuitous into the direct and misfortune into fortune." Zhao She seized the northern mountain to defeat the Qin army, just as Guo Huai stationed troops at Beiyuan to repel Zhuge Liang. He who arrives first by setting out later understands the calculations and the strategy of turning the circuitous into the direct.

Thus, contending in warfare may bring advantage or peril.

Cao Cao said: The adept turn it to advantage; the inept invite peril.

Li Quan said: In warfare, a skilled general brings advantage; an unskilled one invites peril.

Du Mu said: The adept are those who deliberate carefully.

Jia Lin said: If one’s army arrives first and secures advantageous terrain, it brings advantage. If the enemy has already occupied the ground and one’s forces rush to contest it, the enemy will be at ease while one’s own troops are fatigued—this is the path to peril.

Mei Yaochen said: Contending in warfare may bring either advantage or peril. (Another version reads: "Contending in warfare brings advantage; contending with many brings peril.")

He Yan said: This further explains that leading an army, driving the three forces into competition with the enemy to vie for a single day’s victory, brings advantage if successful but peril if not. Such actions must not be undertaken lightly.

Zhang Yu said: The wise contend for advantage; the foolish invite peril. The enlightened understand the circuitous and the direct; the ignorant remain oblivious.

If the entire army contends for advantage, it will not arrive in time.

Cao Cao said: It will be too late.

Li Quan said: The baggage train slows the march.

Jia Lin said: When employing troops, one must pursue advantage. If the entire army rushes to contend for it directly, the distance—whether near or far—makes swift arrival difficult. One may instead secretly devise a stratagem to lengthen the enemy’s route, causing them to misjudge one’s intentions, so that one arrives first while the enemy lags behind.

Du You said: It will be too late. If the entire army marches to contend for advantage, the roads will be too congested to keep pace.

Mei Yaochen said: Mobilizing all the army’s resources slows the advance.

Wang Xi said: Due to the baggage train.

Zhang Yu said: Advancing with the entire army slows the march and prevents timely arrival.

If the army abandons its supplies to contend for advantage, the baggage train will be lost.

Cao Cao said: Abandoning the baggage train risks its loss.

Li Quan said: Forsaking the baggage train depletes military resources.

Du Mu said: Marching with the entire army’s equipment causes sluggishness and delays, preventing timely arrival. Abandoning the baggage train and advancing with light forces risks its loss.

Jia Lin said: One fears the enemy may discover and cut off the supply lines.

Du You said: Leaving behind supplies and advancing with light forces risks the enemy exploiting the vulnerability to raid the rear, causing the entire baggage train to be lost.

Mei Yaochen said: Abandoning the army’s resources means losing the baggage train.

Wang Xi’s note is the same as Cao Cao’s. He Yan’s note is the same as Du You’s.

Zhang Yu said: Leaving behind heavy equipment and advancing with light forces risks the baggage train being plundered by the enemy, hence its loss.

Thus, by rolling up the armor and advancing without rest, day and night,

Cao Cao said: Without respite, the troops will be exhausted.

Marching double the usual distance without rest, contending for advantage a hundred *li* away, will result in the capture of all three generals.

Du You said: If one disregards the two concerns above and seeks only speed, rolling up the armor and binding the weapons, advancing by stealth under cover of night—should the enemy discern the situation and intercept the march, the generals of the three armies will be taken captive. Such was the case when the Duke of Qin raided Zheng, and all three commanders were captured.

The vigorous arrive first, the weary lag behind; by this method, only one in ten reaches the objective.

Cao Cao said: Contending for advantage a hundred *li* away is unwise; all three generals will be taken.

Li Quan said: If one marches a hundred and twenty *li* in a day, this is double the usual distance, yet not twice the effort. If this is done, the strong will arrive first, the weary later. With few vigorous soldiers and many exhausted, perhaps one in ten may reach the destination ahead, while the rest straggle behind. How, then, could the three generals avoid capture? When Emperor Wu of Wei pursued Liu Bei, his forces marched three hundred *li* in a day and night, yet Zhuge Liang remarked that the bolt from a strong crossbow, at its furthest reach, cannot pierce the thinnest silk—meaning it has no force left. Thus came the defeat at Red Cliffs. When Pang Juan pursued Sun Bin, he perished at Ma Ling; the principle is the same.

Du Mu said: This explanation is incomplete. Generally, an army marches thirty *li* in a day, which is called a *she*. Marching double the distance means covering two *she*; to travel a hundred *li* in a day and night without rest is to march double the distance for one *she*. If one contends for advantage in this manner, the troops will be exhausted, and all three generals will surely be captured. As for the method of one in ten arriving: when compelled to contend for advantage, select the most vigorous from every ten men to advance first, while the rest follow in succession. From ten thousand men, choose a thousand; let them arrive at dawn, with the remainder coming later—some by mid-morning, others by afternoon—so that none exhaust their strength, and all arrive in unbroken sequence, maintaining contact with the vanguard. When contending for advantage, one must seize a critical position; even a thousand men can hold it against the enemy until reinforcements arrive. Emperor Taizong once secured Wu Gong with three thousand five hundred cavalry, and Dou Jiande’s army of a hundred and eighty thousand could not advance—this demonstrates the principle.

Chen Hao said: Du’s explanation represents a separate tactical approach, not the meaning of "one in ten arriving." It refers to contending for advantage a hundred *li* away: the vigorous arrive first, the weary later, so that only one in ten reaches the destination, while nine are exhausted. The one who arrives is the vigorous one.

Jia Lin said: When the road is long and the men weary, their strength spent from haste, one exhausts oneself while the enemy remains at ease—how could one not be struck down?

Du You said: Contending for advantage a hundred *li* away is unwise; all three generals will be captured. The strong and weak do not wait for one another, so only one in ten reaches the army.

*Pi* is read as *fatigued*.

Mei Yaochen said: An army typically marches thirty *li* before halting. Now, to march a hundred *li* day and night without rest—this is why the three generals will be captured. Why? The distance is great, the vigorous few, the weary many; only one in ten arrives. The three generals command the three armies.

Wang Xi said: *Pi* means *exhausted*. This speaks of the way of contending for advantage: it is better to do so nearby than far away. The force of a gale wanes until it cannot lift a feather; the bolt from a strong crossbow, at its furthest reach, cannot pierce the thinnest silk. If one marches day and night to cover a hundred *li* in pursuit of advantage, even if one-tenth of the vigorous arrive, they will already be worn out. Should the enemy strike our weary forces with their rested ones, we will surely be defeated without a battle. Thus, Sima Xuanwang said: "Marching double the distance without rest—this is what those who understand warfare avoid." Some ask: Zhao She also rolled up his armor and advanced, covering the distance in two days and one night, yet he defeated Qin—why? The answer: Zhao She had long conserved his strength and accumulated power, reinforced his ramparts, and sent spies to feign weakness and embolden the enemy. Qin did not anticipate his arrival, and his troops were resolute. Moreover, Zhao She encamped fifty *li* from Ye Yu; by the time Qin learned of this and dispatched forces, two or three days would have passed. When they arrived, they would have endured the fatigue of a fifty-*li* march, while Zhao She’s men had rested for two or three days, their vigor undiminished. He then led them into perilous terrain, seized the heights at Gao Yang, and employed both orthodox and unorthodox tactics—how could he not prevail?

He Yan said: When the three generals seek advantage through surprise, abandoning the army’s supplies and rolling up their armor in haste—if they march a hundred *li* day and night without rest, only one in ten of the vigorous may arrive. Weary and outnumbered, even if we strike, victory is not assured; if defeated, all three generals will be captured. This shows how deeply the *Martial* cautions against such actions.

Zhang Yu said: *Juan jia* means *all in armor*. Advancing fully armored refers to both light and heavy forces moving together. Generally, an army marches thirty *li* before halting; exceeding sixty *li* is double the distance, and marching day and night without rest is *jian xing*. To contend for advantage a hundred *li* away, with light troops in front and baggage behind, men and horses weary, the thirsty unable to drink, the hungry unable to eat—should they suddenly encounter the enemy, they would face rested forces while exhausted, sated forces while starving, and their vanguard and rearguard would be unable to support one another. Thus, the commanders of the three armies would surely be captured. Such was the case when the Jin captured the three Qin generals. Among the light troops, perhaps one in ten vigorous and swift men arrives first, while the other nine are exhausted and lag behind—how much more so for the heavy forces? How do we know both light and heavy forces are involved? The following passage states: "Contending for advantage fifty *li* away, half will arrive." If only light troops were involved, a fifty-*li* march in a day would not be far—how could half fail to arrive?

Thus, heavy forces must necessarily accompany the march. Contending for advantage fifty *li* away, the vanguard general will be thwarted, and by rule only half will arrive. Cao Cao notes: "Thwarted, as in defeated." Li Quan says: "Over a hundred *li*, one in ten men arrives; over fifty *li*, five in ten arrive—yet the army’s might is broken, and capture is not the only outcome." This speaks of how shorter distances prevent exhaustion. Du Mu states: "Half arriving means selecting five of ten vigorous men to advance first." Jia Lin says: "Vanguard, as in those who go ahead." Du You says: "Thwarted, as in defeated. The general of the forward army has already been routed by the enemy." Mei Yaochen says: "Even if five of ten arrive, the distance is still too great to secure victory." Wang Xi says: "The peril of exhaustion is reduced by more than half, but defeat is still suffered." Zhang Yu says: "If a fifty-*li* march—where half arrive—still breaks the army’s might, how much worse over a hundred *li*? 'Thwarting the vanguard general' refers to the forward army advancing first."

Someone asked: "When Emperor Taizong of Tang pursued Song Jingang, he marched over two hundred *li* in a day and night and still achieved victory—why?" The answer: "The circumstances were similar, but the strategic conditions differed. Jingang had already been defeated, and his forces’ morale was broken. Pressing the attack to annihilation would have pacified Hedong immediately; delay would have allowed the enemy to regroup. This is why Taizong disregarded fatigue and pursued relentlessly. The principles of contending for advantage as laid out by Sunzi differ from this case."

Contending for advantage thirty *li* away, two-thirds will arrive. Cao Cao says: "Shorter distances mean more arrive, so there is no risk of annihilation." Li Quan says: "Nearby marches do not exhaust, so there are no casualties." Du Mu says: "Within thirty *li*, six or seven of ten men can advance first." The rule is not stated here, as it can be inferred from the preceding passage. Du You says: "Shorter distances mean more arrive, so annihilation is not mentioned—though victory or defeat remains uncertain." In ancient warfare, armies marched thirty *li* a day, with infantry and cavalry supporting one another. Now, rushing toward advantage without such coordination, only two-thirds arrive. Mei Yaochen says: "Shorter distances mean more arrive, perhaps allowing for victory." Wang Xi says: "When the strategic situation demands contention, this may be unavoidable. Though only two-thirds arrive, these are the elite forces, not yet exhausted—so defeat cannot be assumed, which is why the rule is not stated." Zhang Yu says: "Shorter distances prevent exhaustion, and the majority arrive without losing formation or depleting the strength of men and horses—perhaps allowing for victory."

The above three cases all refer to the entire army contending for advantage. Thus, an army without baggage trains perishes, without provisions perishes, without supplies perishes. Cao Cao says: "Lacking these three is the path to destruction." Li Quan says: "Without baggage trains, supplies are lacking. Yuan Shao had a hundred thousand troops, but Cao Cao, following Xun You’s counsel, burned his baggage and defeated him at Guandu. Without provisions, even fortified cities are worthless—food is paramount. The Master said: 'Sufficient food, sufficient arms, and the people’s trust.' Thus, the Red Eyebrows’ million-strong army, lacking food, surrendered at Xingyang with their sovereign bound. Therefore, those skilled in warfare first cultivate and then fight." Without supplies, resources are depleted. "When Emperor Gaozu lacked Guanzhong, when Emperor Guangwu lacked Henei, when Cao Cao lacked Yanzhou—each fled in defeat, unable to recover." Du Mu says: "Baggage trains include weapons and soldiers’ gear; supplies refer to goods and wealth." Chen Hao says: "This explains the difficulty of contending for advantage with the entire army." Mei Yaochen says: "None of these three can be lacking—thus, one cannot contend for advantage with the entire army." Wang Xi says: "Supplies include firewood, salt, vegetables, and materials—armies rely on these three to sustain themselves and cannot lightly abandon them." Zhang Yu says: "Without baggage trains, equipment and tools are lacking; without provisions, rations are insufficient; without supplies, resources are depleted—all are paths to destruction. These three refer to contending for advantage with the entire army."

Those who do not know the schemes of the feudal lords cannot form alliances in advance. Cao Cao says: "Those who do not know the enemy’s plans cannot forge alliances." Li Quan says: "To prepare is to be ready. Knowing the enemy’s intentions requires securing alliances." Du Mu says: "This is incorrect. 'In advance' means beforehand; 'alliances' refers to engaging in battle. One must first know the feudal lords’ schemes before joining battle; without such knowledge, one cannot engage." Chen Hao says: "Cao’s interpretation—that one cannot secure external aid without first knowing the enemy’s plans—is also valid. Both explanations are plausible." Mei Yaochen says: "Without knowing the enemy state’s schemes, one cannot form alliances with neighboring states for support." Zhang Yu says: "Only by first knowing the true conditions of the feudal lords can one forge alliances; ignorance of their schemes risks betrayal. Neighboring states as allies are also part of contending for advantage, as the following passage states: 'The first to arrive and gain the support of the realm holds the central position.'"

Those who do not know the forms of mountains, forests, defiles, marshes, and wetlands cannot march an army. Cao Cao says: "Lofty and towering are mountains; dense clusters of trees are forests; trenches and chasms are defiles; alternating high and low terrain is rough ground; waterlogged grasslands are marshes; where waters gather and do not flow are wetlands. Without prior knowledge of the terrain an army occupies and the forms of mountains and rivers, one cannot advance." Mei Yaochen says: "The forms of mountains, forests, defiles, and the conditions of marshes and wetlands must be thoroughly examined." Zhang Yu says: "Lofty and towering are mountains; dense clusters of trees are forests; pits and trenches are defiles; alternating high and low terrain is rough ground; waterlogged grasslands are marshes; where waters gather and do not flow are wetlands. Only by fully understanding all such terrain can one contend for advantage and march an army."

Without local guides, one cannot secure the advantages of terrain.

Li Quan says: "When entering enemy territory, one fears narrow mountain passes and river gorges, muddy earth, and unfavorable wells and springs. Employing guides allows one to gain the advantages of terrain." The *Yi* states: "Pursuing deer without a forester"—this is its meaning.

Du Mu says: Master Guanzi says: "All military leaders must first thoroughly examine maps. The dangers of narrow passes, the depths of rivers where wheels sink, the locations of famous mountains, connecting valleys, great rivers, hills, mounds, and plateaus, the luxuriance of grasses, forests, reeds, and rushes, the distances of roads, the sizes of cities and towns, the sites of famous settlements, abandoned towns, and cultivated lands—all must be fully known. The intersecting entry and exit points of terrain must be entirely committed to memory, only then can one avoid losing the advantages of terrain."

Duke Wei, Li Jing, says: "All bandits and raiders excel at ambushes. One must select courageous men and discerning officers, and employ local guides to secretly traverse mountains and forests, concealing their sounds and obscuring their tracks. Some may carve beastly footprints and retrace their steps midway; others may don the guise of small birds and lie concealed in thickets. Then, straining their ears to listen afar and raising their eyes to see deeply, they concentrate their wisdom to assess the situation and focus their minds to observe signs. Seeing water marks, they know when the enemy crossed; watching trees sway, they can discern the enemy’s advance. Thus, beacon fires must be tended with utmost care, and banners must be uniform and consistent. Rewards and punishments must be weighty and unerring; punishments and executions must be severe and unrelenting. When the enemy moves or rests, we are prepared; when the enemy schemes, we know it first."

Chen Hao said: All these advantages of terrain cannot be known without employing local guides. Du You said: If one does not employ local guides to lead the army, the convenience of roads cannot be obtained. Mei Yaochen said: The orientation of hills, plateaus, plains, and lowlands, the twists and straightaways of cities and roads—none can be known without guides. He Shi said: The *Xiangdao Lüe* states: A hunter without a mountain warden cannot assess the terrain’s feasibility and will wander into the forest in vain, never catching the deer. A campaigner without a local guide cannot discern the twists and straightaways of roads and will reach the borderlands in vain, never capturing the enemy. To march on a mission of punitive expedition, rushing into unfamiliar lands where civilizing influence has not reached and courier routes are severed, and to plunge deep into their obstacles—is this not perilous? Our lone army advances while they lie in wait in secrecy, the disparity between guest and host already vast. Moreover, they rely on deceit and employ manifold ruses to mislead us. If we advance recklessly without calculation, charging headlong into danger, scaling perilous heights risks blockage and rupture; marching by day risks sudden enemy assaults; halting by night risks false alarms. Caught unprepared in haste, we fall into their snare—this is to lead an army of bears and tigers and cast them into death’s maw. How then can we breach their ramparts or sweep away their cunning lairs? Thus, the mountains and rivers, ridges and hills of the enemy’s land that can be used to set up defenses; the forests, reeds, and lush grasses that can conceal troops; the distances of roads; the size of cities and towns; the breadth of settlements; the fertility of fields; the depth of ditches; the abundance of stores; the number of soldiers and chariots; the sturdiness of weapons—all must be thoroughly known. Then the enemy lies within our grasp, and capture is assured. Formerly, Zhang Qian was sent as an envoy to Daxia and detained long among the Xiongnu. He guided the army to advantageous positions and knew where water and pasture were plentiful, so his troops suffered neither hunger nor thirst. This is how one secures such advantages. When employing local guides, if the army captures them as prisoners, one must guard against their schemes, for they may secretly harbor treacherous plans to mislead and deceive. One must scrutinize their expressions, observe their intentions, and cross-verify the words of several individuals. Only when their accounts remain consistent from beginning to end can they be trusted. Reward them generously to inspire gratitude; enrich their households to bind their hearts. Then they become our own people and will not betray us. Yet it is better to have long-nurtured, capable individuals. Even those merely familiar with the routes need not be locals to be employed. Still, select trusted, wise, and courageous men to accompany them, and every detail will be examined, every trail followed without error.

Zhang Yu said: The smoothness or ruggedness of mountains and rivers, the twists and straightaways of roads—only by employing local guides to lead the way can one know their advantages and contend for victory. When Wu attacked Lu, the people of Zeng guided them to capture Wucheng—this is an example. Thus, warfare is established through deception. Du Mu said: Deceive the enemy so they do not know our true condition, and then victory can be established. Mei Yaochen said: Without cunning, nothing can be accomplished. Wang Xi said: This means turning circuitous paths into straight ones, turning adversity into advantage. He Shi said: Displaying false strength to mislead the enemy. Zhang Yu said: Deception is fundamental; if the enemy cannot discern our shifts between the extraordinary and the orthodox, we can establish ourselves. Move when there is advantage. Du Mu said: Advantage means acting only when opportunity arises. Mei Yaochen said: Without advantage, one cannot act. Wang Xi said: Entice them. He Shi said: Strike only when the enemy is vulnerable. Zhang Yu said: Act when advantage appears, not recklessly. As the *Zuo Zhuan* states: "The Three Armies move when there is advantage." This is to vary with division and union. Cao Cao said: The army divides and unites in response to the enemy. Li Quan said: Employ deceit to exploit advantage; divide or unite to create shifting forms. Du Mu said: Division and union mean separating or combining to confuse the enemy. Observe their response to our maneuvers, then adapt to secure victory. Chen Hao said: Alternate between union and division, shifting accordingly. Meng Shi said: Military strategy relies on deception to entice the enemy’s mind; division and union are the arts of variation. Mei Yaochen and Wang Xi concur with Cao Cao’s annotation. Zhang Yu said: Whether dispersing our forces or concentrating our strength, all variations arise in response to the enemy’s movements. Some say: Variation refers to the interplay of the extraordinary and the orthodox, confounding the enemy. Thus, the *Art of War* of Duke Wei states: When troops are dispersed, unity becomes extraordinary; when united, dispersion becomes extraordinary. Three commands and five admonitions, three dispersals and three unions—all return to the orthodox.

Thus, its swiftness is like the wind. Cao Cao said: Strike where it is empty. Li Quan said: Advance and retreat. Its coming leaves no trace; its retreat is exceedingly swift. Mei Yaochen said: Its arrival leaves no form or trace. Wang Xi said: Swiftly exploit emptiness. He Shi concurs with Mei Yaochen’s annotation. Zhang Yu said: Its arrival is swift and violent, sweeping all before it. Its slowness is like the forest. Cao Cao said: When no advantage is seen. Li Quan said: March in orderly ranks. Du Mu said: *Xu* means slow. When moving slowly, one must maintain formations like a forest to guard against enemy ambush. Meng Shi said: When moving slowly, one must maintain formations like a forest to prevent surprise attacks. Du You said: Without advantage, do not advance. Like the wind stirring the forest, the small may sway, but the great remains unmoved. Mei Yaochen said: Like a forest, solemn and unbroken. Wang Xi said: Solemn and orderly.
