Zhang Yu said: "Xu" means leisurely. To advance at a leisurely pace, as solemn and unbroken as a forest, signifies that no advantage is yet seen. *Wei Liaozi* states: The heavy are like mountains and forests; the light are like gunpowder and wildfire.

**Swift as fire when raiding,**
Cao Cao said: Swift.
Li Quan said: Like fire scorching the plains, leaving no grass unburned.
Du Mu said: Fierce and unstoppable.
Jia Lin said: Invading and plundering the enemy’s land is like wildfire—once set, it cannot be reversed.
Zhang Yu said: As the *Book of Songs* says: "Fierce as fire, none dare withstand me." The momentum is like a raging blaze—who would dare oppose it?

**Unmoving as mountains,**
Cao Cao said: To hold fast.
Li Quan said: To halt the chariots.
Du Mu said: Calm and towering, unshakable.
Jia Lin said: When no advantage is seen and the enemy seeks to deceive, remain unmoved, steadfast as a mountain.
Mei Yaochen said: Too steep to assail.
Wang Xi said: To stand firm.
He Shi said: Still as a mountain’s quiet weight.
Zhang Yu said: This is how one maintains composure. *Xunzi’s "Discourse on Military Affairs"* states: "Encamped in a circle, aligned with square order, one is like bedrock—those who strike it shatter their horns." When unmoving, one is as immovable as stone; those who challenge it break themselves.

**Obscure as shadow,**
Li Quan said: Its force is as unfathomable as darkness, where no form can be discerned.
Du Mu said: Like dark clouds veiling the sky, hiding sun, moon, and stars.
Mei Yaochen said: Hidden and inscrutable.
Wang Xi said: Concealed in form.
He Shi said: Secret and unpredictable.
Zhang Yu said: Like clouds obscuring the heavens, where no celestial sign can be seen.

**Striking like thunder,**
Li Quan said: A fury unleashed.
Du Mu said: Like a bolt from the void—nowhere to flee.
Jia Lin said: Its movement is too swift to counter.
*Taigong* said: "Swift as thunder leaves no time to cover one’s ears."
Mei Yaochen said: Too fast to evade.
Wang Xi said: Arriving before one can prepare.
He Shi said: Concealed strategy erupts thus.
Zhang Yu said: Like sudden thunder—nowhere to hide. Thus *Taigong* said: "Swift as thunder leaves no time to cover one’s ears; fast as lightning leaves no time to blink."

**Plunder villages, divide forces,**
Cao Cao said: Exploit the enemy to secure victory.
Li Quan said: When raiding, split forces into multiple columns to guard against the unexpected.
Du Mu said: In enemy villages and settlements, where no defenses stand, livestock and grain are easily seized. Divide troops in rotation so all may partake—none should monopolize the spoils. Thus, whether strong or weak, all will vie for advantage.
Chen Hao said: Villages and hamlets are scattered; where defenses are lacking, dispatch troops to plunder.
*(Note: "Plunder villages" may also read "direct forces.")*
Jia Lin said: Troops cannot be dispatched by word; thus, banners direct their course. Units cannot be commanded by speech; thus, signals divide their ranks. Seizing the enemy’s formation to shape one’s own—this is the essence of disciplined training.
Mei Yaochen said: To reward the soldiers.
Wang Xi said: Direct forces toward their objective, dividing them accordingly. *(Note: "Xiang" here carries the sound of "direction.")*
He Shi said: Distribute plunder among the troops.
Zhang Yu said: The way of war generally requires provisioning from the enemy. Yet villages hold little store—thus, troops must be divided to plunder widely to meet needs.

**Expand territory, share gains,**
Cao Cao said: Divide the enemy’s advantages.
Li Quan said: When seizing enemy land, distribute control of strategic points.
Du Mu said: *Kuò* means to open. When territory is expanded, divide and grant it to the meritorious. Han Xin told the King of Han: "Xiang Yu awards those due titles by carving seals—yet he hesitates to bestow them. If Your Majesty reverses this, granting cities to your generals, none under heaven will be beyond your grasp." *The Three Strategies* states: "Divide conquered lands."
Chen Hao said: When land is taken, garrison troops to cultivate it, thus dividing the enemy’s resources.
Jia Lin said: *Kuò* means to assess. Evaluate the enemy’s strategic terrain and divide its advantages.
Mei Yaochen said: Reward the meritorious.
Wang Xi said: Survey the terrain to secure advantageous positions, preventing the enemy from monopolizing them.
Zhang Yu said: In open and level terrain, divide forces to hold key points, denying them to the enemy. *(Alternatively: Grant conquered land to the meritorious.)* Yet considering the preceding and following passages, this interpretation seems unlikely.

**Weigh circumstances before acting,**
Cao Cao said: Assess the enemy before moving.
Li Quan said: *Quán* is the scale’s weight. If the enemy’s strength differs from ours by even a fraction, then act. To move first is to be the guest—difficult; to move after is to be the host—easier. The *Taiyi Dunjia* calculations clarify the ease of decisive action.
Du Mu said: Like a scale with its weight suspended—once balanced, then act.
He Shi concurs with Du Mu’s annotation.
Zhang Yu said: Like suspending a weight from a scale—know the measure before acting. *Wei Liaozi* states: "Weigh the enemy’s strength, scrutinize the general’s ability, then strike." This means assessing the enemy’s weight and the general’s wisdom before committing.

**He who first understands the calculus of detours and direct routes prevails—this is the method of military contention.**
Li Quan said: The calculus of circuitous and direct paths. Fatigue, hunger, and cold arise on the march.
Du Mu said: In military contention, one must first calculate the far and near, the circuitous and direct—only then can victory be assured. This calculation must be as precise as a scale, not erring by even a fraction, before one can act and triumph.

This is the method of prevailing in military contention.

Mei Yaochen said: Weighing advantages and harms before acting, victory lies in foreknowledge of the distant and near.
Wang Xi said: Assessing the enemy’s weight and moving accordingly, while also knowing the calculus of detours and direct routes—this is the certain path to victory.
Zhang Yu said: In all contention for advantage, one must first measure the circuitousness and directness of the roads. Only after careful scrutiny and assessment does one act, thereby avoiding the hardships of fatigue, hunger, and cold. Moreover, advance and retreat, speed and slowness, are timed without losing the moment—hence victory.

*The Military Regulations* states:
Mei Yaochen said: An ancient military classic.
Wang Xi said: An old military text.

When voices cannot be heard, drums and bells are used.
Du You said: Bells include *zheng* and *duo*, whose sounds serve as auditory signals.
Mei Yaochen said: To command the ear with authority—its sounds must be clear.
Wang Xi said: Drums, *pi*, *zheng*, and *duo*—whether halting or advancing, hastening or slowing, sparse or dense—all follow their rhythms.

When sights cannot be seen, banners and flags are used.
Du You said: Gazing upon their movements serves as visual signals.
Mei Yaochen said: To command the eye with authority—its colors must be distinct.
Wang Xi said: They mark the ranks and files, ensuring order.

Now, drums and bells, banners and flags serve to unify the ears and eyes of the army.
Li Quan said: Drums signal advance, bells signal retreat; banners reward, flags punish. Ears listen to drums and bells, eyes watch banners and flags—thus order is maintained.
When the brave and the timid alike cannot advance or retreat at will, it is because the drums and flags are precise.
Zhang Yu said: When an army is vast and its positions broad, front and rear are distant, ears and eyes cannot connect. Thus, the sounds of drums and bells make them hear one another, while the forms of banners and flags make them see. When sight and sound are uniform, even a million troops advance and retreat as one. Hence it is said: "Fighting a multitude is like fighting a few"—this is the principle of *xing* and *ming*.

Once the army is unified, the brave cannot advance alone, nor the timid retreat alone—this is the method of commanding multitudes.
Du Mu said: Banners issue commands, flags respond to signals. Flags here refer to today’s signal flags.

Military law states: "Those who fail to advance when ordered, or fail to retreat when ordered, shall be executed."
When Wu Qi fought the Qin, before the battle was joined, a single soldier, unable to contain his valor, charged forward, captured two heads, and returned. Wu Qi had him executed. His officers protested: "This is a skilled warrior—he should not be slain." Wu Qi replied: "He may be skilled, but he disobeyed orders." And so he executed him.

Mei Yaochen said: "Unifying the ears and eyes" means ensuring that the army’s sight and hearing are aligned and undivided. When the drum sounds, advance; when the bell sounds, halt; when the banner waves right, turn right; when it waves left, turn left. Neither the brave nor the timid may act alone.
Wang Xi said: It is through drums, bells, banners, and flags that the multitudes—brave and timid alike—advance and retreat in unison.
Zhang Yu said: Soldiers focus their minds entirely on the signals of drums, bells, banners, and flags. When ordered to advance, they advance; when ordered to retreat, they retreat. Any who disobey must be executed. Thus it is said: "Advancing when not ordered is as culpable as retreating when not ordered."

*Wei Liaozi* states: "When drums sound and banners wave, those who charge first are often men of great strength and valor—yet the fault lies with the general." This means that rewarding those who seize the initiative risks disrupting unity. Hence, night battles employ many torches and drums, while daytime battles employ many banners and flags—to alter the army’s ears and eyes.
Li Quan said: Torches and drums guide sight and hearing at night; banners and flags direct movements by day.
Du Mu said: The army’s ears and eyes shift with the changes of banners, flags, torches, and drums.

Some ask: What is the purpose of using many torches and drums in night battles? After dark, there can be no open-field formations or prearranged engagements. If one raids an enemy camp with drums and torches, it only alerts the enemy’s ears and illuminates their eyes—turning harm upon oneself. What is the principle here?

The answer: A profound question indeed. This is Sunzi’s subtle intent. Night battles occur when the enemy raids our camp, and we are forced to engage. The method lies in the art of encampment, which resembles small-scale formations. As the records say: "When stationary, it is a camp; when moving, it is a formation." Within a great formation, smaller formations are nested; within a great camp, smaller camps are nested. The armies of the front, rear, left, and right each have their own encircling camps. The general’s camp lies at the center, surrounded by the others, arranged in interlocking rings, their curves and angles aligned like the constellations of the celestial wall. Camps are spaced no more than a hundred paces apart, no fewer than fifty, with paths connecting them, allowing troops to deploy and ranks to form. Ramparts are within sight, enabling mutual support with bows and crossbows. At every crossroads, small forts are built, stocked with firewood, with hidden tunnels and scaling ladders for sentries.

After dark, if drums sound on all sides, torches are lit. Thus, if the enemy raids our camp at night, even if they breach the outer gate, they see only towering ramparts and smaller camps on all sides, each firmly defended. Uncertain where to strike, they hesitate. If the general’s camp or any small camp detects the enemy’s approach, they allow them to enter fully before sounding the drums. All camps respond in unison, and the forts ignite their torches, turning night into day. Soldiers in each camp close the gates, ascend the ramparts, and look down upon the enemy, loosing volleys of crossbows and strong bows from all directions. Even if the enemy had generals like Han Xin or Bai Qi, or troops like ghosts and spirits, they would be helpless. They need only fear that the enemy will not raid at night—for if they come, they are certain to be defeated.

If the enemy manages to infiltrate one camp, the surrounding camps light torches and send troops to encircle them. Orders are given within the camp: no one may move hastily. In an instant, right and wrong become clear.

If the raiders attempt to flee, they will all be ensnared in the nets. Thus, when Sima Xuanwang entered Zhuge Liang’s encampment and saw its intricate layout, he remarked: “This is the extraordinary talent of a master strategist.” Today’s camps, however, are built with open thoroughfares, merely secured with ropes to mark their bounds. Should raiders strike at night, ten thousand men would be thrown into chaos at once. Though many scouts may be deployed and defenses strictly maintained, once darkness falls, friend and foe become indistinguishable. Even with superior numbers, their strength cannot be brought to bear.

Chen Hao said: “Du’s claim that after nightfall, one must not array forces in the open and engage the enemy at an appointed time is mistaken. At the end of the Tianbao era, Li Guangbi advanced toward Heyang with five hundred cavalry, lining the route with countless torches whose flames stretched endlessly. Shi Siming’s army, though numbering tens of thousands, dared not approach—how could this tactic be limited to merely awaiting a night raid?”

Jia Lin said: “Fire, drums, banners, and flags serve as signals for both sight and sound, and thus their use differs by day and night.”

Mei Yaochen said: “The purpose of multiplicity is to confuse the enemy’s eyes and ears.”

Wang Xi said: “Multiplicity is meant to strike awe into the enemy’s senses, overawing them with our martial might and imposing qi.”

The *Tradition* states: “Use many drums and clashing sounds when campaigning at night.”

Zhang Yu said: “In all engagements with the enemy, at night keep fires and drums ceaseless, by day maintain an unbroken display of banners and flags. This is how to confound the enemy’s senses, leaving them unaware of how to counter our plans. When Yue attacked Wu, they arrayed their forces on either side of a river. Yue deployed troops in curved formations, advancing now to the left, now to the right under cover of night, drumming and shouting as they moved. Wu’s forces split to meet them and were defeated by Yue—such was the power of fire and drums to deceive. When Jin attacked Qi, its commanders sent scouts to survey even the most perilous mountain and marsh terrain, and wherever they did not go, they still planted banners and spread out their formations. The Qi ruler, unnerved, fled in haste—such was the power of banners and flags to deceive.”

Thus, the *Dao* of war holds: “The qi of the Three Armies can be seized.”

Cao Cao said: “The *Zuozhuan* states that at the first drumbeat, morale surges; at the second, it wanes; at the third, it is exhausted.”

Li Quan said: “To seize qi is to break their sharp courage.”

When Qi attacked Lu at Changshao, the Qi forces sounded their first drum, and the Duke of Lu prepared to engage. Cao Gui said: “Not yet.” After the Qi forces had sounded their drums three times, Cao Gui said: “Now.” They joined battle, and the Qi army was routed. The Duke asked why. Cao Gui replied: “In war, courage is all. The first drumbeat stirs the qi; the second weakens it; the third exhausts it. Their qi was spent, while ours was full—thus we prevailed.” This is what it means to seize the qi of the Three Armies.

Du Mu said: “The *Methods of Sima* states that battles are prolonged by strength but decided by qi.” When Qi attacked Lu and Duke Zhuang prepared to fight at Changshao, he was about to sound the drums. Cao Gui said: “Not yet.” After the Qi forces had sounded their drums three times, Cao Gui said: “Now.” The Qi army was routed. The Duke asked why, and Cao Gui replied: “In war, courage is all. The first drumbeat stirs the qi; the second weakens it; the third exhausts it. Their qi was spent, while ours was full—thus we prevailed.”

When the Jin generals Wuqiu Jian and Wen Qin rebelled, the imperial armies encamped at Lejia. Sima Jingwang advanced in silence, his troops holding wooden gag-bits in their mouths. Qin’s son, Yang, was eighteen and the bravest in the Three Armies. He said: “While their forces are still unsettled, let us scale the walls, sound the drums, and raise a clamor to strike—they can be broken.” After three such assaults, Qin’s forces could not respond. Yang withdrew, and they retreated eastward together. Jingwang said to his generals: “Qin is fleeing.” He dispatched elite troops in pursuit. The generals said: “Qin is an old commander, cautious yet bold. His troops have entered our lines without suffering any losses—he cannot be fleeing.” Jingwang replied: “At the first drumbeat, qi surges; at the second, it wanes; at the third, it is exhausted. Yang sounded the drums, yet Qin did not respond—their momentum is broken. What are they waiting for if not to flee?” Qin indeed withdrew.

Wang Xi said: “When shock and fear lead to weariness and slackness, the army’s qi is seized.”

He Shi said: “The *Huainanzi* states: ‘When the general is full of courage and scorns the enemy, when the troops are resolute and eager for battle, when the Three Armies’ host of a million soldiers is inspired as if reaching for the clouds, their qi like a whirlwind, their voices like thunder, when their accumulated sincerity overflows and their might presses upon the enemy—this is called *qi* and *shi*.’ The *Wuzi* says: ‘The deployment of the Three Armies’ host of a million soldiers, whether lightly or heavily arrayed, rests with one man—this is called the *qi* of opportunity.’ Thus, to seize qi, one must await the right moment and exploit the advantage.”

Zhang Yu said: “Qi is what war relies upon. Men cast aside their lives, shed their blood, and fight to the death without hesitation—this is the work of qi. Therefore, the method of using troops is this: if you rouse your soldiers so that superiors and subordinates share the same fury, their onslaught will be irresistible. When the enemy arrives fresh and their qi is sharp, first wear them down without engaging, then strike when they are weary—thus their keen qi can be seized. This is what the *Weiliaozi* means when it says: ‘When qi is full, they fight; when qi is seized, they flee.’”

Cao Gui spoke of the first drumbeat stirring qi to mean the enemy’s initial surge of vigor; the second waning and the third exhausting it refer to the fatigue of men after prolonged deployment.

Li Jing also said: “Defense does not merely mean fortifying walls and strengthening formations—one must also guard one’s qi and await the right moment. To guard one’s qi means constantly nurturing it, keeping it sharp and unflagging, so that the enemy’s qi may then be seized.”

“The general’s heart can be seized.”

Li Quan said: “Provoke their anger to disorder them, sow discord to estrange them, humble them to make them arrogant—thus their heart can be seized.”

Du Mu said: “The heart is what the general relies upon to command his army.” During the Later Han, when Kou Xun campaigned against Wei Ao, Ao’s general Gao Jun held the first pass at Gaoping. Jun sent his commander Huangfu Wen to meet Xun, but Wen’s words and manner were unyielding. Xun, enraged, had him executed and sent back his deputy. Jun, terrified, opened the city gates and surrendered that very day.

The generals asked: “We venture to ask—why did killing his envoy bring about the surrender of his city?”

Kou Xun replied: “Huangfu Wen was Gao Jun’s most trusted advisor, the one from whom he took counsel. When he came with words and bearing so unyielding, it was clear he had no intention of surrender. To spare him would have allowed him to carry out his plan; to kill him was to strike the gall from Jun. Thus the city surrendered.”

Later, during the Later Yan, Murong Chui sent his son Murong Bao to lead an army against the Later Wei. By the time Bao arrived, Chui was already ill. When Bao reached Wuyuan, Emperor Daowu of Wei cut off his approach, severing all communication between father and son. Daowu then deceived Bao’s messengers, ordering them to announce at the riverbank: “Your father is dead. Why do you not hasten home?” Hearing this, Bao and his brothers were seized by grief and fear, believing it to be true. That night, they fled in haste. Daowu pursued and crushed them at Shenhe Marsh.

Mei Yaochen said: “With changes in drums and banners, one may confuse and seize the enemy’s qi. Once the army’s qi is seized, the general’s heart may also be seized.”

Wang Xi said: “When clamor and chaos arise, the general’s heart is seized.”

He Shi said: “First, one’s own heart must be steadfast; only then can the enemy general’s heart be seized. Thus the tradition states: ‘He who strikes first seizes the enemy’s heart.’ The *Methods of Sima* says: ‘A steadfast heart and fresh qi prevail.’”

Zhang Yu said: “The heart is what the general governs. Whether an army is ordered or disordered, brave or cowardly—all depends on the heart. Thus, one who excels in controlling the enemy unsettles them to bring disorder, provokes them to cause confusion, and presses them to induce fear. In this way, their strategic resolve may be seized. The tradition states: ‘He who strikes first seizes the enemy’s heart’—meaning their fundamental resolve.”

Li Jing also said: “To attack is not merely to assault a city or strike a formation; one must also possess the art of attacking the heart. This ‘attack on the heart’ means constantly nurturing one’s own heart, keeping it calm and unshaken, so that the enemy’s heart may then be seized.”

“In the morning, qi is sharp.”
Chen Hao said: “The qi at first arrival is vigorous and keen—do not contend with it.”
Meng Shi said: “The *Methods of Sima* states: ‘Fresh qi overcomes old qi.’ Fresh qi is the qi of morning.”
Wang Xi said: “This refers to the initial fervor of the troops.”

“By midday, qi slackens.”
Wang Xi said: “As time passes, they grow weary.”

“By dusk, qi returns home.”
Meng Shi said: “Morning qi is the first qi; midday qi is the qi of renewed effort; dusk qi is the qi of exhaustion.”
Mei Yaochen said: “Morning marks the beginning; midday, the middle; dusk, the end.”

“This means that at the outset of battle, troops are sharp, but with time they grow sluggish and long to return home—thus they may be struck. Wang Xi said: ‘When weariness lingers and minds turn to home, there is no longer any principle of combat.’ Therefore, one skilled in warfare avoids their sharp qi and strikes when they are sluggish and retreating—this is the art of managing qi.

Li Quan said: ‘Qi is the courage of the army.’ Du Mu said: ‘Yang qi arises at *zi*, matures at *yin*, wanes at *wu*, and lies hidden at *shen*. Generally, at dawn when yang qi first flourishes, its onset is invariably keen—thus one must avoid it. Wait for its decline and hidden phase, then strike, and victory is assured.’

During the Wude era, Emperor Taizong fought Dou Jiande east of the Sishui River. Jiande arrayed his forces in a line stretching several *li*. Taizong, with a few riders, ascended high ground to observe and said to his generals: ‘The enemy has crossed perilous terrain with clamor—this shows their army lacks discipline. Pressing close to the city to array themselves reveals their contempt for us. Hold our troops back and do not engage; wait for the enemy’s qi to wane. Once their formation has stood long and their soldiers grow hungry, they will surely retreat. Strike as they withdraw, and what force could we not overcome?’

Jiande’s troops remained arrayed from *mao* until *wu*, their soldiers famished and weary, sitting on the rocks and scrambling to drink water. Taizong said: ‘Now we may strike.’ The battle was joined, and Jiande was captured alive.

Chen Hao said: ‘If troops arrayed at *chen* or *si* have not prevailed by *wu* or *wei*, or those arrayed at *wu* or *wei* have not prevailed by *shen* or *you*, one need not insist that only the morning is yang qi or that *shen* and *wu* are the phases of decline. When Taizong attacked Jiande, he ascended high ground to observe and said to his generals: “The enemy comes with all their keenness—we should briefly withdraw. If they retreat, our cavalry can hold them.” This makes clear that one need not wait for dawn.

In all cases, when the enemy possesses sharpness, avoid it thus; otherwise, do not.’”

Du Yu said: “Avoid their qi of sharpness and strike when they grow slack and long to return—this is the principle of managing qi.” This is what Cao Gui’s counsel meant. Mei Yaochen said: “Do not strike when qi is full; when it wanes and slackens, they are easily defeated.” He Shi said: “No one delights in danger or loves death. Driven without cause to the field of corpses, urged to rush onto the battlefield, their hearts harbor no stored-up anger or desire to fight. Yet once roused and goaded, they brave hardship without regard, defy peril without fear—only to regret and falter later. Even the cowardly of the world, when their hearts are stirred, will suddenly contend, no less than Zhu or Gui. Those who grasp blades and seek combat do so when qi rides high; when qi ebbs, they desist, remorseful and abashed. Thus, when the Three Armies view a formidable foe as though it were a maiden, it is because they have seized upon the enemy’s anger and stirred fury. At the siege of Jimo, five thousand men repelled the Yan army by exploiting Yan’s rage over mutilated deserters and desecrated tombs. The Qin warriors, twice our number, were spurred by three unavenged grievances—hence our slackness and Qin’s fervor. In both cases, qi was managed with skill, and the moment was seized. Zhang Yu said: “Morning signifies beginning, midday the middle, evening the end—this is not about time of day. In all men, qi is keen and sharp when first arrived, but after long deployment, weariness sets in and it wanes. Thus, the adept commander, when the enemy’s qi is at its peak, holds firm to avoid it, then strikes when they grow weary and turn to retreat. This is what it means to master one’s own qi and wrest the enemy’s qi.”

“With order await disorder, with stillness await clamor—this is the principle of managing the mind.” Li Quan said: “Watch for the enemy’s shifts and seize the moment.” Du Mu said: “The *Methods of Sima* states: ‘The root is a steadfast mind.’ To assess the enemy and secure victory, the mind must first be settled. Then govern it so that it remains calm and resolute, unshaken by events, unswayed by gain. Await the enemy’s disorder, watch for their clamor, then strike.” Chen Hao said: “When orders are inconsistent and rewards and punishments unclear, that is disorder. When banners are tangled and ranks clamorous, that is clamor. If the enemy is thus, attack.” Jia Lin said: “With our order, await their chaos; with our cool composure, await their clamor—this is managing the mind.” Thus, Taigong said: “Nothing is greater than certain victory; no method is greater than profound silence.” Mei Yaochen said: “Calmly await the enemy, and the host’s heart will be at peace.” Wang Xi’s note agrees with Chen Hao’s.

He Shi said: “A general, alone in person and singular in mind, commands a million men against a tiger-like foe. Amid the tangle of profit and harm, the chaos of victory and defeat, with ten thousand shifts of strategy unfolding in his breast—unless his heart is vast and his mind unclouded, how can he respond to change without exhaustion, handle affairs without confusion, face sudden peril without alarm, or engage the world without bewilderment? Our order suffices to await their disorder; our stillness suffices to await their clamor. Though a million enemies stand before us, we see them as petty bandits. When Ya Fu faced the foe, he lay unmoving; when Luan Zhen met the enemy, he favored discipline and leisure. What art did these two harbor? Their minds were settled and firm, their cultivation ample.” Zhang Yu said: “With order await disorder, with stillness await clamor, with composure await impatience, with endurance await rage, with severity await slackness—this is what it means to master one’s own mind and wrest the enemy’s.”

“With the near await the far, with ease await toil, with fullness await hunger—this is the principle of managing strength.” Li Quan said: “The dynamics of guest and host.” Du Mu said: “As stated earlier: ‘Draw the enemy to you without being drawn by them.’” Du Yu said: “With our nearness await their distance; with our rest await their fatigue; with our fullness await their hunger and emptiness—this is the principle of managing human strength.” Mei Yaochen said: “Do not exhaust human strength to your own detriment.” Wang Xi said: “To control excess with sufficiency is skillful management of strength.” Zhang Yu said: “With the near await the far, with ease await toil, with fullness await hunger, with enticement await approach, with weight await lightness—this is what it means to master one’s own strength and exhaust the enemy’s.”

“Do not intercept banners arrayed in perfect order; do not strike formations drawn up in full might—this is the principle of managing change.” Cao Cao said: “‘Perfect order’ means alignment; ‘full might’ means grandeur.” Li Quan said: “‘Perfect order’ means disciplined ranks; ‘full might’ means proper divisions.” Du Mu said: “‘Full might’ means fearlessness. Warfare adapts to the enemy; if the enemy presents such a front, do not strike—this is managing change.” When Cao Cao of Later Han besieged Ye, Yuan Shang came to relieve it. Cao said: “If Shang comes by the main road, we shall avoid him; if he follows the western hills, he will be ours.” Shang indeed came by the western hills, and Cao struck and crushed him. Mei Yaochen said: “When they come in perfect order and array in full might, showing no fear, they must have some unexpected maneuver.” Wang Xi said: “Though one might intercept and strike, seeing their disciplined and imposing display, one changes course.” He Shi said: “This is what is meant by ‘when strong, avoid them.’” Zhang Yu said: “‘Perfect order’ refers to ranks aligned in form and name.”

“Tangtang” refers to an expansive and grand formation. When the enemy presents such a display, how can one engage lightly? *The Military Regulations* states: “Advance when opportunity arises; retreat when difficulty is known.” It also says: “When strong, avoid them.” This speaks of the need to recognize adaptability. This is what is meant by the Dao of skillfully managing change to respond to the enemy.

Thus, the principles of warfare dictate: do not advance uphill against high ground; do not oppose an enemy descending from a hill. Li Quan said: “This refers to terrain.” Du Mu said: “‘Advance uphill’ means to attack upward; ‘descending’ means to rely on; ‘oppose’ means to meet head-on. If the enemy holds the high ground, do not attack upward; if they descend from hills to seek battle, do not meet them. This is because advancing from low to high exhausts one’s strength, while descending from high to low follows the natural momentum—thus, one must not advance or meet them.” Meng Shi said: “If the enemy arrays its forces with hills at their back, leaving no rear vulnerabilities, one should withdraw to level ground and not engage them.” Du You said: “If the enemy relies on hills or险阻 to array their troops and await battle, do not rashly attack. Not only is the terrain unfavorable, but there is also the danger of falling rocks.” Mei Yaochen said: “‘Do not advance uphill’ means if the enemy holds the high ground, one cannot attack upward. ‘Do not oppose an enemy descending from a hill’ means if the enemy comes from above, one cannot meet them head-on. The circumstances are unfavorable.” Wang Xi said: “In such unfavorable conditions, one must array one’s forces strictly and await developments.” He Shi said:

When Qin attacked Han, the King of Zhao ordered Zhao She to relieve them. Upon hearing this, the Qin forces advanced in full armor. A soldier named Xu Li requested to advise on military matters, saying: “The Qin did not expect our forces to arrive so soon; their momentum is fierce. The general must thicken our ranks to await them—otherwise, we will surely be defeated. Now, whoever first occupies the northern mountain will prevail; those who arrive later will be defeated.” Zhao She followed his advice and dispatched ten thousand men to rush there. The Qin forces arrived later and, unable to seize the mountain, were crushed when Zhao She unleashed his troops.

Later, the Northern Zhou sent generals to attack Northern Qi and besieged Luoyang. The Qi general Duan Shao confronted them and ascended Mangban to observe the Zhou army’s disposition. At Taihe Valley, he encountered the Zhou forces and immediately sent word to his camps, ordering his generals to form ranks and await them. The Zhou army, with infantry in the lead, advanced uphill to meet the battle. Duan Shao, seeing their infantry against his cavalry, feigned retreat while luring them forward, waiting until their strength was spent before sending down his dismounted troops to strike. As soon as close combat began, the Zhou forces collapsed and fled.

Zhang Yu said: “If the enemy arrays its forces on high ground, one cannot attack upward—the movement of men and horses, the release of bows and arrows, are all disadvantageous. Thus, Zhuge Liang said: ‘In mountain warfare, do not attack upward; if the enemy descends from high ground, do not meet them—the momentum is unfavorable. Lure them to level ground before engaging.’”

Do not pursue a feigned retreat. Li Quan and Du Mu said: “This is for fear of ambush.” Jia Lin said: “If the enemy suddenly flees before their strength is spent, there must be a hidden ambush. Command your officers and soldiers strictly not to pursue.” Du You said: “‘Feigned retreat’ means the enemy, while still in battle and with momentum unbroken, suddenly flees and arrays their troops—this must be a ruse. Do not pursue deeply. Thus, the *Tai Gong* states: ‘When troops are deployed in disorderly fashion, it is to create a diversion.’” Mei Yaochen’s note is the same as Du Mu’s. Wang Xi said: “If the enemy does not appear to be truly retreating, there must be deception—do not pursue.” He Shi said:

For example, during the Warring States period, when Qin’s army attacked Zhao, Zhao Kuo replaced Lian Po as general and resisted Qin at Changping. Qin secretly appointed Bai Qi as supreme commander. Zhao’s forces advanced to attack Qin, but the Qin army feigned defeat and fled, deploying two ambush forces to intercept them. Zhao’s troops, pursuing victory, chased to the Qin fortifications but found them impenetrable. Meanwhile, Qin’s ambush of twenty-five thousand cut off Zhao’s rear, while another five thousand cavalry severed Zhao’s supply lines. Zhao’s army was split in two, their provisions cut off. Qin then sent light troops to attack, and Zhao, faring poorly, built fortifications to await reinforcements. When Qin learned Zhao’s supply lines were severed, the king personally went to Henei to mobilize troops to block Zhao’s reinforcements and supplies. Zhao’s soldiers went forty-six days without food, secretly killing and eating one another. Kuo was struck by an arrow and died.

Liu Biao of Shu sent Liu Bei to invade northward to Ye, and Cao Cao dispatched Xiahou Dun and Li Dian to resist him. One morning, Bei burned his camp and withdrew. Dun sent his generals to pursue. Dian said: “The enemy has retreated without cause—there must be an ambush. The southern road is narrow and overgrown; we cannot pursue.” They did not heed him. Dun and the others were indeed enveloped by the ambush. Dian went to rescue them, and when Bei saw reinforcements arrive, he withdrew.

In the late Western Wei, General Shi Ning was sent with the Göktürks to attack Tuyuhun. They reached Shudun, the former capital of Tuyuhun, which held many treasures. The Tuyuhun ruler had already fled to Hezhen City, leaving his Prince of the South and several thousand men to defend. Ning attacked but feigned retreat. The Tuyuhun forces opened the gates and pursued. Ning turned his troops around, seized the gates before they could close, and entered. He captured the Prince of the South and took all the men, women, and treasures, which he handed over to the Göktürks.

When Gao Cheng of Northern Qi ascended the throne, Hou Jing rebelled and defected to Liang, besieging Shan City. Cheng sent Murong Shaozong to suppress him. Before battle, Shaozong, knowing the Liang troops were fierce and fearing his own forces might waver, gathered his commanders and said: “I will feign retreat to lure the Liang forces forward; you strike their rear.” He gave strict orders. Jing also commanded his Liang troops: “Do not pursue beyond two *li*.” When battle was joined, Shaozong fled, but the Liang troops, ignoring Jing’s orders, pursued deeply into defeat.

The Wei forces, believing Shaozong’s words to be true, pressed their attack in fierce pursuit and thus crushed the Liang army.

During the Tang, when An Lushan rebelled, Guo Ziyi besieged Weizhou. The false Prince of Zheng, Qingxu, led reinforcements in three divisions. Ziyi arrayed his troops to meet them, having first selected three thousand archers and concealed them within the ramparts. He instructed them: “When I feign retreat and the enemy presses forward in pursuit, ascend the walls, beat the drums, and raise a clamor—let arrows and crossbow bolts fly together to drive them back.” When battle was joined, Ziyi pretended to retreat, and the enemy indeed took the bait. The gates of the fort swung open, and suddenly the air filled with war drums and shouts; arrows rained down like a storm, striking terror into the enemy ranks. As they rallied to pursue, Ziyi captured Qingxu.

Zhang Yu said: When the enemy flees in defeat, one must discern whether it is genuine or feigned. If their banners and drums remain in perfect order, their commands unified, and their formations—though retreating—are dense and disciplined, this is no rout but a ruse; surely some stratagem lies behind it. Do not pursue. But if their flags droop in disarray, men clamor in panic, and horses rear in fright, this is a true retreat. Do not press the attack with your finest troops.

Li Quan said: This is to avoid their fierce *qi*.
Du Mu said: This is to avoid their strength.
When the Duke of Chu attacked Sui, Sui’s minister Ji Liang said: “The men of Chu favor their left flank. You must take the left and avoid engaging their king. Instead, strike their right flank, where they have no strong commanders—surely they will break. Once one flank collapses, their entire force will falter.” Sui’s Junior Preceptor said: “To avoid the king is to admit we are no match.” They did not heed the advice, and Sui’s army was utterly defeated.
Chen Hao said: This advice is about evading the enemy’s strengths, not the principle of “do not attack with your finest troops.” The true meaning is this: when the enemy’s troops are swift and sharp, do not engage them at once; wait until they grow weary and lax, then strike. As the saying goes: “A thousand *li* away, their edge is unstoppable”—this is what it means.

Mei Yaochen said: Wait until their *qi* is broken.
He Shi said: As when the Former Lord of Shu led a great army east to attack Wu, and Wu’s general Lu Xun resisted him. The Lord of Shu advanced from Jianping, encamping in a continuous line all the way to the borders of Yiling, establishing dozens of camps. He used gold, silk, and titles to entice the tribes. First, he sent the general Wu Ban with several thousand men to set up camp on open ground, seeking to provoke battle. All his generals wished to attack.

Lu Xun said: "Liu Bei has advanced his entire army eastward, his *qi* at its peak and vigor unmatched. Moreover, he occupies the heights and defends the passes—difficult to attack at once. Even if we take a position, complete victory would remain elusive. Should we suffer defeat, our losses would be grievous. For now, we must encourage our officers and men, deploy our strategies broadly, and await developments."

When Liu Bei saw his plans were not succeeding, he led eight thousand concealed troops out from the valley. Lu Xun said: "The reason I did not permit our forces to attack Wu Ban was that I surmised there must be some clever ruse at play."

All the generals said: "We should have struck Liu Bei at the outset. Now, after allowing him to hold us at bay for five or six hundred *li* over seven or eight months, he has fortified all his key positions. Attacking him now will bring no advantage."

Lu Xun replied: "Liu Bei is a cunning foe. When his army first assembled, his thoughts were sharp and focused—we could not have challenged him then. Now, after such a long stalemate, he has failed to gain any advantage, his troops are weary, their morale broken, and no new strategies remain. Crushing this enemy is a matter for today."

He first attacked one of the camps but was repelled. Lu Xun said: "I now understand how to break them." He ordered each soldier to carry a bundle of thatch and set the camps ablaze, capturing them. Liu Bei fled under cover of night.

Later, during the Wei dynasty, the Wu general Zhuge Ke besieged Xincheng. Sima Jingwang dispatched Wuqiu Jian and Wen Qin to resist him. Jian and Qin requested permission to engage in battle, but Jingwang said: "Zhuge Ke has advanced deep into enemy territory, throwing his troops into a desperate position—his edge will not be easily withstood. Moreover, Xincheng is small but well-fortified; attacking it will not lead to its capture." He then ordered his generals to raise high ramparts and wear down the enemy.

After several days of stalemate, Zhuge Ke’s assaults weakened, and over half his forces were dead or wounded. Jingwang then ordered Wen Qin to lead elite troops to Heyu, cutting off the enemy’s retreat. Zhuge Ke, seized by fear, fled.

During the Former Zhao, Liu Yao dispatched his generals to campaign against the Qiang. Their chieftain, Quan Qu, led his forces to defend a perilous stronghold. Liu Yao’s general, You Ziyuan, repeatedly defeated him. Quan Qu wished to surrender, but his son, Yi Yu, declared before the assembled troops: "Last year, when Liu Yao came himself, he could do nothing against us. At dawn, he will press against Ziyuan’s ramparts."

His advisors urged Ziyuan to sally forth, but he said: "I have heard that Yi Yu possesses the bravery of Zhuan Zhu and the swiftness of Qing Ji. His father has just been defeated, and his fury runs high. Moreover, the Western Rong are fierce and formidable—their edge cannot be matched. Better to wait, letting their *qi* exhaust itself before striking." He thus fortified his defenses and refused battle. Yi Yu grew arrogant.

Ziyuan, waiting until they were unprepared, swore his troops to secrecy in the dead of night. They fed their horses and ate their rations early, then at first light, armed themselves, swept the camp clean, and emerged. By daybreak, he had set an ambush and engaged in battle, capturing Yi Yu alive on the field.

During the Wude reign of the Tang, Emperor Taizong led his forces to Hedong to campaign against Liu Wuzhou. The Prince of Jiangxia, Li Daozong, accompanied him. Taizong ascended the walls of Yubi City to observe the enemy and, turning to Daozong, said: "The rebels rely on their numbers and come to challenge us. What do you think?"

Daozong replied: "The enemy’s edge cannot be withstood. It is easier to break them with strategy than by force. Order our troops to deepen their defenses and raise their ramparts to blunt their momentum. These rabble cannot endure for long. When their supplies run out, they will scatter of their own accord. We may capture them without battle."

Taizong said: "Your thoughts align with mine."

Later, when the rebels’ provisions were exhausted, they fled under cover of night. Taizong routed them in a single battle.

Taizong also campaigned against Xue Rengao at Zhezhicheng. The enemy numbered over a hundred thousand, their troops’ edge keen, and they repeatedly provoked battle. The generals requested permission to engage, but Taizong said: "Our troops have just suffered a setback, and their *qi* remains diminished. The enemy, emboldened by recent victories, will surely advance recklessly. We shall close our defenses to break their momentum, then strike once their *qi* has waned. This is a plan of absolute certainty." He thus commanded his army: "Anyone who speaks of battle shall be executed."

After a prolonged stalemate, the enemy’s supplies ran out, and their ranks grew divided. Their generals surrendered one after another. Taizong, knowing that Xue Rengao’s forces were fracturing from within, said to his generals: "Now we may fight." He ordered General Liang Shi to encamp at Shallow Water Plain to lure the enemy.

The enemy’s great general, Zong Luohou, arrogant and fierce, had long sought battle in vain and was seething with frustration. Now, he threw all his strength into attacking Liang Shi, hoping to vent his rage. Liang Shi held his position and refused to emerge, blunting the enemy’s edge. Luohou pressed the assault with increasing ferocity.

Taizong, judging the enemy’s *qi* to be spent, said to his generals: "Their momentum is waning. We shall surely take them now." He issued orders for the generals to join battle at daybreak. He commanded General Pang Yu to array his forces south of Shallow Water Plain, emerging on the enemy’s right flank to bait them. Luohou concentrated his troops and engaged, nearly breaking Yu’s lines.

Taizong personally led the main army, sweeping down from the north of the plain in a surprise attack. Luohou wheeled his forces to resist, but Taizong’s troops, arrayed in a standard-and-enveloping formation, charged with a roar that shook the heavens. Luohou’s *qi* was shattered, and his army collapsed in rout.

Later, Li Jing accompanied Li Xiaogong, the Prince of Hejian, in a campaign against Xiao Xian. When their forces reached Yiling, Xiao Xian’s general, Wen Shihong, led tens of thousands of elite troops to encamp at Qingjiang. Xiaogong wished to attack, but Jing said: "Shihong is Xiao Xian’s most valiant general, and his troops are fierce and brave. Now, having just emerged from Jingmen, they have thrown all their strength into battle. This is an army fighting to stave off defeat—their edge cannot be withstood. We should anchor our forces on the southern bank and avoid clashing with them. Wait until their *qi* wanes, then strike with all our might. Victory will be assured."

Xiaogong did not heed his advice. Leaving Jing to guard the camp, he engaged the enemy and was indeed defeated, fleeing to the southern bank.

Zhang Yu said: "If the enemy advances with high spirits, their edge cannot be withstood. It is best to avoid them briefly and wait for their fatigue and setbacks."

When Jin and Chu clashed, the Chu forces pressed against the Jin army at dawn. The Jin officers were alarmed. Yue Shu said: "The Chu troops are swift and agile. We should fortify our defenses and await them. In three days, they will retreat. Strike them then, and victory will be ours."

Later, when Emperor Taizong campaigned against Xue Qigao, the enemy’s troops were keen and spirited, repeatedly provoking battle.

The generals all urged battle, but Emperor Taizong said: "We should first shut our gates and break their momentum. Wait until their *qi* wanes, then we may strike once and shatter them." And so it came to pass.

**Do not take the enemy’s bait.**
Li Quan said: "The Qin poisoned the upper reaches of the Jing River."
Du Mu said: "If the enemy suddenly abandons food and drink and retreats, one must first test it—do not consume it at once, for fear of poison."

During the reign of Emperor Wen of Later Wei, the Kumo Xi raided the borders. The court ordered Prince Xincheng of Jiyin to lead troops against them. The prince prepared copious amounts of poisoned wine. As the enemy drew near, he feigned retreat, abandoning his camp. The raiders arrived, rejoiced, and drank greedily. Once the wine took effect, the prince dispatched light cavalry to strike, capturing tens of thousands.

Chen Hao said: "This victory was no accident, yet it is no model for generals to emulate. Did Sunzi ever advocate poisoning men’s bellies? His words liken the matter to fish seeing bait—they must not bite. If the enemy dangles advantage, one must not covet it."

When Cao Cao fought Yuan Shao’s generals, including Wen Chou, his officers feared the enemy’s numerous cavalry and urged retreat. Xun Yu said: "This is how they bait us—how can we flee?" Thus, it is clear that "bait" does not refer solely to literal poison.

*(Note: The character 食 here may be a scribal error for 贪, "covet.")*
Mei Yaochen said: "Fish perish for coveting bait; armies fall for coveting bait. If the enemy uses troops to lure us, we must not take the hook."
Wang Xi said: "If they bait us with advantage, there must be hidden ambushes."

He Shi said: "As in the Spring and Autumn period, when Chu attacked Jiao and encamped at its southern gate, Marshal Qu Xia said: 'Jiao is small and reckless—recklessness means poor strategy. Let us cease guarding the firewood-gatherers to entice them.' They followed his counsel."

The men of Jiao captured thirty men. The next day, the Jiao forces rushed out, driving the Chu laborers into the mountains. The Chu troops stationed themselves at Jiao’s northern gate and ambushed them at the foot of the mountains, inflicting a crushing defeat. They forced Jiao to sue for peace before withdrawing.

Another example: the Red Eyebrows feigned defeat, abandoning their supplies and fleeing, with carts loaded with earth covered by beans on top. Deng Hong seized them, only to be routed by the Red Eyebrows. When Cao Cao had yet to cross the river and released his oxen and horses, Ma Chao captured them, allowing Cao to cross safely. Another instance: Cao Cao abandoned his supplies, which Yuan Shao’s generals Wen Chou and Liu Bei divided and took, only to be crushed by Cao. Later, during the Northern Wei, Prince Guangyang Yuan Shen used Bielie He to lure Tucheng, who came to raid but was defeated by Yu Jin’s ambush. These are all examples of the art of baiting.

Zhang Yu said: *The Three Strategies* states, "Beneath fragrant bait, fish must hang." This means that fish perish for coveting bait, just as armies fall for coveting advantage. Baiting troops does not merely mean poisoning food—anything that lures the enemy with profit serves as bait. Examples include Cao Cao using livestock to bait Ma Chao, supplies to bait Yuan Shao, and Li Ju using oxen and horses to bait Shi Le.

**Do not intercept a retreating army.**
Li Quan said: When soldiers long to return home, their resolve cannot be stopped.
Du Mu said: When Cao Cao campaigned against Zhang Xiu at Rang, Liu Biao sent troops to aid Xiu, cutting off Cao’s rear. As Cao prepared to withdraw, Xiu’s forces pursued, preventing his army from advancing. Liu Biao and Zhang Xiu then joined forces to hold the passes, trapping Cao’s army between them. Cao ordered a night operation to tunnel through the terrain, moving all supplies through the passage while setting up a surprise force. At dawn, the enemy, believing Cao had fled, pursued in full. Cao unleashed his ambush, and infantry and cavalry struck from both flanks, crushing them. Cao told Xun Yu: "The enemy cut off our retreating army and drove us into a death trap—this is how I knew we would win."
Meng Shi said: When men yearn to return home, they will fight to the death—do not block and attack them.
Du You said: Every man longs for his home and family—do not intercept them. Observe their movements and respond accordingly.
Mei Yaochen said: The enemy will fight desperately.
Wang Xi said: They will fight for themselves—do not block their path. If they harbor other concerns, you may intercept and strike.

When Cao Cao besieged Ye, Yuan Shang came to relieve it. His generals, believing Shang’s forces were retreating, advised avoiding them. Cao said: "If he comes by the main road, we will avoid him. But if he follows the western mountains, he will be easily captured." For if he took the main road, his resolve to return would be unshaken, but if he clung to the mountains, he would be wary of the terrain and filled with fear.

He Shi said: For example, when Cao Cao initially besieged Zhang Xiu at Rang, Liu Biao sent troops to aid Xiu, cutting off Cao’s rear. As Cao prepared to withdraw, Xiu’s forces pursued, preventing his army from advancing. The camps gradually advanced to Anzhong, where Xiu and Biao joined forces to hold the passes, trapping Cao’s army between them. Cao ordered a night operation to tunnel through the terrain, moving all supplies through the passage while setting up a surprise force. At dawn, the enemy, believing Cao had fled, pursued in full. Cao unleashed his ambush, and infantry and cavalry struck from both flanks, crushing them. Cao told Xun Yu: "The enemy cut off our retreating army and drove us into a death trap—this is how I knew we would win."

In the second year of Jianwu of Qi, the Northern Wei besieged Zhongli. Zhang Xintai, a military commander, accompanied Cui Huijing to relieve the siege. As the Wei forces retreated, over ten thousand remaining troops on Shaoyang Island requested five hundred horses to secure passage. Huijing intended to cut off their path and attack, but Xintai advised: "Do not intercept a retreating army—this is what the ancients feared. Soldiers in a death trap cannot be trifled with." Huijing heeded his counsel and let them pass.

When the Former Qin’s Fu Jian campaigned against Jin and was defeated at Shouchun, he retreated. In Chang’an, Murong Hong raised troops at Huaze, prompting Fu Jian to send Fu Rui, Dou Chong, and Yao Chang to suppress them. Fu Rui, bold and reckless, underestimated the enemy and neglected his troops. When Hong heard of his approach, he grew fearful and prepared to flee east of the passes. Rui rushed to intercept him. Yao Chang warned: "The Xianbei long to return home—drive them out of the passes, but do not block them." Rui ignored him. They clashed at Huaze, where Rui was defeated and killed.

Later Liang’s Lü Hong attacked Duan Ye at Zhangye but failed to prevail and prepared to flee eastward. Duan Ye proposed pursuing him. His general Juqu Mengxun advised: "Do not intercept a retreating army—do not pursue an enemy at bay. These are the cautions of military strategy. Better to let them go and plan for later." Duan Ye replied: "To release the enemy for a day is to invite regret too late to undo." He then led his forces in pursuit and was defeated by Hong.

Zhang Yu said: "When troops are abroad, every man longs to return home. To block their path is to force them into a desperate fight." Han Xin said: "What force can withstand men who yearn to return east?" When Cao Cao had defeated Liu Biao, he told Xun Yu: "The enemy barred my retreating army, and thus I knew victory was assured." Again, when Lü Hong attacked Duan Ye and failed, preparing to flee east, Duan Ye wished to strike. Someone admonished: "Do not intercept a retreating army—this is a military maxim. Better to let them go and plan for later." Duan Ye did not heed this, led his forces in pursuit, and was defeated by Hong. Many such cases exist among the ancients; they cannot all be recounted.

"When besieging an army, leave an opening." Cao Cao said: "The *Methods of Sima* states: 'Surround them on three sides, leaving one side open, to show them a path to life.'" Li Quan said: "When besieging the enemy, one side must be left unblocked to signal that the encirclement is not absolute. If you surround them on all sides, the enemy will hold fast and refuse to yield." Xiang Yu’s massacre at Waihuang and Cao Cao’s siege of Huguan exemplify this principle. Du Mu said: "Show them a path to life so they do not fight to the death, then strike."

During the Later Han, the sorcerer Wei Si’s disciples Shan Chen and Fu Zhen gathered in Yuanwu City, plundered officials and people, and declared themselves generals. Emperor Guangwu dispatched Zang Gong with several thousand northern troops to besiege them. The rebels had ample provisions and repeatedly withstood the attacks, inflicting heavy casualties. The emperor summoned the nobles and feudal lords to ask for a strategy.

At the time, Emperor Ming was the Prince of Donghai. He replied: "When sorcerers coerce one another, their power cannot last long; among them, there must be those who regret their actions. Yet the outer encirclement is too tight, and they cannot escape. If we slightly relax the siege and allow them to flee, even a single constable would suffice to capture them." The emperor immediately ordered the encirclement to be opened and the siege to be eased. The rebel forces scattered, and Shan Chen, Fu Zhen, and their followers were beheaded.

At the end of the Tianbao era of the Great Tang, Li Guangbi led the Shuofang army in battle against Shi Siming at Tumen. The enemy forces retreated and dispersed, and the encirclement closed in on all sides. Guangbi ordered the southeastern corner to be opened to let them flee. Seeing the encirclement open, the rebels abandoned their armor and fled in haste. Pursuing them, Guangbi annihilated their entire force. This, too, was the tactic of leaving one side open.

Du You said: "When besieging the enemy on open ground, one side must be left unblocked to signal vulnerability, so that their resolve to fight or hold fast weakens, and they waver between staying and fleeing. If the enemy is in dire straits and holds a strategic position, with strong reinforcements approaching from outside, then the encirclement must be made unbreakable—there is no need to leave an opening. This is the method of warfare."

Mei Yaochen’s commentary aligns with Cao Cao’s annotation.

He Shi said: "For example, in the early Later Han, Zhang Bu occupied the land of Qi, and the Han general Geng Yan led his troops to suppress him. Bu sent his general Fei Yi to garrison Lixia, while also stationing troops at Zhu’a and Zhongcheng. Yan first attacked Zhu’a, breaching the city before midday. He then opened one corner of the encirclement, allowing the enemy troops to flee to Zhongcheng. When the defenders of Zhongcheng heard that Zhu’a had fallen, they were seized with terror and abandoned their fortifications in flight.

Later, Zhu Hao and Xu Qiu jointly campaigned against the remaining Yellow Turban rebels. Han Zhong, who had occupied Wan, sought to surrender, but they refused. Pressing the attack fiercely, they failed to take the city. Hao ascended a hill to observe and said to Zhang Chao: 'Now I understand. The rebels are now completely surrounded, with our forces pressing in from within and without. Their offer of surrender was rejected, and they have no way out—this is why they fight to the death. Ten thousand men united in resolve are already hard to withstand; how much more so a hundred thousand? The harm is grave. Now, it would be better to lift the siege and concentrate our forces inside the city. When Zhong sees the encirclement loosened, he will surely sally forth. Once they emerge, their morale will scatter, and they will be easy to crush.' Soon after the siege was lifted, Zhong indeed came out to fight, and Hao defeated him.

Earlier, when Cao Cao besieged Huguan, he issued an order: 'When the city falls, slaughter them all.' Months passed, and the city did not yield. Cao Ren said: 'When besieging a city, one must leave an opening to show them a path to life. Now, my lord, you have declared their certain death, so they will defend themselves to the last. Moreover, the city is well-fortified and well-provisioned. Attacking it will only wound our soldiers, while holding the siege will take too long. To camp beneath a stronghold and assault an enemy resolved to die is no sound strategy.' Cao Cao followed his advice, and the city surrendered.

At the end of the Later Wei, Gao Huan of Qi raised righteous troops in Hebei. Er Zhu Zhao, Tian Guang, Erzhu Dulü, and Erzhu Zhongyuan—four generals—joined forces south of Ye, their elite cavalry and infantry numbering two hundred thousand. They surrounded Gao Huan at Nanling Mountain. At the time, Huan had only two thousand horses and fewer than thirty thousand foot soldiers. The Erzhu generals’ encirclement was not fully closed, so Huan tied together oxen and donkeys to block his own retreat. Thus, his officers and men fought desperately, striking out on all sides, and crushed the Erzhu forces.

Zhang Yu said: "Surround the enemy on three sides, leaving one corner open to show them a path to life, so they do not fight with desperate resolve."

Later, when the Later Han general Zhu Jun campaigned against the rebel leader Han Zhong at Wan, his fierce assault failed to take the city. He said to his officers: "The rebels are now completely surrounded, which is why they fight to the death. If we lift the siege, they will surely emerge. Once they do, their morale will scatter, and they will be easy to crush." The outcome was as he predicted.

Earlier, when Cao Cao besieged Huguan, he declared: "When the city falls, slaughter them all." After prolonged assaults, the city still did not yield. Cao Ren said to Cao Cao: "When besieging a city, one must leave an opening to show them a path to life. Now, my lord, you have promised them certain death, so they will defend themselves to the last—this is no strategy." Cao Cao followed his advice, and the city was taken.

'Do not press a desperate foe too hard.'

Du Mu said: "During the Spring and Autumn period, when Wu attacked Chu, the Chu army fled. Reaching Qingfa, King Helü of Wu was about to strike again. Fugai, the king’s uncle, said: 'A cornered beast still fights—how much more so men? If they know they cannot escape and are driven to die, they will surely defeat us. It would be better to let half of them cross the river before attacking.' The king followed his advice and defeated them again.

During the reign of Emperor Xuan of Han, Zhao Chongguo campaigned against the Xianling Qiang. Seeing the Han army, the Qiang abandoned their supplies and sought to cross the Huang River. The path was narrow and treacherous, so Chongguo advanced slowly. Someone said: 'We are pursuing an advantage too slowly.' Chongguo replied: 'They are desperate foes and must not be pressed too hard. If we move slowly, they will flee without looking back; if we press them, they will turn and fight to the death.' The generals said: 'Well said.' The barbarians indeed rushed into the river, and tens of thousands drowned, leading to their total defeat."

Chen Hao said: "A cornered crow will peck; a cornered beast will bite."

Mei Yaochen said: "A trapped beast still fights—this is the way of things."

He Shi said: "Lü Hu of the Former Yan occupied Yewang and secretly communicated with Jin. When his treachery was discovered, Yan generals Murong Ke and others led their forces to suppress him. General Fu Yan said to Ke: 'Hu is a desperate foe clinging to a desperate alliance. Now that the royal army has arrived, his forces are demoralized. Previously, Your Highness took advantage of the natural defenses of Guanggu, where defense was easy and attack difficult, and so adopted a long-term strategy. But the enemy’s situation now is not the same as before—we should attack swiftly to avoid the cost of a prolonged campaign.' Ke replied: 'Hu is an old bandit with much experience in upheavals. Judging by his preparations, he will not be easily overcome. Now, if we besiege him in this desperate city, cutting off his supply routes, with no stores inside and no strong reinforcements outside, within a hundred days, he will surely collapse. Why waste the lives of our soldiers for the sake of a moment’s gain?'"

This is called "triumphing without bloodshed on the blade, and securing victory by sitting still." Thereupon, he arrayed a long siege and held fast. After six months in all, Yewang collapsed, and Hu fled south to Jin, surrendering all his forces.

During the Five Dynasties, Jin generals Fu Yanqing and Du Chongwei passed through Ke’s northern frontier and encountered barbarian forces at Yangcheng. The tribesmen, a hundred thousand strong, surrounded the Jin army in the open wild. Deprived of water, the soldiers dug wells, wrung mud from their clothes to suck, and many men and horses died of thirst. Yanqing said: "Rather than be bound and captured, why not die for the state? We are now desperate." He then led his elite cavalry to sally forth and strike. Just then, a great wind stirred the dust, and they seized the moment to join battle—the tribesmen were utterly routed.

This was Yanqing, besieged by a hundred thousand barbarians, a desperate foe driven to the last extremity, who then exerted every effort to survive. The tribesmen failed to grasp this and so met defeat.

Zhang Yu said: "If the enemy burns their boats and smashes their cauldrons, coming to fight to the death, do not press them too closely, for the desperate will fight like cornered beasts." When the Jin army defeated Qi at An, the Marquis of Qi sought a truce, but the Jin refused. The Marquis of Qi said: "Let me gather the last embers and make one final stand before the city." The Jin, fearing this, agreed to the truce.

King Fuchai of Wu spoke of cornered beasts still fighting, and Han’s Zhao Chongguo said: "If you press them lightly, they flee without looking back; if you press them hard, they turn and fight to the death." These insights are close to the mark. Such is the method of using troops.

*End of Commentary on Sunzi, Scroll Seven*

**Sunzi with Commentaries, Scroll Eight**

*Chapter on the Nine Variations*

Cao Cao said: "To vary from the norm is to grasp the nine applications."

Wang Xi said: Wang Xi believes that nine represents the utmost of numbers; the method of using troops must exhaust all variations. The *Lost Poems* say: "Nine variations return to unity." It is unclear what Cao Gong meant by "nine." Some say it refers to the variations of the nine terrains.

Zhang Yu said: "Variation" means not being bound by conventional methods—adapting to circumstances and acting as the situation demands. Whenever contending for advantage, one must understand the variations of the nine terrains. Thus, this follows the chapter on "Contending for Advantage."

Sunzi said: In the general method of employing troops, the commander receives the ruler’s mandate and assembles the army.

Zhang Yu said: This has already been explained in the preceding text.

Do not encamp in *pi* terrain.
Cao Cao said: There is nothing to rely upon. Land eroded by water is called *pi*.
Li Quan said: Low-lying land is *pi*; marching through it risks flooding.
Chen Hao said: *Pi* means low and depressed. Kongming called it "the hell of terrain"—a place where the center is low and surrounded on all sides by higher ground.
Meng Shi said: If the land is too low, one may be trapped by the enemy.
Du You said: When selecting ground for encampment, one must seek advantage and avoid harm.
Mei Yaochen said: In mountainous forests, perilous defiles, or marshy wetlands—places where one cannot halt—there is nothing to rely upon.
He Yanxi said: The later section states, "In *pi* terrain, we hasten along its paths," meaning that in such precarious ground, one should swiftly depart.
Zhang Yu said: Mountainous forests, perilous defiles, and marshy wetlands—any difficult-to-traverse terrain is *pi* terrain. Since there is nothing to rely upon, one must not encamp there.

In *qu* terrain, establish alliances.

Cao Cao said: Form alliances with the feudal lords.
Li Quan said: A place where four roads converge is called *qu*; it is where one forms alliances with the feudal lords.
Jia Lin said: Forge alliances with the feudal lords to secure support.
Mei Yaochen said: In a land where four roads meet, connected to neighboring states, one must establish alliances.
He Yanxi said: The later section states, "In *qu* terrain, we strengthen our alliances," meaning one should solidify ties with the feudal lords to make them steadfast.
Zhang Yu said: In a land where four roads converge, surrounded by neighboring states, one should first go and form alliances to secure support.

In *jue* terrain, do not linger.
Cao Cao said: Do not remain long.
Li Quan said: A place without springs, wells, livestock, or firewood is *jue* terrain; one cannot stay there.
Jia Lin said: Valleys and perilous defiles with no passage ahead are *jue*; one must swiftly depart without lingering.
Mei Yaochen said: When first leaving one’s state and crossing the border, one should not remain even in *qing* terrain—thus, one cannot linger long.
Zhang Yu said: Leading an army beyond one’s borders into foreign lands is *jue* terrain. Such perilous and isolated ground is even more treacherous than *zhong* terrain, so one must not tarry or remain long.

In *wei* terrain, devise strategies.
Cao Cao said: Employ extraordinary tactics.
Li Quan said: Exploit the terrain to find a way through.
Jia Lin said: Surrounded on all sides by peril is *wei* terrain. While the enemy may come and go freely, our entry and exit are difficult. In such a place, one must devise ingenious strategies to prevent the enemy from becoming a threat—only then can one prevail.
Mei Yaochen said: Where the path is perilous and circuitous, one must employ extraordinary tactics.
He Yanxi said: The later section also states, "In *wei* terrain, devise strategies," meaning that in treacherous ground where one is locked in struggle with the enemy, one must use cunning and deceptive tactics to avoid harm.
Zhang Yu said: In terrain where the front is narrow and the rear is secure, one must employ extraordinary tactics. For example, when Emperor Gao of Han was besieged by the Xiongnu, he used Chen Ping’s ingenious plan to break free—this is a case in point.

In *si* terrain, fight.
Cao Cao said: Fight to the death.
Li Quan said: Placing troops in a place of certain death makes men fight for their own survival, as when Han Xin defeated Zhao.
Mei Yaochen said: When blocked in front and behind, one must fight to the death.
The above outlines the general principles of the nine terrains.
Wang Xi’s commentary on the first five terrains aligns with Cao Cao’s.
He Yanxi said: The later section also states, "In *si* terrain, fight," for in such a place, fighting swiftly to the death may bring survival. If one hesitates and does not fight, morale will falter, supplies will run out—what awaits but death?
Zhang Yu said: When there is nowhere to flee, one must fight to the death, as when Han Xin arrayed his troops with their backs to the river.

From *pi* terrain, where one does not encamp, to this point, the nine variations are discussed, though only five matters are addressed—this is to present the general outline. In the *Nine Terrains* chapter, the nine variations of terrain are discussed, and only six matters are mentioned—this, too, is to present the general outline.

All terrain has its configurations and variations. What is described in the earlier part of the *Nine Terrains* chapter pertains to its configurations, while what follows pertains to its variations. How do we know that the nine variations refer to the variations of the nine terrains? The later text states: "If a general does not understand the nine variations, even if he knows the terrain, he cannot gain its advantages." Furthermore, the *Nine Terrains* chapter states: "The variations of the nine terrains, the advantages of flexibility and restraint—these must not go unexamined." From this, the meaning is clear.

Having already discussed the nine terrains, why does this section again speak of the nine variations? Sunzi wished to expound on the five advantages, so he first presented the nine variations. The nine variations and the five advantages are mutually dependent in application, hence they are discussed together.

There are paths one must not take.
Cao Cao said: In narrow and difficult terrain, one should not proceed; if forced to do so, one must adapt.
Li Quan said: Some roads are perilous and narrow, where ambushes are feared—these must not be taken.
Du Mu said: When Emperor Guangwu of the Later Han sent Generals Ma Yuan and Geng Shu to suppress the Wu Ling Five Streams Barbarians, the army halted at Xiajuan (present-day Chen Zhou). There were two routes to take: one through Hutou was shorter but the waters were treacherous; the other through Chongdao was level but the supply lines were long. The emperor was initially uncertain. When the army arrived, Geng Shu wished to take Chongdao, but Ma Yuan objected, saying it would waste provisions. He proposed advancing to Hutou to seize the enemy’s throat, and the barbarians would be broken. The emperor approved Ma Yuan’s plan, and the army advanced to Hutou. The enemy held the heights and guarded the defiles, the waters ran swift, and the boats could not ascend. The season was hot and humid, and many soldiers died of plague; Ma Yuan himself succumbed to illness and died. Geng Shu wrote to his elder brother, the Marquis of Haotian: "I previously suggested striking Chongdao first. Though the supply lines would be difficult, the troops and horses would be effective. Tens of thousands of soldiers were eager to advance. Now, at Hutou, we cannot proceed, and the army is disheartened, dying in droves—this is truly lamentable."
Jia Lin said: *You* means "to follow." If a path is unfavorable, even if it is short, do not take it.
Du You said: In perilous and difficult terrain, one should not proceed; if forced to do so, one must adapt.
Mei Yaochen said: Avoid perilous and narrow paths.
Wang Xi said: Though a path may be passable, there are times one must not take it, for fear of ambush. For example, when Zhao She advised Zhou Yafu, he warned of ambushes in the narrow defiles of Xiaoguan and advised taking the route through Lantian, exiting Wuguan, and arriving at Luoyang—adding only a day or two to the journey.
Zhang Yu said: In perilous and narrow terrain, chariots cannot travel side by side, nor can cavalry form ranks—thus, one must not proceed. If forced to do so, one must employ adaptive tactics, as when Han Xin, knowing Chen Yu would not use Li Zuoche’s plan, dared to enter the Jingxing Pass.

There are armies one must not attack.
Cao Cao said: Though an army may be attackable, if the terrain is perilous and difficult to hold, lingering would forfeit prior advantages. Even if one gains the position, the benefits would be meager, and the enemy, cornered and desperate, would fight to the death.
Du Mu said: Do not attack elite troops, do not intercept retreating armies, do not press a desperate foe, and do not assault those in *si* terrain.

Should my forces be strong and the enemy weak, yet their vanguard arrive first, still I must not strike—lest I startle them into retreat. Such are the armies one must not attack. This is to say: a general must know there are armies that cannot be attacked, and thus must refrain from attacking them, thereby demonstrating true adaptability. Hence its inclusion in the *Nine Variations*.

Chen Hao said: If seizing a minor advantage cannot overthrow the enemy, do not attack, lest you exhaust your troops in vain. Jia Lin said: When an army can be swayed by awe or conciliation, and its surrender is imminent, do not attack. When bandits are cornered and hold fast to perilous ground, attacking them will provoke a fight to the death—better to fortify your position and await their wavering resolve before seizing them. Du You said: Though an army may be attackable, if the terrain is perilous and difficult to hold, lingering would forfeit prior advantages; even if taken, the gains would be meager. Desperate and trapped troops, armies confined in narrow defiles—these must not be assaulted, for they will fight to the death. Instead, hold fast and await an opening.

Mei Yaochen said: To advance would yield no benefit. Wang Xi said: As Cao Cao observed, though an army may be attackable, if the terrain is perilous and difficult to hold, lingering would forfeit prior advantages; even if taken, the gains would be meager. I say: this also applies to baited troops, elite soldiers, orderly banners, and imposing battle formations. Zhang Yu said: If letting them go incurs no loss, and defeating them brings no gain, then there is no need to attack. Furthermore, if I am weak and they are strong, if I am in the wrong and they are in the right, I must not attack. As when Jin and Chu clashed, Shi Hui said: "The men of Chu do not alter their virtue, punishments, governance, or rites—they cannot be opposed." To refrain from campaigning against them is akin to this principle.

There are cities one must not besiege.
Cao Cao said: A small yet fortified city, rich in provisions, must not be attacked. This is why I bypassed Huaféi and pressed deep into Xuzhou, capturing fourteen counties. Du Mu said: Cao Cao abandoned Huaféi unassailed, thus preserving his full strength to penetrate Xuzhou and seize fourteen counties. This is to say: when the enemy occupies a strategic position with deep moats, towering walls, and abundant stores, intending to stall my forces, besieging it would yield little profit, while failure would demoralize my troops—hence it must not be attacked.

During the reign of Emperor Shun of Song, the governor of Jingzhou, Shen Youzhi, rebelled. He had long amassed soldiers and horses, stockpiled resources, and commanded a hundred thousand troops with two thousand armored steeds. When his army reached Yingcheng, his aide Zang Yin advised: "The dynamics of attack and defense differ—this city cannot be taken in a matter of days. If we delay, our momentum will wane and our prestige suffer. Now, sailing downstream with full force, we may achieve victory in days. Once their root is uprooted, how can Yingcheng hold?" Thus the *Art of War* states: *There are cities one must not attack.* Youzhi ignored this. The governor of Yingjun, Liu Shilong, resisted him. Youzhi threw all his might into the assault but failed. His forces collapsed and fled; he entered the forest and hanged himself.

Later, Emperor Wu of Northern Zhou sought to dispatch troops to Heyang to campaign against Qi. The Minister of Personnel, Yuwen Bi, advised: "When deploying troops, one must choose the ground. Heyang is a vital choke point garrisoned by elite soldiers—attacking it with full force would be difficult to prevail. In my view, the bend of the Fen River, where the garrisons are small and the hills gentle, would be easy to take. No terrain is more suitable for military action." The emperor did not heed his counsel.

The campaign ultimately achieved nothing. Later, when launching another large-scale expedition against Qi, the court ultimately adopted Bi’s strategy and succeeded in destroying Qi.

From the third year of the Yuanhe reign to the present—thirty years in all—the state has launched four attacks against the enemy. The Wei garrison assaulted Nangong County in the enemy’s territory; Shangdang attacked Lincheng County; and Taiyuan struck Hexing Fort. These three enemy strongholds boasted deep moats and sturdy walls, and their granaries, rice, salt, metal, charcoal, hemp, grease—every resource needed for defense—were always stockpiled in quantities that rendered them invincible.
