When our forces struck, the assaults failed to breach the defenses. Exhausted and depleted, they faced the enemy’s reinforcements arriving with fresh troops, and so suffered defeat in every battle. Thus, over thirty years, draining the realm’s strength and manpower to besiege a foe numbering in the tens of thousands, encircling their territory for a cumulative decade, the campaigns yielded not a single foot of gain. The reason: they consistently fell into the enemy’s traps, unable to adapt to changing circumstances.

Jia Lin said: "Even a city defended by loyal and resolute officers who hold fast to their orders must not be attacked."
Mei Yaochen said: "Because it would bring harm."
Wang Xi said: "Though a city may not be strategically vital and thus attackable, one must still fear blunting one’s forces and dulling one’s edge. Or, though the defenses may not be impregnable, if the defenders fight to the death, and even if victory seems assured, should relief forces arrive, the gains would not outweigh the losses."
Zhang Yu said: "If capturing a city cannot be held, or abandoning it poses no threat, then it need not be attacked. Furthermore, if despite deep moats and high ramparts the city cannot be taken, it must not be attacked. As when Shi Gai requested to attack Biyang, and Xun Yu said: 'The city is small but well-fortified. To conquer it would be unworthy; to fail would invite ridicule.'"

**There are lands one must not contest.**
Cao Cao said: "Territory of minor advantage—if contending for it risks losing it, then do not contest it."
Du Mu said: "Such lands are difficult to hold once taken, and losing them brings no harm."
Wu Zixu warned King Fuchai: "If we attack Qi and seize its land, it will be as barren as stone fields."
When Tao Kan was stationed in Wuchang during the Eastern Jin, some advised that the north bank’s Zhu City should be garrisoned with a detachment. Tao never responded, yet the suggestions persisted. One day, he crossed the river to hunt, gathering his officers and saying: "The reason I establish defenses against the enemy is precisely because of the Yangtze. Zhu City lies isolated on the north bank, with no internal support and bordered by barbarian tribes. The tribes covet its wealth, and if we demand tribute, they will not endure it. This would invite the enemy to strike us—thus becoming the cause of disaster, not its prevention. Even if we garrisoned troops there, it would bring no benefit to the south bank. And should the Jie barbarians find an opportunity to exploit, it would be of no use to us." Later, Yu Liang stationed troops there and suffered a crushing defeat.
Mei Yaochen said: "Because gaining it brings no benefit."
Wang Xi said: "Such lands may be strategically vital but already occupied by the enemy, or gaining them would be useless—difficult to hold, for instance."
Zhang Yu said: "If gaining a land offers no advantage in battle, and losing it brings no harm, then do not contest it. Likewise, distant lands, even if taken, will never truly belong to you, and so must not be contested. As when the King of Wu attacked Qi, Wu Yuan warned: 'Gaining land from Qi would be like acquiring stone fields. Better to turn our efforts toward Yue.' The king did not heed him and was ultimately destroyed by Yue."

**There are orders from the sovereign one must not obey.**
Cao Cao said: "If it benefits the situation, do not adhere to the sovereign’s orders."
Li Quan said: "If it benefits the situation, do not adhere to the sovereign’s orders. Examples include Tian Rangju executing Zhuang Jia and Wei Jiang putting Yang Gan to death."
Du Mu said: "As the *Weiliaozi* states: 'War is an inauspicious instrument; contention, a perverse virtue. The general is an office of death. Above, there is no heaven; below, no earth; ahead, no enemy; behind, no sovereign.'"
Jia Lin said: "When decisive victory is assured, one must not defer to the sovereign’s orders. If it benefits the state, acting independently is permissible."
Meng Shi said: "With no enemy ahead and no sovereign behind, matters beyond the frontier are decided by the general."
Mei Yaochen said: "Act according to what is fitting."
These, then, are the five advantages.

Zhang Yu said: "If it benefits the situation, do not obey the sovereign’s orders. As the *Gai Wang* states: 'See what is right and act without waiting for orders.'"

From "There are roads one must not travel" to this point constitute the five advantages. Some say: from "Do not camp on devastated ground" to "There are lands one must not contest" comprise the nine variations. These nine matters all require adapting to circumstances rather than rigid adherence to central directives, hence they are summarized under "There are orders from the sovereign one must not obey."

Thus, a general who understands the advantages of the nine variations knows how to employ troops.
Li Quan said: "This refers to the nine matters above."
Du You said: "The variations of these nine matters all require adapting to circumstances rather than following conventional methods, hence they are called 'variations.'"
Jia Lin said: "The nine variations refer to the general’s responsibility to exercise discretion. Adapting to circumstances and seizing opportunities, not bound by convention, one thereby attains the benefits of flexibility."
Mei Yaochen said: "Mastering the dynamics of the nine terrains and transforming them into advantages."
Wang Xi said: "Only the wise and capable can fully grasp the variations of circumstances."
He Shi said: "Sunzi titled this chapter 'Nine Variations,' yet commentators number over ten, none of whom enumerate the nine variations. Why? From 'Do not camp on devastated ground' onward to 'There are orders from the sovereign one must not obey,' the count reaches ten, inevitably causing confusion. Upon close examination of the text’s intent, it merely describes the advantages and disadvantages of terrain. Moreover, among these ten matters, 'There are orders from the sovereign one must not obey' clearly does not pertain to terrain, standing apart as an obvious outlier. Sunzi’s meaning, I believe, is this: whenever a general receives his commission and assembles his forces, should he encounter any of these nine terrains where harm outweighs benefit, he must adapt—even if the sovereign’s orders command him to camp, stay, attack, or contest, he must not obey."

Moreover, the subsequent text states: "If a general is not versed in the advantages of the nine variations, even though he knows the terrain, he cannot gain the benefits of the land." How could the sovereign’s commands be weighed equally with terrain? Furthermore, in the *Terrain* chapter below, it says: "If the Way of battle ensures victory, though the ruler orders ‘Do not fight,’ one may fight. If the Way of battle does not ensure victory, though the ruler orders ‘Fight,’ one may not fight." The essence of the matter lies entirely here.

Zhang Yu says: "One who alters the constant Way to secure its advantages understands the art of war. If a general is not versed in the advantages of the nine variations, even though he knows the terrain, he cannot gain the benefits of the land."

Jia Lin says: "Though he knows the terrain, if his mind lacks adaptability, not only will he fail to gain its benefits, but he may also suffer harm. The general’s excellence lies in his ability to adapt."

Mei Yaochen says: "Knowing the land but not its variations—how can one gain the land’s benefits?"

Zhang Yu says: "All terrain has form and variation. Knowing the form but not the variation—how can one gain the land’s benefits? If one commands troops without mastering the techniques of the nine variations, even if he knows the five advantages, he cannot employ men effectively."

Cao Cao says: "This refers to the five matters below." The nine variations are also called the five variations.

Jia Lin says: "The five advantages and five variations are included within the nine variations. When encountering circumstances, if one can adapt, then advantage remains; if one cannot adapt, harm befalls men. Thus, there is no fixed form. Only by fully grasping this principle can one employ men effectively."

The five variations are:
- Though the route is near, if one knows there are dangers, ambushes, or hidden perils, one does not take it;
- Though the enemy may be struck, if one knows they are desperate or will fight to the death, one does not strike;
- Though a city appears isolated and vulnerable, if one knows it has ample supplies, sharp troops, a wise general, or loyal ministers, one does not attack;
- Though the land may be contested, if one knows it is difficult to hold, offers no benefit, or risks being seized and causing harm, one does not contest it;
- Though the sovereign’s command seems fitting, if one knows it brings internal discord or disadvantage, one does not accept it.

These five variations require timely adaptation and cannot be predetermined.

Those who covet the five advantages:
- Take the near route;
- Strike the isolated enemy;
- Attack the vulnerable city;
- Contend for the obtainable land;
- Accept commands when the army is usable.

Coveting these five advantages without understanding their variations not only fails to employ men effectively but also leads to military defeat and harm to troops.

Mei Yaochen says: "Knowing advantage but not variation—how can one employ men?"

Wang Xi says: "Though one knows the advantages of the five terrains, without understanding their variations, it is like tuning a zither with glued pegs."

Zhang Yu says: "All warfare involves advantage and variation. Knowing advantage but not variation—how can one employ men effectively?" Cao Cao’s reference to the five matters as the five advantages pertains to the five matters under the nine variations, not the five matters mixed among the advantages and disadvantages below.

Thus, the considerations of the wise must intermingle advantage and harm.

Cao Cao says: "In advantage, think of harm; in harm, think of advantage. When facing difficulties, exercise discretion."

Li Quan says: "Consider both harm to the enemy and benefit to oneself."

Jia Lin says: "Intermingle one for kinship, one for difficulty. Advantage and harm are intertwined; the wise deliberate and act cautiously to secure benefit."

Mei Yaochen echoes Cao Cao’s annotation.

Wang Xi says: "A general versed in the nine variations fully comprehends advantage and harm."

Zhang Yu says: "The wise, in considering matters, though in advantageous terrain, must contemplate potential harm; though in harmful terrain, must contemplate potential advantage. This is also what is meant by adapting to change."

When advantage is intermingled, tasks can be trusted.

Cao Cao says: "If calculations show the enemy cannot use the five terrains to harm me, the tasks at hand can be trusted."

Du Mu says: "‘Trusted’ means to extend. If I seek advantage from the enemy, I must not only see the enemy’s advantages but first weigh the ways the enemy may harm me. Only after intermingling and assessing these can the tasks I undertake be extended."

Jia Lin says: "In times of advantage, contemplate harm to act cautiously. Alternatively: act by intermingling harm with advantage, enforce discipline with authority, and punish with severity. If one’s resolve is unwavering, all tasks will be trusted, and none will dare deceive."

Mei Yaochen says: "Intermingling harm with advantage makes action feasible."

Wang Xi says: "Exhausting all advantages ensures victory."

Zhang Yu says: "Weighing harm against advantage allows one to advance one’s affairs." When Zheng defeated Cai, the people of the state rejoiced. Only Zichan was fearful, saying: "A small state with no civil virtue but military success—nothing could be worse." Later, Chu indeed attacked Zheng. This is an example of considering harm in advantage.

When harm is intermingled, troubles can be resolved.

Cao Cao says: "Having weighed advantage, one must also consider harm. Even with troubles, they can be resolved."

Li Quan says: "The wise, in pursuing matters of advantage and harm, must align with the Way and not reach extremes."

Du Mu says: "To resolve the enemy’s troubles, one must not only see how the enemy may harm me but also weigh how I may gain advantage over the enemy. Only after intermingling and assessing these can troubles be resolved. Thus, the preceding text states: 'The considerations of the wise must intermingle advantage and harm.' For example, if the enemy besieges me, merely breaking out may lead to laxity and pursuit. Better to rouse the troops to fight fiercely, using the advantage of victory to break the siege. One example suffices to illustrate the rest."

Jia Lin says: "In times of harm, contemplate advantage to avoid harm. Thus, placing troops in death ground brings life; casting them into doom brings survival—such is the resolution of trouble."

Mei Yaochen says: "Intermingling advantage with harm dissolves misfortune."

Wang Xi says: "Understanding all harms prevents defeat."

He Shi says: "Advantage and harm give rise to one another; the enlightened constantly deliberate."

Zhang Yu says: "By weighing advantage against harm, one can resolve one’s own difficulties. When Zhang Fang entered Luoyang, he suffered repeated defeats. Some advised him to flee under cover of night, but Fang said: 'The sharpness or dullness of troops is a constant—what matters is turning defeat into victory.' That night, he stealthily advanced to press the enemy and achieved a swift victory. This is an example of contemplating advantage amid harm.

Thus, one bends the feudal lords by means of harm.
Cao Cao says: "Harm what they detest."
Li Quan says: "Harm their governance."
Du Mu says: *Wù* is pronounced as in *yīlù*.
If the enemy has something they detest, and I can exploit and harm it without missing the opportunity, I can bend them to my will.
Jia Lin says: "The methods of inflicting harm are not confined to one path. One may speak of their worthy and wise ministers, causing them to lose their counselors; or send them treacherous men to disrupt their governance; or employ cunning deceit to sow discord between ruler and ministers; or dispatch skilled artisans to exhaust their people and deplete their wealth; or introduce licentious pleasures to corrupt their customs; or present them with beautiful women to confuse and disorder their minds. If these schemes can be secretly executed with hidden plans that do not leak, they will all harm the enemy and bend them to submission."
Mei Yaochen says: "Control them with harm, and they will yield."
Wang Xi says: "Drive them into a position of certain harm, leaving no escape."
Zhang Yu says: "Force them into a position where they must accept harm, and they will submit of themselves. Alternatively, sow distrust between ruler and ministers, or exhaust their people through labor, thereby harming them. Such was Wei Xiaokuan’s estrangement of Hulü Guang, or Gao Lei’s strategy in the conquest of Chen."

One employs the feudal lords through tasks.
Cao Cao says: "Tasks are affairs; exhaust them with toil, as when they enter, we exit, and when they exit, we enter."
Li Quan says: "Burden their agriculture."
Du Mu says: "To labor the enemy is to keep them from rest. One must first have one’s own affairs in order. Affairs refer to a numerous army, a wealthy state, a harmonious people, and effective commands."
Du You says: "To employ the feudal lords through tasks is to keep their people from ease. Examples include the Han forcing the Qin to dig canals."
Or one may entice them with extraordinary skills, extravagant crafts, or excessive abilities, causing them to indulge and become inwardly enslaved. Such are the means to weary the feudal lords."
Mei Yaochen says: "Harass them with tasks, and they will be worn down."
Wang Xi says: "Always appear to be preparing for attack, thereby exhausting the enemy."
Tian Chang said: "Our troops have already been deployed against Lu."
Zhang Yu says: "By burdening them with tasks, one keeps them from rest. Alternatively, one may overawe them with the weight of one’s wealth and strength, thereby making them subservient. Such was the case when Jin and Chu were strong, and the people of Zheng hastened to serve them with sacrificial animals, jade, and silk."

One drives the feudal lords by means of advantage.
Cao Cao says: "Cause them to come of themselves."
Li Quan says: "Entice them with advantage."
Du Mu says: "By luring them with advantage, one causes them to come to me, falling into my plans."
Meng Shi says: "Trend means speed. Skillfully display advantage to make them forget caution and hasten to arrive, whereupon I create a change to control them. This is also called making use of others."
Mei Yaochen concurs with Du Mu’s annotation.
Wang Xi says: "In driving the enemy, one must always maneuver to my advantage."
Zhang Yu says: "Move them with small advantages, ensuring they must hasten toward them."

Thus, the principles of using troops are these: do not rely on their not coming, but rely on our readiness to receive them.
Mei Yaochen says: "What we rely on is unremitting vigilance."
Do not rely on their not attacking, but rely on our having that which cannot be attacked.
Cao Cao says: "Do not forget danger in times of safety; always be prepared."
Li Quan says: "Preparedness must never be lacking."
Du You says: "In safety, think of danger; in existence, think of perishing. Always be prepared."
Mei Yaochen says: "What we depend on is preparedness."
Wang Xi says: "Preparedness means solidity."
He Shi says: "The *Wu Lue* states: 'Even in times of peace, the gentleman does not part with his sword.' When the feudal lords of old met, their armed guards were never dismissed, for though there were civil affairs, there were always military preparations. How much more so when guarding borders and defending fortresses, in the midst of clashing blades? Generally, the reason troops prevail is by striking where the enemy is empty or vulnerable, or raiding where they are lax. If one remains strictly ordered throughout, the enemy will not come. As the *Zuo Zhuan* says: 'Without preparedness for the unexpected, one cannot lead troops.' Formerly, the Jin repelled the Qin by deepening their ramparts and fortifying their army, and the Qin forces could not long endure. When Chu was at Chen, the Wu forces arrived, saw they were prepared, and turned back. When Cheng Bu-shi commanded, he arrayed his troops in proper order, beat the night watches, and managed the military registers, so that the enemy could not encroach. When Zhu Ran served as military commander, though the age was at peace, he would drum the troops morning and evening, and those in camp would all don their armor and form ranks, so that the enemy did not know where to prepare. Thus, his sallies always succeeded. This is what it means to externally repel insult."

The best of the good is ever to dwell in safety while contemplating danger, to dwell in order while contemplating disorder, to be vigilant where there is no sign, and to guard against what has not yet arisen. In decision, nothing surpasses making their retreat perilous, clarifying their ranks and scouts, carefully securing one’s borders, and repairing and completing one’s moats and ramparts. One may increase the supply of military provisions or improve and augment one’s weapons. Thus it is said: "Without prior preparation, one cannot respond to sudden events." And again: "Only through attending to affairs can one be prepared; with preparedness, there is no calamity."

Always ensure that they are wearied while we are at ease, that they are aged while we are vigorous. This may be called seizing the initiative before others and possessing the mind to subdue the enemy’s forces without battle. As for Ju, which relied on its seclusion and was destroyed; Qi, which became complacent with its enemy and was annihilated; Guo, which took Jin lightly and perished; Lu, which attacked Zhu arrogantly and was defeated; Mo’ao, who was careless at Xiao Luo and lost formation; and the Wu ruler, who entered Chao and was reckless—all these may serve as warnings."

Thus, when we possess that with which to await the enemy and that which renders us unassailable, this is called the power of prior preparation.

Zhang Yu says: This means one must consider potential dangers and prepare for them in advance.
The *Tradition* states: "Without preparation against the unforeseen, one cannot send forth an army."

Therefore, a general faces five dangers:
Li Quan and Zhang Yu say: The five matters below.
**Being bent on death may lead to his destruction.**
Cao Cao says: One who is brave yet thoughtless, determined to fight to the death without flexibility, may be ensnared by an ambush.
Li Quan says: Brave yet lacking in strategy.
Du You says: A general who is foolish yet brave is a liability.
Huang Shigong says: The brave act on their will; the foolish disregard their lives.
Wuzi says: When people assess a general, they often focus on bravery—yet bravery is but one small part of generalship. The truly brave may act rashly, and if they do so without regard for advantage, they are unfit to lead.
Mei Yaochen concurs with Li Quan’s annotation. He Shi says: The *Methods of the Sima* states: "To die without victory is inferior." This emphasizes the supremacy of strategy.
Zhang Yu says: A general who is brave yet unstrategic, determined to fight to the death, cannot be matched in direct combat. Instead, one should lure him with an ambush and strike. Thus, the *Methods of the Sima* states: "To die without victory is inferior." If a general lacks cunning and merely leads his troops to die, he cannot prevail.

**Being bent on survival may lead to his capture.**
Cao Cao says: One who sees advantage yet fears the law and does not advance.
Li Quan says: Hesitant and cowardly, he may be captured.
Du Mu says: When the Jin general Liu Yu pursued Huan Xuan along the Yangtze and fought at Zhengrong Shoals, his righteous army numbered only thousands, while Huan Xuan’s forces were vast. Yet Huan Xuan, fearing defeat, kept a light skiff ready beside his flagship, so his troops lacked fighting spirit. Liu Yu’s forces seized the wind, set fires, and charged with all their might, routing Huan Xuan’s army.
Meng Shi says: A cowardly general, intent only on returning alive, avoids close combat. His troops lack discipline, and hesitation pervades his ranks—such a force may be swiftly struck and seized.
The *New Instructions* says: A timid general, seeing opportunity, cannot advance.
Taigong says: To lose advantage and act too late is to invite disaster.
Mei Yaochen says: Cowardly and irresolute.
Wang Xi says: Lacking fighting spirit.
Cao Cao says: Seeing advantage yet hesitating to advance.
Wang Xi adds: Fearing harm, he flees lightly.
He Shi says: The *Methods of the Sima* states: "To survive in doubt is perilous." Doubt is a grave danger.
Zhang Yu says: A general who is timid in battle, intent only on survival, may be overwhelmed by a sudden assault. When Jin and Chu clashed, the Jin general Zhao Yingqi ordered his men to prepare boats on the river in advance, intending to flee first if defeated—this exemplifies such folly.

**Being quick to anger may lead to his humiliation.**
Cao Cao says: A hot-tempered man may be provoked and lured into recklessness.
Li Quan says: An impetuous man, rigid in temperament, may be provoked into certain defeat.
Taizong killed Song Laosheng and pacified Huo Yi.
Du Mu says: "Anger" denotes hot temper; "quickness" denotes impatience, a lack of steadiness. If an enemy general is thus, he may be taunted into reckless advance and defeated.
During the Sixteen Kingdoms, Yao Xiang attacked Huangluo. The Former Qin ruler Fu Sheng sent Fu Huangmei and Deng Qiang to suppress him. Yao Xiang dug deep trenches, raised high ramparts, and refused battle. Deng Qiang advised Fu Huangmei: "Yao Xiang is stubborn and fierce, easily provoked. If we march boldly and press close to his ramparts, he will surely fly into a rage and sally forth—we may then capture him in a single battle." Fu Huangmei followed this counsel. Enraged, Yao Xiang emerged to fight and was slain.
Du You says: An impetuous man may be provoked to his death. One who is quick to anger, rash and reckless, disregards danger—such a man may be goaded into action.
Mei Yaochen says: Impetuous and easily moved.
Wang Xi says: A general must value steadiness; anger and impatience make him easily disturbed.
Zhang Yu says: A stubborn, impatient man may be taunted into action. When Zi Yu of Chu was hot-tempered, the Jin seized his envoy to provoke him, and he indeed pursued the Jin army, only to be defeated.

**Being overly scrupulous may lead to his disgrace.**
Cao Cao says: A man of integrity may be shamed into action.
Li Quan says: A proud and fastidious man may be humiliated.
Du You says: If the enemy fortifies his walls and strengthens his ramparts, seeking to wear down our forces, and we cannot remain, our advantage lies in swift battle. If we discern that their general is quick to anger, we may provoke him; if he is inherently scrupulous, we may shame him.

As when Zhuge Kongming sent a woman’s headdress to Sima Zhongda, seeking to provoke him into battle; Zhongda, enraged, wished to cross the river with his troops, but the Wei emperor dispatched Xin Pi bearing the imperial staff to restrain him. Even Zhongda’s talent could not overcome his fury—how much less might men of ordinary ability?

Mei Yaochen says: Heedless of all but reputation.
Wang Xi concurs with Cao Cao’s note.

Zhang Yu says: A man of purity who cherishes the people may be shamed and thus unsettled—this is certain. A man who cherishes the people may be wearied. Cao Cao says: Strike where he must go; one who cherishes the people will surely hasten by forced marches to their aid, and in rescuing them, he will grow weary. Li Quan says: Attack what he loves, and he will surely roll up his armor to rush to its defense; by loving his people, one may exhaust him with stratagems. Du Mu says: A humane man who loves others fears only slaughter; he cannot abandon the near for the far, nor weigh strength against circumstance. Whatever we attack, he will come to the rescue. Thus may we weary him, wear him down, and then seize our chance. Chen Hao says: There are battles that must be fought and others that need not. Xiang Yu’s relief of Zhao was necessary; the Earl of the West’s abandonment of Liang was not. Jia Lin says: A man of integrity dislikes plunder, a man of benevolence dislikes battle; shame and harass him, and his movements will surely fail. Mei Yaochen says: Exhausted strength leads to collapse. Wang Xi says: Send an unexpected force as if to attack a city; if he cherishes his people, he will repeatedly rush to their aid and thus grow weary. Zhang Yu says: Though the people are to be cherished, one must weigh advantage and harm. If he rescues every minor threat and aids every distant ally, he will be drawn where he must go and thus worn down and trapped.

These five traits are the faults of generals and the disasters of warfare. Chen Hao says: A good general is not thus. He does not cling to death, nor does he cling to life; he adapts to circumstance. He is not quick to anger, nor does he take shame to heart; when opportunity arises, he strikes like a tiger, and when it does not, he shuts his door. His movements and stillness are governed by strategy, not by joy or wrath. Mei Yaochen says: All are the general’s failings, the misfortunes of war. He [the commentator] says: Generals of talent are rare in any age, for their natures often err toward one extreme. Thus Sunzi, in his opening chapter, speaks of the general as possessing wisdom, trustworthiness, benevolence, courage, and strictness—valuing their completeness. Zhang Yu says: Mediocre generals cling to one way and know no change; thus they bring calamity upon themselves and disaster upon their troops. The wise general is not thus: though brave, he does not cling to death; though cautious, he does not cling to life; though resolute, he cannot be shamed; though upright, he cannot be humiliated; though benevolent, he cannot be wearied. The overthrow of armies and the death of generals invariably arise from these five dangers—one cannot but examine them closely. Jia Lin says: These five kinds of men must not be entrusted with high command, for in warfare they will surely fail. Mei Yaochen says: One must be prudent. Zhang Yu says: One must understand adaptability and not cling to a single path.

*End of Scroll Eight of Sunzi’s Commentaries*
*Scroll Nine: The Army on the March*

Cao Cao says: Choose advantageous terrain and advance accordingly.
Wang Xi says: When marching, one must occupy favorable ground and observe the enemy’s situation.
Zhang Yu says: Only after understanding the nine terrains’ variations can one select advantageous positions for marching—hence this follows *Nine Changes*.

Sunzi says: In all matters of positioning troops and assessing the enemy—
Wang Xi says: Positioning troops has four principles; assessing the enemy, thirty-one.
Zhang Yu says: From "crossing mountains and relying on valleys" to "where spies may lurk" pertains to positioning troops. From "if the enemy is near yet still" to "one must scrutinize closely" pertains to assessing the enemy. *Xiang* means to observe and evaluate.

**Cross mountains and rely on valleys.**
Cao Cao says: Stay near water and grass for convenience.
Li Quan says: *Jun* refers to us; *di*, to them. By assessing their positions, the outcome of victory or defeat and the balance of power between us and them become clear. "Crossing mountains" means guarding strategic passes; "relying on valleys" means staying near water and grass. When arraying camps, first dispatch troops to secure defiles, then graze livestock and gather firewood before settling.
Du Mu says: *Jue* means to traverse; *yi*, to approach. When marching through mountainous terrain, one must stay near valleys with water and grass. Wuzi says: "Do not face the mouths of great valleys like celestial furnaces." This means one should not position directly before a valley but may stay near it.
Jia Lin says: When two armies face each other, one must act where advantage lies. "Crossing mountains" means spanning them; "relying on valleys" means staying beside them. Spanning mountains avoids rear vulnerabilities; relying on valleys provides water and grass.
Mei Yaochen says: If mountains block the front, rely on valleys for security.
Wang Xi says: *Jue* means to traverse; *yi*, to stay nearby. Cao Cao says: Stay near water and grass for convenience.
Zhang Yu says: *Jue* means to cross. When marching across mountainous terrain, one must stay near valleys—both for water and grass and to secure defensible positions. During the Later Han, when the Qiang of Wudu raided, Ma Yuan suppressed them. The Qiang held the heights, but Ma Yuan seized advantageous ground, cut off their water and grass, and refused battle. The Qiang, exhausted, all surrendered. They did not know the advantage of relying on valleys.

**Face the sun and occupy high ground.**
Cao Cao says: *Sheng* means yang.
Li Quan says: Facing the sun is *sheng*; being on a mountain is *gao*. One should occupy high, sunlit ground.
Du Mu says: One must position on high ground facing south.
Chen Hao says: If the terrain runs east-west, what then? Answer: Then face east.
Jia Lin says: *Sheng* means yang; "facing the sun" means having no obstructions. Position troops on high ground.
Du You says: *Gao* means yang. *Shi* refers to the ground before one. Position troops on high ground.
Mei Yaochen says: If on a hill, one must face the sun and occupy high ground for advantage.
Zhang Yu says: "Facing the sun" means facing yang; position troops on high ground.

**Do not ascend to engage when the enemy holds the heights.**
Cao Cao says: Do not advance uphill.
Li Quan says: If the enemy descends from high ground, do not ascend to attack them.
Du Mu says: *Long* means high. If the enemy holds the heights, do not advance from below to meet them in battle. Another version reads: "Do not ascend to engage when the enemy descends."
Jia Lin says: Battle favors descending; do not advance uphill.
Du You says: Do not advance uphill. This refers to the foot of mountains. If fighting at the mountain’s base, and the enemy lures you upward, do not pursue.
Mei Yaochen says: If the enemy holds high ground, do not ascend to fight.
Zhang Yu says: If the enemy holds lofty heights, do not ascend to engage. Another version reads: "Do not ascend to engage when the enemy descends." This means if the enemy descends to fight and lures you uphill, do not ascend to meet them.
Mei Yaochen says: When positioning troops in mountains, one must know these three principles.
Zhang Yu says: All elevated and towering terrain is called mountains. When positioning troops in mountains to resist the enemy, these three principles serve as the method.

**When crossing water, stay far from it.**
Cao Cao and Li Quan say: Lure the enemy to ford.
Du Mu says: When Wei general Guo Huai was in Hanzhong, Shu lord Liu Bei sought to cross the Han River to attack. The generals, considering their inferior numbers, wished to array along the riverbank to resist. Guo Huai said: "This would show weakness and fail to thwart the enemy. Better to array far from the water and lure them to cross; strike when half have forded, and Liu Bei can be broken." After arraying, Liu Bei grew suspicious and did not cross.
Mei Yaochen says: If water blocks the front, stay far from it to lure the enemy.
Wang Xi says: We cross the water—Cao’s interpretation is correct.
Zhang Yu says: When marching across water and halting, one must stay somewhat distant from it—both to lure the enemy to ford and to ensure unimpeded advance or retreat. Guo Huai arrayed far from the water, and Liu Bei, realizing this, did not cross.

**If the enemy crosses water to attack, do not meet them in the water; let half ford, then strike—this is advantageous.**
Li Quan says: As when Han Xin slew Long Ju at the Wei River or when Fu Gai defeated the Chu prince at the Qingfa.
Du Mu says: When Chu and Han contended, Xiang Yu personally attacked Peng Yue, leaving his marshal Cao Jiu to guard Chenggao. The Han army taunted him into battle, and Cao Jiu forded the Si River to fight. The Han army struck when half had crossed, routing them. *Shuinei* refers to the riverbank—*nei* was mistakenly written for *rui*.
Mei Yaochen says: If the enemy advances, meeting them at the water’s edge prevents their crossing.
Wang Xi says: *Nei* should be *rui*. Meeting them at the riverbank deters the enemy from fording; staying too far prevents timely advantage. One must act appropriately.
He Shi says: As when the Duke of Song fought the Chu at the Hong River in the Spring and Autumn period.

The Song forces had already formed their ranks, but the Chu army had not yet fully crossed. The Marshal said: "They outnumber us—while they have not yet fully crossed, let us strike." The Duke replied: "We may not." When they had crossed but not yet formed ranks, he was again urged. The Duke said: "Not yet." Only after they had arrayed themselves did he order the attack, and the Song army was utterly routed. The Duke was wounded in the thigh, and his palace guards were annihilated. The Duke of Song’s defiance of this principle led to his defeat.

When Wu invaded Chu, the Chu forces were defeated. As they reached the Qingfa River and prepared to strike, King Fu Gai said: "Cornered beasts still fight—how much more so men? If they know they cannot escape and are determined to die, they will surely defeat us. But if those who cross first believe they can survive, those behind will envy them and lose all will to fight. Strike only when half have crossed." His advice was followed, and they defeated the enemy again.

When the Wei general Guo Huai was in Hanzhong, the Shu lord Liu Bei sought to cross the Han River to attack. The assembled generals counseled: "Our forces are too few." They proposed forming ranks along the riverbank to repel him. Huai said: "This would show weakness and fail to break the enemy—it is no strategy. Better to array our forces far from the water, draw them in, and strike when half have crossed. Then we can shatter Liu Bei." After the ranks were formed, Liu Bei hesitated and dared not cross.

During the Wude era of the Tang, Xue Wanjun and Luo Yi defended You-Yan. Dou Jiande led a hundred thousand men to invade Fanyang. Wanjun said to Yi: "We are outnumbered. If we meet them in battle now, we will lose every time. We must use strategy. Order our weakest troops and feeblest horses to array themselves with their backs to the city, blocking the water to lure them. If the rebels cross and engage, I will conceal a hundred elite cavalry beside the city. Wait until half have crossed, then strike." His plan was adopted. When Jiande crossed the river, Wanjun crushed him.

Zhang Yu says: If the enemy leads their troops across water to fight, do not meet them at the water’s edge. Wait until half have crossed, when their ranks are disordered and their vanguard and rearguard cannot support one another—then strike, and victory is certain. Gongsun Zan defeated the Yellow Turban rebels at Dongguang, and Xue Wanjun crushed Dou Jiande at Fanyang, both employing this tactic.

For those who wish to fight, do not draw near the water to meet the invader.

Cao Cao says: *Fu* means "to draw near."
Li Quan says: If you meet the enemy near the water, they will not dare to cross and engage.
Du Mu says: If we wish to fight, we must not draw near the water to meet the enemy, lest they suspect we will not cross.
The meaning is the same as above, only the roles of host and guest differ.
Du You says:

"Fu" means "to draw near." Drawing near the water to await the enemy prevents them from crossing.

Mei Yaochen says: If you must fight, it is best not to be near the water.
Wang Xi says: If our advantage lies in battle, we should position ourselves farther away, compelling the enemy to cross and engage us.
Zhang Yu says: If we are determined to fight, we must not draw near the water to meet the enemy, lest they fail to cross. If we do not wish to fight, we should block their path with water, preventing them from fording. The Jin general Yang Chufu and the Chu general Zishang encamped on opposite banks of the Zi River. Yang retreated to entice the Chu forces to cross; Zishang likewise retreated, hoping the Jin army would cross. Neither side fought, and both withdrew.

When near water, occupy the high ground.
Cao Cao says: Even by the water, one must take the high ground. Face the water and position the rear against the heights.
Mei Yaochen says: By the water, one should also hold the high ground and face the sun.
Wang Xi says: Cao Cao states, "Even by the water, one must take the high ground." I believe this does not refer to ground near the water. The note below—"lest they flood us"—should likely be placed here.
He Yan says: "Shi sheng" means to face the sun and observe from afar. Encamping on high ground allows one to see the enemy’s movements from a distance, preventing them from approaching stealthily and catching us unawares.
Zhang Yu says: Whether arraying troops along the shore or anchoring ships on the water, one must face the sun and occupy the high ground.

Do not face upstream.
Cao Cao says: Lest they flood us.
Li Quan says: Lest they flood us. When Zhibo inundated Zhao Xiangzi and Emperor Guangwu routed Wang Xun, they defeated them by occupying the high ground upstream.
Du Mu says: Water flows downward; one must not encamp in low-lying areas, lest the enemy breach the banks and flood us. As stated above, "occupy the high ground." Zhuge Liang said: "In naval formations, do not oppose the current." This means our ships must not anchor downstream, for the enemy could then ride the current to press the attack.
Jia Lin says: In low-lying areas near water, the enemy may flood our troops or float poison downstream. "Ying" means "to oppose." Another interpretation: encamping against the current is taboo in warfare.
Mei Yaochen says: Do not station troops downstream to guard against breaches and flooding. In naval battles, facing upstream is also disadvantageous.
Wang Xi says: One should take the upstream position. When Cao Ren of Wei campaigned against Wu and sought to attack the islet of Ruxu, Jiang Ji warned: "The enemy holds the western bank and arrays their ships upstream. If our troops enter the islet, it will be like entering a self-made hell—a path to destruction." Cao Ren ignored this advice and was defeated.
He Yan says: Fighting with the current is easier.
Zhang Yu says: Do not occupy low ground, lest the enemy breach the water and flood us. In naval warfare, one must also avoid the downstream position, for fighting against the current is disadvantageous. One must also guard against the enemy poisoning the water upstream. When the Chu minister resisted the Wu forces, divination foretold an inauspicious battle. Marshal Ziyu said: "We hold the upstream position—why should it not be auspicious?" They joined battle and prevailed. Thus, troops must occupy the upstream position. This refers to naval warfare.

Mei Yaochen says: When stationed by water, one must heed these five principles.
Zhang Yu says: All formations near water refer to naval warfare. When resisting the enemy on water, these five principles serve as the rule.

Avoid salt marshes; if unavoidable, depart swiftly and do not linger.
Chen Hao says: "Chi" refers to saline-alkali land where water and grass are poor, and quicksand makes encampment impossible. The *New Instructions* defines it as land where salt marshes do not yield the five grains.
Jia Lin says: Saline-alkali land often lacks water and grass, making it unsuitable for prolonged stays.
Mei Yaochen says: "Chi" means "remote." Such open, desolate terrain is difficult to defend, so one must not linger.
Wang Xi says: "Chi" means "saline." The land is broad and low-lying, offering no shelter.
Zhang Yu says: The *Book of Punishments* states: "Mountains and rivers, deep marshes, and saline lands." Yan Shigu’s commentary explains: "Shen" refers to deep water; "chi" refers to saline-alkali land. Thus, "chi ze" denotes barren, waterlogged, and marshy areas. Due to their damp, humid qi and poor water and grass, one should pass through quickly.

If forced to engage the enemy in a salt marsh, rely on water and grass and position the rear against dense trees.
Cao Cao says: If unavoidably meeting the enemy in a salt marsh...
Li Quan says: If swift passage is impossible, one must fight near water and with trees at the rear. Where there is water and trees, the ground will not give way or drown.
Du Mu says: Saline-alkali land where no grass or trees grow is called "flying blades." If suddenly encountering the enemy here, one must choose ground with water, grass, and trees to halt.
Du You says: Another version reads "with dense trees at the rear." If unavoidably meeting the enemy in a salt marsh, one should use the trees as a defensive barrier, for terrain aids the army.
Mei Yaochen says: If unavoidably meeting the enemy, rely on nearby water and grass, and position the rear against dense trees.
Wang Xi says: If unexpectedly encountering the enemy here, one must seek advantageous ground and use solid terrain as a rear guard.
Zhang Yu says: If forced to fight in such terrain, one must rely on nearby water and grass for firewood and water, and use the forest as a natural barrier. This refers to warfare in salt marshes.

Mei Yaochen says: When stationed in salt marshes, one must heed these two principles.
Zhang Yu says: When stationed in salt marshes, these two principles serve as the rule.

On level ground, occupy the open and even terrain.
Cao Cao says: This favors chariots and cavalry.
Du Mu says: On level ground, one must choose open, even, and stable terrain to encamp, allowing chariots and cavalry to maneuver freely.
Wang Xi’s note is the same as Cao Cao’s. He Yan’s note is the same as Du Mu’s.
Zhang Yu says: On broad plains and open fields—terrain suited for chariots and cavalry—one must choose flat, even ground without pits or depressions to encamp, facilitating swift charges.

And position the right flank against high ground, with the front low and the rear elevated.
Cao Cao says: This is advantageous for battle.
Li Quan says: All human utility favors the right, hence one leans against it. The front is "dead"—the enemy’s ground; the rear is "alive"—where we station ourselves.
Du Mu says: The *Taigong* states: "An army must have rivers and marshes to its left and hills to its right." "Dead" means low; "alive" means high. The low cannot defend against the high, so this is advantageous for cavalry and chariots.
Jia Lin says: Hills are "alive"; battlefields are "dead." Stationing behind hills stabilizes the army; facing low ground facilitates maneuvering. With high ground to the right, turning movements are smooth.
Mei Yaochen says: Choose level and open terrain for the convenience of chariots and cavalry. With the right flank against hills, one gains strategic advantage. A low front and high rear benefit combat.
Wang Xi says: All armies should face the sun. Since the rear is backed by mountains, the phrase "front dead, rear alive" may be an error in the text.
Zhang Yu says: Even on level ground, one must seek high mounds and position the right flank against them to rely on their strategic advantage. A low front and high rear facilitate charges. This refers to warfare on level ground.
Mei Yaochen says: When stationed on level ground, one must heed these two principles.
Zhang Yu says: When stationed on level ground, these two principles serve as the rule.

In all four types of terrain,
Li Quan says: The four are mountains, waters, salt marshes, and level ground.
Zhang Yu says: The four armies refer to those in mountains, waters, salt marshes, and level ground.
Zhuge Liang says: In mountain warfare, do not ascend to high ground; in water warfare, do not advance against the current; in grassland warfare, do not traverse deep areas; in level-ground warfare, do not charge into empty spaces. These are the advantages of warfare—the means by which the Yellow Emperor triumphed over the Four Emperors.
Cao Cao says: When the Yellow Emperor first established his rule, the feudal lords of the four directions all claimed the title of emperor. He defeated them using these four types of terrain.
Li Quan says: The Yellow Emperor first received the art of war from Feng Hou and then destroyed the four directions, hence the phrase "triumphed over the Four Emperors."
Mei Yaochen says: "Four Emperors" may be a scribal error for "four armies." It means the Yellow Emperor gained the advantages of the four terrains: victorious in mountains when in mountains, victorious on water when on water, victorious in salt marshes when in salt marshes, and victorious on level ground when on level ground.
Wang Xi says: "Four Emperors" may be a corruption of "four armies." Cao Cao says: When the Yellow Emperor first established his rule, the feudal lords of the four directions all claimed the title of emperor, and he defeated them using these four types of terrain. One version omits "also."
He Yan says: Mei’s interpretation is correct.
Zhang Yu says: When the Yellow Emperor first established his rule, the feudal lords of the four directions also claimed the title of emperor, and he defeated them using these four types of terrain. According to the *Records of the Grand Historian*, the Yellow Emperor fought the Flame Emperor at Banquan, the Chiyou at Zhuolu, and drove the Xunyu north. The *Six Secret Teachings of Taigong* states that the Yellow Emperor fought seventy battles to pacify the realm. This confirms the existence of wars with the feudal lords of the four directions. The principles of warfare all originated with the Yellow Emperor, hence the statement.

An army favors high ground and dislikes low,
Mei Yaochen says: High ground is dry and airy, ensuring health and ease while providing strategic advantage. Low ground is damp and humid, breeding illness and hindering combat.
Wang Xi says: There should be descents, not ascents, and one must distance oneself from water hazards.
Zhang Yu says: Occupying high ground facilitates observation and maneuvering; low ground is difficult to defend and prone to illness.

Valuing the yang and disdaining the yin,
Mei Yaochen says: Yang is bright and favorable; yin is dark and adverse.
Wang Xi says: Prolonged exposure to damp, yin terrain breeds illness and damages weapons.
Zhang Yu says: The southeast is yang; the northwest is yin.

Nurturing life and occupying solid ground.
Cao Cao says: Rely on abundance. "Nurturing life" means facing water and grass to graze and raise livestock. "Solid" means high.
Mei Yaochen says: "Nurturing life" means convenient access to water and grass; "solid" means advantageous supply routes.
Wang Xi says: "Nurturing life" refers to resources like water, grass, and grain; "solid" means relying on defensible terrain.
Zhang Yu says: "Nurturing life" means grazing near good water and grass. "Solid" means stationing on elevated ground.

An army free of a hundred illnesses is said to be certain of victory.
Li Quan says: People stationed in low, damp places inevitably contract epidemics; only high, sunny ground is suitable for habitation.
Du Mu says: "Life" is yang; "solid" is high. Stationing on high ground avoids dampness and shade, preventing illness and ensuring victory.
Mei Yaochen says: Mastering these three principles guarantees strategic advantage and prevents disease.
Zhang Yu says: Occupying high ground facing the sun, nurturing life in abundant terrain, ensures victory. The dryness of the earth prevents epidemics.

For hills, embankments, and dikes, one must occupy their yang side and position the right flank against them.
Du Mu says: When encountering hills, embankments, or dikes, one should always station on their southeast side.
Mei Yaochen says: Even if not extremely high, one should face the sun and rely on solid ground to the right.
Wang Xi says: Occupying the yang side ensures comfort and harmony for the troops, and sharpness and utility for weapons.
Zhang Yu says: Facing the sun is prized for clarity; backing high ground provides security.

These are the advantages of warfare, the assistance of terrain.
Mei Yaochen says: The advantages of warfare lie in gaining strategic terrain as assistance.
Zhang Yu says: The benefits of warfare come from the aid of terrain.

If it rains upstream and foam appears in the water, those wishing to ford must wait until the water settles.
Cao Cao says: Fear that the water will suddenly rise mid-crossing.
Li Quan says: Fear of sudden flooding.
Du Mu says: When crossing a mountain stream and seeing foam upstream, it indicates rain in the upper reaches. Wait until the foam subsides and the water calms before fording.

If not, while halfway across, one fears the sudden arrival of a violent flood.
Du You says: Fear that the water will rise abruptly while halfway through the crossing.

When it rains upstream, the water should run clear, yet foam appears instead—this is an omen that the enemy has artificially dammed the water, intending to cut off the army mid-crossing. Whenever terrain holds water that may rise, foam arrives first, all serving to sever the army; one must wait until the water settles.

Mei Yaochen says: While the foam has yet to settle, fear a sudden surge.
Wang Xi says: When water rises, foam appears; *she* means wading on foot. Cao Cao’s explanation is correct.
Zhang Yu says: Before the crossing is complete, a great flood may suddenly arrive. *Mei* refers to the bubbles on the water’s surface.

In all terrain, there are six harmful formations:
**Absolute Gorge**—steep cliffs before and behind, with water flowing transversely through the center.
**Heavenly Well**—encircled by sheer slopes, where ravines converge.
**Heavenly Prison**—three sides enclosed and impassable, easy to enter but difficult to exit.
**Heavenly Net**—dense with grass and trees, where blades and arrows cannot be deployed.
**Heavenly Pit**—low-lying and muddy, impassable for chariots and cavalry.
**Heavenly Fissure**—two mountains facing each other, with a narrow, treacherous ravine between.

All six are noted by Mei Yaochen. One must swiftly depart and not approach them.

Cao Cao says: A deep mountain ravine with high water is an Absolute Gorge; a high perimeter with a low center is a Heavenly Well; a dense, overgrown mountain pass is a Heavenly Prison; terrain that can entrap people like a net is a Heavenly Net; sunken ground is a Heavenly Pit; a narrow mountain ravine, several *chi* deep and several *zhang* long, is a Heavenly Fissure.
Du Mu says: The *Military Records* state: terrain that is sunken and flooded is called a Heavenly Well; a narrow mountain ravine that can cut off passage is a Heavenly Prison; a broad, clear ravine of unknown depth, with muddy roads impassable to men and horses, is a Heavenly Pit; land filled with ditches, pits, and fallen trees is a Heavenly Fissure; forests and thickets that conceal are a Heavenly Net.
Jia Lin says: A deep, wide ravine that severs passage is an Absolute Gorge; a low-lying center is a Heavenly Well; terrain surrounded by ravines and water, with grass interspersed, where the center is uneven and entry and exit are difficult, is a Heavenly Prison; rugged roads, sometimes wide, sometimes narrow, with rough, constricted passage, is a Heavenly Net; marshy ground is a Heavenly Embryo; terrain with steep, impassable sides, narrow and long for several *li*, where passage is nearly impossible and can block entry and exit, is a Heavenly Fissure. These six harmful terrains must not be approached or relied upon.
Mei Yaochen says: If even approaching these six harms is unwise, how much more so is lingering?
Wang Xi says: I believe *Absolute Gorge* should be *Absolute Heavenly Gorge*, with the character *Heavenly* omitted. These six are all natural formations. *Prison* implies confinement like a jail, *Net* like a snare, *Pit* like a muddy trench, and *Fissure* like terrain riddled with gaps and crevices. When marching through such places, do not draw near; otherwise, one may encounter unforeseen dangers where neither wisdom nor strength can be applied.
Zhang Yu says: A deep, precipitous ravine impossible to cross is an Absolute Gorge; a high perimeter with a low center, where waters converge, is a Heavenly Well; terrain encircled by perilous mountains, with a narrow entrance, is a Heavenly Prison; tangled forests and concealed reeds form a Heavenly Net; muddy pools and mires that bog down chariots and cavalry are a Heavenly Pit; narrow, treacherous roads with many pits and hollows are a Heavenly Fissure. Whenever encountering such terrain, one should pass at a distance and not approach. If I keep my distance while the enemy draws near, if I face it while the enemy turns their back—
Cao Cao says: In warfare, one should always keep far from the six harms; if the enemy approaches or turns their back to them, then I gain advantage while they face misfortune.
Li Quan says: A skilled commander leads the enemy into harmful terrain.
Du Mu says: *Facing* means to turn toward; *backing* means to rely upon. When encountering these six harmful terrains, if I keep my distance and face them, my movements remain unrestricted; if the enemy draws near and relies upon them, their actions will be hindered. Thus, I gain advantage while they face misfortune.
Mei Yaochen says: Regarding the six harms, I should keep my distance while the enemy approaches, I should face them while the enemy relies upon them—then I gain advantage while they face misfortune.
Zhang Yu says: In these six harmful terrains, if I keep my distance and face them while the enemy draws near and relies upon them, then my movements are advantageous while theirs are fraught with peril.

When the army marches through terrain with perilous obstacles, stagnant pools, reeds, mountain forests, or dense thickets, one must carefully search and scout them, for these are places where ambushes and spies may lurk.
Cao Cao says: *Perilous* refers to uneven terrain; *obstacles* refer to abundant water. *Stagnant pools* are ponds; *wells* are low-lying areas. *Reeds* are where grasses gather; *mountain forests* are where trees cluster. *Dense thickets* are places that can conceal. The above discusses terrain; the following concerns assessing the enemy’s situation.
Li Quan says: The following cautions against the enemy’s hidden ambushes and deceptions.
Mei Yaochen says: *Perilous obstacles* are narrow passes, the domain of mountains and forests; *stagnant pools* and *wells* are low-lying areas where reeds grow. All are dense enough to conceal. One must thoroughly search them, fearing the presence of hidden troops.

Zhang Yu says: *Perilous obstacles* are hilly terrain where mountain forests thrive; *stagnant pools* and *wells* are low-lying areas where reeds flourish. All are dense enough to conceal. One must thoroughly search them, fearing hidden troops within. Moreover, one must consider that spies may lurk unseen, observing our strengths and weaknesses, listening to our commands—thus, hidden threats encompass two concerns.

When the enemy is near yet remains still, they rely on their terrain’s defensibility.
Mei Yaochen says: If near yet unmoving, they depend on their advantageous position.
Wang Xi says: They trust in their terrain and thus feel no fear.

When the enemy is distant yet provokes battle, they seek to lure us forward.
Du Mu says: If they were close and provoked us, they would press us directly, fearing we would not advance—thus they remain distant.
Chen Hao says: If the enemy is near yet does not provoke battle, they rely on their defensive terrain. If distant yet provokes battle, they aim to entice us forward, then seize the advantage and strike.
Mei Yaochen concurs with Chen Hao’s annotation.
Wang Xi says: They seek to compel our advance. *Provocation* means goading the boldest enemy into battle.

Zhang Yu says: When two armies are close yet remain unmoving, the enemy trusts in their fortified terrain. When two armies are distant yet repeatedly provoke battle, they seek to lure us forward.
Wei Liaozi says: Those who divide and hold perilous terrain lack the will to fight—if the enemy has already secured such ground, do not engage them. It also says: Those who provoke battle lack full vigor. When the enemy is distant and provokes battle to entice us forward, they cannot be met with full force—this aligns with the same principle.

If the enemy occupies level ground, it is to their advantage.
Cao Cao says: Their position is advantageous.
Li Quan says: Occupying level ground benefits their governance.
Du Mu says: If the enemy does not occupy perilous terrain but instead holds level ground, they must have some strategic advantage.
An alternate version reads: When soldiers vie for a position, it is because the ground is both level and advantageous.
Chen Hao says: If the enemy secures advantageous terrain, their officers and troops will compete to occupy it.
Jia Lin says: If the enemy’s position offers many advantages, they provoke us to advance toward their convenience, making victory easier—beware of falling into their trap.
Mei Yaochen says: Their position is level and advantageous, so they come to provoke battle.
Wang Xi follows Cao Cao’s annotation.

Zhang Yu says: If the enemy abandons perilous terrain for level ground, they must have some advantage. Alternatively, they may wish us to advance, so they occupy open ground to display its benefits and entice us.

When trees rustle en masse, the enemy approaches.
Cao Cao says: They fell trees to clear a path, causing movement.
Mei Yaochen concurs with Cao Cao’s annotation.
Zhang Yu says: Armies must send skilled scouts to high ground to observe the enemy. If they see trees swaying, it means the enemy is felling them to clear a path. Alternatively, they may be preparing weapons—like the Jin forces who felled trees to bolster their troops.

When grasses are thick with obstructions, the enemy seeks to deceive.
Cao Cao says: They bind grass into barriers to sow doubt.
Du Mu says: If the enemy’s camp is unfinished or they have secretly withdrawn, fearing pursuit or ambush, they bind grass into clusters to simulate hidden troops, making us hesitate to advance.
Jia Lin says: If they create many grass barriers, they aim to make us suspect the area is unoccupied, while planning a separate attack—we must carefully prepare.
Du You says: Thick grass barriers are meant to make us doubt; dense vegetation with many obstructions suggests the enemy has fled, fearing pursuit, and has set up barriers to make us suspect an ambush.
Zhang Yu says: If the enemy wishes to evade us, they may create many obstructions, leaving false traces before fleeing to avoid pursuit. Or, if they plan to ambush us, they may gather grass and trees to simulate encampments, making us guard one side while they strike another—all to sow deception.

When birds take flight, troops lie in ambush below.
Cao Cao says: Birds rising from below indicate hidden troops.
Li Quan says: Concealed troops are called an ambush.
Du You says: Hidden troops passing below disturb the birds, startling them into flight.
Zhang Yu says: If birds fly calmly but suddenly rise, hidden troops lie below.

When beasts flee in alarm, the enemy seeks to overwhelm.
Cao Cao says: The enemy spreads their forces wide to envelop us.
Li Quan says: An unexpected arrival is called an *overwhelming* attack.
Du Mu says: When the enemy plans to overwhelm us, they approach through perilous terrain or dense forests, startling hidden beasts into flight. *Overwhelm* means they come to ambush us.
Chen Hao says: *Overwhelm* refers to hiding in forests and stealthily advancing to strike. Like scouts during a fierce battle, they may emerge from the flanks or rear, startling hidden beasts.
Mei Yaochen says: If beasts flee in alarm, an ambush lies nearby.
Zhang Yu says: Those who seek to overwhelm others approach through perilous terrain or dense vegetation, startling hidden beasts into flight.

When dust rises high and sharp, chariots approach from the east.
Du Mu says: Chariots and horses move swiftly in single file, so the dust rises high and pointed.
Du You says: Swift-moving chariots and horses stir up dust, making it rise.
Mei Yaochen says: The weight of hooves and wheels ensures the dust rises high and sharp.
Zhang Yu says: Chariots and horses move swiftly with great force, their tracks advancing in succession, so the dust rises high and straight. Armies must station scouts ahead; if they spot enemy dust, they must gallop to report to the commander—like Pan Dang, who observed Jin’s dust and sent a rider to warn.

When dust is low and broad, infantry approaches.
Du Mu says: Foot soldiers move slowly and can advance in formation, so the dust is low and wide.

Mei Yaochen says: Foot soldiers tread lightly and low, so the dust must be low and broad.
Wang Xi says: Chariots and horses raise dust fiercely, while infantry moves more slowly.
Zhang Yu says: Infantry marches slowly with light tracks, and their formations are spaced widely, so the dust is low and spreading.

When dust is scattered and striated, firewood-gatherers approach.
Li Quan says: In the signs of smoke and dust, when the Jin army attacked Qi, they dragged branches behind them. The Qi forces climbed the mountains, saw the dust, and feared their numbers, then fled by night. The coming of firewood embodies this meaning. Here, Quan interprets the two characters for "firewood-gathering" as the single word "firewood."
Du Mu says: Firewood-gatherers move in all directions, so the dust disperses and spreads. "Striated" describes a crisscrossed, broken appearance.
Mei Yaochen says: Firewood-gatherers roam everywhere, so the dust must be scattered and streaked.
Wang Xi says: "Striated" depicts a fine, intermittent, and broken appearance.
Zhang Yu says: Dispatching servants to gather firewood in various places, the dust scatters and forms winding trails.

When dust is sparse and comes and goes, a camp is being established.
Du Mu says: To set up a camp, light cavalry scouts move back and forth, so the dust is minimal.
Mei Yaochen says: Light cavalry scouting for a camp site produces little dust as they come and go.
Zhang Yu says: Whenever a camp is to be divided and fortified, light cavalry must be sent to survey the terrain from all sides, seeking to understand the risks, advantages, breadth, and narrowness of the land. Thus, the dust is faint and intermittent.

When envoys speak humbly yet preparations increase, an advance is imminent.
Cao Cao says: When their envoys arrive with humble words, they send spies to observe—while the enemy strengthens their defenses.
Du Mu says: If the enemy’s envoys arrive speaking humbly, then reinforce ramparts and walls as if fearing us, they aim to make us arrogant and careless, then attack. When Zhao She relieved Yuyu, he halted thirty li from Handan, strengthened his fortifications, and did not advance. When Qin spies arrived, he treated them well and sent them off. The spies reported back to the Qin general, who was overjoyed, saying, "Yuyu will no longer belong to Zhao." After dispatching the Qin spies, Zhao She advanced swiftly by forced marches, caught Qin unprepared, struck them, and crushed the Qin army.
Mei Yaochen says: Those who intend to advance speak humbly outwardly while strengthening defenses inwardly, feigning deference.
Zhang Yu says: When envoys arrive with humble words while the enemy increases their preparations, they seek to make us complacent before advancing. When Tian Dan defended Jimo, the Yan general Qi Jie besieged the city. Dan personally carried baskets and spades, laboring alongside his soldiers, had his wives and concubines weave among the ranks, and distributed food to feast his troops. He then sent women to the city walls to negotiate surrender. The Yan forces rejoiced. He also collected a thousand *yi* of gold from the people, had the wealthy send letters to the Yan general, saying, "When the city surrenders, we beg you not to enslave our wives and concubines." The Yan forces grew even more lax. Then Dan launched a surprise attack and routed them.

When envoys speak boldly while advancing their forces, they intend to retreat.
Cao Cao says: This is deception.
Du Mu says: When King Fuchai of Wu marched north to meet Duke Ding of Jin at Huangchi, King Goujian of Yue attacked Wu. As Wu and Jin vied for supremacy, the outcome remained undecided. Fearing defeat, the King of Wu consulted his ministers: "Is it better to abandon the meeting and return, or to attend and take precedence over Jin?" Wangsun Luo replied, "We must attend and take precedence." The King asked, "How can we do so?" Luo said, "Tonight, we must provoke battle to rally the people’s will, and then we can prevail." That evening, the King of Wu deployed thirty thousand armored troops a *li* from the Jin army, their war cries shaking heaven and earth. Jin sent Dong He to assess the situation. The King of Wu addressed him personally: "Whether I serve you or not, it will be decided today." Dong He said, "I observe the King of Wu’s countenance—he seems deeply troubled. His generals seek to poison us; we cannot engage them in battle." Thus, Jin allowed Wu to take precedence in the covenant. After the meeting, the King of Wu returned home.
Du You says: Deceptive maneuvers and displays of fearlessness signal an intent to retreat.
Mei Yaochen says: When those who wish to retreat employ bold words and advance their forces, they seek to intimidate us.
Wang Xi says: Bold words and feigned advances are meant to prevent us from anticipating their withdrawal.
Zhang Yu says: When envoys speak boldly and their army advances, they aim to coerce us into allowing their retreat. A Qin envoy warned the Jin army at night: "Neither side’s troops wish to fight. Tomorrow, let us meet." Jin’s Yu Ping observed, "The envoy’s darting eyes and unrestrained speech betray his fear." Qin indeed fled under cover of night.

When light chariots emerge first and take position to the side, they are forming battle lines.
Cao Cao says: They deploy troops to signal their readiness for battle.
Du Mu says: Light chariots are sent ahead to establish the boundaries of the battlefield.
Jia Lin says: Light chariots advance to arrange formations before engaging.
Zhang Yu says: Light chariots are war chariots. Their emergence signals the formation of battle lines. As in the *Fish-Scale Formation*, chariots lead while infantry follow. Thus, when chariots appear first, they indicate an intent to fight.

When one requests peace without prior agreement, they harbor schemes.
Li Quan says: Requests for peace without a binding covenant are always ploys against others. The deceptions of Tian Dan against Qi Jie and Ji Xin against Xiang Yu exemplify this.
Du Mu says: In the third year of the Zhenyuan era, the Tubo chieftain Shang Jiezan, having ravaged Hequ and lost half his men and horses to plague, feared he could not return. He feigned sincerity to the Minister Shi Zhong Ma Sui and requested an alliance. Sui agreed. At the time, the military governor of Hezhong, Hun Cheng, memorialized: "If the court stations troops at the border and plans an offensive, we may consider Tubo’s request for an alliance. But now, with no demands from the court, their sudden plea for peace is surely insincere." The emperor did not heed this. Hun Jian led twenty thousand troops to camp at Pingliang County in Jingzhou, where the altar for the alliance stood thirty *li* to the west. On the thirteenth day of the fifth month, Jian led three thousand men to the altar, and Tubo, clad in hidden armor, seized the opportunity to ambush them.
Chen Hao says: Ambushes during alliances are not unique to our dynasty. When Jin and Chu met at Song, Chu’s troops wore concealed armor, intending to surprise Jin. Jin discovered this and thus lost trust.
Du You says: Requests for peace without prior agreement are always ploys.
Mei Yaochen says: Unprovoked pleas for peace conceal treachery.
Wang Xi says: Sudden, unprovoked requests for peace warrant vigilance against ulterior motives.
Zhang Yu says: Unprovoked pleas for peace always conceal treachery. When Emperor Gaozu of Han sought to strike the Qin army, he sent Li Yiji to bribe the Qin general Jia Shu with precious gifts. The general indeed sought peace. Gaozu exploited his complacency and attacked, crushing the Qin forces. Later, the Jin general Li Ju defended Rongyang against Liu Chang’s thirty thousand troops. Ju sent envoys bearing oxen and wine to feign surrender, concealing his elite troops while exposing only weak soldiers. Chang feasted his men, leaving them drunk and sated. Ju launched a night raid, and Chang barely escaped with his life.

When troops rush to arrange chariots and formations, they have set a time.
Li Quan says: When battle is imminent, troops hasten to prepare.
Du Mu says: Earlier, light chariots emerged first to establish the battlefield’s boundaries, planting flags as markers. Troops now rush to these markers to form ranks. Flags serve as the appointed time, signaling the people to assemble below. As the *Rites of Zhou* states: "Chariots gallop, infantry rush, and all halt at the markers."
Jia Lin says: Ordinary appointments do not require rushing; this signals reinforcements from afar. If a specific time is set, they intend to coordinate an attack. Prepare swiftly.
Mei Yaochen says: Flags are planted as markers, and troops rush to form ranks.
Wang Xi says: Forming ranks and setting a time for the people signals an intent to fight.
Zhang Yu says: Flags serve as markers, and the people are summoned to assemble below. Thus, they rush to their positions. As the *Rites of Zhou* states: "Chariots gallop, infantry rush, and all halt at the markers."

Half-advancing, half-retreating—this is a lure.
Li Quan says: Scattered at the front.
Du Mu says: Feigning disarray and disorder to entice me into advancing.
Mei Yaochen says: Advancing and retreating inconsistently to lure me.
Wang Xi says: A deceptive display of chaos.
Zhang Yu says: A false show of disorder—this is to lure me. As when Wuzi used prisoners to feign disarray and entice the Chu forces.

Leaning on weapons while standing—this signals hunger.
Li Quan says: Exhausted and unable to stand in formation.
Du Mu says: Without food, they must be weary, hence leaning on their weapons. Another version begins with "leaning" here.
Du You says: Leaning on spears and halberds while standing reveals their hunger.
Mei Yaochen says: Leaning on their weapons while standing clearly shows the signs of hunger and exhaustion.
Wang Xi says: Leaning on weapons is the mark of distress and starvation.
Zhang Yu says: Without food, all grow weary, so they lean on their weapons. When the Three Armies eat and drink, all do so together; thus, if one is hungry, the entire army must be.

Drawing water and drinking before others—this signals thirst.
Li Quan says: Drawing water but drinking before it reaches camp reveals the soldiers’ thirst.
Du Mu says: Ordered to draw water, they drink before it arrives—this is thirst. Observing one, the state of the Three Armies may be known.
Mei Yaochen echoes Du Mu’s note.
Wang Xi says: This reveals the urgency of their hunger and thirst.
Zhang Yu says: Drawing water but drinking before returning to camp shows the entire army is parched.

Seeing advantage but not advancing—this signals exhaustion.
Cao Cao says: The weariness of the soldiers.
Li Quan says: The soldiers are difficult to employ.
Du You says: The soldiers are fatigued. When the enemy approaches and sees an opportunity but cannot press forward, they are exhausted.
Mei Yaochen says: When men are worn out, what advantage can they pursue?
Zhang Yu says: When soldiers are fatigued, they cannot be made to fight; thus, though they see an advantage, the general dare not advance.

Birds gathering—this signals emptiness.
Li Quan says: Birds on the city walls signal the army’s retreat.
Du Mu says: Leaving behind a false form to conceal a retreat. When Qi and Jin were locked in stalemate, Shu Xiang remarked: "The crows’ cries are joyful—Qi’s army must be retreating." Later, when Zhou King Xian’s general Yuan attacked Northern Qi, he prepared to withdraw, using cypress leaves as tents and burning dung heaps before departing. The Northern Qi forces observed this for two days before realizing the camp was empty, but by then, pursuit was futile. This was leaving behind a false form to conceal a retreat.
Chen Hao says: This means that if the enemy has departed, their camp must be empty; with no one to fear them, birds will gather and cry upon it. When Prince Yuan of Chu attacked Zheng and was about to flee, his advisors warned: "There are birds on the Chu camp." He halted, realizing it was a false form left to conceal a retreat.

This passage expounds upon Master Sun’s method of discerning the enemy’s true and false intentions. Du You states: When the enemy constructs grand fortifications to display their might, yet crows gather and perch within, it signals emptiness. Mei Yaochen says: Once the enemy has departed, their fortifications lie vacant; with no cause for fear, birds alight and gather upon them. Zhang Yu observes: Whenever an enemy retreats in secret, they abandon their encampments; seeing them empty, birds cry out and congregate above. When Chu attacked Zheng and the Zheng forces prepared to flee, a scout reported: "Birds are on the Chu camp." They halted. Again, when Jin attacked Qi, Shu Xiang remarked: "Birds are on the city walls—Qi’s army must be retreating." This is leaving behind a false form to conceal a retreat.

As for nighttime cries, they betray fear. Cao Cao says: When soldiers cry out at night, their commander lacks courage. Li Quan notes: If troops are timid and their general weak, they will cry out in alarm. Du Mu explains: Fear and unease drive them to shout in the night to bolster their spirits. Chen Hao states: If one in ten men is brave, even if the other nine are cowardly, they may find reassurance in that single man’s courage. Yet when soldiers cry out at night, it reveals their commander’s lack of valor. Cao’s interpretation is correct. The Meng family concurs with Chen Hao’s annotation.

Zhang Yu states: The Three Armies take their commander as their master. If the commander lacks courage, he cannot reassure his troops, so the soldiers grow fearful and cry out at night. As was the case when the Jin army’s clamor filled the night.

When the army is in turmoil, the commander lacks gravity. Li Quan says: If the commander lacks dignity and weight, the army will be in turmoil. Du Mu explains: If his advance and retreat, his every movement, are frivolous and hasty, lacking gravity, the soldiers will likewise grow disordered. Chen Hao states: When the commander’s laws and orders are not strict, his bearing not grave, the troops will fall into chaos. Mei Yaochen concurs with Chen Hao’s annotation.

Zhang Yu says: When the army is frequently alarmed and disordered, the commander lacks steadfastness. When Zhang Liao was stationed at Changshe, one night the camp suddenly erupted in chaos, and the entire army was thrown into confusion. Zhang Liao ordered his attendants to remain still, saying: This must be someone plotting mischief, seeking to stir up unrest. He then commanded the soldiers to sit calmly, while he himself stood in their midst. Soon, order was restored. This is what it means to maintain steadfastness.

When banners and flags sway, there is disorder. Du Mu says: When Duke Zhuang of Lu defeated Qi at Changshao, Cao Gui requested permission to pursue. The Duke asked: How so? Cao Gui replied: Observe their chariot tracks in disarray and their banners drooping—thus we pursue. Du You explains: When banners and flags move erratically, swaying east and west, leaning and toppling, there is disorder. Mei Yaochen says: When banners and flags constantly shift, falling out of rank and order, there is no discipline. Zhang Yu states: Banners and flags serve to unify the troops; when they sway without stability, the ranks are in disarray.

When officers grow angry, the troops are weary. Du Mu says: When all are exhausted and worn, officers no longer fear and vent their anger. Chen Hao states: When the commander launches unnecessary campaigns, everyone grows weary and worn. Jia Lin says: When men are distressed, they grow angry. Mei Yaochen explains: When officers and soldiers are weary and vexed, they rage without fear. Zhang Yu says: When policies and orders are inconsistent, the people grow weary, and officers often grow angry. When Jin and Chu clashed, Jin’s deputy generals Zhao Zhan and Wei Qi, enraged and seeking to sabotage the Jin army, both offered their services to Chu. As Que Ke said: Two resentments have gone forth—if we do not prepare, we will surely be defeated.

When horses are fed grain and men eat meat, when the army has no suspended cooking pots and does not return to its quarters, it is a desperate enemy. The *Lost Duke* states: To slaughter horses and eat their meat means the army is out of provisions; when there are no suspended cooking pots and they do not return to their quarters, it is a desperate enemy. Li Quan says: They kill their horses to eat the meat, hence the army is out of provisions. Not returning to camp means they are too pressed to tend to cooking fires.
