Du Mu explains: "Feeding horses grain" means using stored grain to feed the horses. "Eating meat" means slaughtering oxen and horses to feast the troops. "No suspended cooking pots" means smashing them all, showing they will not cook again. "Not returning to their quarters" means maintaining formations day and night. All of this marks a desperate enemy, one determined to fight to the death. *Fou* (缶) is pronounced *fu*, a cooking vessel. Mei Yaochen says: They feed their horses with grain and feast their troops with slaughtered livestock, discard their pots and no longer cook, expose themselves without returning to camp—this is their resolve to fight to the finish. Wang Xi states: Feeding horses grain and eating meat strengthens their force for a prolonged struggle. Having no cooking pots means they will not eat again. Not returning to camp means they have no intention of retreat. All of this signifies a final, desperate battle. Against such an enemy, one should hold fast and await their exhaustion. Zhang Yu says: They discard grain to feed their horses, slaughter livestock to feast their troops, smash their cauldrons and pots, no longer cook, expose their forces, and do not return to camp—this is a desperate enemy. Examples include Meng Ming burning his boats and the Chu army smashing their cauldrons.

When officers speak softly and repeatedly, whispering among themselves, they have lost the support of their troops. Cao Cao says: *Zhunzhun* describes their manner of speech; *xixi* describes their loss of resolve. Li Quan explains: *Zhunzhun xixi* depicts furtive whispering. When the soldiers’ hearts fear their superiors, they speak in hushed, private tones—this is losing the support of the troops. Du Mu says: *Zhunzhun* describes a voice lacking vigor, short of breath; *xixi* describes a state of disarray and confusion. Such behavior reveals inner turmoil, the loss of the troops’ loyalty. Jia Lin says: *Zhunzhun* depicts secret discussions; *xixi* depicts unease. Speaking slowly with others, asking questions in turn.

Such behavior will surely lead to the dispersal and loss of one's troops.

Mei Yaochen says: *Zhunzhun* describes the utterance of earnest sincerity; *xixi* describes the neglect of duties. Speaking slowly with forced composure betrays fear of losing the troops.

Wang Xi says: *Zhunzhun* depicts the manner of earnest speech; *xixi* reveals anxiety toward one’s superiors. When a commander has lost the hearts of his men, the troops whisper among themselves, speaking earnestly while harboring grievances against their leader.

He Shi says: When two men whisper in secret, they are criticizing their commanding officer.

Zhang Yu says: *Zhunzhun* describes speech; *xixi* describes gathering. When soldiers huddle together in private, speaking in low, slow tones to disparage their superiors, it shows they have lost the support of their troops.

Frequent rewards indicate distress.
Li Quan says: Distress leads to frequent rewards to encourage progress.
Du Mu says: When strength is exhausted and one fears rebellion, frequent rewards are given to please the troops.
Meng Shi says: Military resources are depleted. Fearing the soldiers’ morale will wane, small favors are bestowed separately.
Mei Yaochen says: When one’s position is desperate and fear of desertion arises, repeated rewards are given to appease the troops.
Wang Xi says: When the troops are distressed and lack harmony, frequent rewards are used to please them.
Zhang Yu says: When one’s strength is exhausted and loyalty wavers, repeated rewards are used to pacify the soldiers.

Frequent punishments indicate hardship.
Li Quan says: Hardship leads to frequent punishments to spur the troops.
Du Mu says: When manpower is exhausted and soldiers no longer fear punishment, frequent punishments are used to intimidate them.
Mei Yaochen says: When the troops are worn out and unable to endure orders, repeated punishments are used to establish authority.
Wang Xi says: When the troops are exhausted and lack diligence, frequent punishments are used to coerce them.
Zhang Yu says: When strength is depleted and soldiers are difficult to command, frequent punishments are used to instill fear.

To act harshly first and then fear one’s troops is the height of imprudence.
Cao Cao says: To first underestimate the enemy and later fear one’s own troops is to harbor ill will toward them.
Li Quan says: To act recklessly first and then fear one’s troops is the mark of a warrior without strategy—utter imprudence.
Du Mu says: This is the height of imprecise assessment of the enemy.
Jia Lin says: When orders are unclear and the troops are untrained, a commander who first acts with brutal force will grow fearful as the troops rebel—this is the extreme of cowardice.
Mei Yaochen says: To first enforce harsh discipline and later fear the troops’ desertion is the height of imprudent command.
Wang Xi says: When the enemy first acts with violence and later fears the desertion of their troops, it shows the commander’s extreme lack of prudence.
He Shi says: Balancing leniency and severity is the essence of precise generalship.
Zhang Yu says: To first underestimate the enemy and later fear one’s own men. Alternatively, to first rule with harshness and later fear rebellion is to wield power without care—utter imprudence. Thus, the preceding passage refers to frequent rewards and punishments.

Those who come bearing tribute and apologies seek respite.
Li Quan says: To advance slowly and retreat swiftly is called *commission and apology*.
Du Mu says: Those who offer tribute and apologies do so because their strength is exhausted or for other reasons—they seek respite.
Jia Lin says: Those who appear dejected and speak apologetically seek mutual resolution.
Du You says: When neither side has gained the upper hand, yet one side lowers its morale and offers apologies, it seeks respite.
Mei Yaochen says: When strength is depleted and one desires to cease hostilities, tribute is offered in apology.
Wang Xi says: Their strength cannot endure.
Zhang Yu says: To send beloved envoys bearing tribute and apologies shows their strength is utterly exhausted—they seek to rest and end the conflict.

When troops advance in fury yet do not engage in battle for a long time, nor do they withdraw, one must observe them carefully.
Cao Cao says: Beware of hidden ambushes.
Li Quan says: Such an army must have a hidden ambush—observe carefully.
Du Mu says: When troops advance in rage but do not clash, nor do they retreat, they are waiting for something. One must carefully observe them, lest a hidden ambush arise from the flanks.
Meng Shi says: Prepare for other contingencies.
Mei Yaochen says: When troops advance in anger but do not engage in battle, nor do they withdraw, they must have a hidden ambush awaiting. The above discusses enemy conditions.

Zhang Yu says: When troops advance in fury but neither engage nor retreat, one must secretly observe them—there must be a hidden ambush.

An army does not benefit from mere numbers.
Cao Cao says: Strength and power are balanced. (An alternate version reads: An army does not prize greater numbers.)
Jia Lin says: Victory does not lie in outnumbering the enemy, but in using the few to defeat the many.
Wang Xi says: I say that balanced strength and power suffice; greater numbers are not advantageous.
Zhang Yu says: An army does not gain advantage by outnumbering the enemy—it means strength and power are balanced.

Only avoid reckless advance.
Cao Cao says: No opportunity has presented itself.
Jia Lin says: One must not rely solely on martial prowess; reckless advance leads to brutality.
Wang Xi says: One must not rely solely on force, but assess the enemy with strategy and wisdom.
Zhang Yu says: *Wu* means strength. To advance recklessly without assessing the advantage means no opportunity has arisen.

It is sufficient to combine strength, assess the enemy, and seize the advantage.
Cao Cao says: Even auxiliary forces suffice.
Li Quan says: When troops are numerous and strength is balanced, victory goes to the one who gains the right men.
Du Mu says: When my strength and the enemy’s are equal, yet I cannot advance recklessly, it is because I have not yet found the right men. But even among auxiliary forces, selecting the capable is enough to combine strength, assess the enemy, and secure victory—no need to seek elsewhere.
Chen Hao says: My strength is not greater than the enemy’s, nor is there an advantageous opportunity to advance. I need not seek reinforcements from other states; by combining strength and selecting the right men from auxiliary forces, I can still defeat the enemy.
Jia Lin says: Though lacking the martial strength for reckless advance, one can still use strategy to assess the enemy, combine forces, and seize victory.
Mei Yaochen says: *Wu* means to continue.

Though the troops may be insufficient to press forward, they suffice to combine the strength of auxiliary forces, assess the enemy, and secure victory.

Wang Xi says: I say that one skilled in the variations of division and unity can combine forces to exploit the enemy’s gaps and seize victory—this is all. Thus, even auxiliary troops will do; how much more so elite soldiers? Cao’s explanation is correct.

Zhang Yu says: When military strength is equal and no opportunity presents itself, though not yet sufficient for a bold advance, one may still select men from auxiliary forces, combine their strength, assess the enemy, and secure victory—no need to borrow troops from others. Thus, the *Wei Liaozi* says: "When the world sends auxiliary troops, they are called a hundred thousand, but in truth, they number no more than tens of thousands. Those who come will all tell their generals: 'Do not be the first to fight.' This means auxiliary troops are of no benefit; one’s own troops, governed by military methods, are better." Only those who act without forethought and underestimate the enemy will surely be captured.

Du Mu says: Those who lack deep strategy and far-reaching plans, relying only on individual courage and acting recklessly without caution, will surely be captured by the enemy.

Chen Hao says: *Wei* means "only." This speaks of those utterly lacking foresight, who merely underestimate the enemy—they will surely be captured, and this is not merely a matter of courage. As the *Zuozhuan* says: "Wasps and scorpions have venom; how much more so a state?" Thus, even a small enemy must not be taken lightly.

Wang Xi says: Only those who cannot assess the enemy but advance recklessly will surely be captured, making clear that the danger lies not in insufficient numbers.

Zhang Yu says: Those who cannot assess their men but instead underestimate the enemy and advance recklessly will surely be captured. When Qi and Jin attacked each other, the Marquis of Qi said: "Let me first destroy these and then eat breakfast." He did not armor his horses but charged and was defeated by Jin.

If punishment is meted out before the troops have grown close, they will not submit; if they do not submit, they will be difficult to employ.

Du Mu says: When kindness and trust have not yet permeated, one cannot use punishments to bring order.

Mei Yaochen says: *Zhuan* means "to reach." Virtue reaches them, kindness draws them close. If kindness and virtue have not spread, punishment will not be accepted, and thus they will resent and be difficult to employ.

Wang Xi says: If kindness and trust have not long been deeply established in the hearts of the people, they have not yet grown close.

Zhang Yu says: One who suddenly assumes the position of general, without having first extended kindness and trust to the people, and then hastily imposes punishments to bring order, will provoke anger and make them difficult to employ. Thus, Tian Rangju said: "I have long been lowly and humble; the soldiers have not grown close, and the common people do not trust me." And Wu Can said: "Jin’s officials in power are new and cannot yet enforce orders."

If punishment is not enforced after the troops have grown close, they cannot be employed.

Cao Cao says: When kindness and trust have already permeated, if there are no punishments, the troops will grow arrogant and indolent, making them difficult to employ.

Mei Yaochen says: When kindness and virtue have already permeated, if punishments are not enforced, the troops will grow arrogant and cannot be employed.

Wang Xi says: This is what is meant by "like spoiled children."

Zhang Yu says: When kindness and trust have long permeated and the hearts of the soldiers have grown close, if punishments are lenient, the troops will grow arrogant and cannot be employed.

Thus, command them with civility and bring them into order with martial discipline.

Cao Cao says: Civility is benevolence; martial discipline is law.

Li Quan says: Civility is kindness and grace; martial discipline is awe and punishment.

Du Mu says: Yanzi cited Tian Rangju as one whose civility could unite the people and whose martial discipline could overawe the enemy.

Wang Xi says: Wu Qi said: "One who unites civility and martial discipline is the general of an army; one who combines hardness and softness is the master of warfare." This is called "surely seizing victory."

Du Mu says: When civility and martial discipline are both enacted, victory is surely seized.

Mei Yaochen says: Command with kindness and grace, bring order with awe and punishment. When kindness and awe are both evident, one can surely win.

Zhang Yu says: Use civility and grace to please them, martial discipline and awe to discipline them. When reverence and love are combined, battles will be won and attacks will surely succeed.

Someone asked: The *Book of Documents* says: "When awe overcomes love, success is truly achieved; when love overcomes awe, success is truly thwarted." This speaks of putting awe first. Why does Sun Wu put love first?

The answer: What the *Book of Documents* praises is the army of a benevolent ruler. For a true king, kindness and virtue toward the people are long-established, and their hearts have already grown close. When he employs them, the only concern is a lack of awe. What Sun Wu describes is the army of the Warring States. For a hegemon, laws and orders are long harsh, and the people’s hearts are easily estranged. When he employs them, the only concern is a lack of kindness.

If commands are consistently enforced to instruct the people, the people will submit.

Mei Yaochen says: *Su* means "long-established." When awe and commands are long-established, instruction will be heeded and obeyed.

Zhang Yu says: When commands have long been enforced, the people already trust them. Instruct and employ them, and each person will submit.

If commands are not consistently enforced to instruct the people, the people will not submit.

Wang Xi says: If the people have not long been instructed, they will be difficult to employ at once.

He Shi says: If the people have already lost their training, how can they submit to instruction?

When commands are consistently enforced, one is in harmony with the multitude.

Du Mu says: *Su* means "in advance." This speaks of a general in ordinary times, when there is no conflict. Kindness, trust, awe, and commands must first be established among the people. Then, when facing the enemy, issuing commands and establishing laws will be met with the people’s trust and submission. Han Xin said: "I have not long been able to care for and lead the officers and gentlemen; this is what is called driving market rabble into battle." Thus, he made them fight with their backs to the water, so that each man would fight for himself. This was because they had not long received kindness and trust, nor followed awe and commands.

Chen Hao says: When Duke Wen of Jin first entered his state, he instructed his people for two years and wished to employ them. Zifan said: "The people do not yet know righteousness and have not settled into their homes." This meant he wished the people not to value their lives lightly. Thereupon, he went out to secure King Xiang. This meant demonstrating the great righteousness of serving one’s lord, entering to work for the benefit of the people. When the people cherished life, he again wished to employ them. Zifan said: "The people do not yet know trust and have not seen their use."

Thus, he attacked Yuan to demonstrate trust. This meant that in the previous year, when attacking Yuan, he did not seize its benefits but upheld his trust. The people, though easily swayed, did not seek abundance—this showed that none were greedy or deceitful, clearly proving his words.

The Duke said: "It is sufficient." Zifan said: "The people do not yet know ritual and have not cultivated reverence." Thereupon, he conducted a grand review to demonstrate ritual. When it came time for battle, young and old observed ritual, and thus they could be employed.

These five—righteousness, trust, kindness, awe, and ritual—are the foundations of teaching the people. For commands, the essential lies in first clarifying them so that people heed them without confusion. For laws, the essential lies in their certain enforcement so that people uphold them without taking trust lightly.

Three commands and five clarifications ensure the people are not confused. When laws and orders are simple and appropriate, and their execution is certain, only then can one harmonize with the multitude.

Mei Yaochen says: When trust has been long established, what matter will they not follow?

Wang Xi says: One who understands this may then speak of uniting strength to overcome the enemy.

Zhang Yu says: When the ruler employs trust to lead the people, and the people respond with trust, there is harmony between above and below.

The *Wei Liaozi* states: In the method of issuing commands, minor faults are not altered, and minor doubts are not reclarified. Once orders are given, they must not be reversed. Unless there is a great fault or great doubt, there is no need for revision or reclarification—this is how trust is maintained among the people.

When Zhuge Liang fought the Wei army, though outnumbered, if any soldier was found equal to the task, he would not detain them but sent them away, saying: "Trust must not be broken." Thus, every man wished to stay and fight, and they greatly defeated the Wei forces.

*Annotations to Sunzi, Scroll Nine* concludes.

---
*Annotations:*
1. Originally "度" (*du*), emended to "疑" (*yi*) according to the Song edition.
2. Originally "上世" (*shangshi*), emended to "至也" (*zhiye*) according to the Song edition.

*Sunzi Annotations, Scroll Ten: The Terrain Chapter*

Cao Cao said: "When desiring battle, examine the terrain to establish victory."
Li Quan said: "After the army marches forth, the terrain must shift."
Wang Xi said: "One must thoroughly know the advantageous forms of terrain—its defiles, narrows, forks, and snares."
Zhang Yu said: "Whenever the army advances, first survey the mountains and rivers within fifty *li*, dispatching scouts to watch for ambushes. The general must personally inspect the lay of the land, then map it to discern its hazards and ease."

Thus, when campaigning beyond borders, victory is established by scrutinizing the terrain. This follows the discussion of marching the army.

Sunzi said: "Terrain has six forms: *tong* (open), *gua* (entangled), *zhi* (stalemated), *ai* (narrow), *xian* (precipitous), and *yuan* (distant)."
Cao Cao said: "These six are the forms of terrain."
Mei Yaochen said: "*Tong* is where roads intersect; *gua* is a snaring land where advance ensures entanglement; *zhi* is a deadlocked ground; *ai* lies between two mountains in a shared valley; *xian* comprises mountains, rivers, and hills; *yuan* is level ground."
Du You said: "These are the six names of terrain. Teaching the people to occupy them with advantage ensures victory."
Zhang Yu said: "Terrain is distinguished by these six forms."

Where I may advance and the enemy may come is called *tong*.
Du You said: "This refers to both sides being on level ground, where advance and retreat are unimpeded."
Zhang Yu said: "Both sides being on level ground, where movement is unobstructed."

For *tong* terrain, first occupy the high and sunny ground, secure the supply routes, and battle will be advantageous.
Cao Cao said: "Better to make the enemy come to you than to go to them."
Li Quan said: "Occupy it first to await the enemy."
Du Mu said: "*Tong* is a ground of fourfold conflict. One must first seize the high and sunny positions, lest the enemy gain them before you arrive. Securing the supply routes means fortifying or constructing covered ways at river crossings or enemy choke points to protect them."
Jia Lin said: "*Tong* is unobstructed, without ridges or defiles, allowing free movement. Occupying high ground eases observation, facing the sun aids visibility, and supply routes facilitate transport—thus, battle is advantageous here."
Du You said: "Better to make the enemy come to you than to go to them. First occupy the high ground, then divide forces to guard the return routes, preventing the enemy from cutting off your supplies."
Mei Yaochen said: "First occupy the high and sunny ground, secure the supply routes, and when the enemy approaches, battle will be advantageous."
Wang Xi’s annotation matches Cao Cao’s. He Shi’s matches Du You’s.
Zhang Yu said: "Occupy the battleground first to await the enemy, thus drawing them in rather than being drawn. Though occupying high and sunny ground to lure the enemy, one must also ensure supplies remain unbroken for advantage."

Where advance is possible but return is difficult is called *gua*.
Du You said: "*Gua* means entanglement."

For *gua* terrain, if the enemy is unprepared, advance and defeat them; if the enemy is prepared, advance but fail to win, and return will be difficult—this is disadvantageous.
Li Quan said: "*Gua* is where advance is difficult and return impossible."
Du Mu said: "*Gua* is treacherous, obstructed ground shared with the enemy, interlocking like dog’s teeth, where movement is hindered. If the enemy is unprepared when you attack, you will surely win—though the terrain is treacherous, the enemy’s defeat will prevent them from intercepting your return. If the enemy is prepared and you cannot win, they will hold the treacherous ground to block your retreat, making return difficult."
Chen Hao said: "If forced into such a position, prepare for protracted warfare, plunder the enemy’s supplies, and await an opportunity to strike."
Du You said: "If the enemy is unprepared, attack and victory is possible; if prepared, victory is unattainable, and return will be difficult."
Mei Yaochen said: "Strike unexpectedly, and advance will yield advantage; if the enemy is prepared, advance will bring defeat."
Zhang Yu said: "If you ascertain the enemy is truly unprepared, a single assault may win. If they are prepared, advance without victory, and you cannot remain to fight nor retreat—this is not advantageous."

Where advance is disadvantageous for me and disadvantageous for the enemy is called *zhi*.
Du You said: "*Zhi* means prolonged—neither side benefits from protracted stalemate."
Zhang Yu said: "Each holds fast to perilous ground to support the other."

For *zhi* terrain, even if the enemy lures me with advantage, I do not advance; instead, feign retreat, then strike when half the enemy has emerged—this is advantageous.
Li Quan said: "*Zhi* is where neither side gains advantage, like *gua* terrain, so each divides their forces."
Du Mu said: "*Zhi* is where both sides hold high and perilous ground, facing each other across a narrow, elongated plain. Advancing troops cannot form ranks, and meeting the enemy means fighting uphill—neither side benefits. In such cases, withdraw openly, then lie in ambush. If the enemy pursues, wait until half have emerged, then strike—this is advantageous. If the enemy retreats first to lure you, do not advance."
Chen Hao said: "This explanation is overly complex and inverted. Simply put: if both sides advance and the terrain is unfavorable, and the enemy offers bait to lure you away, do not pursue. If you withdraw and the enemy halts, so be it; if they attack, wait until half have emerged, then strike swiftly."
Jia Lin said: "*Zhi* is where perilous ground allows mutual interception, so neither benefits from advancing first."
Du You said: "If the enemy feigns retreat to lure me, I do not pursue. Wait for them to advance, then strike—they can be defeated."

Mei Yaochen said: "When both sides occupy perilous ground, the first to advance will surely be defeated. If the enemy lures me with advantage, I must not covet it. Feign retreat to draw them out, then strike when half have emerged."

Wang Xi said: "If the enemy refuses to advance, set an ambush and withdraw, feigning deception to ensure they emerge."

Zhang Yu said: "‘Luring me’ means feigning retreat. Do not pursue; if I abandon the perilous ground, I will be vulnerable to their ambush. Instead, withdraw first. If the enemy pursues, wait until half have emerged—when their ranks are unsettled—and attack with elite troops to secure victory."

Li Jing’s *Art of War* states: "On terrain where neither side benefits, feign retreat and wait for half the enemy to emerge before intercepting them. For constricted terrain, if I occupy it first, I must fill it to await the enemy." Du You said: "‘Fill’ means to saturate. Deploy troops to fill the constriction, preventing the enemy from advancing or retreating."

If the enemy occupies it first, fill it and do not engage; if they do not fill it, then engage. Cao Cao said: "Constricted terrain refers to a pass between two mountains where the enemy cannot outmaneuver me. If I occupy it first, I must align my front with the pass, array my forces, and guard it to launch surprise attacks. If the enemy holds this ground first and aligns their front with the pass, do not engage. If they occupy only half the constriction, then engage to share the advantage."

Li Quan said: "‘Fill’ means to level. If the enemy guards the pass first, I should withdraw. Zhao Kuo failed to hold the mouth of Jingxing, allowing Han Xin to take it; Chen Xi did not hold the Zhang River, enabling Gaozu to seize it."

Du Mu said: "‘Fill’ means to saturate. When encountering a pass between two mountains with a connecting valley, one must camp at the mouth, aligning with the mountain passes like water filling a vessel to the brim." Du You said: "‘Aligning with the mouth’ also means filling it, like water filling a vessel to the rim. If I occupy it, I control both ease and peril, then launch surprise attacks to subdue the enemy. If the enemy occupies half the constriction, unaware of the principle of filling the pass, I should enter to engage them—for both sides share the terrain, and victory or defeat depends on me, not the ground."

The technique of aligning and filling applies not only to constricted terrain but also to any situation where a single path exists amid impassable plains, marshes, or unnavigable waters. One must control the route to prevent the enemy’s advance. All such cases are self-evident.

Chen Hao said: "It is correct to speak of arraying forces at the pass, but not of encamping there."

Jia Lin said: "‘Engage’ means to pursue; ‘fill’ means to solidify. If the enemy solidifies and saturates the pass, do not pursue or attack. If they are unprepared and empty, enter and strike."

Mei Yaochen’s annotation aligns with Du Mu’s; Wang Xi’s with Cao Cao’s.

Zhang Yu said: "Between high mountains lies a level valley. If I arrive first, I must fill the mountain pass with my array to block the enemy’s advance. I can launch surprise attacks, but they cannot disrupt me. If the enemy occupies this ground first and fills the pass with their array, do not engage. If they guard the pass but do not fill it, enter and engage to share the perilous advantage."

Wu Qi said: "Do not face the ‘heavenly furnace.’ The ‘heavenly furnace’ refers to the mouth of a great valley—one must not confront the pass directly."

For perilous terrain, if I occupy it first, I must hold the high and sunny ground to await the enemy. Du You said: "Occupy high and sunny ground to await the enemy. If they approach from the low and shady side, strike to prevail."

If the enemy occupies it first, withdraw and do not engage. Cao Cao said: "Perilous terrain must never be ceded to the enemy." Li Quan said: "On perilous ground, one must not lag behind others." Du Mu said: "Perilous terrain—steep mountains and deep valleys—cannot be altered by human effort. One must occupy the high and sunny ground to await the enemy. If the enemy seizes it first, do not contest it; withdraw instead. ‘Sunny’ refers to south-facing ground, lest the enemy endure while I, on the shady side, suffer illness. For example, at Xiaoshan or Mengjin, one should first occupy the northern mountains—this is facing shade and turning one’s back to the sun. Between height and sun, one may abandon the sun for height, but never height for the sun. Sunzi speaks generally."

Du You said: "Seizing perilous ground first prevents the enemy from gaining it." Mei Yaochen said: "Securing perilous and fortified ground, occupying the high and sunny, strengthens one to await the enemy. If the enemy seizes it first, engaging is perilous—withdraw without hesitation."

Wang Xi said: "This is also contested ground. Emperor Taizong of Tang first occupied Wugong to await Dou Jiande, exemplifying this principle."

Zhang Yu said: "Even on level ground, one should occupy advantageous positions first—how much more so on perilous terrain? Thus, securing the high and sunny allows one to await the enemy’s fatigue and prevail. If the enemy already holds this ground, retreat swiftly and do not engage."

When Pei Xingjian campaigned against the Turks, he once pitched camp at dusk, completing the moat and ramparts, then suddenly ordered a move to higher ground. His officers protested, deeming it unwise to exhaust the troops, but Xingjian insisted, and the relocation was swiftly executed. That night, a violent storm struck, flooding the original campsite to a depth of over ten feet. The officers were astonished and convinced. From this, it is clear that occupying high and sunny ground not only facilitates battle but also avoids the peril of flooding.

For distant terrain, when forces are evenly matched, it is difficult to provoke battle, and fighting is disadvantageous. Cao Cao said: "Provoking battle means luring the enemy." Li Quan said: "When evenly matched, provoking battle leaves the outcome uncertain."

Du Mu said: Suppose my camp and the enemy’s are thirty *li* apart. If I advance toward the enemy’s camp to provoke battle, I exhaust my own vigor while wearing down the enemy’s sharpness—thus fighting is disadvantageous. If the enemy advances toward my camp to provoke battle, I rest while the enemy labors—this too is disadvantageous for them. Hence, when forces are evenly matched, what then? He said: If battle is unavoidable, then move closer to one another.

Chen Hao said: When camps are far apart and forces evenly matched, provoking battle is difficult, and fighting brings no advantage. Thus, the following text states that when forces are equal, attacking ten with one is called "flight." To provoke battle, one must first assess whether one’s troops are strong enough to overpower the enemy; if not, one must not advance recklessly and court defeat.

Meng Shi said: When forces are evenly matched, provoking battle from afar is disadvantageous.

Du You said: "To provoke" means to engage the enemy. "Distant terrain" means being far from one’s homeland. When terrain and forces are evenly matched, with no unique advantage, initiating battle is unwise.

Mei Yaochen said: When forces are evenly matched, provoking battle brings fatigue, while luring the enemy brings ease.

Wang Xi said: Drawing the enemy from afar is exhausting.

Zhang Yu said: When camps are far apart and forces evenly matched, one should sit and lure the enemy, not provoke them to seek battle.

These six principles pertain to terrain; a general’s highest duty is to examine them closely.

Li Quan said: These are the strategic advantages of terrain. A general who does not understand them will fail.

Jia Lin said: Heaven creates terrain, which can be observed with the eyes.

Mei Yaochen said: Terrain is the foundation of military victory—how can one not assess it?

Zhang Yu said: A general must know the six types of terrain. Thus, armies may face flight, laxity, entrapment, collapse, disorder, or rout. These six outcomes are not the fault of heaven but of the general.

Jia Lin said: Flight, laxity, entrapment, collapse, disorder, and rout are all names for defeats of varying severity.

Zhang Yu said: These six failures stem from human error.

When forces are evenly matched, attacking ten with one is called "flight."

Cao Cao said: Failing to assess strength.

Li Quan said: Overestimating one’s strength. If one secures advantageous terrain and employs ambush tactics, it may yet succeed.

Du Mu said: The principle of attacking ten with one requires that the enemy’s generals be inferior in wisdom, their troops inferior in courage, and that they suffer from unfavorable weather, terrain, hunger, or fatigue—tenfold disadvantages. Only then can one attack ten with one. If forces are evenly matched and one cannot assess whether one’s single force can overcome the enemy’s tenfold, then one must flee and cannot regroup or hold position.

Mei Yaochen said: Though forces may be evenly matched, if one’s troops are few, attacking the many is the path to flight.

Wang Xi said: Fleeing without engaging.

Zhang Yu said: "Evenly matched" means the generals’ wisdom and courage, and the troops’ sharpness or dullness, are all comparable. When forces are balanced, one cannot lightly engage; how much less can one attack the many with the few without fleeing?

When soldiers are strong and officers weak, it is called "laxity."

Cao Cao said: Officers cannot command, so discipline collapses.

Du Mu said: When soldiers are unruly and generals weak, unable to control them, discipline slackens and the army disintegrates. In the early years of the Changqing era, Tian Bu was ordered to lead the Wei forces against Wang Tingcou. Bu had long been in Wei, and the people there treated him lightly. Tens of thousands of soldiers rode donkeys to camp, and Bu could not stop them. After several months, when he sought battle, the troops scattered. Bu died by his own hand.

Jia Lin said: When commands are disobeyed and authority unheeded, disorder arises—how can the army not collapse?

Mei Yaochen said: Without proper command, military governance decays.

Wang Xi’s note is the same as Cao Cao’s.

He Shi said: When soldiers are unruly and generals weak, unable to lead, discipline collapses and the army disintegrates.

Zhang Yu said: When soldiers are fierce and officers weak, unable to command or restrain them, military governance decays. During the Wu-Chu conflict, Wu’s Prince Guang said: "The Chu army has many favorites, its policies are inconsistent, its commanders lowly and undisciplined, lacking decisive authority—Chu can be defeated." Indeed, he crushed the Chu forces.

When officers are strong and soldiers weak, it is called "entrapment."

Cao Cao said: Officers press forward, but weak soldiers are easily trapped—defeat follows.

Li Quan said: Entrapment means defeat. When soldiers are weak and divided, they cannot fight, so the strong are trapped.

Du Mu said: When seeking to attack, if soldiers are cowardly and weak, yet one advances recklessly without assessing strength, they will be trapped in deadly terrain.

Chen Hao said: All men have blood and spirit—who lacks the will to fight? But if generals lack discipline and virtue, and soldiers lack training, all become cowardly and useless.

Jia Lin said: When soldiers are feeble and will not advance even when drums beat, and officers fight alone, they are merely trapped.

Mei Yaochen said: Though officers press forward, they cannot inspire courage, so they are trapped in death.

Wang Xi said: Trapped by their subordinates.

Zhang Yu said: When officers are bold and eager to fight, but soldiers lack training and cannot unite in courage, using them will lead to destruction.

When high-ranking officers are angry and insubordinate, and in battle rush forward without assessing their ability, it is called "collapse."

Cao Cao said: High-ranking officers are junior generals. If the supreme commander angers them and they remain unyielding, rushing into battle in fury without weighing the odds, they will surely collapse.

Li Quan said: When a general is enraged by the enemy and, without assessing strength or weakness, drives his soldiers forward as if their lives were his to command, collapse is certain.

Du Mu said: During the Spring and Autumn period, the Viscount of Chu attacked Zheng, and the Jin army came to its aid. Wu Can advised the Viscount of Chu: "Jin’s governance is newly established, and its commands cannot yet be enforced. Its deputy, Xian Gu, is stubborn and unkind, unwilling to follow orders. Its three commanders act independently, ignoring superiors and leaving the troops without clear direction. On this campaign, the Jin army will surely be defeated." Jin’s Wei Qi, angered at being denied a noble rank, sought to sabotage the Jin army. He requested to lead the vanguard but was refused; he then asked to be sent as an envoy, and this was permitted. He went to provoke battle and returned. Zhao Zhan, denied a ministerial post, requested to challenge the enemy but was refused; he then asked to summon an alliance, which was allowed. Both he and Wei Qi were dispatched with orders. Que Ke said: "Two resentful men have gone forth. If we do not prepare, we will surely be defeated." Sui Hui said: "If these two enrage the Chu forces, and Chu attacks while we are unprepared, our army will be lost without doubt." But Xian Gu said: "We must not prepare." Sui Hui then sent Gong Shuo and Han Chuan to set seven ambushes before Mount Ao. Thus, the upper army was not defeated, but the central and lower armies were indeed routed. The "seven ambushes" were seven concealed positions; Mount Ao is a mountain. Chen Hao said: This occurs when a high-ranking general, without reason, angers a junior officer, leaving him inwardly resentful. Seizing on this grievance, the officer, upon encountering the enemy, fights recklessly without regard for his own ability, leading to utter defeat. Jia Lin said: "Collapse" means to fall from above. When high-ranking and junior officers do not submit to one another, collapse is inevitable. If the general further fails to assess his own ability or know his soldiers’ courage or cowardice, forcing them to fight the enemy, he invites destruction—is this not collapse from above? Mei Yaochen said: When a junior officer is angry and insubordinate, and upon meeting the enemy fights with blind resentment, bringing about his own collapse, it is because the general did not know his ability. Wang Xi said: This refers to a general who angers others without reason and does not recognize his subordinates’ talents, provoking their reckless fury—like a mountain collapsing. He Shi said: When the three armies unite in strength and the ranks are of one mind, victory is assured. Zhang Yu said: Generally, when all officers share a single purpose and the three armies unite in strength, they can defeat the enemy. But when junior officers are resentful and defy the high general’s commands, seeking mutual destruction, and upon meeting the enemy fight without assessing their ability, collapse is inevitable. This was the case when Jin attacked Qin, and Xun Yan issued his order: "At cock’s crow, harness the chariots—follow only the direction of my horse’s head." Yue Shu, enraged, said: "The fate of Jin does not rest with this man!" and abandoned the campaign to return home. It was also seen when Zhao Chuan, hating Yu Pian, drove him to Qin, and when Wei Qi, furious with the Jin army, turned against Chu.

When a general is weak and undisciplined, his teachings unclear, officers and soldiers lack constancy, and battle formations are deployed haphazardly, it is called "disorder."

Cao Cao said: For a general to act thus is the way to disorder. Li Quan said: If a general exhibits even one of these faults, it is the way to disorder. Du Mu said: When officers and soldiers are not bound by regular standards, and troops are drawn up in battle lines that are sometimes vertical, sometimes horizontal, this is self-inflicted disorder. Jia Lin said: When authority and commands are not strict and clear, soldiers lack consistent discipline—how could the army not be disordered? This refers to a general who lacks strict commands and fails to enforce rewards and punishments. Mei Yaochen said: If a general is weak and undisciplined, soldiers lack constant restraint; if his teachings are unclear, battle formations will be deployed in disarray. This is the way to disorder. Wang Xi said: Disorder leads to defeat without end. Zhang Yu said: A "weak and undisciplined" general lacks virtue and power; "teachings unclear" means training lacks ancient methods; "officers and soldiers lack constancy" means commanders are not long in their posts; "battle formations deployed haphazardly" means soldiers lack discipline. For a general to act thus is the way to self-inflicted disorder.

When a general cannot assess the enemy, matching the few against the many, the weak against the strong, and his troops lack a vanguard of elite soldiers, it is called "rout."

Cao Cao said: In such a situation, the army is certain to flee. Li Quan said: Military defeat is called "rout," stemming from failure to assess the enemy. Du Mu said: The military methods of Duke Wei Li Jing include the "vanguard formation," referring to the selection of bold and courageous soldiers to serve as the forward assault in every battle. The *Methods of the Sima* states: "Select the finest and decide the battle, augmenting the army’s strength." The commentary explains: "Brave, swift, and agile soldiers who fail to achieve victory in the first engagement must be selected from the rear for the next, to rouse their sharpness." When the Eastern Jin Grand General Xie Xuan was stationed in Guangling, facing the formidable Fu Jian, he recruited many brave and vigorous men. Liu Laozhi, He Qian, Zhuge Kan, Gao Heng, Liu Gui, Tian Luo, and Sun Wuzhong, renowned for their ferocity, answered the call. Xie Xuan placed Liu Laozhi in command of the elite vanguard, achieving victory in every battle and earning the name "Northern Prefecture Army." The enemy feared them, and wherever they advanced, they triumphed. Jia Lin said: If the vanguard’s sharpness is not selected, and soldiers’ courage or cowardice is unknown, deploying troops in this manner is to invite retreat. Mei Yaochen said: Failing to gauge the enemy’s strength, matching the few against the many, and not selecting elite soldiers to strike the strong with the weak—these are all reasons for rout. He Shi said: Fatigued and brave soldiers must not be mixed together. If they are, the brave will not be encouraged, and the weary will find refuge in their ranks, advancing without fighting and bringing about their own defeat. Thus, the military methods state: "An army without a vanguard of elite soldiers is called 'rout.'" In ancient times, Qi relied on its "skilled strikers," Wei on its "martial troops," Qin on its "sharp soldiers," and Han on the "knights of the Three Rivers," "sword experts," and "men of extraordinary talent." Wu called them "trouble-solvers," Qi "life-riskers," and Tang "leaping raiders"—all alternative names for elite vanguards. No military tactic is more effective than this. Once the army is assembled, the high general must command each camp to select its finest and most agile soldiers—those whose martial skills surpass the rest. These are organized into separate units, roughly one selected from every ten men, one thousand from every ten thousand.

The selection must be kept few in number but must be precisely chosen. Appoint trusted and capable generals to command them. From the general’s personal guard to the vanguard and ambush troops, all should be allocated according to their rank and ability.

Zhang Yu states: If one deploys a small force against a large one, drives the weak against the strong, and yet fails to select the most valiant soldiers to serve as the vanguard, defeat is certain.

The reason every battle must employ the finest troops as the vanguard is twofold: first, to bolster our own morale, and second, to break the enemy’s spirit. Thus, the *Wei Liaozi* states: "If martial warriors are not selected, the army will not be strong." Cao Cao used Zhang Liao as his vanguard to defeat the Xianbei, and Xie Xuan placed Liu Laozhi in command of his elite troops to resist Fu Jian—these are examples of such strategy.

These six factors are the paths to defeat. Chen Hao says: The first is failing to assess relative strength; the second, lacking discipline and virtue; the third, neglecting training; the fourth, waging war out of irrational anger; the fifth, failing to enforce orders; and the sixth, not selecting the bold and resolute. These are called the Six Defeats. A general’s paramount duty is to examine them carefully.

Zhang Yu states: The six matters above are the certain roads to defeat.

Terrain is an auxiliary to warfare.
Du Mu says: The essence of warfare lies in benevolence, righteousness, and discipline. If one gains the advantage of terrain, it can serve as an aid to victory.
*Assist* is also written as *ease*.
Chen Hao says: Heaven’s timing is not as advantageous as terrain.
Meng Shi says: Terrain’s advantage depends on human effort.
Jia Lin says: Though victory lies with the troops, favorable terrain makes success easier—hence it is called the army’s ease. Mountains can block, water can flood, high ground defeats low, and rugged terrain defeats flat.

Wang Xi says: The Dao of warfare lies in people.
Zhang Yu says: To scrutinize terrain is merely an auxiliary to warfare, a secondary matter. Assessing the enemy and securing victory is the foundation. Assessing the enemy, calculating the advantages of terrain—whether perilous or open, near or far—this is the way of a supreme general.
Du Mu says: The costs of provisions, the strength of men and horses, the advantages of attack and defense—all depend on the peril or openness of the terrain and its distance. To master these in order to control the enemy is the pinnacle of generalship.
Wang Xi says: To exhaustively analyze the enemy’s situation and the benefits or harms of terrain’s peril or distance—this is the Dao of warfare.
He Shi says: To know the enemy and the land is the general’s duty.
Zhang Yu says: If one can discern the enemy’s true strength or weakness and also gauge the shape of the terrain—whether perilous or open, near or far—grasping both root and branch, then the way of generalship is complete. He who knows these and wages war will surely triumph; he who does not and wages war will surely fail.

Du Mu says: This refers to knowing the peril or openness of the terrain and its distance.
Mei Yaochen says: If a general knows both terrain and military governance, he will win; if he knows neither, he will lose.
Zhang Yu says: If one knows both the enemy’s situation and the advantages of terrain, victory in battle is assured; if one knows neither, defeat is certain.

Thus, if the way of battle ensures victory, even if the ruler orders no battle, one may fight. If the way of battle does not ensure victory, even if the ruler orders battle, one may refrain.
Li Quan says: If the way to victory is secured, one must fight; if it is lost, one must not fight.

The ruler is the one who initiates action.
Du Mu says: The ruler is the sovereign.
The *Huangshi Gong* states: When leading troops into battle, the general must have independent authority. If the ruler interferes in advance or retreat, success will be difficult. Thus, enlightened sovereigns and wise kings kneel and push the chariot wheel, saying: "Matters beyond the frontier are for the general to decide."
Meng Shi says: Better to disobey the ruler than to go against the will of the troops.
Mei Yaochen says: When the general is in the field, he need not heed the ruler’s commands.
Zhang Yu says: If there is a certain path to victory, even if the ruler orders no battle, one may fight. If there is no certain path to victory, even if the ruler orders battle, one may refrain. Better to disobey orders and succeed than to follow them and fail—hence it is said: "Within the army, one does not hear the Son of Heaven’s decrees."

Thus, in advancing, one does not seek fame; in retreating, one does not avoid blame.
Wang Xi says: This is all for the sake of loyalty to the state.
He Shi says: In advancing, does one seek fame? No—one advances when it benefits the state and the people. In retreating, does one avoid blame? No—if advancing would harm the state and devastate the people, even if the ruler orders it, one does not advance, and though punishment may fall, one does not regret it.

One protects the people alone, and aligns with the ruler’s interests—such a one is the state’s treasure.
Li Quan says: In advancing or retreating, one protects the people, not oneself.
Du Mu says: One does not seek the fame of victory in advancing, nor does one avoid the crime of disobedience in retreating. Such a general is the state’s precious treasure—rare to find.
Chen Hao says: *Aligns* means *returns to*.
Mei Yaochen says: Better to disobey orders and win than to obey and lose.
Wang Zhe says: Whether to fight or not, all is for the sake of protecting the people and benefiting the ruler.
Zhang Yu says: In advancing or retreating against orders, one does not act for oneself but to protect the people and align with the ruler’s interests. Such a one is a loyal minister, the state’s treasure.

Regard your soldiers as infants, and they will follow you into the deepest ravines; regard them as beloved sons, and they will die with you.
Li Quan says: If one treats them thus, they will give their lives in return.
Thus, with a single word from the Lord of Chu, the soldiers of his three armies felt as if wrapped in warm silk.
Du Mu says: In the Warring States period, Wu Qi served as a general. He dressed, ate, and slept like the lowest of his soldiers. He did not use a mat when resting, did not ride a horse when traveling, and personally carried supplies, sharing their hardships. When a soldier suffered from an ulcer, Wu Qi sucked the pus from it. The soldier’s mother, upon hearing this, wept. Someone asked: "Your son is but a common soldier, yet the general himself sucked his wound—why do you weep?" The mother replied: "Years ago, Lord Wu sucked his father’s wound, and his father did not turn back but died in battle."

Now again he sucks this child’s wound—how can I know where he will die?
Mei Yaochen says: Nurture them with care, and they will remain close and never leave; love them and urge them on, and they will trust without doubt. Thus, though death comes, they die with you; though peril comes, they face it with you.
Wang Xi says: This is to bind men’s hearts with kindness and grace.
He Shi says: As in later Han, when Duan Jiong served as General Who Breaks the Qiang. On campaign against the Western Qiang, he showed deep care for his soldiers, personally visiting the wounded and binding their wounds with his own hands. For over a decade on the frontier, he never once slept on a mat, sharing hardship with his officers and men—thus all were glad to fight to the death for him. When Jin’s Wang Jun was governor of Ba Commandery, which bordered Wu, the soldiers suffered under heavy labor, and many newborn sons were abandoned. Wang Jun then tightened regulations but lightened corvée duties, granting those who bore children exemption and rest. Thousands of lives were thus preserved. Later, when he launched his campaign against Wu, those saved in Ba Commandery were all fit for military service. Their parents exhorted them: “Lord Wang gave you life—you must strive for him and not shrink from death.” Thus Wu’s forces faced an army of fathers and sons.
Zhang Yu says: If a general treats his soldiers as sons, they will regard him as a father. Never has a father faced danger without his sons rushing to die for him. Thus Xun Qing said: “A minister’s service to his lord, a subordinate’s to his superior, is like a younger brother’s to an elder, like hands and feet protecting the head and eyes.” Fine wine flows freely, and the whole army grows drunk; a single warm word, and the soldiers feel as if wrapped in silk. Truly, the way one treats subordinates with grace was prized by the ancients. Thus the *Art of War* says: When the army toils, the general must go first—he does not raise an umbrella in the heat, nor wear layered robes in the cold; on perilous paths, he dismounts and walks. He drinks only after the army’s well is dug, eats only after the army’s food is cooked, rests only after the army’s camp is built. If you are generous but cannot command, loving but cannot order, disorderly but cannot govern, they are like spoiled children—useless.
Cao Cao says: Grace alone cannot be relied upon, nor punishment alone. Like a spoiled child’s whims, they will turn to harm and prove useless.
Li Quan says: Though you love them deeply, if they become like spoiled children with rebellious hearts, they cannot be used.
Du Mu says: The *Three Strategies of Huang Shigong* states: “Soldiers may be humbled but must not be spoiled.” Grace nurtures them, humility treats them well—hence “may be humbled.” Law restrains them—hence “must not be spoiled.” The *Yinfu Jing* says: “Harm arises from grace.” Wu Qi said: “Drums, gongs, and bells strike awe into the ears; banners and insignia strike awe into the eyes; prohibitions and punishments strike awe into the heart. Ears awed by sound cannot but be sharp; eyes awed by sight cannot but be clear; hearts awed by punishment cannot but be stern. If these three are not established, defeat by the enemy is certain.” Thus, when a general raises his hand, none fail to follow; where he points, none fail to advance to their deaths.
Li Jing, Duke of Wei, said: The finest generals of old could execute three out of ten soldiers, and the next best one out of ten. Executing three out of ten strikes terror into enemy nations; executing one out of ten ensures orders are obeyed throughout the army. Those who fear me do not fear the enemy; those who fear the enemy do not fear me. Reward every good deed, no matter how small; punish every fault, no matter how minor. When Ma Su’s army was defeated, Zhuge Liang wept as he carried out the execution. When a villager stole a bamboo hat, Lü Meng shed tears before beheading him. When a horse trampled crops, Cao Cao cut his own hair as punishment. When two officials were found guilty, Huang Gai interrogated and executed them both. Thus, when awe overcomes love, even a small force will prevail; when love overcomes awe, even a large force will fail.
Meng Shi says: If you focus only on grace, once the power of grace is established, punishment will breed resentment. If you focus only on punishment, once resentment runs deep, grace will not win them over. You must blend grace and awe, reward and punishment—only then can you be a general, only then can you lead the masses.
Mei Yaochen says: If you nurture them generously but cannot command, dote on them but do not teach them, allow disorder but do not govern them, they are like spoiled children—how can they be of use?
Wang Xi says: Grace without strictness cannot succeed.
He Shi says: Grace cannot be used alone; used alone, it will turn to harm.
Zhang Yu says: Grace cannot be used exclusively, nor punishment alone. If you rely solely on grace, soldiers become like spoiled children and cannot be commanded. This is why Cao Cao cut his own hair as punishment, why Zhuge Liang wept as he carried out executions, why Yang Su’s blood flowed before him yet he laughed as if nothing had happened, why Li Jing executed three out of ten to ensure his men feared him more than the enemy. If you rely solely on punishment, soldiers will not draw close and cannot be used. This is why ancient generals would pour out wine, why the Lord of Chu wrapped his men in silk, why Wu Qi shared his clothes and food, why Helü shared their labor and rest. In the *Book of Changes*, the *Initial Six* of the *Army* hexagram says, “The army marches by law,” meaning to unite the masses through discipline. The *Nine in the Second* says, “The army receives Heaven’s favor,” meaning to encourage soldiers with rewards. From this we see that the armies of true kings balance virtue and punishment, grace and awe.
The *Weiliaozi* says: “If you do not please their hearts, they will not serve you; if you do not strike awe into their hearts, they will not rise for you.” Thus, the good general relies on both love and awe.
If you know your troops are fit to strike but do not know the enemy cannot be struck, your chance of victory is half. Mei Yaochen says: Knowing yourself but not the enemy may still bring victory.
If you know the enemy can be struck but do not know your troops are unfit to strike, your chance of victory is half. Du Mu says: “Can be struck” means they are brave and scorn death.

Those unfit to strike are weary, exhausted, timid, or weak. Chen Hao says: This explanation is incorrect. Whether an enemy can or cannot be struck depends on which army is stronger in numbers, which troops are better trained, and which side enforces rewards and punishments more clearly. Mei Yaochen says: Knowing the enemy but not yourself may still bring victory. Wang Xi says: Knowing yourself but not the enemy, or knowing the enemy but not yourself, neither guarantees victory. Zhang Yu says: Some know themselves but not the enemy; some know the enemy but not themselves—thus, victory or defeat may follow.

Emperor Taizong of Tang says: When I once faced the enemy in battle, I first assessed whether the enemy’s mind or my own was more discerning, and only then could I truly know them. I examined whether the enemy’s *qi* or my own was more ordered, and only then could I truly know myself. This means weighing the clarity of intentions and the order of *qi* to discern strength and weakness—whether to engage or not.

If you know the enemy can be struck and your troops are fit to strike, but do not know the terrain is unfit for battle, your chance of victory is half. Cao Cao and Li Quan say: A fifty-percent chance of victory is still uncertain. Du Mu says: Terrain refers to its hazards or ease, distance or proximity, entry and exit points, and whether it is circuitous or direct. Mei Yaochen says: Knowing the enemy and yourself but not the terrain may still lead to defeat. Wang Xi says: Even if you know both enemy and self are fit for battle, you must not forfeit the advantage of terrain. Zhang Yu says: Though you know yourself and the enemy, without the aid of terrain, you cannot secure complete victory.

Thus, one who truly knows warfare moves without confusion and acts without exhaustion. Du Mu says: Before moving or acting, victory or defeat is already decided—hence, movement is never confused, and action is never exhausted. Another version reads: moves without entanglement and acts without weariness. Chen Hao says: *Exhaustion* here means entanglement. If I understand the enemy’s movements and my own, and assess the gains and losses of terrain, then advancing brings no confusion, and battle brings no entanglement. Mei Yaochen says: Knowing all, one moves without blindness and acts without exhaustion. Wang Xi says: One skilled in strategy is never confused; one skilled in warfare is never exhausted. Zhang Yu says: Not moving recklessly ensures no mistakes; not acting lightly ensures no entanglement. Only after discerning the enemy’s and one’s own strengths and weaknesses, and securing the advantages of terrain, should one fight.

Thus it is said: Know the enemy and know yourself, and victory will not be imperiled. Zhang Yu says: Mastering the arts of attack and defense ensures victory without danger. Know Heaven and know Earth, and victory will know no bounds. Li Quan says: When human affairs, Heaven’s timing, and Earth’s advantages are all understood, every battle is won. Du You says: Know the advantages of terrain and the seasons of Heaven. Terrain’s advantages lie in relying on hazards, facing high and sunny ground; Heaven’s seasons lie in following cold and heat, and aligning with punitive and virtuous *qi*. If you know both enemy and self, and further align with terrain and the Dao of Heaven, victory can be complete—what difficulty remains? Mei Yaochen says: Knowing the enemy’s advantage and your own ensures no peril; knowing Heaven’s timing and Earth’s terrain ensures no limit. Wang Xi’s note agrees with Mei Yaochen. Zhang Yu says: Following Heaven’s timing and securing Earth’s advantages, victory has no end.

*Commentary on Sunzi, Scroll Ten ends*
*Commentary on Sunzi, Scroll Eleven: "Nine Terrains"*

Cao Cao says: There are nine types of terrain for battle.
Li Quan says: There are nine terrains where victory over the enemy may be decided, hence the discussion follows "Terrain."
Wang Xi says: In the terrains where armies operate, advantages and disadvantages number nine.
Zhang Yu says: In the terrains where armies operate, their strategic conditions number nine. This discusses terrain’s strategic conditions, hence it follows "Terrain."

Sunzi says: In the methods of using armies, there is *dispersive terrain*, *light terrain*, *contentious terrain*, *intersecting terrain*, *thoroughfare terrain*, *heavy terrain*, *impassable terrain*, *encircled terrain*, and *death terrain*.

Cao Cao says: These are the names of the nine terrains.
Zhang Yu says: These are the names of the nine terrains.

When feudal lords fight on their own land, it is *dispersive terrain*.
Cao Cao says: Soldiers cling to their homeland; the proximity makes them easily scattered.
Li Quan says: Soldiers rely on their native soil, cherish their wives and children, and scatter when pressed—this is *dispersive terrain*.
Du Mu says: Soldiers are near their homes; they lack the resolve to fight to the death and have places to flee.
Du You says: Fighting on one’s own territory, soldiers’ minds are not single-minded; they harbor thoughts of breaking and scattering—hence it is called *dispersive terrain*.
Mei Yaochen concurs with Du Mu’s note.
Wang Xi concurs with Cao Cao’s note.
He [Yan]shi says: In *dispersive terrain*, soldiers rely on it, cherish their wives and children, and scatter when pressed—this is *dispersive terrain*. One says: terrain without strategic passes, where soldiers easily scatter; those stationed here cannot fight repeatedly. Another says: terrain far and level on all sides, without critical strongholds, where resolve is weak and easily broken—hence it is called *dispersive terrain*.

King of Wu asked Sun Wu: In *dispersive terrain*, soldiers think of home and cannot be made to fight; thus, one must firmly defend and not sally forth. If the enemy attacks our small cities, plunders our fields, cuts off our firewood-gathering, blocks our vital roads, and waits until we are depleted before launching a swift attack—what should be done?
Sun Wu replied: If the enemy deeply penetrates our capital, they will have many soldiers with their backs to cities, treating the army as home, and will fight with single-minded ferocity. Our troops, being in our own land, are attached to the soil and cling to life; in formation, they are not steadfast, and in battle, they do not prevail. One should gather the people in great numbers, amass grain and silk, defend cities and secure strategic points, and send light troops to sever their supply lines. If they challenge for battle and receive no response, and their provisions fail to arrive, with nothing to plunder in the wilds, the three armies will be distressed and starving. Seizing this opportunity to lure them will yield success. If one wishes to fight in the open, one must rely on the terrain, set ambushes in strategic points, or, lacking such points, conceal oneself in overcast, dark, or foggy weather to exploit their laxity. Striking unexpectedly and ambushing their negligence can yield success.

Zhang Yu says: Fighting within one’s borders, soldiers think of home—this is terrain where they are easily scattered. When the people of Yun intended to attack the Chu army, Chu’s Dou Lian said: "The Zheng army is camped in their outskirts and will surely not be on guard; relying on the proximity of their city, they will lack fighting spirit." Indeed, they were defeated by Chu—this is an example.

When entering another’s land but not deeply, it is *light terrain*.
Cao Cao says: Soldiers all lightly return.
Du Mu says: When the army crosses the border, one must burn boats and bridges to show the people there is no thought of retreat.
Li Quan says: They are light in retreating.
Mei Yaochen says: Entering the enemy’s land but not far, the way back is short and retreat is easy.
Wang Xi says: Initially entering enemy territory, the situation is light, and soldiers lack fighting spirit.
He [Yan]shi says: *Light terrain* is terrain where retreat is easy. Entering enemy territory but not deeply, going and returning are easy, and one cannot halt or rest; the general must not repeatedly exhaust the people.

King of Wu asked Sun Wu: When I reach *light terrain*, having just entered enemy territory, soldiers long to return, advancing is difficult and retreating easy; without the protection of strategic hazards, the three armies are fearful. The commander wishes to advance, but the soldiers wish to retreat—superiors and subordinates are at odds. If the enemy defends their fortifications, arrays their chariots and cavalry, and either blocks our front or strikes our rear—what should be done?
Sun Wu replied: When the army reaches *light terrain*, soldiers are not yet single-minded; entering is their priority, not fighting. Thus, do not approach their famous cities or pass through their thoroughfares. Set up deceptive and confusing displays, feigning departure. Select elite cavalry, have them hold bits in their mouths, and enter first to seize their cattle, horses, and livestock. When the three armies see gains, they will no longer fear advancing. Divide our elite troops and secretly set ambushes; if the enemy comes, strike without hesitation. If they do not come, leave and depart.

Another says: When the army enters enemy territory and the enemy fortifies their ramparts without fighting, soldiers long to return, and retreat is difficult—this is *light terrain*. One should select elite soldiers to ambush key roads; if we retreat and the enemy pursues, strike when they come.

Zhang Yu says: Initially entering enemy territory, soldiers long to return—this is terrain where retreat is easy. The *Wei Liaozi* says: When conscripted troops are divided and return, or face battle and flee north, the losses are severe. This means that when civilian soldiers are assembled from all directions and divided into encampments occupying land, those coming from the north are assigned to the northern route, and many flee because the way is open.

Where what we gain is advantageous and what the enemy gains is also advantageous, it is *contentious terrain*.
Cao Cao says: Where the few can defeat the many, the weak can strike the strong.
Li Quan says: This is terrain guarding strategic choke points; the first to occupy it wins—hence it is *contentious terrain*.
Du Mu says: Terrain that must be contested is strategically critical. Former Qin’s Fu Jian first sent his general Lü Guang to subdue the Western Regions. After Fu Jian’s defeat, Lü Guang returned from the Western Regions. When his army reached Yihe, Fu Jian’s Liangzhou Prefect Liang Xi plotted to resist him. Yang Han, the Governor of Gaochang, said: "Lü Guang has just pacified the Western Regions; his troops are strong and their morale is high—their advance cannot be withstood. If they emerge from the Flowing Sands, their intentions will be hard to gauge. The Gaowu Gorge is a strategic point; we should first defend it and cut off their water supply..."

When the enemy is thus trapped and parched with thirst, their troops will naturally cast down their weapons. If one deems the Gaowu Gorge too distant to hold, the Yiwu Pass may also serve to block them. Should both these critical points be abandoned, no strategy will avail. There are places one must contest—this is precisely such an opportunity. Liang Xi refused to heed this counsel and was ultimately destroyed by Lü Guang.

Chen Hao says: If either side first secures such terrain, it may use the few to defeat the many, the weak to overcome the strong.
Du You says: This refers to mountain passes or river choke points, where the advantages of natural barriers make them contested by both sides.
Mei Yaochen says: Neither side holds it first—whoever seizes it gains the advantage.
Wang Xi concurs with Chen Hao’s annotation.
He Shi says: *Contentious terrain* is strategically advantageous ground; the first to occupy it prevails, hence it is contested.

King Helü of Wu asked Sun Wu: “If the enemy arrives first, occupies key positions, fortifies their defenses, trains their troops, and deploys flexibly—sometimes advancing, sometimes holding—while preparing to counter my surprise maneuvers, what should I do?”
Sun Wu replied: “The principle of *contentious terrain* is that the first to secure it gains the advantage. If the enemy has already taken such a position, do not attack it. Instead, feign retreat, raise banners, beat drums, and rush toward what they cherish, dragging branches to raise dust and confuse their senses. Meanwhile, conceal elite troops in ambush. The enemy will surely sally forth to rescue their interests. Where others desire, I advance; where others abandon, I seize—this is the way to contest the initiative. If I arrive first and the enemy employs this tactic, select my finest troops to hold the position firmly, send light forces to pursue them, and conceal ambushes in treacherous terrain. When the enemy turns to fight, the ambush will strike from the flanks—this is the way to complete victory.”

Zhang Yu says: Where natural barriers offer strategic advantage, whichever side secures them—whether us or the enemy—can use the few to defeat the many, the weak to overcome the strong. Such is the terrain that must be contested. Emperor Taizong of Tang, with three thousand men guarding the strategic pass at Chenggao, immobilized the hundred thousand troops of Dou Jiande—this is such a case.

Where I may advance and the enemy may approach is called *intersecting terrain*.
Cao Cao says: The roads intersect directly.
Du Mu says: Broad plains and level ground allow free movement for both sides, facilitating direct confrontation.
Chen Hao says: *Intersecting* means the roads crisscross, enabling both sides to come and go. In such terrain, troops must maintain unbroken formations, and vigilance is essential. Hence the following text states: “In *intersecting terrain*, I will carefully guard my positions”—its meaning is evident.
Du You says: *Intersecting terrain* has multiple routes, allowing free passage that cannot be severed.
Mei Yaochen concurs with Chen Hao’s annotation.
He Shi says: *Intersecting terrain* refers to open plains with interconnected roads. Alternatively, it may mean terrain where alliances can be forged but not severed, for severing them breeds enmity. It is also described as land where routes extend in all directions, making blockades impossible.

King Helü of Wu asked Sun Wu: “In *intersecting terrain*, if I wish to cut off the enemy’s approach, I must order my border cities to fortify their defenses, sever communication routes, and secure the passes. But if I fail to plan ahead and the enemy is already prepared, they may come while I cannot advance, our forces are evenly matched—what then?”
Sun Wu replied: “If I cannot advance but the enemy can approach, I will divide my troops and conceal them, appearing lax in defense to lull them into complacency. When the enemy nears, set ambushes in hidden shelters and strike unexpectedly—this can yield success.”

Zhang Yu says: Terrain with multiple routes, allowing free passage that cannot be blocked, is *intersecting terrain*.

Where the lands of several feudal lords converge, the first to arrive and win the allegiance of the realm is said to hold *thoroughfare terrain*.
Cao Cao says: The first to arrive gains the support of the state.
Li Quan says: Beside the enemy lies a neighboring state; the first to establish communication with it wins its allegiance.
Du Mu says: *Thoroughfare terrain* is where three states converge. I must arrive first at the strategic junction, control its topography, and forge alliances with the neighboring states. “The realm” here refers to the feudal lords.
Mei Yaochen says: Where my forces and the enemy’s face each other, and a neighboring state lies at the convergence of three fronts, the first to arrive gains the support of the feudal lords.
Wang Xi says: Cao Cao states that the first to arrive gains the state’s support. I interpret “first to arrive” as establishing diplomatic ties first. “Winning the realm” means securing broad support, thereby uniting the feudal lords.
He Shi says: *Thoroughfare terrain* is a strategic hub controlling multiple routes. The first to occupy it will win the allegiance of the masses—hence securing it brings stability, while losing it invites peril.

King Helü of Wu asked Sun Wu: “In *thoroughfare terrain*, the first to act gains the advantage. But if my forces are distant and set out later, even with swift chariots and galloping horses, I cannot arrive first—what then?”
Sun Wu replied: “Where the lands of several feudal lords converge, with routes extending in all directions, my forces and the enemy’s face each other, and a neighboring state lies nearby. The so-called ‘first’ refers to dispatching envoys with generous gifts and humble demeanor to negotiate alliances with the neighboring states, fostering friendship and goodwill. Even if my troops arrive later, the people’s allegiance will already be secured. With their support, the enemy loses their allies. The neighboring states will coordinate their efforts, drums will thunder in unison, and the enemy, struck with terror, will not know where to turn.”

Zhang Yu says: *Thoroughfare* refers to land with routes extending in all directions. My enemy faces me on one side, while neighboring states lie on the other three, all connected. I must go and forge alliances with them as my support. “First to arrive” means dispatching envoys with generous gifts to negotiate alliances with the neighboring states. Even if my troops arrive later, I will have already secured their support.

Where one penetrates deeply into enemy territory, leaving many cities and towns behind, it is called *heavy terrain*.
Cao Cao says: Land from which return is difficult.
Li Quan says: Terrain requiring unwavering resolve. Bai Qi’s campaign against Chu and Yue Yi’s conquest of Qi were both *heavy terrain*.
Du Mu says: Having advanced deep into enemy territory and passed many cities, with river crossings and key routes already under their control, retreat becomes impossible.

Du You says: Difficult to return. *Bei* (背) means "to leave"; *bei* is interchangeable with *bei* (倍). *Many* refers to distance. Far from one’s own cities and fortresses, deeply penetrating enemy territory, with single-minded resolve—this is called *heavy terrain*.

Mei Yaochen says: Advancing through a gap, the deeper one penetrates, the more cities one passes. With river crossings and strategic passes cut off, this is called *heavy and difficult terrain*.

Wang Xi says: When troops reach this point, the situation is grave.

He [Shi] says: In *heavy terrain*, having entered deep into enemy territory, provisions from the homeland are hard to supply. If the officers and soldiers do not plunder, what will they take? King Helü of Wu asked Sun Wu: “If I lead my troops deep into *heavy terrain*, crossing many obstacles, with supply lines severed, and wish to return, the situation makes passage impossible. If I seek provisions from the enemy while keeping my forces intact, what should I do?”

Sun Wu replied: “In *heavy terrain*, when the soldiers are bold and transport routes are blocked, plunder to sustain yourselves. Whatever grain and silk the troops obtain should be offered to their superiors. Those who contribute more will be rewarded, and the soldiers will have no thought of returning. If you wish to withdraw, prepare defenses: dig deep moats and raise high ramparts to signal to the enemy that you intend to stay long. When the enemy suspects the main routes, secretly clear the critical paths. Then send light chariots moving silently, using oxen and horses as bait. If the enemy emerges, sound the drums and pursue them. Conceal your troops and coordinate with them at the appointed time. With internal and external forces responding in unison, their defeat is assured.”

Zhang Yu says: Deeply penetrating enemy territory, passing many enemy cities, with the soldiers’ hearts set and no thought of returning—this is *terrain difficult to retreat from*. As Sima Jingwang said of Zhuge Ke’s armored advance: “His momentum is unstoppable.”

Where one traverses mountains, forests, perilous defiles, or marshes—any terrain difficult to traverse—this is called *ruined terrain*.

Cao Cao says: Lacking solidity.

Jia Lin says: *Ruined* refers to land destroyed by water. In marshy, ruined terrain, one cannot stay long and should depart swiftly.

Mei Yaochen says: Land ruined by water is already difficult to traverse—how much more so for battle or defense?

He [Shi] says: *Ruined terrain* is land lacking solidity, unsuitable for building fortifications or digging moats. One should leave quickly.

King Helü of Wu asked Sun Wu: “If I enter *ruined terrain*—mountains, rivers, perilous defiles, and difficult paths—marching for a long time, my troops exhausted, with the enemy before me and ambushes behind, their camps to my left and defenses to my right, their elite chariots and swift cavalry blocking my narrow passes—what should I do?”

Sun Wu replied: “First send light chariots ten *li* ahead to scout and engage the enemy at the perilous points. Some may turn left, others right. The commander surveys the four directions, chooses gaps to exploit, and converges on the main route, halting only when weary.”

Zhang Yu says: In perilous, marshy terrain, advancing and retreating are both difficult, with no place to rely on.

Where the route of entry is narrow and the path of return circuitous, and the enemy can use a small force to strike our larger one, this is called *encircled terrain*.

Li Quan says: Movement is difficult.

Du Mu says: Entry and exit are arduous, making it easy to set ambushes and achieve victory.

Du You says: The route of entry is treacherous, and the return path is distant. If delayed, provisions will run out. Thus, where the enemy can use a small force to strike our larger one, this is *encircled terrain*.

Mei Yaochen says: Surrounded by mountains and rivers, entry is narrow, and return is circuitous.
