He [Shi] says: In *encircled terrain*, entry is through narrow defiles, and return is roundabout. With no freedom to advance or retreat, what use is a large force? Only through ingenious maneuvers can one traverse such terrain.

King Helü of Wu asked Sun Wu: “If I enter *encircled terrain*, with a strong enemy ahead and perilous obstacles behind, the enemy cutting off my supply lines and favoring my retreat, their drums sounding but their troops not advancing to test my resolve—what should I do?”

Sun Wu replied: “In *encircled terrain*, one must block the gaps to show there is nowhere to go. Treat the army as your home, uniting ten thousand hearts as one. The three armies should cook together for several days without showing smoke. Appear disorganized and weak. When the enemy sees this, they will surely underestimate your preparations. Then rally the officers and soldiers, rouse their fury, and array your best troops in ambush on the left and right. Sound the drums and charge. If the enemy resists, strike fiercely and break through. Advance to fight while the rear expands, and the flanks support from the sides.

“If the enemy is within our encirclement, lying in ambush with deep plans, luring us with advantages and entangling us with flags, appearing chaotic and directionless—what then?”

Sun Wu replied: “Have a thousand men hold banners, blocking the key routes. Send light troops to provoke the enemy, forming ranks but not engaging, clashing but not retreating. This is the method to defeat their schemes.”

Zhang Yu says: In terrain narrow ahead and perilous behind, where one man can hold off a thousand, victory comes through ambushes and surprise. Swift battle ensures survival; failure to fight swiftly leads to destruction—this is *deadly terrain*.

Cao Cao says: Ahead lie high mountains, behind great waters. Advance is impossible, and retreat is obstructed.

Li Quan says: Blocked by mountains, backed by water, with provisions exhausted—swift action is advantageous, delay is not.

Du Mu says: As Duke Wei Li Jing said: “Some armies, advancing without local guides, fall into peril and defeat, controlled by the enemy. To the left, valleys; to the right, mountains—paths so narrow that horses must be led and chariots suspended. Ahead, impassable; behind, cut off—cliffs where troops must march in single file like geese or fish. Before the battle lines are set, a strong enemy suddenly appears. There is no ground to advance upon, no position to retreat to. Seeking battle is impossible, and self-defense is untenable. If halted, time drags on; if moving, front and rear are vulnerable. The wilds lack water and grass, the army lacks supplies, horses are exhausted, and men are weary. Wisdom is exhausted, and strength is spent. One man guarding the pass can hold off ten thousand. If the enemy seizes such a critical point first, even with elite troops and sharp weapons, what use can they be?”

If in such a death ground, swift battle ensures survival; failure to fight swiftly leads to annihilation. One must unite the ranks in single purpose, combine qi and strength as one, spill guts and splatter blood, resolve to die at the forefront, turning defeat into victory and calamity into fortune. This is the way.

Chen Hao said: “Men in a death ground are like those sitting in a leaking boat or crouching beneath a burning roof.”
Jia Lin said: “With high mountains on both sides and impassable ravines ahead and behind, entry is easy but exit is difficult. If trapped in such a place, only a desperate battle brings life; if one waits until the soldiers’ morale is broken and supplies are exhausted before holding out, what awaits but death?”
Mei Yaochen said: “Unable to advance, retreat, or flee to the side, one has no choice but to fight swiftly.”
He Shi said: “In a death ground, fighting fiercely may bring survival; clinging to a corner means death.”

King Wu of Wu asked Sun Wu: “If my army marches beyond our borders and encamps in enemy territory, and the enemy arrives in great force, surrounding us in multiple layers, with no way to break out—every path blocked—how can I rouse my officers and inspire the ranks to risk their lives and shatter the encirclement?”
Sun Wu replied: “Dig deep moats and raise high ramparts to show preparedness for defense. Remain calm and still to conceal our strength. Proclaim to the three armies that we have no choice. Slaughter oxen and burn chariots to feast the troops. Burn all provisions, fill in wells and hearths, cut hair and discard caps to cast aside thoughts of survival. Let the general have no remaining schemes, and the soldiers embrace the resolve to die. Then sharpen armor and whet blades, unite qi and strength as one. Attack on both flanks, beat drums furiously, and raise a clamor—even the enemy will be alarmed, not knowing where to turn. Dispatch elite troops in separate columns to strike swiftly at their rear. This is seeking life by abandoning the Way.”
Thus it is said: “Besieged yet without strategy, one is doomed; doomed yet without battle, one perishes.”

King Wu asked: “If I am the one besieging the enemy, what then?”
Sun Wu replied: “When mountains are steep and valleys perilous, making escape impossible, they are called desperate bandits. The method to strike them: conceal troops in hidden shelters, open a path for their retreat, and show them an escape route. Desperate to survive, they will have no will to fight. Strike then, and even a large force will be broken.”
The Art of War also states: “If the enemy is in a death ground and their soldiers are fierce, the method to strike them is to yield rather than resist, secretly guard advantageous positions, and open a path for their retreat. Dispatch elite cavalry to block key routes, send light troops to provoke them, array forces but do not engage—this is the way to thwart their plans.”
Zhang Yu said: “When trapped by steep mountains and rivers, unable to advance or retreat, with supplies cut off within and the enemy pressing from without, one must rouse the troops to decisive battle—delay is not an option.”

Thus:
In dispersed ground, do not fight.
Li Quan said: “For fear of scattering.”
Du Mu said: “As explained above.”
Jia Lin said: “With no passes or gates, troops easily scatter. In such terrain, one cannot fight repeatedly. This is one school’s view on terrain. But if commands are strict and clear, and soldiers are devoted and obedient, they would not shrink even from death—how could they scatter?”
Mei Yaochen said: “When our troops are in their homeland, attached to life and land, formations are unstable, and combat is unwinnable—thus one cannot fight.”
Wang Xi said: “If forced to fight, they will fear scattering.”
Zhang Yu said: “With soldiers clinging to life, one cannot lightly engage in battle.”

King Wu asked Sun Wu: “If in dispersed ground one cannot fight, then one must hold fast and not sally forth. But if the enemy attacks our small cities, plunders our fields, blocks our firewood-gathering, and severs our vital roads, waiting until we are weakened before launching a fierce assault, what then?”
Sun Wu replied: “When the enemy has penetrated deeply, their resolve is to fight lightly. Our troops, attached to their homeland, form unstable lines and cannot prevail in battle. We must gather people and store grain, defend cities and fortify strongholds, and send light troops to cut off their supply lines. If they challenge us to battle and find no response, and their provisions fail to arrive, with nothing to plunder in the wilds, their three armies will be starved and exhausted. Then we can lure them into a trap and achieve success. If we wish to fight in the open, we must seize the opportunity, rely on natural barriers to set ambushes, or, lacking such advantages, conceal ourselves in darkness to strike unexpectedly at their moment of slackness.”

In light ground, do not halt.
Li Quan said: “For fear of flight.”
Du Mu said: “The so-called light ground in the Art of War refers to when an army first enters enemy territory, not yet having passed through perilous terrain. Soldiers long to return, finding advance difficult and retreat easy. As entering is hard, it is called light ground. One must select elite cavalry and set hidden ambushes. If the enemy suddenly arrives, strike without hesitation; if they do not come, cross swiftly and depart.”
Du You said: “With resolve not yet firm, one cannot engage the enemy.”
Mei Yaochen said: “When first entering enemy territory, not yet having passed through perilous terrain, soldiers’ hearts are not steadfast—there is no reason to fight. Do not approach famous cities or take main roads; swift advance is advantageous.”
Wang Xi said: “There is no reason to halt without cause.”
Zhang Yu said: “With soldiers eager to return, one cannot linger.”

King Wu asked: “If soldiers long to return, advance is difficult and retreat easy, and the army is not yet past perilous terrain, with the three armies fearful, what then?”
Sun Wu replied: “When the army is in light ground and soldiers are not yet steadfast, the priority is to advance, not to fight. Thus, do not approach famous cities or take main roads. Feign confusion and deception, as if preparing to withdraw. Then select elite cavalry, have them hold bits in their mouths to silence them, and lead the way in. Seize their livestock, and when the three armies see the gains, their fear will turn to eagerness. Divide our finest troops to set hidden ambushes. If the enemy comes, strike without hesitation; if they do not, abandon the position and depart.”

In contested ground, do not attack.
Cao Cao said: “Do not attack; the advantage lies in arriving first.”
Li Quan said: “If the enemy has already occupied perilous terrain, do not attack.”
Du Mu said: “Do not attack means that if the enemy has already seized the ground, one must not attack.”

Mei Yaochen said: “In terrain of strategic advantage, the first to occupy gains the benefit; if the enemy has already seized the position, do not attack.” Zhang Yu said: “One should not contend by attacking; rather, act later but arrive first.” The King of Wu asked: “If the enemy arrives first, occupies key positions to secure advantage, selects and trains troops, and alternates between offense and defense to prepare for our surprise attacks, what should be done?” Wu replied: “The method for contested ground is this: those who yield gain, while those who seek lose. If the enemy has taken the position, take care not to attack. Instead, feign retreat, raise banners and sound drums, rush toward what they cherish, burn brush and raise dust to confuse their senses. Divide our finest troops to set hidden ambushes, and the enemy will surely come to their aid. When others desire, I yield; when others abandon, I seize—this is the way to contend for the initiative. If we arrive first and the enemy employs this tactic, select our elite troops to firmly hold the position, send light cavalry in pursuit, and set ambushes in perilous terrain. When the enemy turns to fight, ambushes will rise from the flanks—this is the way to complete victory.”

In intersecting ground, do not sever connections.
Cao Cao said: “They are mutually connected and adjacent.”

Li Quan said: “One must not sever connections.”
Du Mu said: “In broad rivers and level terrain, where battles rage on all sides, chariots and cavalry must maintain formation, linking head to tail without break, lest the enemy exploit the gap.”
Jia Lin said: “One may forge alliances, but never cut them off—severance breeds enmity.”
Du You said: “They are mutually connected and adjacent. All may advance or retreat; one must not use force to break their bonds.”
Mei Yaochen said: “Where roads crisscross, fear interception; ensure units remain linked, never broken.”
Wang Xi said: “This refers to supply routes. Such intersecting terrain, also called ‘open ground,’ demands occupying high ground to await the enemy—one must not cut off the grain route.”
Zhang Yu said: “Where paths intersect freely, one must not block the enemy’s way with force; instead, secure victory through ambushes.”
King of Wu said: “In intersecting ground, I shall sever the enemy’s approach. First, order border cities to fortify defenses, block thoroughfares, and secure passes. If we fail to plan ahead, the enemy will prepare, allowing them to advance while we cannot, and with forces evenly matched—what then?”
Wu replied: “If we cannot go forth while they may come, divide troops to conceal them. Guard but feign weakness; when the enemy nears, set ambushes in hidden valleys to strike unexpectedly.”

In thoroughfare ground, unite in alliance.
Cao Cao said: “Form alliances with the feudal lords.”
Li Quan said: “Forge connections.”
Du Mu said: “The feudal lords are the neighboring states mentioned above.”
Meng said: “With alliances, safety; without them, peril.”
Mei Yaochen said: “Though the land lies open on all sides, how gain the world’s aid? Offer rich gifts to unite them.”
Wang Xi said: “In a land of four crossings, strength comes only through alliances.”
Zhang Yu said: “In such terrain, first secure ties with neighboring states.”
King of Wu said: “In thoroughfare ground, priority is paramount. If our path is long and we march late, even with swift chariots and galloping horses, we cannot arrive first—what then?”
Wu replied: “Where feudal lords converge and roads extend in all directions, with the enemy and us facing each other while other states lie nearby—what is called ‘priority’ demands heavy gifts and light envoys to negotiate peace with neighboring states, forging bonds of kinship and favor. Though our troops arrive later, the masses will already be ours. Train soldiers, fortify positions with strategic advantage, and with our allies’ support, the enemy loses their faction. With states attacking from all sides, none can withstand us.”

In heavy ground, plunder.
Cao Cao said: “Stockpile grain and provisions.”
Li Quan said: “Deep in enemy territory, one must not act unjustly and lose the people’s hearts. When Emperor Gao of Han entered Qin, he forbade harming women or seizing treasures, thus winning the people’s favor.” Quan took *plunder* to mean *not plundering*.
Du Mu said: “In heavy ground, with no advantage in advancing and no retreat possible, one must transport grain for a prolonged campaign to await the enemy.”
Meng said: “Draw provisions from the enemy.”
Mei Yaochen said: “Far from one’s state, with many cities at one’s back and supply routes severed, plunder to stockpile and sustain food.”
Wang Xi said: “Deep in enemy territory, raid fertile fields to enrich stores. In difficult terrain, scarcity of food brings danger.”
Zhang Yu said: “Deep in enemy lands, with no supply of provisions, rouse the troops to plunder for sustenance to prevent want.”
King of Wu said: “Heavy ground often lies beyond cities, with supply routes cut off. If we seek to return, the path may be impassable—what then?”
Wu replied: “In heavy ground, where soldiers are bold and transport is blocked, plunder to sustain food. Let those below obtain grain and silk, all to be offered to their superiors, with rewards for the abundant. If seeking to withdraw, dig deep moats and raise high ramparts to signal a prolonged stay. The enemy, suspecting open routes, will secretly clear key points. Then order light chariots to march in silence, raising dust and scattering cattle and horses as bait. If the enemy emerges, sound drums to pursue, while hidden troops lie in wait, coordinating inside and out—their defeat is assured.”

In broken ground, march.
Cao Cao said: “Do not linger.”
Li Quan said: “Do not dig trenches; hasten away.”
Mei Yaochen said: “Where the land is ruined and uninhabitable, move swiftly without delay.”
Wang Xi said: “Gather the army; broken ground is no place to halt.”
Zhang Yu said: “In treacherous terrain, do not tarry.”
King of Wu said: “Where mountains and rivers are perilous and paths arduous, prolonged marching exhausts the troops. If the enemy lies ahead and ambushes behind, camps to the left and guards to the right, with elite chariots and swift cavalry blocking narrow passes—what then?”
Wu replied: “First send light chariots ten *li* ahead to scout and await the enemy, timing their arrival at perilous points. Some may turn left, others right; the commander surveys all, choosing gaps to seize, converging midway before resting.”

In encircled ground, devise strategy.
Cao Cao said: “Deploy ingenious plans.”
Li Quan said: “The wise are never trapped.”
Du Mu said: “In treacherous terrain, locked in stalemate with the enemy, employ cunning and deceptive tactics.”
Du You said: “Here, one must rely on crafty schemes to escape peril.”
Mei Yaochen said: “With narrow fronts, perilous rears, and circuitous return routes, devise strategies to prevail.”
Zhang Yu said: “Hard to conquer by force, easy to seize by stratagem.”
King of Wu said: “With a strong enemy ahead and peril behind, the enemy cuts off our supply routes, forcing retreat. They beat drums but do not advance, testing our resolve—what then?”
Wu replied: “In encircled ground, block all gaps to signal no escape, making the army one’s home. With ten thousand united in heart and the three armies in concert, cook for days without showing smoke, feigning disorder and weakness.”

When the enemy sees us and prepares lightly, rally and inspire the troops, rouse their fury, array disciplined warriors, flank them with perilous terrain, and charge forth with drumbeats. If the enemy stands firm, strike swiftly and break through—press forward in combat while securing the rear, and flank them from both sides. In death ground, fight.

Cao Cao said: “This is fighting in death ground.”
Li Quan said: “A fight to the death, with no thought of survival.”
Chen Hao said: “Trapped in death ground, every soldier fights for himself. Thus it is said: ‘Place them in death ground, and they will live.’”
Jia Lin said: “Fight with all one’s might, and one may survive; cling to a corner, and one will die.”
Mei Yaochen said: “With no escape before, behind, left, or right, show them they must die, and every man will fight for himself.”
Zhang Yu said: “Trapped in death ground, men fight of their own accord.”

The King of Wu said: “If a vast enemy force surrounds us in multiple layers, and we seek to break out but find all paths blocked, how can we rouse the troops to fight with their lives?”
Wu replied: “Dig deep trenches and raise high ramparts; remain still and do not move. Proclaim to the three armies that there is no choice but to fight. Slaughter oxen and burn wagons to feast the soldiers. Burn all provisions and fill in wells and hearths. Cut your hair and cast aside your cap, abandon all thought of survival, sharpen armor and blades, and unite in spirit and strength. Attack on both flanks, beat drums furiously, and raise a clamor—let the enemy tremble, unsure where to turn. Dispatch elite troops in separate columns to strike swiftly at their rear. This is how one loses the Way yet seeks life.”

Thus it is said: “Besieged and without strategy, one is doomed; doomed and unwilling to fight, one perishes.” The ancients skilled in warfare could ensure the enemy’s front and rear could not support each other.

Mei Yaochen said: “Through sudden assaults and ambushes.”
Their many and few could not rely on one another.

Mei Yaochen said: “Through shock and disruption.”
Their noble and base could not rescue each other.

Mei Yaochen said: “Through scattering and chaos.”
Their superiors and subordinates could not regroup.

Mei Yaochen said: “Through panic and haste.”
Soldiers scattered and could not reassemble; troops gathered but could not unite.

Li Quan said: “Deploy deceptions to confuse them—strike their right when they rush to save their left, leaving them too flustered to plan.”
Du Mu said: “Employ manifold deceptions to throw the enemy into disarray. Charge their front while ambushing their rear, feign an attack to the east while striking west, create false formations, and display unexpected maneuvers. We engage without fixed form, while the enemy must prepare on all sides, their forces divided.”
Meng Shi said: “Create numerous distractions—appear in the east while striking the west, attack the south to draw them north, leaving them bewildered and scattered, unable to concentrate.”
Mei Yaochen said: “Some may scatter and fail to regroup; others may gather but fail to unite.”
Wang Xi said: “This arises from the disparity between commanders. The key lies in the interplay of the orthodox and the unexpected, like limbs responding in unison.”
Zhang Yu said: “Strike where they least expect, exploit their unpreparedness, and charge with elite troops in a sudden, shattering assault. When they rush to save their front, their rear is exposed; when they respond to the left, their right is vulnerable. Leave them flustered and disordered, unsure where to defend. Officers and soldiers cannot reinforce one another; their troops scatter and cannot reassemble, or gather but cannot unite.”

Act when it is advantageous; cease when it is not.

Cao Cao said: “Provoke them to scatter, disrupt them to prevent unity, then move to engage.”
Li Quan said: “Agitate them until they see an opening, then act; if they remain unshaken, hold back.”
Mei Yaochen said: “To bring the enemy to such a state, one must act only when advantageous, and cease when it is not.”
Zhang Yu said: “Even if they are startled, act only when advantageous, and cease when it is not.”

May I ask: If the enemy arrives in great numbers and strict order, how should we await them?

Cao Cao said: “This is a hypothetical question.”
Mei Yaochen said: “This is a self-posed query. If the enemy is numerous and well-disciplined, how should we face them?”
Zhang Yu said: “The previous discussions assume forces of comparable size. Thus, someone asks Wu: ‘If the enemy outnumbers us and is well-ordered, how should we confront them?’”

Wu replied: “First seize what they cherish, and they will comply.”
Cao Cao said: “Take away what they rely upon. If we first occupy advantageous ground, we will surely obtain what we desire.”
Li Quan said: “Sunzi posed this question to reveal a secret principle. ‘What they cherish’ refers to what benefits the enemy—whether wealth, women, or children. If we first harass and humiliate them, the enemy’s advance and retreat will be at our command.”
Du Mu said: “Secure our advantageous positions, raid their fields, and cut off their supply routes—these are what the enemy cherishes and depends upon. If we seize them all, even a strong enemy’s movements, victories, and defeats will be subject to our will.”
Chen Hao said: “What they cherish is not limited to what they rely upon; anything the enemy values can be seized.”
Mei Yaochen said: “First take what they hold dear, so our will prevails, then throw them into shock and disarray, leaving them with no recourse.”
Wang Xi said: “First occupy advantageous ground, use unexpected forces to sever their supply lines, and they will follow our plans.”
Zhang Yu said: “What the enemy cherishes is advantageous ground and provisions. If we seize them first, they will follow our designs without exception.”

The essence of warfare lies in speed—exploit the enemy’s unpreparedness, advance by unexpected routes, and strike where they are unguarded.

Cao Cao said: “Sunzi responds to the question by reiterating the nature of warfare.”
Li Quan said: “Strike where they are unprepared and unguarded—this is the swiftness of breaking the enemy.”
Du Mu said: “This summarizes the nature of warfare, seizing the enemy’s vulnerabilities. Advance by unexpected routes and strike where they are unguarded—this is the profound essence of warfare and the supreme task of a commander.”

Chen Hao said: “This speaks of seizing the enemy’s unpreparedness, unforeseen vulnerabilities, and unguarded moments—one must advance swiftly without hesitation. For Sunzi’s essential teaching is that the use of arms values speed above all.”

Mei Yaochen said: “The mechanism of warfare prizes speed; one must strike where the enemy is unprepared. To strike where they are unprepared is to advance by unexpected routes and attack where they are unguarded.”

Wang Xi said: “In warfare, supreme swiftness is paramount—seizing what the enemy cherishes is only natural.”

He Shi said: “Consider the case of Meng Da, a Shu general who defected to Wei. The Wei court appointed him Governor of Xincheng. Later, Meng Da secretly allied with Wu to reinforce Shu, plotting against the Central States. When his scheme was exposed, Sima Xuanwang, then in power, feared Meng Da would act swiftly. He sent a letter to reassure him, but Meng Da remained indecisive upon receiving it. Xuanwang then advanced his troops in secret. His generals urged caution, saying Meng Da was colluding with two enemies and that they should investigate before acting. Xuanwang replied: ‘Meng Da is treacherous and untrustworthy—this is the moment of his hesitation. We must strike before he regains resolve.’ He doubled the pace of his march and reached Meng Da’s city in eight days. Wu and Shu each dispatched their generals to Xicheng, constructing barricades at Anqiao and Mulanse to relieve Meng Da, but Xuanwang divided his forces to block them. Earlier, Meng Da had written to Zhuge Liang: ‘Wan is eight hundred *li* from Luoyang and one thousand one hundred *li* from me. If they learn of my uprising, they will petition the Son of Heaven, and by the time their orders reverse course, a month will have passed—my city will be fortified, and my troops fully prepared. The terrain is treacherous; Sima will not come himself, and if his generals do, I will have nothing to fear.’ When the army arrived, Meng Da wrote again to Zhuge Liang: ‘I rose in revolt eight days ago, and now their troops are at my gates—how could they be so swift?’ Shangyong City was protected on three sides by water, and Meng Da built wooden palisades at its base to fortify it. Xuanwang crossed the water, breached the palisades, and advanced directly to the city walls, attacking from eight directions. After sixteen days, Meng Da’s nephew Deng Xian and his general Li Fu opened the gates and surrendered, and Meng Da was executed.

When Li Jing campaigned against Xiao Xian, he assembled his troops at Kuizhou. Xiao Xian, believing the autumn floods had swollen the rivers and submerged the paths through the Three Gorges, assumed Li Jing could not advance and thus relaxed his defenses. In the ninth month, Li Jing led his army forward. As they prepared to descend the gorges, his generals urged him to halt and wait for the waters to recede. Li Jing said: ‘Warfare values divine speed; opportunity must not be lost. Our troops have just assembled, and Xiao Xian remains unaware. If we exploit the rising waters to appear suddenly at his gates, it will be like thunder too swift to cover one’s ears—this is the supreme strategy of warfare. Even if he learns of us, he will be unable to muster troops in time, and we will surely capture him.’ Thus, Xiao Xian was subdued.

The *Art of War of Duke Wei* states: ‘In warfare, employ the divine and prize speed. Train and refine your soldiers, clarify commands, instruct their eyes with banners and flags, accustom their ears to drums and bells, enforce strict rewards and punishments to discipline them, provide ample provisions to sustain them, deepen moats and trenches to defend them, point out mountains and rivers to guide them, appoint the capable to lead them, and teach them the interplay of orthodox and unorthodox tactics. Thus, even if the enemy strikes with the swiftness of lightning, we will be prepared. If troops lack prior preparation, they will not respond in time; if they fail to respond, they will miss the opportunity; if they miss the opportunity, they will lag behind events; if they lag, they will fail to secure victory, and the army will be destroyed.’ Thus, the *Lüshi Chunqiu* says: ‘In warfare, one must act swiftly to achieve a decisive victory; it cannot be prolonged.’ Therefore, it is said: ‘The essence of warfare lies in speed, exploiting the enemy’s unpreparedness.’

Yet if the enemy’s commander is cunning, his troops harmonious, his orders obeyed without question, his weapons sharp, his armor sturdy, his *qi* keen and disciplined, and his strength full and vigorous—how can one swiftly engage him? The answer: In such cases, one must conceal one’s tracks and silence one’s movements, accumulate strength while awaiting the enemy’s exhaustion, evade his momentum, and wear him down over time—how could one recklessly attack? This is why Lian Po resisted Bai Qi by holding his ground without fighting, and why Xuanwang opposed Wu Hou by restraining his advance.

Zhang Yu said: “Again, addressing another: The principle of warfare values divine speed. What is prized in speed is seizing the enemy’s moment of panic, leaving him no time to prepare. Advance by unexpected routes to strike where he is unguarded, so that he is thrown into confusion, his forces scattered, his front and rear unable to coordinate, and his many and few unable to support one another.

The way of the invader: When deeply entrenched, his resolve is single-minded, and the defender cannot overcome him.”

Li Quan said: “As an invader, the deeper one penetrates, the firmer one’s resolve, and the defender cannot resist.”

Du Mu said: “Generally, in the way of attack, if one deeply enters enemy territory, the soldiers will be determined to fight to the death, their minds unified, and the defender will be unable to prevail.”

Mei Yaochen said: “As an invader, the deeper one enters enemy land, the more the soldiers concentrate their resolve, and the defender cannot overcome them.”

Zhang Yu said: “When deeply penetrating enemy territory, the soldiers’ minds are single-minded, and the defender cannot prevail. This is because the invader is in a position of grave importance, while the defender is in a position of ease. This is why Zhao’s Guang Wujun told Han Xin: ‘When you fight far from your state, your momentum is irresistible.’”

“Plunder fertile fields so the Three Armies have ample provisions.”

Wang Xi said: “Fertile fields yield abundant crops.”

“Carefully sustain them without exhausting them; consolidate their *qi* and accumulate their strength. Devise strategies that are unfathomable.”

Cao Cao said: “Nurture the soldiers, consolidate their *qi*, and devise strategies that cannot be predicted.”

Li Quan said: “With *qi* abundant and strength accumulated, combined with strategic planning, the enemy will be unable to fathom one’s intentions.”

Du Mu said: “This means that when deeply entering enemy territory, one must plunder the fields to ensure ample provisions, then rest the troops behind fortifications without wearing them out.”

With *qi* complete and strength abundant, strike once to secure victory. Act with adaptability and transformation, so the enemy cannot fathom my intentions.

Chen Hao said: “The terrain where one encamps must have nearby water and grass, with ample supplies and no shortages. Carefully regulate movements and well tend to the soldiers.”

When Wang Jian campaigned against Chu, the Chu forces challenged him to battle, but he refused to engage. He diligently nurtured and guided his troops, consolidating their strength as one. Hearing that his soldiers tossed stones in play, he knew they had been nurtured in courage and longed for battle—only then did he deploy them, and in a single stroke, he destroyed Chu.

Yet when deeply entering enemy territory without seeing an opportunity for victory, one must employ this strategy.

Mei Yaochen said: “Plunder their fertile lands to ensure ample provisions for the army. Rest the soldiers’ strength and consolidate their forces to devise unfathomable plans.”

Wang Xi said: “‘Carefully sustain’ means to meticulously tend to their food and drink. Consolidate their sharp *qi*, accumulate surplus strength, conceal one’s form, and keep strategies secret, so the enemy cannot predict them. Wait for an exploitable opening, then advance.”

Zhang Yu said: “When the army is in perilous terrain, one must plunder grain from fertile fields to enrich one’s provisions. Then fortify defenses, diligently tend to the soldiers, and not allow them to suffer hardship. Thus, their *qi* will be abundant and strength complete, and one may constantly devise unfathomable strategies. When the enemy presents an opportunity to strike, seize it in a single decisive move. Wang Jian’s campaign against Jing frequently employed this tactic.”

Placed where there is no retreat, they will fight to the death without fleeing.

Li Quan said: “When their strength is secured, place them where there is no escape.”

Du Mu said: “‘Placed where there is no retreat’ means that whether advancing or retreating, there is nowhere to go. Thus, the soldiers will strive to fight with all their might, and even in death, they will not flee.”

Mei Yaochen said: “Set them in a position where they must fight, knowing they will die rather than retreat.”

Zhang Yu said: “Placed in perilous terrain, with no escape in any direction, they will defend and fight to the death rather than flee.”

If death is certain, how can victory not be won?

Cao Cao said: “If the soldiers are resolved to die, how can victory not be assured?”

Du Mu said: “When soldiers are determined to die, how can there be no chance of victory?”

Meng Shi said: “If the soldiers are resolved to die, there is nothing they cannot achieve.”

Mei Yaochen said: “How can the troops fail to obey commands?”

Zhang Yu said: “If the soldiers fight to the death, how can their aims not be realized?”

The *Wei Liaozi* states: “If a lone bandit wields a sword and strikes in the marketplace, ten thousand will flee from him—not because he alone is brave, nor because the ten thousand are all cowardly, but because the resolve to die and the will to live are not equal. Thus, the soldiers will exert all their strength.”

Cao Cao said: “In dire terrain, their hearts unite.”

Mei Yaochen said: “How can the soldiers not exhaust all their strength to join the battle?”

Wang Xi said: “When men are placed in a position of certain death, how can they not give their all?”

He Shi said: “Even beasts, when cornered, will fight, and birds, when desperate, will peck. How much more so for humans, the most intelligent of creatures?”

Zhang Yu said: “When all are in dire straits together, how can they not unite in giving their utmost?”

When soldiers are deeply entrapped, they feel no fear.

Du Mu said: “Trapped in peril, they know they cannot die alone, and the entire army shares one heart—thus, they feel no fear.”

Mei Yaochen echoed Du Mu’s annotation.

Wang Xi said: “Placed in dire terrain, they feel no fear; without fear, their fighting spirit is unyielding.”

Zhang Yu said: “Trapped in a position of imminent destruction, men hold fast to the resolve to die—how could they still fear the enemy?”

With no escape, they stand firm; deeply entrenched, they are constrained.

Cao Cao said: “Constrained, as if bound.”

Li Quan said: “Firm, as in resolute.”

Du Mu said: “‘Escape’ means to flee. When deeply entering enemy territory with no path to survival, the soldiers’ hearts become as steadfast as if bound.”

Mei Yaochen said: “Placed where there is no escape, their hearts naturally stand firm; deeply entrenched, their resolve naturally becomes single-minded.”

Zhang Yu said: “With no direction to flee, their hearts stand firm. When the army is in perilous terrain with nowhere to turn, they are as if bound.”

When there is no alternative, they will fight.

Cao Cao said: “When men are desperate, they will fight to the death.”

Li Quan said: “Resolved to their fate.”

Du Mu said: “‘No alternative’ means they suspect they are trapped in a position of certain death and will not survive. Using death to avert death, utterly without recourse, they will all fight with everything they have.”

Mei Yaochen and He Shi echoed Du Mu’s annotation.

Zhang Yu said: “When circumstances leave no choice, they must fight with all their might.”

Thus, their troops require no discipline to remain vigilant, no demands to yield results, no pacts to foster closeness, no commands to inspire trust.

Cao Cao said: “Without seeking, their intent is naturally fulfilled.”

Li Quan said: “Placed in a position of certain death, they will obey without command.”

Du Mu said: “This means that when the army is in a position of certain death, superiors and subordinates share a single purpose. They do not need to be disciplined to remain vigilant, nor do they need to be gathered to align their hearts. Without pacts or commands, they naturally become close and trustworthy.”

Meng Shi said: “Without seeking victory, victory is naturally attained.”

Mei Yaochen said: “Without discipline, the troops remain vigilant on their own; without seeking, their sentiments are naturally fulfilled; without pacts, the masses naturally become close; without commands, the people naturally trust. All this arises from being placed in peril, so the entire army shares one heart.”

Wang Xi said: “This refers to the natural state of men’s hearts in a position of certain death.”

Zhang Yu said: “In perilous terrain, men naturally unite in strength. Without discipline, they remain vigilant on their own; without seeking, they attain mutual understanding; without restraints, they become close to their superiors; without commands, they trust in their orders. This is what is meant by ‘crossing a river in the same boat—how could the Hu and Yue fear discord?’”

Banish omens and dispel doubts, so that even unto death, there is nowhere to turn.

Cao Cao said: “Banish talk of inauspicious omens and dispel doubtful strategies.” (An alternate version reads: “Even unto death, there is no disaster.”)

Li Quan said: “Banish talk of omens and doubtful matters, so there will be no disaster.”

Du Mu said: “The *Huangshi Gong* states: ‘Banish shamans and diviners; do not allow scribes or soldiers to inquire about the army’s fortunes, lest they disturb the soldiers’ hearts.’”

Once the path of doubt and confusion is barred, soldiers will hold fast to their resolve even unto death.

Mei Yaochen said: “When omens and portents do not arise, and doubtful words do not enter, the army will not falter—fighting on until death.”

Wang Xi said: “Disasters, omens, and divine anomalies can bewilder men, so they must be forbidden.”

Zhang Yu said: “To ensure soldiers fight to the death, military officers must be forbidden from speaking of omens, lest they unsettle the ranks. By removing doubtful strategies, soldiers will hold no other thoughts even unto death. As the *Methods of Sima* states: ‘Eliminate baleful omens.’ This is what it means.

If soldiers lack unwavering resolve, one may even employ omens to rally them—as Tian Dan did when defending Jimo. He ordered a soldier to pose as a spirit, and in every command, invoked the spirit’s name, ultimately breaking the Yan forces.

My soldiers possess no surplus wealth—not because they despise riches; they hold no thought for survival—not because they scorn long life.

Cao Cao said: “All wealth is burned and destroyed—not because they hate riches, but because abandoning possessions to embrace death is unavoidable.”

Du Mu said: “If soldiers cling to wealth, they may hesitate and seek survival, lacking the resolve to die.”

Mei Yaochen said: “They exhaust their wealth and fight to the death—not by choice, but by necessity.”

Wang Xi said: “Enough is sufficient; if soldiers covet riches, they will cling to life. They must fight to the death; if they seek survival, they will lack the will to die.”

Zhang Yu said: “Wealth and life are what men cherish. To cast away treasures and sacrifice their lives is not out of hatred, but necessity.”

When the command is given, seated soldiers weep until their collars are soaked, and those lying down let tears stream down their cheeks.

Cao Cao said: “All hold to a resolve of certain death.”

Li Quan said: “Abandoning wealth and life, they embrace a will to die—hence the tears.”

Du Mu said: “Soldiers pledge themselves to death. Before battle, they are told: ‘Today’s affair hinges on this single strike. If you disobey, your bodies will rot in the wild, devoured by beasts.’”

Mei Yaochen said: “Resolved to fight to the death, as Du Mu describes.”

Wang Xi said: “Moved and inspired, they weep.”

Zhang Yu said: “Stirred to emotion, they shed tears.”

Before battle, they are told: “Today’s affair hinges on this single strike. If you disobey, your bodies will rot in the wild, devoured by beasts.”

Some say: “When marching, feast the soldiers with wine, draw swords and dance, engage in wrestling matches, beat drums, and raise battle cries to bolster their spirit. Would weeping not dampen their fighting resolve?”

The answer: “First secure their resolve to die, then rouse their spirit—thus, victory is assured. Without the will to die, even with high morale, how can they prevail? When Jing Ke stood by the Yi River, his men wept; yet when he struck up a defiant tune, their eyes blazed, and their hair bristled beneath their caps.”

Cast them into a place with no retreat, and they will fight with the courage of Zhuan Zhu and Cao Mo.

Li Quan said: “When beasts are cornered, they claw; when birds are trapped, they peck. Under dire urgency, men display the bravery of Zhuan Zhu and Cao Gui.”

Du Mu said: “Wherever they are sent, they will fight with the courage of Zhuan Zhu and Cao Gui.”

Mei Yaochen said: “Once ordered to certain death, they will charge with the bravery of Zhuan Zhu and Cao Mo.”

Zhang Yu said: “With death in their hearts, they will advance with the courage of Zhuan Zhu and Cao Gui.”

Zhuan Zhu was the assassin sent by Prince Guang of Wu to kill King Liao of Wu. *Gui* should read *Mo*—Cao Mo served Duke Zhuang of Lu with his bravery, once seizing a dagger to coerce Duke Huan of Qi.

Thus, one skilled in warfare is like *shuairan*.

Mei Yaochen said: “The ease of mutual response.”

*Shuairan* is the snake of Mount Chang. Strike its head, and its tail comes; strike its tail, and its head comes; strike its middle, and both head and tail come.

Mei Yaochen said: “The nature of the snake is such that it cannot be struck—if attacked, it responds swiftly.”

Zhang Yu said: “*Shuai* means swift; when struck, it reacts instantly. This illustrates the principles of battle formations. The *Eight Formations Diagram* states: ‘The rear becomes the front, the front becomes the rear; four heads, eight tails—wherever touched, it becomes the head. If the enemy strikes the center, both head and tail rush to the rescue.’”

One asks: Can troops be made to move like *shuairan*?

Mei Yaochen said: “Can troops be made to respond as one, like a single body?”

The answer: Yes. The people of Wu and Yue despise each other, yet when crossing a river in the same boat and caught in a storm, they rescue one another like left and right hands.

Mei Yaochen said: “Circumstances compel them.”

Zhang Yu said: “Wu and Yue are bitter enemies, yet in shared peril, they aid each other like two hands. How much more so for those who are not enemies—will they not respond like *shuairan*?”

Thus, tying horses in formation and burying wheels are not sufficient to rely upon.

Cao Cao said: “*Fang* means to bind horses. Burying wheels signals immobility. This speaks of inflexibility being inferior to adaptability. Thus, even with horses bound and wheels buried, it is not enough to rely upon.”

Li Quan said: “Cast troops into a place with no retreat, and they will fight on their own, like the head and tail of a snake. Thus, the people of Wu and Yue, though enemies, rescue each other in the same boat—even with horses bound and wheels buried, it is not enough to rely upon.”

Du Mu said: “Binding horses into a square formation and burying wheels to prevent movement—even so, this is not sufficient to be called steadfast or reliable. One must employ adaptability, place soldiers in a situation of certain death, and let them fight for themselves. When they aid each other like two hands, this is the way to secure victory.”

Chen Hao said: “No enmity is greater than that between Wu and Yue, yet when sharing a boat in a storm, they still rescue each other.”

Why is this so? The circumstances compel it. In the Dao of employing troops, if they are placed in a position where battle is inevitable and share the fear of certain death, then head and tail, front and rear, cannot but rescue one another. Even with the enmity of Wu and Yue, they still aid each other like two hands—how much more so when no such enmity exists? This speaks to the value of creating change to unify them, for then the hearts of the brave and the timid become as one.

Mei Yaochen concurs with Du Mu’s annotation. Wang Xi said: “This refers to mutual rescue in dire straits. The head and tail of a snake, the left and right hands of a man—both illustrate the swiftness of mutual aid. Crossing a river in the same boat is a perilous situation; even Wu and Yue harbor no differing intentions, so how much more so for an entire army? Thus, this is far more reliable than binding horses and burying wheels.” Cao Cao’s explanation is correct.

Zhang Yu said: “The preceding text repeatedly speaks of placing troops in a desperate situation to focus their resolve. Yet this alone is not sufficient. Even when placed in peril, one must employ adaptability and ingenuity, causing them to aid one another like left and right hands—only then can victory be secured. Thus, while binding horses and burying wheels may not be relied upon for steadfastness or victory, what can truly be depended upon is ensuring that soldiers respond as one body. When courage is unified as one, this is the Dao of governance.”

Li Quan said: “Unifying courage is the general’s Dao.”
Du Mu said: “When courage and rectitude are aligned, the entire army acts as one—this all depends on governance.”
Chen Hao said: “When commands are strict and clear, the brave cannot advance alone, nor the timid retreat alone; the entire army moves as one.”
Mei Yaochen said: “To make men’s courage unified as one heart without fear is to master the Dao of military governance.”
Wang Xi concurs with Mei Yaochen’s annotation.
Zhang Yu said: “Having placed them in peril and ensured mutual rescue, the entire army’s strength and courage become as one man—this is the Dao of military governance rightly attained.”

When both firmness and flexibility are achieved, this is the principle of terrain.
Cao Cao said: “Strength and weakness become one in circumstance.”
Li Quan said: “Achieving firmness and flexibility depends on the terrain’s advantage.”
Du Mu said: “The dynamics of strength and weakness must be shaped by the land’s configuration.”
Mei Yaochen said: “Whether troops are strong or weak, all can be employed effectively—this is the advantage of terrain.”
Wang Xi said: “Firmness and flexibility are like strength and weakness. When soldiers of an army, strong and weak alike, are all utilized effectively, it is the terrain’s advantage that makes this so.” Cao Cao’s statement that “strength and weakness become one in circumstance” is correct.
Zhang Yu said: “With the advantage of terrain, even weak troops can overcome the enemy—how much more so with strong ones? When both firmness and flexibility are fully employed, it is the terrain’s advantage that makes this possible.”

Thus, one skilled in employing troops guides them as if leading a single man—this is necessity.
Cao Cao said: “Unity in appearance.”
Li Quan said: “Governing the many as one governs the few.”
Du Mu said: “This means making the entire army’s soldiers like one man led by the hand, compelled to follow commands without choice—an easy comparison.”
Jia Lin said: “‘Guiding by the hand’ describes a fluid, shifting formation, allowing seamless transitions: front becomes rear, rear becomes front, left becomes right, right becomes left. Thus, even a million troops move as one.”
Mei Yaochen said: “Employing the entire army as if guiding a single man by the hand is a matter of necessity, for they naturally follow every command.”
Wang Xi said: “Guiding them left and right, front and rear, they all follow.”
Zhang Yu said: “Though the army is vast, it is like leading a single man by the hand—this speaks to unity.”

Thus it is said: Where the general points, none fail to advance to their deaths.
The general’s affairs: stillness with depth, rectitude with order.
Cao Cao said: “This means clarity, stillness, depth, and impartiality.”
Du Mu said: “Clear, still, and simple; deep and unfathomable; impartial and unbiased—thus governance is achieved.”
Mei Yaochen said: “Still and profound, beyond human comprehension; upright and self-governed, beyond human disruption.”
Wang Xi said: “Stillness prevents disturbance, depth prevents predictability, rectitude prevents negligence, order prevents chaos.”
Zhang Yu said: “In planning, he is calm and profound, unfathomable to others; in command, he is just and disciplined, leaving no room for disrespect.”

He can dull the ears and eyes of his soldiers, leaving them ignorant.
Cao Cao said: “To dull is to deceive. The people may share in the joy of success, but they cannot partake in the planning of beginnings.”
Li Quan said: “When plans are not yet mature, soldiers should not be made aware. They may share in the joy of success, but not in the deliberation of beginnings. Thus, their ears and eyes are first dulled, leaving them without knowledge.”
Du Mu said: “This means that apart from the general’s commands, soldiers know nothing else, as if deaf and blind.”
Mei Yaochen said: “All military strategies are to be carried out without being understood.”
Wang Xi said: “Obscure their perceptions.”
He [the commentator] concurs with Du Mu’s annotation.
Zhang Yu said: “Soldiers remain ignorant, following orders without question.”

He alters his actions and changes his plans, leaving no one the wiser.
Li Quan said: “Plans may shift, leaving no trace of their origin.”
Du Mu said: “In the execution of affairs and the formulation of plans, he does not let others discern the intent behind them or recognize their underlying purpose.”
Mei Yaochen said: “He changes what has been done and alters what has been planned, leaving no one able to comprehend.”
Wang Xi said: “Once actions are taken and plans are executed, they must be altered and cannot be repeated.”
He [the commentator] said: “The art of generalship lies in endless adaptability.”
Zhang Yu said: “All previously undertaken actions and formerly devised plans are changed, leaving no one able to discern them.”

If Pei Xingjian ordered his troops to pitch camp and then suddenly commanded them to relocate to a higher ridge, at first the officers and soldiers would be displeased. That night, a violent storm arose, and the original campsite was submerged under over ten feet of water. The officers and soldiers were astonished and came to admire his foresight. They asked, “How did you know the storm was coming?” Xingjian smiled and replied, “From now on, simply follow my commands—why ask how I knew?”

He alters their position and diverts their path, leaving no one able to anticipate his intent.
Li Quan said: “The convenience of the route remains unknown to all.”
Du Mu said: “To alter their position is to move from safety to danger; to divert their path is to abandon the near for the far, instilling in the soldiers a resolve to fight to the death.”
Chen Hao said: “In every action a general undertakes, he must execute it with meticulous care, allowing no one to discern his calculations.”
Jia Lin said: “If our position is advantageous, he compels us to move; if the path is near, he forces us to take a detour. The trigger is pulled so subtly that no one perceives it.”
Mei Yaochen said: “He changes their secure position and diverts their intended path, leaving no one able to anticipate his plans.”
Wang Xi said: “To occupy an exposed position is to lure the enemy into battle; to take a circuitous route is to feign distance while launching a covert attack.”
Zhang Yu said: “He abandons the perilous for the accessible in positioning, and forsakes the near for the distant in routing—no one grasps his intent until victory proves his wisdom.”

The Supreme White Mountain Recluse said: “The art of war prizes deception—not only deceiving the enemy but also deceiving one’s own soldiers, guiding them without revealing the purpose.”

The commander sets a goal for them as if ascending a height and removing the ladder.
Mei Yaochen said: “They can advance but cannot retreat.”
The commander leads them deep into the enemy’s territory and springs the mechanism.
Du Mu said: “This eliminates any thought of retreat—like Meng Ming burning the boats.”
One version reads: *The commander leads them to ascend a height.*
Chen Hao said: “He triggers their resolve.”
Jia Lin said: “He deploys his strategic flexibility, adapting to circumstances as they arise.”
Mei Yaochen said: “He releases the perilous mechanism, compelling them to fight with all their might.”
Wang Xi said: “All these measures steel their determination for decisive battle.”
Once the mechanism is sprung, there is no turning back.
Jia Lin urged Cao Cao, saying, “You must decisively pull the trigger.”
Zhang Yu said: “Removing the ladder allows advance but not retreat; springing the mechanism permits forward movement but no return—like Xiang Yu crossing the river and scuttling the boats.”

Burn the boats and smash the cauldrons, then drive the troops like a flock of sheep—herded forth, herded back, none knowing where they go.
Cao Cao said: “Unite their hearts.”
Li Quan said: “When returning, they burn the boats and bridges, steeling their resolve. Deprived of strategy and without thought of retreat, they are like sheep driven forward.”
Du Mu said: “The army knows only the commands to advance or withdraw, not the purpose of the attack.”
Mei Yaochen said: “They follow obediently, unaware of anything else.”
He Shi said: “The soldiers’ movements depend solely on the general’s orders, like sheep following the shepherd.”
Zhang Yu said: “Sheep come and go at the shepherd’s will; the army advances and retreats at the general’s command.”

Gather the army’s forces and cast them into peril—this is the general’s duty.
Cao Cao said: “Peril means difficulty.”
Mei Yaochen said: “Placing the army in danger to secure victory is the general’s essential task.”
Zhang Yu said: “Removing the ladder and springing the mechanism, placing troops in peril to achieve victory—this is the general’s responsibility.”

The variations of the nine terrains, the advantages of contraction and expansion, and the principles of human nature must be thoroughly examined.
Cao Cao said: “Human nature advances when it sees advantage and retreats when it sees harm.”
Du Mu said: “The benefits and harms of contraction and expansion, the constant principles of human nature—all change according to the nine terrains. Here, the text reintroduces the nine terrains to set the stage for what follows.”
Mei Yaochen said: “The variations of the nine terrains offer opportunities for contraction or expansion; the constant principles of human nature must be carefully observed.”
Wang Xi said: “One must fully understand the advantages and disadvantages of the nine terrains and their transformations. Contraction occurs when no opportunity is seen; expansion follows when an advantage appears. The principles of human nature refer to deep commitment, shallow dispersion, and encirclement.”
Zhang Yu said: “The methods of the nine terrains cannot be rigidly applied; one must recognize adaptability. Contract when appropriate, expand when advantageous—discern what is beneficial. This is the constant principle of human nature and must not be overlooked.”

The way of the invader: the deeper the penetration, the greater the unity; the shallower, the more dispersed.
Mei Yaochen said: “Deep penetration fosters solid unity; shallow entry leads to dispersion.”
From this point onward, the text revisits the nine terrains, for Sunzi emphasizes their variations repeatedly.
Zhang Yu said: “The first to deploy is the invader. Deep penetration ensures unity; shallow entry causes the troops to scatter.”
Below, the variations of the nine terrains are discussed.

An army that leaves its own state and crosses borders operates in *jue* terrain.
Mei Yaochen said: “Neither light nor dispersed, it lies between the two.”
Wang Xi said: “This refers to crossing the borders of neighboring states, entering isolated terrain where swift resolution is essential—like the King of Wu’s campaign against Qi. Such cases are rare and thus not categorized among the nine terrains.”
Zhang Yu said: “An army that departs its own state and invades another’s territory operates in perilous, isolated terrain—like the Qin army passing through Zhou to strike Zheng. This is mentioned outside the nine terrains because such scenarios occurred during the Warring States period.”

Where four roads converge lies *qu* terrain.
Mei Yaochen said: “Highways radiate in all directions; the enemy faces one front.”
Zhang Yu said: “The enemy holds one front while neighboring states surround the rest.”

Deep penetration leads to *zhong* terrain.
Mei Yaochen said: “The soldiers treat the army as home, so their resolve does not waver.”

Shallow penetration leads to *light* terrain.
Mei Yaochen said: “The return to one’s state is still near, so the heart cannot focus.”

Where one’s back is secure and the front is narrow lies *encircled* terrain.
Mei Yaochen said: “The rear abuts perilous defenses while the front faces a constricted pass.”
Zhang Yu said: “With narrow front and treacherous rear, advance and retreat are controlled by the enemy.”

Where there is nowhere to go lies *dead* terrain.
Mei Yaochen said: “Exhausted with nowhere to turn.”
Zhang Yu said: “Surrounded on all sides with nowhere to turn.”

Thus, in *dispersive* terrain, I will unify their resolve.
Li Quan said: “Unify the hearts of the soldiers.”
Du Mu said: “In defense, resolve is unified; in battle, it easily scatters.”
Mei Yaochen said: “Guard the city and prepare the defenses, unifying resolve for firm defense; await their laxity, then sally forth to strike.”
Zhang Yu said: “Gather the people and store grain, unifying resolve for steadfast defense; rely on perilous terrain to set ambushes and attack the enemy unawares.”

In *light* terrain, I will ensure their cohesion.
Cao Cao and Li Quan said: “Ensure they remain connected.”
Du Mu said: “Formations and encampments should be closely linked, for in light terrain—first, to prevent escape, and second, to ensure mutual support should the enemy arrive.”
Du You said: “*Shi* means to remain connected. When withdrawing through light terrain, one must secure the roads and hasten the march, ensuring continuous cohesion to guard against the unforeseen.”
Mei Yaochen said: “On the march, units follow in succession; at rest, encampments remain linked, so that if the enemy arrives, the troops will not scatter.”
Wang Xi said: “If cut off, men cannot rely on one another.”
Zhang Yu said: “Form dense encampments and tight formations, ensuring continuous cohesion to guard against the unforeseen and prevent flight.”

In *contested* terrain, I will hasten to their rear.
Cao Cao said: “When advantageous terrain lies ahead, swiftly advance to their rear.”
Li Quan said: “Advantageous terrain must be contested; reinforce its defenses.” (Li Quan takes *qu* as a superfluous character.)
Du Mu said: “In terrain that must be contested, if I arrive later, I should swiftly advance to seize it—how much more so if I am not late?”
Chen Hao said: “Both explanations are incorrect. If the enemy holds the advantage, and I arrive later to contest it, would this not mean arriving late to the battlefield and hastening to battle in exhaustion? The true meaning of ‘hasten to their rear’ in contested terrain is this: if the advantage lies ahead, first dispatch elite troops to secure it; if the enemy relies on numbers to contest it, I will advance my main force to their rear—there is nothing that cannot be overcome, as when Zhao She defeated the Qin army.”
Du You said: “When advantageous terrain lies ahead, advance to their rear. In contested terrain, the first to secure it prevails; the one who fails to do so is defeated. Thus, follow their rear to ensure mutual support.”
Mei Yaochen said: “If the enemy has not yet reached the terrain and I am behind, I must swiftly advance to contest it.”
Zhang Yu said: “Contested terrain values speed; if the vanguard arrives but the rear does not, it will not suffice. Thus, one must swiftly advance to their rear, ensuring the head and tail arrive together. Alternatively, ‘hasten to their rear’ may mean setting out later but arriving first.”

In *intersecting* terrain, I will carefully guard.
Du Mu said: “Fortify the ramparts.”
Mei Yaochen said: “Carefully guard the ramparts and sever their lines of communication.”
Wang Xi said: “Fear that they will ambush me.”
Zhang Yu said: “One should not block their paths but instead fortify the defenses and await their arrival, then set ambushes to strike.”

In *hub* terrain, I will solidify our alliances.
Du Mu said: “Form alliances with the feudal lords, making them firm.”
Mei Yaochen said: “Solidify alliances with the feudal lords, ensuring they remain steadfast so the enemy does not gain them first.”
Wang Xi said: “Strengthen them with virtue, propriety, awe, and trust, while demonstrating the advantages and disadvantages of the situation.”
Zhang Yu said: “Use wealth and goods to benefit them, and oaths to bind them, ensuring their firm and unwavering support so they will surely aid me.”

In *deep* terrain, I will ensure the continuity of provisions.
Cao Cao said: “Plunder their supplies.”
Li Quan said: “Quarter troops and procure grain from the enemy.” (*Ji* is also written as *lüe*.)
Jia Lin said: “Ensure provisions follow in unbroken succession.”
Du You said: “When deeply penetrating enemy territory, one must ensure the continuity of provisions.”
Mei Yaochen said: “With the roads distant and severed, one cannot return to the state for grain, so one must plunder the enemy to feed the army.”
Zhang Yu said: “When the army is in deep terrain, supply lines are cut off, and provisions must not run short; one must plunder the enemy to sustain the army.”

In *impassable* terrain, I will advance swiftly.
Cao Cao said: “Pass through quickly.”
Li Quan said: “One cannot linger.”
Du You said: “March swiftly without halting in such terrain.”
Mei Yaochen said: “With nothing to rely on, one must pass through quickly.”
Zhang Yu said: “When encountering impassable terrain, one should lead the troops through swiftly.”

In *encircled* terrain, I will seal the gaps.
Cao Cao and Li Quan said: “Unify the hearts of the soldiers.”
Du Mu said: “In military strategy, when encircling an army, one must leave a gap to show them a path to life, preventing them from resolving to die, and then strike. But if I am in encircled terrain and the enemy opens a path to life to lure my troops, I will instead seal it myself, instilling in the soldiers a resolve to fight to the death. In the late years of the Northern Wei, Gao Huan raised righteous troops in Hebei. Four generals—Erzhu Zhao, Erzhu Tianguang, Erzhu Dulü, and Erzhu Zhongyuan—converged south of Ye with elite cavalry and infantry, numbering two hundred thousand, and encircled Gao Huan at Nanling Mountain. At the time, Gao Huan had two thousand cavalry and fewer than thirty thousand infantry. The Erzhu generals left their encirclement unclosed, but Gao Huan tied together oxen and donkeys to seal the gaps. Thus, his officers and soldiers fought desperately, charging on all sides, and decisively defeated the four Erzhu generals.”
Meng Shi said: “Intending to break out of the encirclement, one demonstrates firm resolve in defense.”
Du You said: “Sealing the gaps expresses the intent not to flee.”
Mei Yaochen said: “Seal the side paths oneself, ensuring the soldiers fight to the death.”
Wang Xi said: “Fear that the men will harbor thoughts of escape.”
Zhang Yu said: “When I am encircled by the enemy and they leave a path to life, I must seal it myself to unify the hearts of the soldiers.”

Qi’s Divine Marshal tied oxen and horses to block the roads, ensuring his soldiers fought to the death—this is the principle. In a desperate situation, I show them no hope of survival.

Cao Cao and Li Quan said: “To steel their resolve.”
Du Mu said: “Show them they must die, so they will rouse themselves to seek life.”
Jia Lin said: “Forbid valuables, abandon grain, block wells, and smash hearths—this demonstrates certain death.”
Du You said: “To inspire the troops.”
Burn the baggage, discard provisions, block wells, and level hearths—this shows no intent to live, ensuring they fight to the death.
Mei Yaochen said: “Only when death is certain can life be found; men will then exert all their strength.”
Wang Zhe echoed Mei Yaochen’s note. He Shi echoed Du Mu’s note.
Zhang Yu said: “Burn the baggage, discard provisions, block wells, and level hearths—this shows no hope of survival, inspiring them to fight to the death.”

Thus, the nature of soldiers is this: when encircled, they resist;
Cao Cao said: “They resist and hold firm.”
Li Quan said: “When the enemy encircles us, we resist them.”
Du Mu said: “When troops are in a besieged position, each man harbors the resolve to resist the enemy and hold victory—this is mutual resistance.”
Mei Yaochen echoed Du Mu’s note.
Zhang Yu said: “When encircled, they naturally hold and resist.”

When desperate, they unite in defense.
When they have no choice, they fight;
Cao Cao said: “Circumstances leave them no choice.”
Li Quan said: “When there is no choice, they fight.”
Mei Yaochen said: “When there is nowhere to turn, they must fight.”
Wang Xi said: “To escape death and hardship, they can only fight.”
Zhang Yu said: “When circumstances allow no alternative, they must fight with all their strength.”

When pressed beyond measure, they submit.
Cao Cao said: “When they are deeply trapped, they follow orders.”
Li Quan said: “When pressed beyond measure, they carefully observe.”
He also said: “When deeply pressed, they follow plans.”
Meng Shi said: “When deeply pressed, there is nothing they will not follow.”
Mei Yaochen echoed Meng Shi’s note.
Zhang Yu said: “When deeply mired in peril, they will follow any plan.”

As when Ban Chao, in Shanshan, wished to kill the barbarian envoys with his few dozen men, he earnestly exhorted them. His soldiers said: “Now, in this desperate situation, life and death follow the Marshal’s command.” This is the principle.

Thus, one who does not know the schemes of the feudal lords cannot form alliances in advance;
One who does not know the forms of mountains, forests, defiles, and marshes cannot march armies;
One who does not employ local guides cannot gain the advantages of terrain.
Cao Cao said: “These three matters were already stated above. They are repeated here because those who are incapable of using troops must be reminded again.”
Li Quan said: “These three are the essentials of warfare.”
Mei Yaochen said: “This was already explained in the *Military Disputes* chapter. Repeating these three matters emphasizes that one must know the enemy’s circumstances and the advantages and disadvantages of terrain in advance.”
Wang Xi said: “They are restated as a warning.”
Zhang Yu said: “Only by knowing these three can one assess the advantages and disadvantages of the nine terrains, hence their repetition here.”

If one is ignorant of even one of these four or five, they are not the troops of a hegemon-king.
Cao Cao said: “This refers to the advantages and disadvantages of the nine terrains.” Or he said: “The four or five matters above.”
Zhang Yu said: “The four or five refer to the advantages and disadvantages of the nine terrains. If even one is unknown, complete victory cannot be achieved.”

Now, the troops of a hegemon-king, when attacking a great state, prevent its forces from assembling;
Their might imposed upon the enemy prevents its alliances from uniting.
Li Quan said: “When troops are united and might is displayed, the feudal lords look to themselves and dare not form alliances.”
Du Mu said: “With power to spare, one can disperse the enemy.”
Meng Shi said: “Using virtue to restrain others, who would dare resist?”
Chen Hao said: “Even with the power of a hegemon-king, when attacking a great state, one’s forces cannot assemble—what matters is forming external alliances. Without this, merely imposing might upon the enemy and flaunting one’s strength will lead to defeat.”
Mei Yaochen said: “When attacking a great state, if one can divide its forces, then power is abundant. With power abundant, might is imposed upon the enemy; with might imposed, neighboring states fear; with neighboring states in fear, the enemy’s alliances cannot unite.”
Wang Zhe said: “If one knows the enemy’s schemes, gains the advantages of terrain, and shapes circumstances so they cannot aid or rely on one another, then even a great state—how could it assemble its forces to resist? The greater the might imposed, the less the enemy’s alliances can unite.”
Zhang Yu said: “Relying on wealth and strength to repeatedly attack great states, one’s own people will grow resentful and unable to assemble. When the might of one’s arms is twice that of the enemy state, the feudal lords will fear and dare not form alliances with it.” Or he said: “When invading a great state, if the great state suffers a defeat, smaller states will scatter and not assemble. As when Jin and Chu contended over Zheng: if Jin prevailed, Zheng submitted; if Jin faltered, Zheng rebelled. With smaller states scattered, the enemy’s power is divided and weakened. Or if one’s military might can prevail over them, then how would the feudal lords dare ally with the enemy?”

Thus, one does not contend for the alliances of all under Heaven, nor cultivate the power of all under Heaven. Extending one’s private will and imposing might upon the enemy, one can seize their cities and overthrow their states.
Cao Cao said: “A hegemon does not form alliances with the power of all the feudal lords under Heaven. Cutting off their alliances and seizing their power, one’s might can thus be extended for private ends.”
Li Quan said: “Only by cutting off the alliances of all under Heaven can one extend one’s private will—might without external alliances.”
Du Mu said: “*Xin* means to extend. One does not form neighborly aid nor cultivate plans of strategic power, but merely imposes military might upon the enemy state, valuing the extension of one’s private desires. Thus, their cities can be seized, their states overthrown.”
When Duke Huan of Qi asked Guan Zhong: “Must one first exhaust arms and armor, cultivate civil virtue, rectify borders, and befriend the four neighbors—then it will suffice?”

Thus, he restored the lands of Lu, Wei, and Yan that had been seized, and through amicable relations, the four neighbors grew deeply allied. Then he launched a southern campaign against Chu, a northern expedition against the Mountain Rong, controlled Lingzhi in the east, broke the Gubuzhu, and subdued the Flowing Sands in the west. He convened six assemblies of chariot armies and three assemblies of war chariots, then led the feudal lords to pay court to the Son of Heaven.

When Wu Fuchai crushed Yue at Kuaiji, defeated Qi at Ailing, dug canals through Shang and Lu, and met Jin at Huangchi, he contended for supremacy before returning. His might pressed upon the feudal lords, who dared not oppose him. Yet Goujian of Yue attacked him. Seeking aid from Qi and Chu, neither responded. His people exhausted and his troops worn, he was destroyed by Yue.

King Goujian of Yue asked Shen Baoxu about warfare: “To the south of Yue lies Chu, to the west Jin, and to the north Qi. In spring and autumn, we offer skins, silk, jade, and maidens to serve them, never daring to sever ties. I seek to avenge Wu—may I do so through battle?” Baoxu replied: “Excellent! Nothing could surpass this.” Thereupon, he attacked Wu and annihilated it.

Jia Lin said: “When the feudal lords are already fearful, they cannot form attachments or dare to unite in alliance. With our wisdom, strategies, and might in surplus, the feudal lords submit of their own accord—what need is there to cultivate alliances?” (Some versions read: *not serve*.)

Chen Hao said: “With wisdom and strength intact, authority and power in our grasp, we need only nurture our soldiers and devise invincible strategies. The feudal lords possess no power worth serving. Benevolence, wisdom, righteousness, and strategy are our private endowments, used to aid the multitudes. Thus it is said: extending private might shakes the world, virtue illuminates the four seas, grace moistens all beings, and trust reaches even swine and fish. The people’s hearts turn toward us, and none think of disobedience. Therefore, attacking cities ensures their capture, and striking states ensures their ruin.”

Mei Yaochen said: “When the enemy cannot unite with the feudal lords in alliance, we too need not contend for their alliances, nor nurture their power—we rely solely on our own strength. Our might thus surpasses the enemy’s, so their cities can be seized and their states overthrown. This is the art of hegemonic and kingly warfare.”

Wang Xi said: “By forming alliances and nurturing power, one can unite all under Heaven; by pursuing private ends and diminishing might, cities and states cannot be preserved.”

Zhang Yu said: “Without contending for alliances, one’s position becomes isolated and aid dwindles; without nurturing power, people scatter and the state weakens. Extending one’s private grudges and displaying military might against enemy states will ultimately lead to defeat. Alternatively: when enemy states cannot assemble their multitudes nor unite their alliances, we should sever their ties, seize their power, extend our desires, and double our might over theirs. Thus, their cities can be captured and their states overthrown.”

Enact rewards without law, suspend orders without governance.
Jia Lin said: “When seizing cities and overthrowing states, one suspends rewards and punishments beyond the state’s norms, issuing commands beyond regular governance—hence, *without law, without governance*.”

Mei Yaochen said: “Rewarding merit is not predetermined by law; swearing oaths before the enemy is not preordained by policy.”

Wang Xi said: “This deters treachery and overreach.”

Cao Cao said: “Military laws and orders are not preemptively suspended.”

The *Methods of the Sima* states: “Facing the enemy, swear oaths and reward merit.” This is what is meant.

Zhang Yu said: “Laws are not preemptively enforced, policies not preannounced—all are established in the moment to inspire the soldiers’ hearts. The *Methods of the Sima* states: ‘Facing the enemy, swear oaths and reward merit.’”

Command the masses of the Three Armies as if directing one man.
Cao Cao said: “*Fan* means to employ. With clear rewards and punishments, even when commanding many, it is as if directing one.”

Li Quan said: “One skilled in warfare enacts laws for attack without others knowing; suspends orders without decrees, yet people follow. Thus, commanding the masses is like commanding one.”

Mei Yaochen said: “*Fan* means to employ. With strict and clear rewards for transgressions, employing many is like employing few.”

Zhang Yu said: “Rewarding merit without delay, punishing crimes without deferral—with rewards and punishments both clear and swift, commanding many is like commanding few.”

Employ them in action, do not inform them with words.
Mei Yaochen said: “Use them for battle, do not reveal strategies to them.”

Wang Xi said: “If intentions leak, plans falter.”

Zhang Yu said: “Deploy them in combat, do not instruct them in stratagems—if people know the plans, they will doubt.” As when Pei Xingjian did not disclose to his soldiers the reason for relocating the camp.

Employ them with profit, do not inform them of harm.
Cao Cao said: “Do not let them know of harm.”

Li Quan said: “*Fan* means to employ. If soldiers know the words and the harm, they will grow suspicious and hesitant.”

Mei Yaochen said: “Issue commands that reveal profit, not harm.”

Wang Xi said: “To prevent doubt and fear.”

Zhang Yu said: “Human nature advances when seeing profit and avoids when knowing harm—thus, do not inform them of harm.”

Cast them into desperate terrain, then they will survive; plunge them into deadly ground, then they will live.
Cao Cao said: “They must fight to the death—those in desperate terrain do not fail.”

Sun Bin said: “Soldiers fear not being cast into deadly ground.”

Li Quan said: “Troops in deadly ground will fight resolutely to survive.” As when Han Xin’s army was stationed by the river.

Mei Yaochen said: “Though the terrain is called desperate, striving in battle averts desperation; though the ground is called deadly, fighting to the death averts death. Thus, desperation is the foundation of survival, and death the root of life.”

He Yan said: “As when the King of Han sent General Han Xin to strike Zhao. Thirty *li* from the Jingxing Pass, he halted and encamped. At midnight, he issued orders, selecting two thousand light cavalry, each carrying a red banner, to observe the Zhao army from a hidden path through the grassy mountains. He instructed: ‘When Zhao sees us retreat, they will empty their camp to pursue us. You must swiftly enter the Zhao camp, tear down their banners, and raise the Han flags.’ He ordered his deputy to proclaim: ‘Today we will crush Zhao and feast together.’”

Han Xin then dispatched ten thousand men to advance, arraying them with their backs to the river. The Zhao forces saw this from afar and laughed heartily. At dawn, Han Xin raised the great general’s banner and drums, marching out through the Jingxing Pass. Zhao opened their camp and struck, engaging in fierce battle for a long while. Thereupon, Han Xin feigned retreat toward his troops by the water. Zhao abandoned their camp to pursue him, but once Han Xin reached his water-bound formation, his men fought with desperate resolve, unable to be defeated. Meanwhile, the two thousand cavalry Han Xin had sent earlier galloped into the Zhao camp, tearing down all Zhao banners and raising the red flags of Han. The Zhao forces, failing to capture Han Xin and returning to their camp, saw the Han banners and were struck with terror. Their ranks fell into chaos, and they fled in disarray. The Han forces then launched a pincer attack, crushing the Zhao army of Chen Yu and Lu. Chen Yu was slain by the river, and the King of Zhao was captured.

The generals then questioned Han Xin: “The *Art of War* states: ‘To the right, mountains; to the front and left, water and marsh.’ Yet today, General, you ordered us to array with our backs to the water. You said, ‘We will crush Zhao and feast together.’ We did not understand, yet in the end, we prevailed. What strategy was this?”

Han Xin replied: “This is indeed in the *Art of War*—it is only that you did not perceive it. Does the *Art of War* not say: ‘Ensnare them in deadly ground, and they will live; place them in desperate ground, and they will survive’? Moreover, I had not the time to train and nurture these officers and men. This was what is called ‘driving market rabble into battle.’ In such a situation, unless they are placed in deadly ground where each man fights for his own life, how could I have used them?
