Had I given them ground for survival, they would have fled. Could I have relied on them then?”

The generals all bowed in admiration, saying: “This is beyond our understanding.”

When Liang General Chen Qingzhi defended the city of Woyang against the Later Wei army, the two sides remained locked in stalemate from spring to winter, engaging in dozens—even hundreds—of battles. The Wei reinforcements then began constructing fortifications behind his army. His deputy generals, fearing an attack from both front and rear, proposed a retreat.

Chen Qingzhi said: “We came here together and have endured a full year. The cost in provisions and arms has been immense. Now, none of the troops have the will to fight; all plot retreat. Is this how one seeks glory? No—it is mere banditry. I have heard that placing troops in deadly ground is the only way to seek survival. We must wait for a great confrontation, and only then will victory be certain.”

His generals, inspired by his resolve, followed his command. The Wei forces had established thirteen encampments in a flanking formation. Chen Qingzhi led his men in a night march, muffling their movements, and captured four of the Wei forts. The remaining nine still bristled with soldiers and arms. He then displayed the severed heads of the enemy, raised a battle cry, and attacked. The Wei forces collapsed in rout, and nearly all were slain or captured.

In the late years of the Later Wei, Gao Huan of Qi raised righteous troops in Hebei. At the time, the four generals of Erzhu Zhao and their forces, numbering two hundred thousand, encamped on either side of the Wen River. Gao Huan’s own troops numbered fewer than thirty thousand. Outnumbered, he arrayed his men in a circular formation at Hanling Mountain, blocking the roads with oxen and donkeys. Thus, his officers and men fought to the death, striking from all sides, and crushed the enemy.

When Gao Huan of Qi was outnumbered tenfold by the forces of Tian Guang and others, they surrounded him but left a gap. Gao Huan then sealed the gap himself, ensuring his men were resolved to die. Thus, he defeated the enemy.

When Sima Xiaonan, the Northern Prefect of Qi’s Northern Yu Province, defected to the Later Zhou, Zhou General Yang Zhong and Grand Marshal Daxi Wu were sent to reinforce him. Together, they led five thousand cavalry, each man riding a single horse, and galloped five hundred *li* into Qi territory via a hidden route. They sent three messengers ahead to inform Xiaonan, but none returned. When they were thirty *li* from Yu Province, Wu grew suspicious and wished to turn back.

Yang Zhong said: “There is only death in advancing; no life in retreat.”

With a thousand cavalry, he raced through the night to the city walls, where sheer cliffs surrounded them on all sides, and only the sound of watchmen’s clappers could be heard. Wu arrived later with several hundred cavalry from the west, while Zhong held the remaining troops in place, waiting for the gates to open before charging in. He then dispatched riders to summon Wu.

At the time, the Qi garrison commander, Fu Jingyuan, had arrayed two thousand armored soldiers at the eastern camp, raising signal beacons and maintaining strict vigilance. Wu, daunted, did not wish to hold the city. Instead, he took much wealth and treasure, escorting Xiaonan and his followers back first. Zhong remained with three thousand cavalry as the rear guard. When they reached the south of Luo, they all dismounted and rested. The Qi forces pursued them to the north of Luo.

Zhong addressed his officers and men: “Eat your fill. Now we are in deadly ground, and the enemy will not dare cross the water to face our charge.”

After they had eaten, the Qi forces feigned an attempt to cross the river. Zhong led a swift charge against them, and the Qi troops dared not press forward, eventually withdrawing in an orderly retreat.

Zhang Yu said: “Placing men in deadly ground forces each to fight for himself, and thus they may live. When Xiang Yu rescued Zhao, he smashed his cauldrons and burned his huts, showing his men there was no retreat. The feudal lords watched from their ramparts as the Chu warriors fought with the strength of ten men each, and thus they captured the Qin general. Only when the masses are plunged into peril can victory or defeat be decided.”

Mei Yaochen said: “If they are not placed in desperate ground, the soldiers’ hearts will not be single-minded. Only when they are plunged into peril does victory or defeat rest in human effort.”

Zhang Yu said: “When soldiers are resolved to obey commands, victory or defeat lies in our own hands. Thus, the art of war lies in discerning the enemy’s intent.”

Cao Cao said: “‘Feigning’ means to deceive. If the enemy wishes to advance, set an ambush and retreat; if they wish to withdraw, leave an opening and strike.”

Li Quan said: “If the enemy wishes to attack, I await them with defense; if they wish to engage, I await them with the unexpected. Retreating, ambushing, or enticing with advantage—all follow their desires.”

Du Mu said: “To follow the enemy’s intent means that when I wish to strike but see no opening, I conceal my form and hide my traces, allowing the enemy to act without alarm. If they press hard against me, I feign weakness and lie in wait, submitting to their strength to inflate their arrogance, then strike when they grow careless. If they wish to retreat, I open a path for their withdrawal, letting them depart without fight, then attack as they leave. All these are ways of following the enemy’s intent.”

Chen Hao said: “Following the enemy’s intent requires no elaborate explanation—merely show strength as weakness, advance as retreat, so the enemy grows careless, and then strike to break them.”

Mei Yaochen said: “Feigning fear, feigning weakness, feigning disorder, feigning flight—when the enemy comes lightly, my will prevails.”

Zhang Yu said: “If they wish to advance, entice them to advance; if they wish to retreat, ease their retreat, complying with their intent while setting ambushes to seize them. Some say: when the enemy has desires, comply with their wishes to make them arrogant, preserving them for later advantage. As when the Eastern Hu sent envoys to Modu, saying, ‘We desire the thousand-mile horse of Touman.’ Modu gave it. Again they sent envoys, saying, ‘We wish for the chanyu’s lady.’ Modu gave her as well. When they grew arrogant and careless, he struck and destroyed the Eastern Hu.”

“Unite against the enemy, and though a thousand *li* away, you may slay their general.”

Cao Cao said: “Uniting forces against the enemy, even a thousand *li* distant, you can capture their general.”

Du Mu said: “The above speaks of the art of war lying in following the enemy’s intent—this refers to when no opening is yet seen. If an opening appears and an opportunity to attack arises, then concentrate forces and direct them against the enemy; though a thousand *li* away, you may slay their general.”

Jia Lin said: “If you can entice the enemy with advantage, making them rush toward it, then even a thousand *li* distant, you may capture and kill their general.”

Mei Yaochen said: “Follow the enemy’s direction, then unleash ambushes and surprises to seize their general from afar.”

Wang Xi said: “Follow the enemy’s intent and adapt to their movements; when they are unprepared and vulnerable, unite your forces and strike with full strength. Seizing the momentum, you may overturn their army and slay their general a thousand *li* away.”

Zhang Yu said: “Once the enemy grows arrogant and indolent, concentrate your forces to strike them, overturning their army and killing their general—as Modu destroyed the Eastern Hu. This is called skillfully accomplishing the task.”

Cao Cao said: “This is the skill of accomplishing the task.” (Alternatively: “This is called skillfully attacking to accomplish the task.”)

Mei Yaochen said: “To follow the enemy and secure victory is the work of cunning.”

He Shi said: “One who can do this accomplishes the task through skillful attack.”

Zhang Yu said: “First following their intent, then slaying their general—this is the skill of accomplishing the task.”

“Thus, on the day the campaign is launched, close the passes, break the tallies, and allow no communication with their envoys.”

Cao Cao said: “Once the plan is set, close the passes to sever their tallies and tokens, and do not receive their envoys.”

Li Quan said: “Once the orders are issued, close the passes and break the tallies, allowing no dissent to disturb the soldiers’ resolve.”

Du Mu said: “What is meant by ‘not allowing communication’—surely not the enemy’s envoys? If we refuse their envoys, why must we close the passes and break the tallies to prevent communication? I answer: closing the passes and breaking the tallies prevents our people from entering or leaving, lest enemy spies slip in unseen, hiding their forms and traces, traversing perilous paths, or stealing tallies and seals, impersonating others to spy on us. ‘Allow no communication with their envoys’ means that if the enemy sends envoys, we should not receive them, for they may include wise and capable men like Zhang Mengtan or Lou Jing, who could discern our weaknesses from subtle signs and gauge our strength. This is when our military posture is not yet established, and we fear the enemy may act first to control us. Once our posture is set and we cross the border, then envoys may move between sides—this is the ancient way.”

Mei Yaochen said: “‘Yi’ means to erase; ‘zhe’ means to break. On the day the campaign is launched, erase and block the passes and bridges, break and destroy the tallies, allowing no communication. ‘Allow no communication’ means fearing the leakage of our plans.”

Zhang Yu said: “Once the temple calculations are complete and the military plans are set, block the passes and bridges, break the tallies and tokens, and allow no communication of orders, lest our plans be revealed. If the enemy sends envoys, they should be received—hence the following text says: ‘When the enemy opens or closes, swiftly enter.’”

“Sharpen your plans in the temple halls to settle the matter.”

Cao Cao said: “‘Zhu’ means to manage. Sharpen the plans in the temple halls to settle the matter beforehand.”

Du Mu said: “‘Li’ means to whet. In the temple halls, refine and settle the plans, determining success or failure before raising the army.” (Alternatively: “to devise the plans.”)

Mei Yaochen said: “Prepare rigorously in the temple halls to devise the plans—this speaks of secrecy.”

He Shi said: “Hone the victorious strategies of the temple to ensure the task is accomplished.”

Zhang Yu said: “War is a grave matter; it must not be lightly discussed. One must be vigilant in the temple halls, secretly managing the plans, valuing secrecy to prevent leaks.”

“When the enemy opens or closes, swiftly enter.”

Cao Cao said: “If the enemy has an opening, swiftly enter it.”

Li Quan said: “If the enemy’s opening or closing is uncertain, act swiftly.”

Meng Shi said: “‘Opening and closing’ refers to gaps. If an opening appears, swiftly enter it.”

Mei Yaochen’s note agrees with Meng Shi.

Zhang Yu said: “‘Opening and closing’ refers to envoys.”

If the enemy sends an agent, swiftly receive them.

Some say: This refers to when the enemy’s movements—opening or closing, advancing or retreating—are unpredictable and disordered; one should swiftly seize the opportunity.

**Strike first at what they cherish.**
Cao Cao said: Seize advantageous positions.
Li Quan said: First attack their supplies and their wives and children, for profit admits no hesitation.
Du Mu said: Whatever the enemy cherishes and relies upon for their campaign, strike there first.
Mei Yaochen said: First discern what they value and find advantageous.
He Shi agrees with Du Mu’s note.

**Secretly set a rendezvous with them.**
Cao Cao said: Let others depart later, but arrive first.
Du Mu said: *Wei* means “concealed.” Set the rendezvous at a place the enemy cherishes or finds advantageous, then secretly hasten to the appointed time to deceive them without their knowledge.
Chen Hao said: If I seize the advantageous ground first but the enemy does not come, what use is the gain? Thus, to take what they cherish, one must first secretly arrange a meeting with the enemy, misleading them so they will surely arrive.
Mei Yaochen said: Reveal the rendezvous subtly, so the agent returns to report; then, though I depart later, I arrive first. Departing later ensures they will come; arriving first allows me to seize what they cherish.
Wang Xi said: *Quan* means “deception.” *Wei* is how one feigns secrecy. Cao [Cao] said: Arrive before the enemy.
Zhang Yu said: What the enemy cherishes is advantageous ground. If I wish to occupy it first, I must subtly reveal my intent and arrange a meeting with them. As they rush toward it, I depart later but arrive first. The reason for letting the enemy move first is the fear that if I arrive and they do not come. Thus it is said: In contending for ground, I will hasten to follow behind.

**Follow the inked line, adapting to the enemy, to decide the battle.**
Cao Cao said: Follow the compass and square, but without fixed form.
Li Quan said: *Mo* means “the path.” If one takes a slow route and follows it, one may not arrive in time.
Du Mu said: *Mo* means “compass and square.” One must always follow the compass and square, strictly adhering to regulations, while adapting to the enemy’s movements. If an exploitable opportunity arises, then sally forth to decide the battle.
Chen Hao said: Though speed is essential in battle to decide the outcome, from beginning to end one must uphold regulations. Even in victory, one must not act recklessly or in disorder. At the Battle of Chengpu, Duke Wen of Jin ascended the ruins of Youxin and surveyed his army, saying: “Young and old observe propriety—this army can be used.” *Jianmo* is also written as *chanmo*.
Jia Lin said: *Chan* means “to remove”; *mo* means “the inked line.” In deciding battle according to the enemy’s plans, only victory is advantageous; one cannot be bound by the inked line.
Mei Yaochen said: Every movement must adhere to regulations, yet one must adapt to the enemy’s shifts, advancing in order as opportunity allows.
Wang Xi said: Follow the military method like an inked line, then one can adapt to the enemy and secure victory.
Zhang Yu said: Uphold regulations, follow the compass and square, adapt to the enemy’s changes, and remain formless—only then can one decide battle and achieve victory. *Mo* is the inked line. As with a woman’s movements—left and right, forward and back, kneeling and rising—all must conform to the compass, square, and inked line.

**Thus, at first be like a maiden—when the enemy opens the door, later be like a fleeing hare—the enemy will be unable to resist.**
Cao Cao and Li Quan said: The maiden appears weak; the fleeing hare moves swiftly.
Du Mu said: At first, the enemy will think me incapable, like a maiden’s weakness. Then I strike suddenly, swift and perilous as a hare’s escape—unassailable.
Some say: I evade the enemy like a fleeing hare.
They say: That is incorrect.

Mei Yaochen said: "At first like a maiden" refers to adhering to the compass and square; "later like a fleeing hare" describes the swiftness of responding to the enemy and deciding battle. Wang Xi said: The maiden follows the enemy; "opening the door" means unpreparedness; the fleeing hare signifies speed. As when Tian Dan defended Jimo and broke the Yan army. Zhang Yu said: In defense, be as weak as a maiden to make the enemy negligent, thus creating an opening; in attack, be as swift as a fleeing hare to exploit the enemy’s haste, leaving them no means to resist. When Sima Qian spoke of Tian Dan defending Jimo and attacking Qi Jie, was this not precisely the case?

*End of Commentary on Sunzi, Scroll Eleven*

[Textual Notes:]
1. Originally written as *jié* (竭), amended to *kě* (渴) according to the Song edition.
2. Originally written as *xī* (息), amended to *píng* (凭) according to the Song edition.
3. Supplemented from Guo Huazhang’s *Eleven Commentaries on Sunzi*: "If the enemy has already seized the ground, it cannot be attacked." Mei Yaochen said: On terrain of strategic advantage, one who first occupies the position gains the benefit; if the enemy has already secured it, do not attack. Zhang Yu said: Do not contend for it when it should not be attacked—rather, arrive later but strike first. King of Wu said: If the enemy arrives first...
4. Originally omitted, *shēng yǐ* (生矣) added according to the Song edition.
5. Originally omitted, *Wǔ yuē bǐ bīng* (武曰彼兵) added according to the Song edition.
6. Originally written as *chū* (初), amended to *mèng* (孟) according to the Song edition.
7. Originally written as *shàng* (上), amended to *bǐ* (匕) according to the Song edition.
8. Originally written as *shè* (设), amended to *suī* (虽) according to the Song edition.
9. Originally written as *gù shùn* (故顺), amended to *jiē xū* (皆须) according to the Song edition.

*Fire Attack, Commentary on Sunzi, Scroll Twelve*

Cao Cao said: When using fire to attack others, one must choose the proper time and day. Wang Xi said: Employ auxiliary forces to secure victory, and guard against reckless deployment. Zhang Yu said: When using fire to attack the enemy, first send spies to secretly reconnoiter—the distances of the terrain, the ease or peril of the routes—so that all is thoroughly known before advancing. Thus it follows the section on *Nine Terrains*.

Sunzi said: There are five methods of fire attack. The first is *fire against personnel*.
Li Quan said: Burn their encampments to kill their soldiers.
Du Mu said: Set fire to their stockades and thereby burn their troops.
Wu Qi said: When an army camps in wild marshes where grasses and trees are dense and rank, it may be destroyed by fire.
When the Former Lord of Shu attacked Wu, Wu’s general Lu Xun resisted him at Yiling. After an initial assault on one camp failed, the generals said: “We are merely wasting our troops.” Lu Xun replied: “I have now grasped the method to break the enemy.” He then ordered each man to carry a bundle of thatch, and with fire they overran the camp. Once the momentum was established, he led all his forces in a simultaneous assault. They slew Zhang Nan, Feng Xi, and the barbarian king Sha Mo Ke, destroyed over forty camps, and killed tens of thousands. The Former Lord fled under cover of night, abandoning nearly all his supplies and equipment, and later coughed up blood and died.
Mei Yaochen said: Burn their stockades and wild thickets to support the attack.
He Shi said: In the time of Duke Huan of Lu, the state of Zhu Lou’s Xianqiu was burned—this was the first recorded use of fire attack. Later military strategists developed the five methods of fire attack to aid in securing victory.
For example, during the Later Han, Ban Chao was sent to the Western Regions and arrived at Shanshan. In the early night, he led his officers and soldiers to raid the barbarian camp. A great wind arose, so Ban Chao ordered ten men to conceal themselves behind the enemy’s quarters with drums, instructing them: “When you see the fire ignite, all must beat the drums and shout loudly.” The rest took up arms and crossbows, lying in ambush on either side of the gate. Ban Chao set fire with the wind, and the drums and war cries erupted from front and rear. The barbarians were thrown into panic and confusion. Ban Chao personally slew three men, and the rest were all burned to death.
Another example: Huangfu Song led troops to suppress Zhang Jiao’s Yellow Turban rebels. Song defended Changshe, but when the rebels besieged the city, his forces were few and the army was terrified. He summoned his officers and said: “Victory in war depends on extraordinary tactics, not numbers. Now the rebels have built their camps amid grasses—easily vulnerable to wind and fire. If we set fire at night, they will surely be thrown into chaos. If we then sally forth to strike, success will be assured.” That evening, a great wind arose. Song gave orders to his troops, stationing them all on the eastern side of the city walls. He sent elite soldiers to secretly exit the siege and set fire to the enemy camp while shouting loudly, and the defenders on the walls raised torches in response. Song then sounded the drums and charged their formation. The rebels, thrown into panic, fled in disarray, and he crushed them.
Another example: During the Qianning era of the Later Liang’s Taizu, he personally led a great army east from Yunzhou, halting north of Yushan. Zhu Xuan, having learned of this, immediately advanced his troops to seek a swift battle. The Emperor arrayed his forces and marched out of the camp. By then, Xuan’s troops had already formed ranks before them. Soon, a strong southeast wind arose, and the Emperor’s army’s banners fell into disarray, with many showing fear. The Emperor immediately ordered his cavalry to crack their whips and shout, and suddenly a northwest wind arose. As both armies were amid grasses and brush, the Emperor ordered the fires set. The flames and smoke stretched across the sky, and he seized the opportunity to attack the rebel formation. Zhu Xuan was utterly routed, and the remnants fled into the Qing River. A *jingguan* was erected at the foot of Yushan.
Another example: During the Later Tang’s campaign against Shu, the Minister of Works, Ren Huan, led a great army to Hanzhou, where Kang Yanxiao came to meet him in battle. Huan ordered Dong Zhang to engage with the eastern Shu’s weak troops at the vanguard, while concealing elite soldiers behind them. When Yanxiao repelled the eastern Shu forces and pursued them in haste, he encountered the ambush. Yanxiao was defeated and fled into Hanzhou, shutting the gates and refusing to emerge. Meng Zhixiang of western Shu led twenty thousand troops to join forces with Huan in attacking the city. Hanzhou’s defenses were reinforced on all sides with bamboo and wooden stockades. In the third month, Huan arrayed his troops at Jinyan Bridge and then led his forces forward with drums and war cries, setting fires on all sides. The wind and flames filled the sky. Yanxiao, in dire straits, led his cavalry out to form ranks at Jinma Bridge, where he was again soundly defeated.
Zhang Yu said: Burn their encampments to converge and slaughter their troops—this is the primary method of fire attack. Ban Chao’s burning of the Xiongnu envoys is an example.

The second is *fire against supplies*.
Li Quan said: Burn their accumulated stores.
Du Mu said: *Supplies* refers to accumulated provisions—grain, fodder, and firewood.
When Gaozu was locked in stalemate with Xiang Yu at Chenggao, he was defeated by Yu and fled north across the river, where he joined Zhang Er and Han Xin’s forces. They fortified Xiuwu with deep moats and high ramparts. He sent Liu Jia with twenty thousand troops and several hundred cavalry across the Boma Ford into Chu territory, where they burned the enemy’s supplies, crippling their operations. The Chu army was left without food.
During Emperor Wen of the Sui, Gao Jiong proposed a strategy to conquer Chen, saying: “The soil of Jiangnan is thin, and its dwellings are mostly thatched and bamboo. Their stored provisions are not buried underground. We may secretly dispatch agents to set fires when the wind rises. After they repair the damage, we may burn them again. Within a few years, their resources and strength will be exhausted.” The Emperor implemented his plan, and the people of Chen grew increasingly impoverished.
Mei Yaochen said: Burn their stored supplies to deplete their fodder and grain.
Zhang Yu said: Burn their accumulated stores to leave them without fodder and grain. Thus it is said: An army without supplies will perish. Liu Jia’s burning of Chu’s supplies is an example.

The third is *fire against baggage trains*, and the fourth is *fire against storehouses*.
Li Quan said: Burn their baggage and incinerate their storehouses.
Du Mu said: *Baggage* refers to weapons, goods, and soldiers’ belongings carried in wagons while on the march and not yet settled. *Storehouses* are structures in cities or camps where such items are kept after halting. The contents of both are the same.
At the end of the Later Han, when Xu You defected from Yuan Shao to Cao Cao, he said: “Yuan Shao’s baggage train now numbers over ten thousand wagons, and his encampment is poorly defended. If you send light troops to raid them unexpectedly, burning their supplies, within three days Yuan Shao will defeat himself.”

Cao Cao rejoiced greatly and selected five thousand elite cavalry. They all bore Yuan Shao’s banners, held bits in their horses’ mouths, and advanced along secret paths, carrying bundles of firewood. When questioned along the way, they replied: “Lord Yuan fears Cao Cao will raid his rear guard, so he has sent reinforcements to bolster defenses.” Those who heard this believed it to be true and remained unalarmed. Upon reaching the encampment, they set great fires, throwing the camp into chaos. Seizing the moment, they crushed the enemy, and all the baggage trains were consumed in flames.

Chen Hao said: If the enemy cherishes certain possessions, these too may be targeted. Should they sally forth to rescue them, the fire will divide their forces. If their minds are further unsettled, their army may be broken and their generals slain.

Mei Yaochen said: Burn their baggage trains to deplete their supplies; burn their storehouses to empty their reserves.

He Shi said: As when Former Qin’s Fu Jian sent General Wang Meng to campaign against Former Yan’s Murong Wei. When the army reached the Lu River, Yan’s general Murong Ping led forty thousand troops to resist, seeking to wear them down through protracted warfare. Wang Meng dispatched General Guo Qing with five thousand infantry and cavalry, who advanced by night along a secret path, setting fire to Jin Mountain and burning Murong Ping’s baggage trains. The flames were visible even in Ye, and thus Former Yan was destroyed.

Zhang Yu said: Burn their baggage trains to cut off their supplies. Thus it is said: “An army without baggage trains will perish.” Cao Cao’s burning of Yuan Shao’s baggage trains is an example. Burn their storehouses to deplete their resources. Thus it is said: “An army without wealth will not attract soldiers.”

The fifth is *fire against formations*.
Li Quan said: Burn their formations and weapons.
Du Mu said: Burn their ranks and strike amid the ensuing chaos.
Mei Yaochen said: Burn their formations to deprive them of arms. *Formations* may also be written as *paths*.
Jia Lin said: *Paths* refer to supply routes. Burn to sever their grain and transport lines.
He Shi concurred with Jia Lin’s annotation.
Zhang Yu said: Burn their formations to leave them without weapons. Thus it is said: “If weapons are not sharp, it is difficult to meet the enemy.”

Fire must have its cause.
Cao Cao said: Rely on traitors.
Li Quan said: Rely on traitors to provide internal support.
Chen Hao said: One must seize the opportunity, not merely depend on traitors.
Jia Lin said: Take advantage of dry winds to set fires.
Zhang Yu said: In all fire attacks, one must exploit dry weather, flammable materials in the camp—such as thatch, bamboo, piled fodder, and stored grain—along with proximity to grasslands, and set fires when the wind rises.

Smoke and fire must be prepared in advance.
Cao Cao said: Smoke and fire refer to the tools for burning.
Li Quan said: Such as firewood, straw, mugwort, reeds, and dung.
Du Mu said: Mugwort, reeds, firewood, straw, grease, and oil must be prepared beforehand. Military methods include fire arrows, fire curtains, fire apricots, fire soldiers, fire beasts, fire birds, fire thieves, and fire crossbows—all of which may be employed.
Mei Yaochen said: Spies must lie in wait for opportunities, and fire must be prepared in advance. As the saying goes: “Only with preparation in all matters can there be no disaster.”
Zhang Yu said: The tools for storing and igniting fire must always be ready, awaiting the opportune moment to strike.

Fire has its proper time, and its ignition has its proper day.
Mei Yaochen said: It must not be set recklessly.
Zhang Yu said: It cannot be done haphazardly; one must await the right time and day.

The *time* refers to the dryness of Heaven.
Cao Cao said: Dryness means drought.
Mei Yaochen said: When it is dry and parched, fire spreads easily.
Zhang Yu said: When the weather is dry, fire burns readily.

The *day* refers to when the moon is in the *Ji*, *Bi*, *Yi*, or *Zhen* constellations. These four lodges are the days when wind arises.
Li Quan said: The *Tianwen Zhi* records that when the moon lodges in these constellations, winds often blow. The *Yu Jing* states: “Regularly observe the moon’s position relative to the sun, counting fifteen from *Yingshi* to *Yi*, where the moon lodges.”
Du Mu said: *Lodges* refer to the moon’s position. These four lodges are the harbingers of wind.
Mei Yaochen said: *Ji* is the Dragon’s Tail; *Bi* is the Eastern Wall; *Yi* and *Zhen* are the Quail’s Tail. *Lodging* means the moon’s position. When the moon passes through these four, wind will surely rise.
Zhang Yu said: These four stars favor wind; when the moon lodges among them, wind arises. One must calculate the moon’s position to know the day it lodges there, then set the fire.

Another method: In spring, the days of *bing* and *ding*; in summer, *wu* and *ji*; in autumn, *ren* and *gui*; in winter, *jia* and *yi*—on these days, fierce winds and heavy rains often occur. Another method for divining wind: Take a chicken feather weighing eight taels, hang it on a fifty-foot pole, and observe the direction from which the wind blows. The four lodges are *Ji*, *Bi*, *Yi*, and *Zhen*.

In all fire attacks, one must respond to the changes of the five fires.
Mei Yaochen said: Exploit the changes of fire and respond with troops.
Zhang Yu said: Respond to the fire’s changes with troops. The five fires are *fire against personnel*, *fire against supplies*, *fire against baggage trains*, *fire against storehouses*, and *fire against formations*.

If fire is set within, respond swiftly from without.
Cao Cao said: Respond with troops.
Li Quan said: Exploit the fire’s momentum to strike.
Du Mu said: Fire is used to throw the enemy into confusion, then strike—it is not meant to defeat the enemy with fire alone. Attack as soon as the fire is lit; if you wait until the fire subsides and the enemy regains composure, it will be of no benefit. Thus it is said: “swiftly.”
Du You said: Have agents set fire within the enemy camp, then swiftly advance to attack from without.
Mei Yaochen said: If they are thrown into chaos within, strike with troops from without.
Zhang Yu said: As soon as fire is set within, strike swiftly from without. Attacking both inside and out will easily throw the enemy into disarray.

If fire is set but the enemy remains calm, wait and do not attack.
Du Mu said: If the fire causes no alarm, the enemy is well-prepared; do not attack hastily but wait for developments.

Mei Yaochen said: If they show no alarm or confusion, they are surely prepared.
Wang Xi said: Because they do not change.
He Shi said: If fire is set yet the enemy makes no outcry, they are prepared; if we attack, we may instead suffer harm.
Zhang Yu said: Though fire is lit, if their troops remain unshaken, the enemy is prepared; they are also guarding against developments, so we must not attack. Exhaust the fire’s power—proceed if you can, desist if you cannot.
Cao Cao said: Advance when you see opportunity, withdraw when you know difficulty.
Li Quan said: When fire is lit but the enemy remains orderly, do not attack.
Du Mu said: Wait until the fire has burned itself out; if the enemy becomes disordered, attack; if they remain calm throughout, withdraw your troops.
Du You said: Advance when you see advantage, retreat when you know peril. *Ji* means to exhaust. Exhaust the fire’s power—respond if you can, desist if you cannot, lest the enemy discern your intentions.
Mei Yaochen said: Exhaust the fire’s force; if they change, attack; if they do not change, do not attack.
Wang Xi said: Watch for signs of disorder and exploit them; if they remain orderly to the end, tend to your own forces and conserve your strength.
He Shi said: When Wei’s Man Chong campaigned against Wu, he instructed his generals: “Tonight the wind is fierce, and the enemy will surely come to burn our camp—prepare accordingly.” All the troops were alerted.

At midnight, the enemy indeed came to burn the camp, and Chong ambushed and crushed them—such was the case. Zhang Yu said: Exhaust the fire’s force; if they become disordered, attack; if they remain calm, retreat. Fire may be set externally without waiting for an internal response—strike when the moment is narrow.

Li Quan said: When Cao Cao defeated Yuan Shao at Guandu, he followed Xu You’s plan and burned over ten thousand carts of supplies—this was its meaning. Du Mu said: The preceding text states that the five fires’ changes must be set from within. If the enemy camps in wild marshes or overgrown grass, or in a position where their stockades can be burned, then fire must be set at once, without waiting for an internal response before acting, lest the enemy burn the wild grass themselves and render our fire useless. When Han’s Li Ling campaigned against the Xiongnu and was defeated, he was pursued by the Chanyu to the great marshes. The Xiongnu set fire upwind, but Ling had already burned the reeds to create a firebreak, cutting off the flames’ advance.

Chen Hao said: Set fire at the proper time—this refers to the dryness of the heavens and the lodging of the moon among the Four Stars. Jia Lin said: Fire may be set externally without waiting for an internal response; seize the moment to act, not bound by rigid convention. Mei Yaochen’s note agrees with Du Mu’s.

Zhang Yu said: Fire may also be set externally without needing to wait for action from within; strike when opportunity arises. When the Yellow Turban rebel Zhang Jiao besieged Han General Huangfu Song at Changshe, the rebels camped among the grass. Song sent elite troops to slip beyond the encirclement, set fire, and raise a great shout, while those on the city walls lit torches in response. Song then sounded the drums and charged their ranks; the rebels, thrown into chaos, were routed.

Set fire upwind; do not attack downwind. Cao Cao said: It is not advantageous. Li Quan said: When the Jiangdong rebel Liu Yuanjin attacked Wang Shichong at Yanling during the Sui, he ordered grass piled to the east and set alight with the wind. Suddenly the wind shifted, and Yuanjin’s own camp was consumed, many of his troops perishing.

Du Mu said: If the fire is set to the east, then burn the enemy’s east, and we too shall attack their east. If fire is set to the east but we attack the west, we share the same fate as the enemy. Thus, do not attack downwind—this means following the wind. If one can act in the east, the same applies to other directions. Mei Yaochen said: Attacking against the fire’s force is not advantageous, for the enemy will fight to the death.

Wang Xi said: One may strike their flanks instead. Zhang Yu said: When they are burned, they must retreat; to attack them as they retreat is to force them into a desperate fight—this is not advantageous.

Day winds last long; night winds cease. Cao Cao said: This is the natural order. Li Quan said: They do not endure from beginning to end. Du Mu said: Laozi said: Whirlwinds do not last the morning. Mei Yaochen said: Day winds must cease by night, and night winds by day—this is the natural order. Wang Xi’s note agrees with Mei Yaochen’s.

Zhang Yu said: Winds that rise by day subside by night—this is the natural order. Thus Laozi said: Whirlwinds do not last the morning.

In all warfare, one must know the five fires’ changes and observe their patterns. Du Mu said: One must calculate the stars’ positions and await the day the wind rises before setting fire; it cannot be done haphazardly. Du You said: Having understood the five fires and their five changes, one must further weigh their feasibility by the numbers. Mei Yaochen said: Track the stars’ movements to determine the day the wind will rise, then set fire—but one must also guard against its changes.

Zhang Yu said: One must not only know how to attack with fire but also guard against being attacked by it. By reckoning the Four Stars’ degrees, one knows the day the wind will rise and can prepare defenses accordingly.

Thus, those who assist attack with fire achieve clarity; Mei Yaochen said: Victory is plain and easy. Zhang Yu said: Using fire to aid assault clearly secures victory.

Those who assist attack with water gain strength. Du You said: Water is used to breach, hence its strength. Mei Yaochen said: Its force is mighty. Zhang Yu said: Water can divide the enemy’s forces; when their strength is split, ours grows strong.

Water can cut off, but it cannot seize. Cao Cao said: Fire as an aid brings clear victory. Water as an aid can only cut off the enemy’s supply routes, divide their forces, and cannot seize their stores. Li Quan said: In warfare, one must adhere to strategic principles and employ water and fire as aids—this is how clarity and strength are achieved. Emperor Guangwu’s defeat of Wang Mang and Cao Cao’s capture of Lü Bu both exemplify this principle. Water can divide the enemy’s forces in two, but it is difficult to seize their stores with it.

Du Mu said: Water can cut off the enemy’s supply routes, reinforcements, escape routes, and assaults, but it cannot seize their strategic reserves or stockpiles. Wang Xi said: Strength lies in its sudden, overwhelming force. Zhang Yu said: Water can only isolate the enemy’s forces, preventing front and rear from supporting each other, securing a temporary victory; yet it does not compare to fire, which can burn and seize the enemy’s supplies, bringing about their annihilation. When Han Xin breached the waters to slay Chu’s general Long Ju, it was a temporary victory; when Cao Cao burned Yuan Shao’s supplies, Shao was thereby defeated—this was annihilation. Water is not fire’s equal, hence fire is discussed in detail while water is treated briefly.

In victory and conquest, if one does not cultivate its achievements, misfortune follows—this is called "wasteful delay." Cao Cao said: Like stagnant water, it does not return. Some say: If rewards are not timely, they become mere wasteful delay; rewards for good must not exceed a day. Li Quan said: Rewards must not exceed a day; punishments must not exceed an hour. If merit is established but not rewarded, or crimes committed but not punished, soldiers will grow doubtful, and daily waste will accrue.

Du Mu said: "Cultivate" means to act. In victory and conquest, if one does not promptly reward those of merit, the officers and troops will not give their full effort.

then misfortune and calamity arise, leaving only stagnation, waste, and depletion—nothing is ever accomplished.

Jia Lin said: "Wasteful delay" means begrudging expenditure.

Mei Yaochen said: To ensure that every battle is won and every assault succeeds, one must seize advantage and act upon opportunity, thereby establishing merit. Establishing merit involves employing fire attacks, water attacks, and the like—one cannot merely sit and guard the advantage. To sit and guard the advantage is inauspicious; this is called "wasteful delay."

Wang Xi said: In victory and conquest, if one does not promptly distinguish and reward merit, the people will not be encouraged; without encouragement, resources are squandered and the army grows weary—this is indeed misfortune.

Zhang Yu said: The reason battles and assaults are certain to succeed lies in the aid of water and fire. The reason water and fire can shatter armies and defeat enemies lies in the soldiers’ wholehearted effort. If one does not act upon and reward merit, it is the path to misfortune. When resources are exhausted and the army grows weary without hope of return, this is called "wasteful delay." Thus it is said: The enlightened ruler deliberates, the good general acts.

Du Mu said: Huang Shigong said: Those who seek hegemony control their officers with authority, bind them with trust, and employ them with rewards. If trust wanes, officers grow distant; if rewards are lacking, officers will not be of use.

Jia Lin said: The enlightened ruler deliberates the matter, the good general establishes the merit.

Mei Yaochen said: At first, the ruler initiates the deliberation; in the end, the general establishes the merit.

Zhang Yu said: The ruler should plan the affairs of attack and war, while the general should act upon and establish the merit of victory. Without advantage, do not move.

Li Quan said: Enlightened rulers and worthy generals do not raise arms unless they see advantage.

Du Mu said: First perceive the advantage of raising arms, then raise them.

Mei Yaochen said: Generally, armies are not mobilized unless they benefit the people. Another version reads: Without advantage, do not move. Without gain, do not employ.

Du Mu said: First perceive that the enemy can be overcome, then employ troops.

Jia Lin said: Without gaining advantage, do not employ them.

Without peril, do not engage in battle.

Cao Cao said: Troops are employed only when there is no alternative.

Li Quan said: Only in utmost peril is battle waged.

Mei Yaochen said: Generally, troops are not employed unless in dire emergency, for arms are instruments of ill omen.

Zhang Yu said: Arms are instruments of ill omen; battle is a perilous affair. One must guard against calamity and defeat, acting only when there is no alternative.

The ruler must not raise arms in anger.

Wang Xi said: One must not act merely out of anger, as when the Marquis of Xi attacked Zheng.

Zhang Yu said: To raise arms in anger is seldom not to perish. When the Marquis of Xi exchanged harsh words with the Earl of Zheng and then attacked him, the noble man knew thereby that Xi was doomed to fall.

The general must not provoke battle in resentment.

Wang Xi said: One must not act merely out of resentment, as when Zhao Chuan of Jin did.

Zhang Yu said: To engage in battle out of resentment is seldom not to be defeated. When Yao Xiang, enraged by Fu Jian’s general Huang Mei pressing against his ramparts, arrayed his troops and sallied forth, he was defeated by Huang Mei.

Anger is greater than resentment, hence it is spoken of in relation to the ruler; resentment is lesser than anger, hence it is spoken of in relation to the general. The ruler may raise arms, but the general may only speak of battle.

Act when it accords with advantage; cease when it does not accord with advantage.

Cao Cao said: One must not employ troops out of one’s own joy or anger.

Jia Lin said: When resentment and anger arise within, heedless of safety, it is surely impermissible.

Du You said: When the ruler assembles troops and raises arms, he must base his calculations of victory and defeat on reason, not on his own private anger. When the general deploys troops, he must do so by strategy, not out of personal resentment when engaging in battle.

Mei Yaochen said: Arms are employed for righteousness, not raised in anger; battle is won by advantage, not lost in resentment.

Zhang Yu said: One must not employ troops out of one’s own joy or anger, but must consider where advantage and harm lie.

The *Wei Liaozi* states: Troops are not raised out of indignation; they are raised when victory is foreseen, and cease when victory is not foreseen.

Anger may turn to joy again, resentment may turn to delight again.

Zhang Yu said: What is shown in countenance is called joy; what is felt in the heart is called delight.

A lost state cannot be restored, the dead cannot be brought back to life.

Du Mu said: To lose a state is not to destroy another’s state. It means failing to measure one’s virtue and power, raising arms in anger, and engaging in battle in resentment, so that one’s troops perish of themselves and one’s state falls of itself.

Du You said: When a ruler raises arms in anger to attack another, without prior planning or clear strategy, he will be ruined. When a general, in resentment, hastily engages in battle, the casualties will surely be many. Though anger and resentment may later turn to joy and delight, a lost state cannot be restored, nor can the dead be brought back to life—this is to say, one must be cautious.

Mei Yaochen said: A moment’s anger may be turned back to joy; a moment’s resentment may be turned back to delight. But a lost state and a dead army cannot be undone.

Wang Xi said: When joy and anger are inconstant, authority and trust depart.

Zhang Yu said: When a ruler raises arms in anger, his state will surely perish; when a general engages in battle in resentment, his troops will surely die. Therefore, the enlightened ruler is cautious, the good general is vigilant—this is the way to secure the state and preserve the army.

Du Mu said: *Jing* means to be warned and to take heed.

Mei Yaochen said: The ruler must be prudent, the general must be vigilant.

Zhang Yu said: When the ruler is ever cautious in employing troops, he may secure the state; when the general is ever vigilant against engaging lightly in battle, he may preserve the army.

*Annotations to Sunzi, Scroll Twelve ends*
*(Originally “知,” emended to “终” according to the Song edition.)*
*Annotations to Sunzi, Scroll Thirteen: “Employing Spies”*

Cao Cao and Li Quan said: In warfare, one must employ spies to ascertain the enemy’s true circumstances.

Zhang Yu said: To consistently know the enemy’s situation, nothing surpasses the use of spies. Yet the way of employing spies must be especially subtle and secret, which is why this follows the chapter on fire attacks.

Sunzi said: When an army of a hundred thousand is raised and dispatched a thousand *li*, the expenses borne by the people and the state treasury amount to a thousand gold pieces daily. The realm is thrown into turmoil, and those wearied on the roads, unable to tend to their work, number seven hundred thousand households.

Cao Cao said: In ancient times, eight households formed a neighborhood; when one household sent a soldier, the other seven supported him. Thus, when an army of a hundred thousand is mobilized, seven hundred thousand households cease farming.

Li Quan said: In ancient times, when one household’s soldier was conscripted, the three clans of his neighbors jointly provided for him. Hence, seven hundred thousand households, unable to till the land, sustained a hundred thousand troops.

Du Mu said: In ancient times, one man farmed one *qing* of land. Nine *qing* formed a well-field: the central *qing* was dug for a well and planted with a hut, while eight households lived around it. *Dai* means weariness. Thus, seven hundred thousand households supporting a hundred thousand troops exhausted themselves transporting supplies on the roads.

Mei Yaochen said: The burden of transporting grain and provisions, both public and private, wearies the people on the roads and keeps them from their plows. Cao’s explanation is correct.

Zhang Yu said: According to the well-field system, eight households formed a neighborhood; when one household sent a soldier, the other seven supported him. Thus, raising an army of a hundred thousand meant seven hundred thousand households ceased farming.

Someone asked: In hostile territory, one plunders, yet here it speaks of weariness from transport—why? The answer: It is not only about moving grain but also supplying equipment. Moreover, the principle of plundering the enemy refers to deep incursions into enemy territory, where one must prepare for shortages and thus plunder to sustain provisions—not solely relying on the enemy’s stores. In barren lands where no grain can be found, must one not still supply the troops? To contend for years over a single day’s victory yet begrudge ranks, emoluments, or a hundred gold pieces, remaining ignorant of the enemy’s situation, is the height of inhumanity.

Li Quan said: To begrudge rewards and not employ spies to observe the enemy’s movements is the height of inhumanity.

Du Mu said: It means being unable to use generous rewards to employ spies.

Mei Yaochen said: After years of stalemate, the expenses of seven hundred thousand households are vast; to begrudge even a hundred gold pieces in ranks and emoluments, refusing to use them to employ spies and secure victory, is the utmost inhumanity.

Wang Xi said: To be stingy with wealth and rewards is to refuse to use spies.

Zhang Yu said: After prolonged conflict, the resources of seven hundred thousand households are exhausted; to show no concern for this yet begrudge even small rewards, refusing to use them to employ spies and learn the enemy’s situation, is the height of inhumanity.

Such a one is no leader of men,
Mei Yaochen said: Not one who leads men to success.

no aide to a ruler,
*(One version reads: no humane aide.)*
Mei Yaochen said: Not one who aids the state with humanity.

no master of victory.
Mei Yaochen said: Not one who secures the ruler’s advantage.

Zhang Yu said: Such a one cannot lead men, cannot aid a ruler, and cannot secure victory. The lament in these words is profound.

Thus, enlightened rulers and worthy generals, who act and prevail over others, whose achievements surpass the multitude, do so through foreknowledge.

Li Quan said: By employing spies.

Du Mu said: By knowing the enemy’s situation.

Mei Yaochen said: The ruler does not act recklessly but prevails when he does; the general does not seek success lightly but achieves it beyond others. Why? Because they foreknow the enemy’s situation.

Wang Xi said: To foreknow the enemy’s situation is to secure victory as if by divine means.

He Shi said: The *Zhouli*’s *Officer of Justice* oversees state spies, referring to those who secretly observe foreign lands. Thus, military strategists speak of the four opportunities and two advantages—*affairs-opportunity* and *wisdom-advantage*—both of which depend on skillful use of spies. Only then can the enemy’s movements be known in advance.

When Wei Xiaokuan served as General of the Cavalry and defended Yubi, he excelled in governance and won the people’s hearts. All the spies he sent into Qi worked with utmost dedication; some Qi men, receiving Wei’s gold and goods, secretly sent him letters. Thus, the court always knew Qi’s movements in advance. At the time, there was a commander named Xu Pen, whom Wei trusted as his right-hand man and stationed to guard a fortress. Xu then surrendered the city and defected to Qi. Enraged, Wei sent agents to capture him. Soon after, they returned with his severed head. Such was his mastery of human affairs.

Moreover, when Li Da served as Area Commander of Yizhou, Hongnong, and twenty-one other defensive garrisons, he consistently treated those beyond the borders with great generosity, employing them as spies. Thus, every movement within the enemy camp was known to him in advance. Even when some were exposed and executed, they felt no regret—such was his ability to win hearts.

Zhang Yu remarked: "To know the enemy’s situation beforehand is to act with advantage over others, achieving towering merit that surpasses the multitude. Yet this foreknowledge cannot be sought from ghosts or spirits." Zhang Yu said: "Invisible to sight, inaudible to hearing, it cannot be obtained through prayers or sacrifices." Nor can it be inferred from events. Cao Cao said: "It cannot be sought through prayers or by analogy to other affairs." Li Quan said: "It cannot be taken from ghosts, spirits, or analogies—only spies can know the enemy’s true condition." Du Mu said: "Analogy means resemblance—one cannot seek it by likening it to other matters." Mei Yaochen said: "It cannot be known through divination or by analogy to other events." Zhang Yu said: "It cannot be inferred by likening it to similar affairs."

Nor can it be verified by measurement. Cao Cao said: "It cannot be determined by numerical calculations." Li Quan said: "Measurement refers to quantities—length and breadth, distance and proximity, size and scale can all be verified by measurement, but the truth or falsehood of human affairs cannot be known through such means." Mei Yaochen said: "It cannot be verified by calculations—this speaks to the difficulty of foreknowledge." Zhang Yu said: "It cannot be deduced through measurement or verification."

It must be obtained from people—those who know the enemy’s situation. Cao Cao said: "Through people." Li Quan said: "Through spies." Mei Yaochen said: "The will of ghosts and spirits may be known through divination; the forms and qi of things may be sought by analogy; the principles of heaven and earth may be verified by measurement. But the enemy’s true condition can only be known through spies." Zhang Yu said: "Ghosts, spirits, analogies, and measurements—none can provide foreknowledge. Only through people can the enemy’s situation be known."

Thus, there are five types of spies: *local spies*, *internal spies*, *double agents*, *expendable spies*, and *surviving spies*. Mei Yaochen said: "These are the names of the five types of spies." Zhang Yu said: "*Local spies* should be called *native spies*—as the text below states: 'Native spies can be employed.'"

When all five types are deployed simultaneously, none can discern their methods—this is called the *divine principle*, the treasure of a ruler. Cao Cao said: "Employing all five types of spies at once." Li Quan said: "The five types of spies are used through five different people." Du Mu said: "When all five types are active, the enemy cannot perceive how their secrets are exposed—this is the divine thread of strategy, the ruler’s most precious asset." Mei Yaochen said: "When all five types of spies are deployed against the enemy, none can fathom how they are used—this is the sublime and wondrous *divine principle*, what a ruler must value." Wang Xi said: "When all five types are active, none can predict their workings—this is the great *divine principle* of martial strategy, the ruler’s most treasured asset." Jia Lin said: "*Principle* means reason—when the enemy cannot discern by what means their secrets are known, it is as if through divine reason." Zhang Yu said: "When the five types of spies are used in rotation, none can grasp their logic—this is the wondrous *divine principle*, the ruler’s most precious treasure."

A *local spy* is one employed from among the enemy’s own people. Du Mu said: "Using the enemy’s own countrymen, treating them generously to make them spies." When Zu Ti, Inspector of Yuzhou, garrisoned Yongqiu, he cherished the people and humbled himself before scholars. Even those of distant acquaintance or lowly status were treated with kindness and courtesy. Along the Yellow River, there were fortresses where some had family hostages among the Hu barbarians, and he allowed them to maintain ties with both sides. At times, he would send raiding parties to feign attacks, making clear their lack of allegiance. The fortress lords, moved by his virtue, would secretly report any disloyal schemes among the Hu. Through this, he achieved repeated victories. When Wei Xiaokuan sent Qi men to behead Xu Pen and return, it was of the same principle. Jia Lin said: "Read *local spies* as *native spies*." Du You said: "Using the enemy’s own people to learn their internal and external conditions, then employing them to gather intelligence." Mei Yaochen said: "Using the enemy’s people, enticing them to serve." He Shi said: "As in the Spring and Autumn period, when the Chu army besieged Song for nine months without success and prepared to withdraw, the Chu minister Shen Shushi said: 'Build houses and return to farming—Song will surely submit.' The Chu ruler followed his advice. The Song people, alarmed, sent Hua Yuan to sneak into the Chu camp by night. He climbed onto the bed of Zi Fan, woke him, and said: 'Our lord sent me to report our distress: our city resorts to exchanging children for food and breaking bones for fuel. Yet though we face such hardship, we cannot accept a treaty sworn beneath your walls. Withdraw thirty li, and we will obey your commands.' Zi Fan, fearful, made a pact with him and reported to the Chu ruler, who then withdrew thirty li. Thus, Song and Chu made peace." Zhang Yu said: "Using the enemy’s people to learn their internal and external conditions, then employing them to gather intelligence—Wei Xiaokuan’s use of gold and silk to entice Qi men, who then secretly sent letters, is an example of this."

An *internal spy* is one employed from among the enemy’s officials. Li Quan said: "Using disaffected officials of the enemy—like Wei’s use of Xu You." Du Mu said: "Among the enemy’s officials, there may be worthy men who have lost their positions, those punished for offenses, those favored but greedy for wealth, those trapped in low ranks, those denied proper employment, those seeking to prove their talents amid defeat, or those who vacillate between sides. Such officials can all be secretly contacted, lavished with gifts and gold, and won over."

Seeking to learn the enemy state’s internal conditions, one observes their schemes against us, then sows discord among their ruler and ministers to prevent unity. Du You said: "Among their officials, there are those who have lost their positions—such as the descendants of the executed or the families of the punished. Exploit their grievances and employ them." Mei Yaochen said: "Through their officials and subordinates, one may form ties and utilize them." He Shi said:

For example, when Luo Shang, Governor of Yizhou, dispatched General Kui Bo to attack the Shu rebel Li Xiong at Pi City, the two sides fought with alternating victories. Li Xiong then recruited Pu Tai, a man from Wudu, had him flogged until bloodied, and sent him to deceive Luo Shang, pretending to serve as an internal ally with fire as the signal. Shang believed him. He sent out all his elite troops, ordering Kui Bo and others to follow Pu Tai in attacking Li Xiong. Li Xiong’s general Li Xiang set an ambush along the way. Pu Tai leaned a long ladder against the city wall and lit the fire. When Kui Bo’s forces saw the flames, they scrambled up the ladder, but Pu Tai then hauled up over a hundred of Shang’s soldiers with ropes and executed them all. Li Xiong then unleashed his troops, striking from both inside and outside, and crushed Shang’s army. This was the power of employing an *internal spy*.

Another case: During the Sui Dynasty, Yin Shou, as Governor of Youzhou, faced a rebellion by Gao Baoning. Shou marched to suppress it, and Baoning fled to the northern deserts. After Shou withdrew his main force, he left General Cheng Da’ang to garrison the region. Baoning sent his son, Sengga, with light cavalry to raid the city before retreating, then soon returned with Khitan and Mohe troops to attack. Da’ang fought desperately for months before repelling them. Troubled, Shou heavily bribed Baoning while secretly sending agents to subvert his trusted aides, Zhao Shimu and Wang Wei. Within a month, Shimu led his followers to surrender. Baoning fled again to the Khitan, where he was killed by his subordinate Zhao Xiuluo, and the northern frontier was thus pacified.

Later, when Emperor Taizong of Tang campaigned against Dou Jiande and entered Wugong, he pressed close to Jiande’s camp, inflicting heavy casualties. Ling Jing then advised: "You should lead all your troops across the Yellow River, seize Huaizhou and Heyang, stationing a strong general to hold them. Then, with drums beating and banners flying, cross the Taihang Mountains into Shangdang, creating a clamor to precede your advance. Issue proclamations to secure the region, then march toward Hukou, alarming Putjin, and take the lands of Hedong. This is the best strategy." Its execution would yield three advantages: first, entering undefended territory, ensuring the army’s absolute security; second, expanding territory and gaining troops; and third, lifting the siege of Zheng without effort.

Jiande was about to adopt Ling Jing’s plan when Wang Shichong’s envoy, Changsun Anshi, secretly delivered gold and jade to bribe his generals, disrupting the strategy. The assembled officers all urged, "Ling Jing is but a scholar—how can one discuss warfare with him?" Jiande followed their counsel and, withdrawing, apologized to Jing: "Now the army’s morale is at its peak—this is Heaven aiding me. By engaging in decisive battle, we shall surely achieve great victory. I have heeded the majority’s advice and cannot follow your proposal." Jing persisted in his argument, but Jiande grew enraged and had him beaten and expelled. Thereupon, Jiande led all his forces to press the siege at Wugong. Taizong held his position, blunting their momentum. Jiande was struck by a spear and fled to Niukou Shoal, where Cavalry General Bai Shirang and Yang Wuwei captured him alive.

Earlier, when Wang Jian served as Qin’s general attacking Zhao, Zhao dispatched Li Mu and Sima Shang to resist him. Li Mu repeatedly routed the Qin forces, slaying Qin’s general Huan Yi. Jian, resenting this, lavished gold upon Zhao King’s favored minister Guo Kai and others, instructing them to spread slander: "Li Mu and Sima Shang intend to betray Zhao and ally with Qin, seeking vast rewards from Qin." The Zhao King grew suspicious, replacing them with Zhao Cong and Yan Ju, executing Li Mu and dismissing Sima Shang. Three months later, Jian launched a swift assault on Zhao, crushing its forces, killing Zhao Cong, and capturing King Qian of Zhao along with his general Yan Ju.

Zhang Yu remarked: "Exploit those in disfavor, or the kin of the executed, or any with grievances—entice them with generous rewards." The cases of Jin’s Ren Xigong and Wu’s acceptance of Wu Zixu both exemplify this. The *fan jian* (turned agent) operates by co-opting the enemy’s spies.

Li Quan said: "When the enemy sends spies to observe our strengths and weaknesses, we may bribe them heavily to turn them into our own agents."

Du Mu said: "When the enemy dispatches spies to observe us, we must first detect them. Either we entice them with generous rewards to serve us, or we feign ignorance, feed them false intelligence, and release them—thus the enemy’s spies become our own."

When Chen Ping first served as the Han King’s Chief Military Inspector, Xiang Yu besieged him at Rongyang. The Han King, distressed, offered to cede all lands west of Rongyang in exchange for peace, but Xiang Yu refused. Chen Ping said: "Yet there is a way to sow discord in Chu. Xiang Yu’s upright ministers—only the likes of Fan Zeng, Zhongli Mo, Long Ju, and Zhou Yin—number but a few. If Your Majesty can expend tens of thousands of *jin* of gold to spread rumors and divide their ruler and ministers, fostering mutual suspicion—Xiang Yu is by nature suspicious and prone to heeding slander—then they will surely turn on one another. The Han can then raise its armies and attack, and the destruction of Chu is assured." The Han King approved, allocating forty thousand *jin* of gold to Chen Ping, granting him free rein without questioning expenditures. Chen Ping distributed the gold liberally, disseminating rumors among the Chu forces: "Generals like Zhongli Mo have rendered great service to Xiang Yu, yet they will never be enfeoffed as kings or granted lands. They now seek to ally with Han to overthrow Xiang’s house and divide its territory among themselves." Xiang Yu indeed grew suspicious and dispatched envoys to Han. The Han prepared a grand feast, but upon seeing the Chu envoy, feigned shock: "I thought this was Fan Zeng’s envoy—it is Xiang Yu’s!" They then withdrew the feast and served the envoy coarse fare instead. The envoy returned and reported all to Xiang Yu, who grew deeply suspicious of Fan Zeng. Fan Zeng urged an immediate assault on Rongyang, but Xiang Yu, distrustful, refused to heed him. When Fan Zeng learned of Xiang Yu’s suspicions, he flew into a rage, spat blood, and died. In the end, Chen Ping’s stratagem was used to destroy Chu.

Mei Yaochen said: "Either deceive them with falsehoods or entice them with generous rewards."

Wang Xi said: "*Fan jian* means turning them into our own agents. Either detain them to extract intelligence or present them with deceptive appearances before sending them back."

He Shi said: "When King Zhao of Yan appointed Yue Yi as his general, Yue conquered over seventy cities of Qi. But upon King Hui’s ascension, he grew estranged from Yue Yi. Qi’s general Tian Dan then spread rumors in Yan: 'The Qi king is already dead, and only two cities remain unconquered. Yue Yi, fearing execution, dares not return. Under the pretext of attacking Qi, he truly seeks to amass troops, face south, and claim the throne of Qi. The Qi people have not yet submitted, so he delays at Jimo to await developments. What Qi fears most is the arrival of another general—then Jimo will fall.'" The Yan king believed this and replaced Yue Yi with Qi Jie. The Yan army’s morale collapsed. Tian Dan then spread further rumors: "We fear the Yan forces will desecrate our ancestral tombs outside the city and defile our forebears." The Yan troops did so, enraging the people of Jimo, who begged to fight. They crushed the Yan forces, recovering all seventy-odd lost cities.

When the Qin army besieged Zhao at Yeyu, Zhao’s general Zhao She came to its relief but halted thirty *li* from the Zhao capital without advancing. A Qin spy arrived, and Zhao She treated him well and sent him back. The spy reported to the Qin general that Zhao She’s army was timid and inactive. Zhao She then feigned retreat, swiftly armored his troops, and rushed the Qin forces, defeating them.

Later, when Fan Sui served as Chancellor to King Zhao of Qin, he dispatched Zuo Shuzhang Wang He to attack Han and seize Shangdang. The people of Shangdang fled to Zhao, and Zhao’s forces garrisoned Changping. Wang He then attacked Zhao. Zhao sent Lian Po to command its troops. Lian Po fortified his defenses and awaited the Qin assault. Qin repeatedly provoked battle, but the Zhao troops did not emerge. The Zhao king repeatedly reproached Lian Po. Meanwhile, Fan Sui sent agents to Zhao with a thousand *jin* of gold to spread rumors: "What Qin fears most is Zhao Kuo. Lian Po’s army is easily dealt with and is on the verge of surrender." The Zhao king, already angered by Lian Po’s losses and defeats, his passive defense, and the Qin rumors, replaced Lian Po with Zhao Kuo. Upon learning Zhao Kuo would command, Qin appointed Bai Qi as supreme general. Bai Qi slew Zhao Kuo and buried alive four hundred thousand surrendered Zhao soldiers.

Zhang Yu said: When enemy spies come, one may either bind them with generous bribes and lavish gifts, feeding them false words, or feign ignorance, treat them with cold indifference, and show them empty matters, so that when they return to report, it redounds to our advantage. Zhao She’s lavish feasting of Qin spies and the Han army’s feigned alarm before the Chu envoys are examples of this.

Dead spies are those who spread deceptive information abroad, allowing our own spies to learn of it and transmit it to enemy spies. Li Quan said: When truth and deception are indistinguishable, and I know it, I now act through these spies and await the outcome. Here, Quan takes *await* to mean *not transmit*. Du Mu said: *Deceive* means to fabricate. It means that when our spies are among the enemy but unaware of the true situation, we fabricate events, allowing our spies to rely on these false traces to convey sincerity to the enemy and gain their trust. If our actual movements differ from the fabricated traces, the spies cannot escape and will be killed by the enemy—hence, *dead spies*. When King Han sent Li Yiji to persuade Qi to surrender, Qi relaxed its defenses, and Han Xin then launched a surprise attack. Tian Heng, enraged, boiled Li Yiji alive—this case is similar.

Du You said: Fabricate deceptive matters abroad, feigning leaks so that our spies learn of them. When our spies reach the enemy camp and are captured, they will relay the fabricated information to the enemy, who will prepare accordingly. If our actual actions differ, the spies will die. It is also said: When enemy spies come and hear our fabricated information, they take it back, but it is not what we intend. Neither type of spy can discern hidden depths—hence, *dead spies*.

Xiao Shicheng said: Among captured enemy soldiers or our own deserters, those imprisoned for grave crimes may be pardoned, instructed not to leak information, and then feign carelessness, allowing enemy spies to overhear. We then release them to flee, and they will surely return to the enemy, who will trust them. Their mission will be fatal—hence, *dead spies*.

Mei Yaochen said: To deceive the enemy with falsehoods is to invite certain death.

Wang Xi said: Fabricate and leak it so the enemy obtains it; the spies relay our deception to the enemy, and once the matter is decided, they will surely be killed.

He Shi said: For example, when Duke Wu of Zheng wished to attack the Hu, he first married his daughter to the Hu ruler. He then asked his ministers, “I wish to use military force—whom may I attack?” Minister Guan Siqi said, “The Hu.” The duke flew into a rage and executed him, saying, “The Hu are a brotherly state—why do you speak of attacking them?” When the Hu ruler heard this, he took Zheng as a close ally and let down his guard. Zheng then launched a surprise attack and seized the Hu. This was the use of *dead spies*.

Again, Ban Chao mobilized the armies of Yutian and other states to strike the states of Shache and Kucha. He spread word that his forces were too few to prevail and disbanded them. Secretly, he detained a few captives and sent them back to report this. The king of Kucha rejoiced and let down his guard. Ban Chao then stealthily rallied his troops, rushed to Shache, and crushed them, forcing their surrender. This, too, was akin to *dead spies*.

Again, when Li Jing campaigned against the Eastern Turks, the Khagan, believing Tang Jian was still among the Turks and had arranged a marriage alliance, let down his guard. Li Jing then launched a surprise attack and routed them.

Zhang Yu said: To induce the enemy to kill their own worthy and capable men, dispatch doomed agents bearing falsehoods to them. When our spies reach the enemy and are captured, the enemy will take the fabricated information as truth and execute them all. In our dynasty, the Grand Commandant Cao once pardoned a condemned man, had him pose as a monk, and swallow wax pellets to smuggle them into Western Xia. Upon arrival, he was imprisoned. The monk revealed the pellets, and they were confiscated.

Upon opening the letter, it was revealed to be a missive sent to the enemy’s strategist. Enraged, the barbarian lord executed his minister and put the spy-monk to death as well. Such is the principle at work.

Yet the use of *dead spies* is not confined to this single method. At times, one may dispatch our spies to the enemy to propose peace, only to launch an attack upon their arrival—thus dooming the spies to immediate execution. Examples include Li Yi being boiled alive by the King of Qi and Tang Jian being slain by the Turks.

*Living spies* are those who return with intelligence. Li Quan states: "Envoys who come and go." Du Mu says: "Those who travel back and forth to exchange information." For *living spies*, one must select individuals who appear dull-witted outwardly but possess keen insight within, whose bearing may be unremarkable but whose resolve is unyielding, who are swift and vigorous, adept at menial tasks, and capable of enduring hunger, cold, filth, and humiliation. Jia Lin observes: "Though their bodies move in plain sight, their hearts conduct covert observation; they journey forth and return with reports, ever unharmed—hence they are called *living spies*." Du You adds: "Choose those among our ranks who possess talent and wisdom, who can ingratiate themselves with the enemy’s trusted advisors, observe their movements, and discern their affairs. By assessing their actions, we uncover their true intentions; then, returning to report, they are called *living spies*." Mei Yaochen says: "Send those of wit and eloquence to reconnoiter the enemy’s situation and return with intelligence." He Shi notes: "As when Hua Yuan ascended Ziyin’s couch and returned." Another example: During the time when Daxi Wu served as Prefect of Eastern Qin under the Sui, Emperor Shenwu of Qi advanced toward Shayuan. The Supreme Ancestor dispatched Wu to spy on him. Wu, accompanied by three riders, all clad in enemy attire, approached at dusk. Halting a few hundred paces from the camp, they dismounted and listened in secret, learning the military passwords. Then, mounting their horses, they rode through the encampment as if conducting night patrols; whenever they encountered those who violated regulations, they would strike them.

Having thoroughly learned the enemy’s dispositions, he reported them to the Supreme Ancestor, who deeply commended him and subsequently crushed the foe. Zhang Yu says: "Select men of intelligence and ability to observe the enemy’s situation and return with intelligence." Such was the case when Lou Jing discerned the strength of the Xiongnu and reported it to Emperor Gaozu.

Yet the work of living spies is manifold: at times, when one intends to retreat, one may inform the enemy of an impending battle; or when one intends to attack, one may inform the enemy of a retreat. For instance, a Qin envoy warned the Jin army by night: "Let us meet tomorrow." Yu Pian remarked: "The envoy’s darting eyes and unrestrained speech betray his fear of us." Qin indeed fled by night. Another example: Lü Yan attacked Qifu Qiangui and routed him. Qiangui then dispatched a spy to falsely claim he was fleeing east to Chengji. Yan, believing the report, pursued him. Geng Zhi cautioned: "The informant’s upward gaze and shifting countenance reveal a ruse." Yan ignored the warning and was defeated.

Thus, in all military affairs, none are closer than spies. Du Mu says: "Receiving their reports and tracing their steps occurs even within the commander’s private quarters." Du You observes: "If one does not treat them with intimacy and reward them generously, they may turn and serve the enemy, divulging our secrets." Mei Yaochen remarks: "Those who enter the tent to receive commands are most intimately trusted." Wang Xi states: "Bind them as confidants to one’s heart." Zhang Hen says: "Though all in the army are treated with care, only spies are entrusted as if to one’s very heart—this is the deepest intimacy."

No rewards are richer than those for spies. Du You notes: "Bestow lavish rewards upon them, relying on their service." Mei Yaochen says: "Titles, emoluments, gold, and silk—I spare nothing." Wang Xi states: "No military reward surpasses this." Zhang Yu observes: "Without high rank and generous benefits, one cannot employ spies." Chen Ping declared: "I wish to expend forty thousand catties of yellow gold to sow discord between the Chu ruler and his ministers."

No affairs are more secret than those of spies. Du Mu says: "Spoken in one’s ear, heard in another’s." (An alternate reading: "scrutinized.") Du You remarks: "If spycraft is not kept secret, it becomes self-harm." Mei Yaochen states: "If matters are not kept secret, harm will ensue." Wang Xi says: "Only the commander and the spy share the knowledge." Zhang Yu asks: "When only the commander and the spy are privy to the matter, is this not secrecy?"

None but the sagacious and wise can employ spies. Du Mu says: "First assess the spy’s nature—only those of integrity and intelligence may be used. Their demeanor may be thick, their emotions deep, more treacherous than mountains and rivers; none but a sage can fathom them." Mei Yaochen observes: "Discern their sincerity or deceit, distinguish their uprightness or perversity—only then can one employ them." Wang Xi states: "The sagacious perceive all, the wise foresee events." Zhang Yu says: "The sagacious leave no matter unpenetrated; the wise discern the subtlest signs—only then can one engage in spycraft." Some say: "Only the sagacious and wise can truly know others."

None but the benevolent and righteous can command spies. Chen Hao remarks: "The benevolent extend kindness to others; the righteous act with propriety. If a commander can bind spies with benevolence and direct them with righteousness, they will devote themselves to reconnaissance and gladly serve." The Meng clan notes: "As the Grand Duke said: 'The benevolent and righteous attract the worthy.' When the worthy are drawn to them, their spies may be employed." Mei Yaochen states: "Treat them with benevolence, demonstrate righteousness—only then can one command them." Wang Xi says: "Bind their hearts with kindness, rouse their resolve with righteousness—what cannot be achieved by employing righteousness to direct others?" Zhang Yu observes: "Benevolence spares no expense in rewards; righteousness acts decisively without hesitation. Entice them with generous benefits, treat them with utmost sincerity—then spies will exert themselves fully."

None but the subtle and profound can obtain the truth from spies. Du Mu says: "Spies may be swayed by wealth and treasure; if they do not secure the enemy’s true situation but instead bring false reports to fulfill their bargain, one must employ a mind of profound subtlety to discern their veracity or deceit." Du You remarks: "One’s intentions must be secret and unbreachable." Mei Yaochen states: "Guard against spies being turned by the enemy; thus, one’s deliberations must attain the most refined subtlety." Wang Xi says: "Spies must possess a nature and insight of exquisite subtlety to obtain the enemy’s true circumstances." Zhang Yu observes: "When spies report based on advantage or harm, one must employ a mind of profound subtlety and precision to discern their truth or falsehood."

How subtle! How subtle! There is nothing in which spies are not employed. Du Mu says: "Every matter must be known in advance." Mei Yaochen remarks: "The more subtle, the more nothing remains unknown." Wang Xi states: "This is emphasized: one must know the enemy’s situation in every affair." Zhang Yu says: "The more secret, the more no matter—great or small—remains unknown."

If spycraft is exposed before its execution, both the spy and the informant must die. Du Mu says: "If the informant is not luring the spy, they cannot learn the spy’s secrets—killing them is justified." Chen Hao remarks: "If a spy’s mission is exposed before execution and someone reports it, both the informant and the one who heard the report must be killed alongside the spy to silence them and prevent the enemy from learning of it." Mei Yaochen states: "Kill the spy to punish their leak; kill the informant to silence their words." He Shi says: "In military plans of great import, those who leak secrets must be executed; even those who report such leaks must be killed, lest the matter spread among the ranks." Zhang Yu observes: "When spying on the enemy, if the plan is set but not yet executed and someone suddenly reports it, both the spy and the informant must be killed—first to punish the leak, second to silence the witness.
