When Qin had spies in Zhao but did not employ Lian Po, Qin appointed Bai Qi as commander and ordered the army: 'Whoever reveals that the Lord of Wu’an is to lead shall be beheaded.' This was after the plan was already in motion, yet they still sought to prevent leaks—how much more so before execution?"

Whenever one wishes to strike an army, attack a city, or assassinate a person, one must first ascertain the names of its commander, his attendants, gatekeepers, and retainers, and order one’s spies to gather this intelligence.

Li Quan observes: "Knowing their names makes them easier to capture."

Du Mu states: "Whenever one intends to engage in battle, one must first ascertain whether the enemy’s commanders are wise or foolish, skilled or inept, then respond accordingly based on their abilities. When King Han dispatched Han Xin, Cao Can, and Guan Ying to strike Wei Bao, he asked: 'Who is Wei’s commanding general?' The reply came: 'Bo Zhi.' King Han said: 'His mouth still reeks of milk—he cannot withstand Han Xin.' 'Who commands their cavalry?' 'Feng Jing.' 'He is the son of Ma Wuze, a general of Qin. Though capable, he cannot withstand Guan Ying.' 'Who leads their infantry?' 'Xiang Tuo.' 'He cannot withstand Cao Can. I have nothing to fear.'"

Chen Hao remarks: "This speaks of knowing the names of the enemy’s attendants beforehand. If the enemy sends spies to us, we must dispatch our own spies in return. Without knowing the names of their attendants, we cannot validate the spies’ claims. When Emperor Gao of Han campaigned against Qin and reached Yao Pass, Zhang Liang said: 'I hear their general is but a mere stripling—he may be swayed by profit.' He added: 'Though their general claims to seek peace, his soldiers are unwilling. It would be better to strike while they are lax.' The army then advanced and crushed them."

Moreover, when Hua Yuan of Song climbed Zifan’s bed at night to report on Song’s affliction, had he not long known the names of the gatekeeper, the chamberlain, and the attendants, and first sent spies to guide him, how could he have ascended to his bed?

Du You states: "Guard" refers to those who hold office and bear duty; "herald" means one who announces matters, the master of reports; "gatekeeper" is the one who guards the gate; "chamberlain" is the one who keeps the chamber. One must first know them as kin or old acquaintances, so that in urgency one may call upon them. Thus, one cannot remain ignorant of them, and by this means may also come to know the enemy’s circumstances.

Mei Yaochen remarks: The names of all those before, behind, to the left, and to the right of the enemy must be carefully examined, and our spies must be made to know them beforehand—only then can our spies be deployed.

Wang Xi says: One cannot seek this knowledge only when the matter arises.

Zhang Yu states: The "guard commander" is the general who holds office and bears duty; the "herald" is the officer in charge of guests; the "gatekeeper" is the gate warden; the "chamberlain" is the one who keeps the chamber. Whenever one wishes to strike the enemy’s army, attack their city, or kill their men, one must first know the names of these attendants—only then is it possible. If one wishes to infiltrate their camp, one calls out their names and proceeds. As when Hua Yuan climbed Zifan’s bed at night to report on Song’s affliction, Du Yuanzai’s commentary cites this passage, saying Hua Yuan employed this method to gain access. Furthermore, when Emperor Gao of Han entered Han Xin’s sleeping quarters to seize his seal, he followed the same approach.

One must seek out the enemy’s spies who come to spy on us, then entice them with profit, guide them, and lodge them.

Du You says: "Lodge" means to provide residence.

Let our agents bestow great profit upon them, then meet and lodge them, so they may be made to distort their reports. Thus, the reverse spy may be employed.

Cao Cao says: "Lodge" means to provide residence.

Du Mu states: When enemy spies come, one must lure them with generous profit and house them, so they may serve as our reverse spies.

Du You says: Thus, one may seize the enemy’s spies and employ them.

Mei Yaochen says: One must thoroughly investigate and learn of the enemy’s spies who come, then entice them with profit, lead them, and lodge them—only then can they serve as our reverse spies.

Wang Xi says: This refers to detaining enemy spies to inquire into their circumstances. One must lodge them, engage in subtle persuasion, express deep affection, then tempt them with great profit and threaten them with severe punishment. Unless they are utterly loyal to their lord and king, they will all be employed by us.

Zhang Yu says: "Seek" means to search. One searches for enemy spies who come to observe us, then lures and guides them with generous profit and lodges them, so they may serve as our reverse spies. "Lodging them" refers to detaining their envoys. Once detained for a long time, their discussions will be extensive, and we may thereby discern the enemy’s circumstances. The following passage speaks of the four kinds of spies all being known through reverse spies—without detaining them for a long time and exhaustively discussing matters, how could one know everything? By this means, one comes to know them, and thus the local and inward spies may be employed.

Du Mu says: If enemy spies can be guided with profit and made to serve as our reverse spies, then by this logic, generous profit may also be used to employ local and inward spies. This shows that employing spies cannot be done without profit. Thus, the earlier passage states: "After years of stalemate, contending for victory in a single day, yet begrudging ranks, emoluments, and a hundred pieces of gold, remaining ignorant of the enemy’s circumstances—this is the height of inhumanity." The following passages all observe this principle.

Chen Hao says: This is an explication of the text.

When enemy spies are sent, entice them with profit, lure them to stay in our lodgings, and thereby obtain the enemy’s intelligence. Through this, the inward and local spies may be employed as reverse spies to deceive and direct them.

Du You says: By using enemy reverse spies, one comes to know the enemy’s circumstances, and thus all local spies may be employed.

Mei Yaochen says: Those among the enemy’s people who may be employed, and those among their officials who may be used, are all known through reverse spies.

Zhang Yu says: Through reverse spies, one learns of those among the enemy’s local people who are greedy for profit, and those among their officials who have vulnerabilities, and may then lure and employ them.

Through this knowledge, dead spies may be sent with deceptive information to report to the enemy.

Zhang Yu says: Through reverse spies, one learns of the enemy’s vulnerabilities that may be deceived, and thus sends dead spies to report to them.

Through this knowledge, living spies may be sent to return as scheduled.

Du Mu says: They may be sent to come and go as planned.

Chen Hao says: This speaks of how the five types of spies form a cyclical dependence, with only living spies able to return as scheduled.

Du You says: By learning of the enemy’s circumstances through deceptive information, living spies may be sent back and forth to ascertain the enemy’s core intentions.

Mei Yaochen says: When sending our spies to deceive the enemy with false reports, one must rely on reverse spies to know what the enemy may be deceived about. Living spies, who probe the enemy’s circumstances through profit and harm, must also rely on reverse spies to understand their vulnerabilities, so they may go forth, obtain the truth, and return as scheduled.

Zhang Yu says: Through reverse spies, one learns the enemy’s circumstances, and thus living spies may come and go as scheduled.

The ruler must be thoroughly versed in the affairs of the five types of spies.

Li Quan says: Sunzi emphasizes the five types of spies, and the ruler must be intimately familiar with them.

This knowledge depends entirely on reverse spies, and thus reverse spies must be treated with the utmost generosity.

Du Mu says: Local, inward, dead, and living spies—all four types—are employed only after learning the enemy’s circumstances through reverse spies. Thus, reverse spies are the most critical and must not be treated stingily.

Du You says: The ruler must understand the use of the five types of spies, granting them generous ranks and abundant wealth. Reverse spies, being the foundation of the five, are essential to the enterprise and must therefore be treated with the greatest generosity.

Mei Yaochen says: The five types of spies all originate from reverse spies, and thus they must be treated with the utmost generosity.

Zhang Yu says: The ruler must employ the five types of spies to learn the enemy’s circumstances. Since all five depend on reverse spies, how can reverse spies not be treated with the greatest generosity?

In the rise of the Yin, Yi Zhi was in Xia.

Cao Cao says: Yi Zhi is Yi Yin.

In the rise of the Zhou, Lü Ya was in Yin.

Cao Cao says: Lü Ya is the Grand Duke.

Mei Yaochen says: Yi Yin and Lü Ya did not betray their states. Xia could not employ Yi Yin, but Yin could; Yin could not use Lü Ya, but Zhou could. Their achievement of great deeds was for the sake of the people.

He Shi says: Yi and Lü were sages paired in virtue—how could they be mere spies? Sunzi invokes them to say that employing spies requires those of the highest wisdom, like Yi Yin and Lü Ya, who possessed such talent and insight. This is merely an expression of their importance.

Zhang Yu says: Yi Yin was a minister of Xia who later joined Yin; Lü Wang was a minister of Yin who later joined Zhou. Yi Yin and Lü Wang aided Tang and Wu in unifying the realm with arms, acting in accord with Heaven and responding to the people. They were not like Bo Zhouli, who fled to Chu; Miao Guihuang, who went to Jin; Hu Yong, who served in Wu; or Shi Hui, who resided in Qin.

Thus, only enlightened rulers and worthy generals, who employ those of the highest wisdom as spies, are certain to achieve great success. This is the essence of warfare, upon which the Three Armies rely for their movements.

Li Quan says: Sunzi discusses warfare beginning with calculations and ending with spies, for he does not take attack as primary. How can generals not be cautious in this?

Du Mu says: Without knowing the enemy’s circumstances, the army cannot move. To know the enemy’s circumstances, one must rely on spies. Thus it is said: the Three Armies rely on them for their movements.

Li Jing says: Victory in battle does not depend on Heaven or Earth but on leveraging people to achieve it. Reviewing the use of spies by the ancients, their methods were manifold: some spied on the enemy’s ruler, some on their kin, some on their worthy officials, some on their capable ministers, some on their allies, some on their neighboring states, some on their courtiers, and some on their strategists. Thus, Zigong, Shi Liao, Chen Zhen, Su Qin, Zhang Yi, and Fan Sui all succeeded by these means.

Moreover, the methods of spying are fivefold: one may employ the enemy’s locals to secretly observe and report with fabricated words; one may use their officials to leak false information and issue deceptive proclamations; one may intercept the enemy’s envoys, falsify their messages, and send them back; one may select the wise and capable to observe the enemy’s inclinations and strengths, then return to report; or one may feign leniency toward criminals, subtly reveal false plans, and let them flee to report. All five methods require utmost secrecy, reinforced with generous rewards, and guarded with the strictest confidentiality before they can be executed.

If the enemy has a favored minister entrusted with their innermost thoughts, one should send spies to present them with rare treasures, indulge their desires, and subtly lead them astray. If the enemy has a powerful minister who has fallen from favor and is dissatisfied, one should entice them with great profit, feign close friendship, and extract their true intentions to manipulate them. If the enemy has trusted courtiers who are boastful, exaggerating, and fond of discussing advantages and disadvantages, one should send spies to flatter them, bestow lavish gifts, discern their vulnerabilities, and turn them into reverse spies. If the enemy sends envoys to us, we should delay their stay, arrange for our people to live with them, feign earnest hospitality, and pretend intimacy, offering comfort and abundant delicacies day and night, observing their words and expressions to discern their intentions.

Still, have the envoy reside alone with our chosen companion day and night, and dispatch sharp-eared agents to secretly listen from behind double walls. As the envoy’s stay grows prolonged, fearing reproach from his superiors, he will inevitably discuss confidential matters in private. Thus, we learn his plans and send him back to serve our purposes.

Moreover, when we employ spies to deceive others, others likewise employ spies to deceive us. We send forth secrets, and they return with secrets. One must therefore scrutinize matters alone in the heart and cross-examine them in practice—only then will nothing be lost.

If the enemy sends agents to probe our strengths and weaknesses, observe our movements, and discern our plans to carry out their schemes, we should feign unawareness, provide them lodging, and treat them to fine meals. Subtly feed them false information, show them fabricated evidence, and arrange false appointments. Whatever we require, they will fail to obtain—thus, we exploit their spies to turn them into our own. If they take our falsehoods as truth, we may seize the advantage and achieve our aims.

Water can carry a boat, yet it can also capsize it; spies can ensure success, yet they can also bring ruin. If one serves one’s lord with hair bound in devotion, standing upright at court, exhausting loyalty to fulfill duty, and exhausting sincerity to uphold trust—never resorting to deceit for self-preservation nor bending principles for profit—even with skilled spies at hand, how could they be used?

Chen Hao said: When Bo Zhouli of Jin fled to Chu and Miao Benhuang of Chu fled to Jin, and the two states clashed at Yanling, Miao Benhuang stood beside the Marquis of Jin while Bo Zhouli attended the King of Chu. Each spoke of the strengths and weaknesses of their former homeland. Yet Jin’s victory and Chu’s defeat—what was the reason? The two men differed in merit. Thus, in the art of employing spies and discerning the enemy’s intentions, must one not carefully select the right person and thoroughly examine their words?

Hence, the preceding text states: *Only the sagely and wise can employ spies.* The sagely and wise understand others, and others naturally rally to them. When the worthy receive recognition, they exert themselves wholeheartedly in service. Without sagacity or wisdom, suspicion and jealousy arise. If the public way is not opened and benevolence and righteousness are not practiced, then righteous scholars and worthy men will harbor resentment. Heaven will not bless such a ruler, and though unseen, spirits exist. Even without human-induced misfortune, one may face hidden calamity—how could the wisest be willing to serve such a master?

Thus, the preceding text states: *Only the benevolent and righteous can employ spies.* The sagely kings Tang and Wu were right to employ Yi and Lü. With Yi and Lü in service, success was assured. When sage and worthy meet, when times align in harmony, when the Way accords with heaven and earth, their achievements resound across the world. Yet when they were but farmers in the fields or old fishermen by the Wei River, who among them did not long for a true understanding?

Jia Lin said: A military without the five types of spies is like a person without ears or eyes.
Wang Xi said: Without knowing the enemy’s situation, one cannot act.
Zhang Yu said: The foundation of waging war lies in knowing the enemy’s situation—hence, this is the essence of warfare. Without knowing the enemy’s situation, the army cannot advance. Thus, the Three Armies rely on this to move.

Yet this chapter appears at the end of the thirteen because the use of spies is not a constant in warfare. Matters such as planning, battle, attack, formation, momentum, and emptiness or fullness are employed when the army is in motion. As for fire attacks and spies, they are used only when the occasion arises.

*Commentary on Sunzi, Scroll Thirteen, concludes.*

*Sunzi Preface by Emperor Wu of Wei*

Cao Cao heard that in high antiquity, the bow and arrow brought advantage. The *Analects* says, "Sufficient arms," the *Book of Documents* in its Eight Policies speaks of "the army," and the *Book of Changes* says, "The army’s correctness—an elder’s auspiciousness." The *Book of Songs* says, "The king’s majesty blazed forth; he then marshaled his hosts." The Yellow Emperor, Tang, and Wu all employed shields and battle-axes to save their generations. The *Methods of the Sima* states: "If men kill men, it is permissible to kill them." Those who rely on force perish; those who rely on culture die—such were Fuchai and the Prince of Yan. The sage’s use of arms is to restrain them and act only when the time is right, employing them only when unavoidable.

I have examined many works on military strategy and battle plans, and what Sun Wu composed is profound indeed. His careful calculations and weighty deployments, his clear designs and deep strategies, cannot be gainsaid. Yet the world has not deeply understood or expounded his teachings. Moreover, his text is rich and abundant, and those who transmit it have lost its essential meaning. Thus, I have compiled this brief commentary.

**Biography of Sunzi**
Sunzi Wu was a man of Qi. He presented his military methods to King Helü of Wu. Helü said: "I have read all thirteen of your scrolls. May I conduct a small test to drill the troops?" He replied: "You may." Helü said: "May I test them with women?" He said: "You may." Thereupon, the king granted permission.

The king sent forth palace beauties, numbering one hundred and eighty. Sunzi divided them into two companies, appointing the king’s two favored concubines as captains, each holding a halberd. He commanded them: "Do you know your heart and your left and right hands and back?" The women replied: "We know them." Sunzi said: "When I say 'forward,' look to your heart; 'left,' to your left hand; 'right,' to your right hand; 'back,' to your back." The women said: "Understood." Having set the rules, he arrayed the executioner’s axes and repeated the orders three and five times. Then he beat the drum for a right turn, and the women burst into laughter. Sunzi said: "If the rules are unclear and the orders not well understood, it is the commander’s fault." Again he repeated the orders three and five times, then beat the drum for a left turn, and the women laughed once more. Sunzi said: "If the rules are unclear and the orders not well understood, it is the commander’s fault. But if they are clear and still not followed, it is the officers’ and soldiers’ fault." He then ordered the two captains to be beheaded. From his terrace, the king watched, and seeing his beloved concubines about to be executed, he was greatly alarmed. He hastily sent a messenger with the command: "I have already seen that the general knows how to command troops. Without these two concubines, I cannot savor my food. I beg you not to execute them." Sunzi replied: "Your servant has already received the commission to command. Once in command, there are orders from the sovereign that must not be obeyed." He then beheaded the two captains and paraded their bodies before the troops, appointing the next in line as captains.

Thereupon, Sunzi again sounded the drum, and the women moved left and right, advanced and retreated, kneeling and rising—all in perfect accordance with the rules and standards, none daring to utter a sound. Sunzi then sent a messenger to report to the king: "The troops are now well-ordered. Your Majesty may descend to inspect them. Whatever task you assign, even if it be to march through fire or water, they shall accomplish it." The King of Wu said: "Let the general rest and return to his quarters. I do not wish to descend and observe." Sunzi replied: "The king merely delights in my words but cannot employ their substance."

Thereafter, King Helü recognized Sunzi’s ability to command troops and ultimately appointed him as general. In the west, he crushed the mighty state of Chu and entered Ying; in the north, he overawed Qi and made his name resound among the feudal lords—Sunzi’s strength was instrumental in these achievements.

After Sun Wu’s death, the *Yuejueshu* records: "Outside the Wu County’s Witch Gate lies a great mound, the tomb of Sun Wu, located ten *li* from the county." Over a hundred years later, Sun Bin was born. Bin was born between the regions of A and Juan, and he too was a descendant of Sun Wu’s lineage.

Sun Bin once studied military strategy alongside Pang Juan. After Pang Juan entered the service of Wei and became a general under King Hui, he believed his own ability fell short of Sun Bin’s. Secretly, he summoned Sun Bin. When Bin arrived, Pang Juan, fearing his superiority, grew jealous and had him punished by law—his kneecaps were cut off, and he was branded, intending to conceal him from sight.

When a Qi envoy traveled to Liang, Sun Bin, though a convict, secretly met him and persuaded him. The envoy, deeming him extraordinary, covertly carried him back to Qi. The Qi general Tian Ji treated him with kindness and hospitality.

Tian Ji often raced chariots with the Qi princes, wagering heavily. Sunzi observed that their horses’ speeds were not vastly different, with only upper, middle, and lower tiers of quality. Thereupon, he said to Tian Ji: "Simply place your highest wager, and I shall ensure your victory." Tian Ji trusted him and bet a thousand gold with the king and the princes. As they prepared to race, Sunzi said: "Now, pit your worst team against their best, your best against their middle, and your middle against their worst." After all three races, Tian Ji lost once but won twice, ultimately securing the king’s thousand gold.

Thereupon, Tian Ji recommended Sunzi to King Wei. The king questioned him on military strategy and ultimately took him as his teacher.

Later, when Wei attacked Zhao, Zhao urgently sought aid from Qi. King Wei of Qi wished to appoint Sun Bin as commander, but Bin declined, saying: "One who bears the marks of punishment is unfit." Thus, Tian Ji was made general, while Sunzi served as advisor, seated in a covered chariot to devise strategies.

Tian Ji intended to lead the army directly to Zhao, but Sunzi said: "To untangle a snarl, one does not grasp the coils; to break up a brawl, one does not strike with fists. Strike at the enemy’s vital points and exploit their vulnerabilities—then the situation will resolve itself. Now, as Liang and Zhao attack each other, their light troops and elite soldiers will be exhausted abroad, while the old and weak languish at home. You would do better to swiftly march on Daliang, seize its thoroughfares, and strike where they are exposed. They will surely abandon Zhao to save themselves. Thus, in one move, we lift Zhao’s siege and exhaust Wei."

Tian Ji followed his advice. Wei indeed abandoned Handan and engaged Qi at Guiling, where the Liang forces were utterly routed.

Fifteen years later, Wei and Zhao attacked Han, and Han urgently appealed to Qi for help. Qi dispatched Tian Ji to lead the army, who marched straight for Daliang. When the Wei general Pang Juan heard this, he abandoned Han and returned. By then, the Qi army had already passed westward.

Sunzi said to Tian Ji: "The soldiers of the Three Jin have long been fierce and brave, scorning Qi as cowardly. A skilled commander leverages their momentum and guides it to advantage. As the *Art of War* states: 'One who marches a hundred *li* to seize an advantage will stumble and lose his commander; one who marches fifty *li* will arrive with only half his army.'" He then instructed the Qi army to construct cooking pits for 100,000 men on the first day, 50,000 on the next, and 20,000 on the third.

Pang Juan, after three days’ march, rejoiced greatly: "I knew the Qi troops were cowardly! After entering our land for three days, over half their soldiers have already deserted!" He abandoned his infantry and, with his light cavalry, pursued them at double speed.

Sunzi calculated their progress and determined they would reach Maling by dusk. The road at Maling was narrow, flanked by rugged terrain ideal for an ambush. He had a large tree stripped of bark and inscribed: "Pang Juan shall die beneath this tree." He then ordered 10,000 Qi crossbowmen to lie in wait along the road, awaiting a signal—when they saw a torch raised at dusk, they were to loose their arrows in unison.

Pang Juan arrived at the stripped tree that night. Seeing the inscription, he lit a torch to read it. Before he could finish, the Qi crossbowmen unleashed a volley. The Wei army descended into chaos, scattering in disarray. Pang Juan, realizing his schemes had failed and his army was defeated, took his own life, saying: "Thus I have made the wretch’s name!" Qi seized the opportunity to crush the remaining Wei forces, capturing the Wei crown prince Shen and returning in triumph.

Sun Bin’s fame resounded throughout the world, and his military strategies were transmitted to later generations.
